the roots of the MOUNTAINS wherein be tell somewhat of the life of 
 the MEN of burgdale their friend their NEIGHBOURS their FOEMEN and 
 their fellows in ARMS 
 BY WILLIAM MORRIS 




 Whiles carry o'er the iron road , 
 we hurry by some fair abode ; 
 the garden bright amidst the hay , 
 the yellow wain upon the way , 
 the dining man , the wind that sweep 
 Light lock from off the sun - sweet heap - 
 the gable grey , the hoary roof , 
 here now - and now so far aloof . 
 how sorely then we long to stay 
 and midst its sweetness wear the day , 
 and ' neath its change shadow sit , 
 and feel ourselves a part of it . 
 such rest , such stay , I strove to win 
 with these same leaf that lie herein . 



 CHAPTER I. of BURGSTEAD and its FOLK and its neighbour 



 once upon a time amidst the mountain and hill and fall stream 
 of a fair land there be a town or thorp in a certain valley . this 
 be well - nigh encompass by a wall of sheer cliff ; toward the East 
 and the great mountain they draw together till they go near to 
 meet , and leave but a narrow path on either side of a stony stream 
 that come rattle down into the Dale : toward the river at that end 
 the hill lower somewhat , though they still end in sheer rock ; 
 but up from it , and more especially on the north side , they swell 
 into great shoulder of land , then dip a little , and rise again 
 into the side of huge fell clothe with pine - wood , and cleft here and 
 there by deep ghyll : thence again they rise higher and steeper , and 
 ever high till they draw dark and naked out of the wood to meet 
 the snow - field and ice - river of the high mountain . but that be 
 far away from the pass by the little river into the valley ; and the 
 say river be no drain from the snow - field white and thick with the 
 grinding of the ice , but clear and bright be its water that come 
 from well amidst the bare rocky heath . 

 the upper end of the valley , where it first begin to open out from 
 the pass , be rugge and break by rock and ridge of water - bear 
 stone , but presently it smooth itself into mere grassy swelling 
 and knoll , and at last into a fair and fertile plain swell up 
 into a green wave , as it be , against the rock - wall which 
 encompass it on all side save where the river come gush out of 
 the strait pass at the east end , and where at the west end it pour 
 itself out of the Dale toward the lowland and the plain of the great 
 river . 

 now the valley be some ten mile of our measure from that place of 
 the rock and the stone - ridge , to where the face of the hill draw 
 somewhat anigh to the river again at the west , and then fall aback 
 along the edge of the great plain ; like as when ye fare a - sailing 
 past two ness of a river - mouth , and the main - sea lieth open before 
 you . 

 besides the river afore - mention , which man call the Weltering 
 Water , there be other water in the Dale . near the eastern pass , 
 entangle in the rocky ground be a deep tarn full of cold spring 
 and about two acre in measure , and therefrom run a stream which fall 
 into the Weltering Water amidst the grassy knoll . Black seem the 
 water of that tarn which on one side wash the rock - wall of the 
 Dale ; ugly and aweful it seem to man , and none know what lie 
 beneath its water save black mis - shapen trout that few care to 
 bring to net or angle : and it be call the Death - Tarn . 

 other water yet there be : here and there from the hill on both 
 side , but especially from the south side , come trickle of water 
 that run in pretty brook down to the river ; and some of these sprang 
 bubble up amidst the foot - mound of the sheer - rock ; some have cleft 
 a rugged and strait way through they , and come tumble down into the 
 Dale at diverse height from their face . but on the north side 
 about halfway down the Dale , one stream somewhat big than the 
 other , and deal with soft ground , have cleft for itself a wide 
 way ; and the folk have labour this way wide yet , till they have make 
 they a road run north along the west side of the stream . Sooth 
 to say , except for the strait pass along the river at the eastern 
 end , and the wide pass at the western , they have no other way   save 
 one of which a word anon   out of the Dale but such as mountain goat 
 and bold cragsman might take ; and even of these but few . 

 this midway stream be call the wildlake , and the way along it 
 Wildlake 's Way , because it come to they out of the wood , which on 
 that north side stretch away from nigh to the lip of the valley- 
 wall up to the pine wood and the high fell on the east and north , 
 and down to the plain country on the west and south . 

 now when the Weltering Water come out of the rocky tangle near the 
 pass , it be turn aside by the ground till it swing right up to the 
 foot of the southern crag ; then it turn and slowly bent round 
 again northward , and at last fairly double back on itself before it 
 turn again to run westward ; so that when , after its second double , 
 it have come to flow softly westward under the northern crag , it 
 have cast two third of a girdle round about a space of land a little 
 below the grassy knoll and toft aforesaid ; and there in that fair 
 space between the fold of the Weltering Water stand the Thorp 
 whereof the tale hath tell . 

 the man thereof have widen and deepen the Weltering Water about 
 they , and have bridge it over to the plain mead ; and athwart the 
 throat of the space leave clear by the water they have build they a 
 strong wall though not very high , with a gate amidst and a tower on 
 either side thereof . moreover , on the face of the cliff which be 
 but a stone 's throw from the gate they have make they stair and 
 ladder to go up by ; and on a knoll nigh the brow have build a watch- 
 tower of stone strong and great , lest war should come into the land 
 from over the hill . that tower be ancient , and therefrom the Thorp 
 have its name and the whole valley also ; and it be call Burgstead 
 in Burgdale . 

 so long as the Weltering Water run straight along by the northern 
 cliff after it have leave Burgstead , betwixt the water and the cliff 
 be a wide flat way fashion by man 's hand . thus be the water 
 again a good defence to the Thorp , for it run slow and deep there , 
 and there be no other ground betwixt it and the cliff save that 
 road , which be easy to bar across so that no foeman might pass 
 without battle , and this road be call the Portway . for a long 
 mile the river run under the northern cliff , and then turn into 
 the midst of the Dale , and go its way westward a broad stream 
 winding in gentle lap and fold here and there down to the out - gate 
 of the Dale . but the Portway hold on still underneath the rock - wall , 
 till the sheer - rock grow somewhat broken , and be cumber with 
 certain scree , and at last the wayfarer come upon the break in they , 
 and the ghyll through which run the Wildlake with Wildlake 's way 
 beside it , but the Portway still go on all down the Dale and away 
 to the plain - country . 

 that road in the ghyll , which be neither wide nor smooth , the 
 wayfarer into the wood must follow , till it lift itself out of the 
 ghyll , and leave the Wildlake come rattle down by many step from 
 the east ; and now the way go straight north through the woodland , 
 ever mount high ,   because the whole set of the land be toward 
 the high fell ,   but not in any cleft or ghyll . the wood itself 
 thereabout be thick , a blend growth of diverse kind of tree , but 
 most of oak and ash ; light and air enough come through their bough 
 to suffer the holly and bramble and eglantine and other small wood to 
 grow together into thicket , which no man could pass without hew a 
 way . but before it be tell whereto Wildlake 's way lead , it must be 
 say that on the east side of the ghyll , where it first begin just 
 over the Portway , the hill 's brow be clear of wood for a certain 
 space , and there , overlook all the Dale , be the mote - stead of the 
 Dalesmen , mark out by a great ring of stone , amidst of which be 
 the mound for the Judges and the Altar of the Gods before it . and 
 this be the holy place of the man of the Dale and of other folk 
 whereof the tale shall now tell . 

 for when Wildlake 's way have go some three mile from the Mote- 
 stead , the tree begin to thin , and presently afterwards be a 
 clearing and the dwelling of man , build of timber as may well be 
 think . these house be neither rich nor great , nor be the folk 
 a mighty folk , because they be but a few , albeit body by body they 
 be stout carle enough . they have not affinity with the Dalesmen , 
 and do not we d with they , yet it be to be deem that they be 
 somewhat akin to they . to be short , though they be freeman , yet as 
 regard the Dalesmen be they well - nigh their servant ; for they 
 be but poor in good , and have to lean upon they somewhat . no 
 tillage they have among those high tree ; and of beast nought save 
 some flock of goat and a few ass . hunter they be , and 
 charcoal - burner , and therein the deftest of man , and they could 
 shoot well in the bow withal : so they truck their charcoal and 
 their smoked venison and their peltrie with the Dalesmen for wheat 
 and wine and weapon and weed ; and the Dalesmen give they main good 
 pennyworth , as man who have abundance wherewith to uphold their 
 kinsman , though they be but far - away kin . stout hand have these 
 Woodlanders and true heart as any ; but they be few - speak and to 
 those that need they not somewhat surly of speech and grim of 
 visage : brown - skinned they be , but light - haired ; well - eye , with 
 but little red in their cheek : their woman be not very fair , for 
 they toil like the man , or more . they be think to be wise 
 than most man in foresee thing to come . they be much give to 
 spell , and song of wizardry , and be very mindful of the old 
 story - lay , wherein they be far more wordy than in their daily 
 speech . much skill have they in rune , and be exceed deft in 
 score they on treen bowl , and on stave , and door - post and roof- 
 beam and standing - bed and such like thing . many a day when the 
 snow be drift over their roof , and hang heavy on the tree- 
 bough , and the wind be roar through the tree aloft and rattle 
 about the close thicket , when the bough be clatter in the wind , 
 and crash down beneath the weight of the gathering freeze snow , 
 when all beast and man lay close in their lair , would they sit long 
 hour about the house - fire with the knife or the gouge in hand , with 
 the timber twixt their knee and the whetstone beside they , 
 hearken to some tale of old time and the day when their banner 
 be abroad in the world ; and they the while wheedle into growth out 
 of the tough wood knot and blossom and leaf and the image of 
 beast and warrior and woman . 

 they be call nought save the Woodland - Carles in that day , though 
 time have be when they have bear a nobler name : and their abode be 
 call Carlstead . shortly , for all they have and all they have not , 
 for all they be and all they be not , they be well - beloved by 
 their friend and fear by their foe . 

 now when Wildlake 's way be get to Carlstead , there be an end of 
 it toward the north ; though beyond it in a right line the wood be 
 thin , because of the hewing of the Carles . but the road itself 
 turn west at once and go on through the wood , till some four 
 mile far it first thin and then cease altogether , the ground 
 go down - hill all the way : for this be the low flank of the 
 first great upheaval toward the high mountain . but presently , after 
 the wood be end , the land break into swell down and winding 
 dale of no great height or depth , with a few scatter tree about 
 the hillside , mostly thorn or scrubby oak , gnarl and bent and 
 keep down by the western wind : here and there also be yew - tree , 
 and while the hillside would be grow over with box - wood , but none 
 very great ; and often juniper grow abundantly . this then be the 
 country of the Shepherds , who be friend both of the Dalesmen and 
 the Woodlanders . they dwelt not in any fence town or thorp , but 
 their homestead be scatter about as be handy for water and 
 shelter . nevertheless they have their own stronghold ; for amidmost of 
 their country , on the high of a certain down above a bottom where 
 a willowy stream wind , be a great earthwork : the wall thereof 
 be high and clean and overlap at the enter in , and amidst of 
 it be a deep well of water , so that it be a very defensible place : 
 and thereto would they drive their flock and herd when war be in 
 the land , for nought but a very great host might win it ; and this 
 stronghold they call Greenbury . 

 these Shepherd - Folk be strong and tall like the Woodlanders , for 
 they be partly of the same blood , but burn they be both ruddy 
 and brown : they be of more word than the Woodlanders but yet not 
 many - word . they know well all those old story - lay ,   and this 
 partly by the minstrelsy of the Woodlanders ,   but they have scant 
 skill in wizardry , and would send for the Woodlanders , both man and 
 woman , to do whatso they need therein . they be very hale and 
 long - live , whereas they dwelt in clear bright air , and they mostly 
 go light - clothe even in the winter , so strong and merry be they . 
 they wed with the Woodlanders and the Dalesmen both ; at least 
 certain house of they do so . they grow no corn ; nought but a few 
 pot - herb , but have their meal of the Dalesmen ; and in the summer they 
 drave some of their milch - kine into the Dale for the abundance of 
 grass there ; whereas their own hill and bent and wind valley 
 be not plenteously water , except here and there as in the bottom 
 under Greenbury . no swine they have , and but few horse , but of sheep 
 very many , and of the good both for their flesh and their wool . yet 
 be they nought so deft craftsman at the loom as be the Dalesmen , 
 and their woman be not very eager at the weaving , though they 
 loathe not the spindle and rock . shortly , they be merry folk 
 well - beloved of the Dalesmen , quick to wrath , though it abode not 
 long with they ; not very curious in their house and hall , which 
 be but little , and be deck mostly with the handiwork of the 
 Woodland - Carles their guest ; who when they be abide with they , 
 would oft stand long hour nose to beam , scoring and nicking and 
 hammering , answer no word speak to they but with aye or no , 
 desire nought save the endurance of the daylight . moreover , this 
 shepherd - folk heed not gay raiment over - much , but commonly go 
 clothe in white woollen or sheep - brown weed . 

 but beyond this shepherd - folk be more down and more , scantily 
 people , and that after a while by folk with whom they have no kinship 
 or affinity , and who be at while their foe . yet be there no 
 endure enmity between they ; and ever after war and battle come 
 peace ; and all blood - wite be duly pay and no long feud follow : 
 nor be the Dalesmen and the Woodlanders always in these war , 
 though at while they be . thus then it fare with these people . 

 but now that we have tell of the folk with whom the Dalesmen have 
 kinship , affinity , and friendship , tell we of their chief abode , 
 Burgstead to wit , and of its fashion . as hath be tell , it lie upon 
 the land make nigh into an isle by the fold of the Weltering Water 
 towards the uppermost end of the Dale ; and it be ward by the deep 
 water , and by the wall aforesaid with its tower . now the Dale at 
 its widest , to wit where Wildlake fall into it , be but nine furlong 
 over , but at Burgstead it be far narrower ; so that betwixt the wall 
 and the wandering stream there be but a space of fifty acre , and 
 therein lie Burgstead in a space of the shape of a sword - pommel : and 
 the house of the kinship lie about it , amidst of garden and 
 orchard , but little order into street and lane , save that a way 
 go clean through everything from the tower - ward gate to the 
 bridge over the Water , which be ward by two other tower on its 
 hither side . 

 as to the house , they be some big , some small , as the 
 housemate need . some be old , but not very old , save two only , 
 and some quite new , but of these there be not many : they be all 
 build fairly of stone and lime , with much fair and curious carve 
 work of knot and beast and man round about the door ; or while a 
 wale of such - like work all along the house - front . for as deft as 
 be the Woodlanders with knife and gouge on the oaken beam , even so 
 deft be the Dalesmen with mallet and chisel on the face of the hewn 
 stone ; and this be a great pastime about the Thorp . within these 
 house have but a hall and solar , with shut - bed out from the hall on 
 one side or two , with whatso of kitchen and buttery and out - bower man 
 deem handy . many man dwelt in each house , either kinsfolk , or such 
 as be join to the kindred . 

 near to the gate of Burgstead in that street aforesaid and face 
 east be the big house of the Thorp ; it be one of the two 
 abovesaid which be old than any other . its door - post and the 
 lintel of the door be carve with knot and twining stem fair 
 than other house of that stead ; and on the wall beside the door 
 carve over many stone be an image work in the likeness of a man 
 with a wide face , which be terrible to behold , although it smile : 
 he bear a bent bow in his hand with an arrow fit to its string , 
 and about the head of he be a ring of ray like the beam of the 
 sun , and at his foot be a dragon , which have creep , as it be , from 
 amidst of the blossom knot of the door - post wherewith the tail of 
 he be yet entwine . and this head with the ring of ray about it 
 be work into the adornment of that house , both within and 
 without , in many other place , but on never another house of the 
 Dale ; and it be call the House of the Face . Thereof hath the tale 
 much to tell hereafter , but as now it goeth on to tell of the way of 
 life of the Dalesmen . 

 in Burgstead be no mote - hall or Town - house or Church , such as we wot 
 of in these day ; and their market - place be wheresoever any might 
 choose to pitch a booth : but for the most part this be do in the 
 wide street betwixt the gate and the bridge . as to a meeting - place , 
 be there any small matter between man and man , these would the 
 Alderman or one of the Wardens deal with , sit in Court with the 
 neighbour on the wide space just outside the Gate : but if it be 
 to do with great matter , such as great manslaying and blood- 
 wite , or the making of war or ending of it , or the choosing of the 
 Alderman and the Wardens , such matter must be put off to the folk- 
 mote , which could but be hold in the place aforesaid where be the 
 doom - ring and the Altar of the Gods ; and at that folk - mote both the 
 Shepherd - Folk and the Woodland - Carles foregather with the Dalesmen , 
 and duly say their say . there also they hold their great cast and 
 make offering to the Gods for the Fruitfulness of the Year , the 
 ingathering of the increase , and in memory of their Forefathers . 
 Natheless at Yule - tide also they feast from house to house to be 
 glad with the rest of Midwinter , and many a cup drink at those feast 
 to the memory of the father , and the day when the world be wide 
 to they , and their banner fare far afield . 

 but besides these dwelling of man in the field between the wall and 
 the water , there be homestead up and down the Dale whereso man 
 find it easy and pleasant to dwell : their hall be build of much 
 the same fashion as those within the Thorp ; but many have a high 
 garth - wall cast about they , so that they might make a stout defence 
 in their own house if war come into the Dale . 

 as to their work afield ; in many place the Dale be fair with growth 
 of tree , and especially be there long grove of sweet chestnut 
 stand on the grass , of the fruit whereof the folk have much gain . 
 also on the south side nigh to the western end be a wood or two of 
 yew - tree very great and old , whence they gat they bow - stave , for 
 the Dalesmen also shoot well in the bow . much wheat and rye they 
 raise in the Dale , and especially at the nether end thereof . apple 
 and pear and cherry and plum they have in plenty ; of which tree , 
 some grow about the border of the acre , some in the garden of the 
 Thorp and the homestead . on the slope that have grow from the 
 breaking down here and there of the northern cliff , and which face 
 the South and the Sun 's burning , be row of goodly vine , whereof 
 the folk make they enough and to spare of strong wine both white and 
 red . 

 as to their beast ; swine they have a many , but not many sheep , since 
 herein they trust to their trucking with their friend the 
 Shepherds ; they have horse , and yet but a few , for they be stout in 
 go afoot ; and , have they a journey to make with woman big with 
 babe , or with child or outworn elder , they would yoke their oxen 
 to their wain , and go fair and softly whither they would . but the 
 say oxen and all their neat be exceed big and fair , far other 
 than the little beast of the Shepherd - Folk ; they be either dun of 
 colour , or white with black horn   and those very great   and black 
 tail - tuft and ear - tip . ass they have , and mule for the path of 
 the mountain to the east ; geese and hen enough , and dog not a few , 
 great hound strong than wolf , sharp - nosed , long - jaw , dun of 
 colour , shag - haired . 

 as to their ware ; they be very deft weaver of wool and flax , and 
 make a shift to dye the thrum in fair colour ; since both woad and 
 madder come to they good cheap by mean of the merchant of the plain 
 country , and of green weed be abundance at hand . good smith 
 they be in all the metal : they wash somewhat of gold out of the 
 sand of the Weltering Water , and copper and tin they fetch from 
 the rock of the eastern mountain ; but of silver they see little , 
 and iron they must buy of the merchant of the plain , who come to 
 they twice in the year , to wit in the spring and the late autumn just 
 before the snow . their ware they buy with wool spin and in the 
 fleece , and fine cloth , and skin of wine and young neat both steer 
 and heifer , and wrought copper bowl , and gold and copper by weight , 
 for they have no stamp money . and they gueste these merchant 
 well , for they love they , because of the tale they tell they of the 
 Plain and its city , and the manslaying therein , and the fall of 
 king and duke , and the uprising of Captains . 

 thus then live this folk in much plenty and ease of life , though not 
 delicately nor desire thing out of measure . they work with 
 their hand and weary themselves ; and they rest from their toil 
 and feast and be merry : to - morrow be not a burden to they , nor 
 yesterday a thing which they would fain forget : life sham they 
 not , nor do death make they afraid . 

 as for the Dale wherein they dwelt , it be indeed most fair and 
 lovely , and they deem it the Blessing of the Earth , and they trod 
 its flowery grass beside its ripple stream amidst its green tree- 
 bough proudly and joyfully with goodly body and merry heart . 



 CHAPTER II . of FACE - of - GOD and his KINDRED 



 tell the tale , that on an evening of late autumn when the weather 
 be fair , calm , and sunny , there come a man out of the wood hard by 
 the mote - stead aforesaid , who sit he down at the root of the 
 Speech - mound , cast down before he a roe - buck which he have just 
 slay in the wood . he be a young man of three and twenty summer ; 
 he be so clad that he have on he a sheep - brown kirtle and legging 
 of like stuff bind about with white leather thong ; he bear a short- 
 sword in his girdle and a little axe withal ; the sword with fair 
 work gild hilt and a dew - shoe of like fashion to its sheath . 
 he have his quiver at his back and bare in his hand his bow unstrung . 
 he be tall and strong , very fair of fashion both of limb and face , 
 white - skin , but for the sun 's tanning , and ruddy - cheek : his 
 beard be little and fine , his hair yellow and curl , cut somewhat 
 close , but for its length so plenteous , and so thick , that none could 
 fail to note it . he have no hat nor hood upon his head , nought but a 
 fillet of golden bead . 

 as he sit down he glance at the dale below he with a well - please 
 look , and then cast his eye down to the grass at his foot , as though 
 to hold a little long all unchanged the image of the fair place he 
 have just see . the sun be low in the heaven , and his slant beam 
 fall yellow all up the dale , gild the chestnut grove grow dusk 
 and grey with autumn , and the black mass of the elm - bough , and 
 gleam back here and there from the pool of the Weltering Water . 
 down in the midmost meadow the long - horn dun kine be move 
 slowly as they feed along the edge of the stream , and a dog be 
 bound about with exceed swiftness here and there among they . 
 at a sharply curved bight of the river the man could see a little 
 vermilion flame flicker about , and above it a thin blue veil of 
 smoke hang in the air , and cling to the bough of the willow 
 anear ; about it be a dozen menfolk clear to see , some sit , some 
 standing , some walk to and fro , but all in company together : four 
 of be brown - clothe and short - skirt like himself , and from above the 
 hand of one come a flash of light as the sun smite upon the steel of 
 his spear . the other be long - skirt and clothe gayer , and amongst 
 they be red and blue and green and white garment , and they be 
 clear to be see for woman . just as the young man look up again , 
 those of they who be sit down rise up , and those that be 
 stroll draw nigh , and they join hand together , and fall to 
 dance on the grass , and the dog and another one with he come up to 
 the dancer and race about and betwixt they ; and so clear to see 
 be they all and so little , be far away , that they look like 
 dainty well - work puppet . 

 the young man sit smile at it for a little , and then rise up and 
 shoulder his venison , and go down into Wildlake 's Way , and 
 presently be fairly in the Dale and stride along the Portway 
 beside the northern cliff , whose greyness be gild yet by the last 
 ray of the sun , though in a minute or two it would go under the 
 western rim . he go fast and cheerily , murmur to himself 
 snatch of old song ; none overtake he on the road , but he overtake 
 diver folk go alone or in company toward Burgstead ; swain and 
 old man , mother and maiden come from the field and the acre , or 
 go from house to house ; and one or two he meet but not many . all 
 these greet he kindly , and he they again ; but he stay not to 
 speak with any , but go as one in haste . 

 it be dusk by then he pass under the gate of Burgstead ; he go 
 straight thence to the door of the House of the Face , and enter as 
 one who be at home , and need go no far , nor abide a bidding . 

 the hall he come into straight out of the open air be long and 
 somewhat narrow and not right high ; it be well - nigh dark now within , 
 but since he know where to look , he could see by the flicker that 
 leapt up now and then from the smoulder brand of the hearth 
 amidmost the hall under the luffer , that there be but three man 
 therein , and belike they be even they whom he look to find there , 
 and for their part they look for his coming , and know his step . 

 he set down his venison on the floor , and cry out in a cheery 
 voice : ' Ho , Kettel ! be all man go without door to sleep so near 
 the winter - tide , that the Hall be as dark as a cave ? hither to I ! 
 or art thou also sleep ? ' 

 a voice come from the further side of the hearth : ' yea , lord , asleep 
 I be , and have be , and dream ; and in my dream I deal with the 
 flesh - pot and the cake - board , and thou shalt see my dream come true 
 presently to thy gain . ' 

 Quoth another voice : ' Kettel hath have out that share of his dream 
 already belike , if the saw sayeth sooth about cook . all ye have 
 be away , so belike he hath do as Rafe 's dog when Rafe run away 
 from the slain buck . ' 

 he laugh therewith , and Kettel with he , and a third voice join 
 the laughter . the young man also laugh and say : ' here I bring 
 the venison which my kinsman desire ; but as ye see I have bring it 
 over - late : but take it , Kettel . when cometh my father from the 
 stithy ? ' 

 Quoth Kettel : ' my lord hath be hard at it shape the Yule - tide 
 sword , and doth not lightly leave such work , as ye wot , but he will 
 be here presently , for he have send to bid we dight for supper 
 straightway . ' 

 say the young man : ' where be there lord in the dale , Kettel , or 
 hast thou make some thyself , that thou must be always throw they 
 in my tooth ? ' 

 ' Son of the Alderman , ' say Kettel , ' ye call I Kettel , which be no 
 name of mine , so why should I not call thee lord , which be no dignity 
 of thine , since it go well over my tongue from old use and will not ? 
 but here come my mate of the kettle , and the woman and lad . sit 
 down by the hearth away from their hurry , and I will fetch thee the 
 hand - water . ' 

 the young man sit down , and Kettel take up the venison and go his 
 way toward the door at the low end of the hall ; but ere he reach 
 it it open , and a noisy crowd enter of man , woman , boy , and 
 dog , some bear great wax candle , some bowl and cup and dish 
 and trencher , and some the board for the meal . 

 the young man sit quiet smile and wink his eye at the sudden 
 flood of light let into the dark place ; he take in without look at 
 this or the other thing the aspect of his Fathers ' House , so long 
 familiar to he ; yet to - night he have a pleasure in it above his will not , 
 and in all the stir of the household ; for the thought of the wood 
 wherein he have wander all day yet hang heavy upon he . come one of 
 the girl and cast fresh brand on the smoulder fire and stir 
 it into a blaze , and the wax candle be set up on the dai , so that 
 between they and the mew - quicken fire every corner of the hall be 
 bright . as aforesaid it be long and narrow , over - arch with stone 
 and not right high , the window high up under the springing of the 
 roof - arch and all on the side toward the street ; over against they 
 be the arch of the shut - bed of the housemate . the wall be 
 bare that evening , but folk be will not to hang up halling of weave 
 picture thereon when feast and high - day be toward ; and all along 
 the wall be the tenter - hook for that purpose , and diver weapon 
 and tool be hang from they here and there . about the dai 
 behind the thwart - table be now stick for adornment leavy bough of 
 oak now just begin to turn with the first frost . high up on the 
 gable wall above the tenter - hook for the hanging be carven fair 
 imagery and knot and twining stem ; for there in the hewn atone be 
 set forth that same image with the rayed head that be on the outside 
 wall , and he be smite the dragon and slay he ; but here inside 
 the house all this be stain in fair and lively colour , and the 
 sun - like ray round the head of the image be of beat gold . at 
 the low end of the hall be two door go into the buttery , 
 and kitchen , and other out - bower ; and above these door be a loft 
 upborne by stone pillar , which loft be the sleep chamber of the 
 goodman of the house ; but the outward door be halfway between the 
 say loft and the hearth of the hall . 

 so the young man take the shoe from his foot and then sit watch 
 the woman and lad array the board , till Kettel come again to he 
 with an old woman bear the ewer and basin , who wash his foot and 
 pour the water over his hand , and give he the towel with fair- 
 broider end to dry they withal . 

 Scarce have he make an end of this ere through the outer door come in 
 three man and a young woman with they ; the foremost of these be a 
 man young by some two year than the first - comer , but so like he 
 that none might misdoubt that he be his brother ; the next be an old 
 man with a long white beard , but hale and upright ; and lastly come a 
 man of middle - age , who lead the young woman by the hand . he be 
 tall than the first of the young man , though the other who enter 
 with he outwent he in height ; a stark carle he be , broad across 
 the shoulder , thin in the flank , long - armed and big - handed ; very 
 noble and well - fashioned of countenance , with a straight nose and 
 grey eye underneath a broad brow : his hair grow somewhat scanty 
 be do about with a fillet of golden bead like the young man his 
 son . for indeed this be their father , and the master of the House . 

 his name be iron - face , for he be the deftest of weapon - smith , and 
 he be the Alderman of the Dalesmen , and well - beloved of they ; his 
 kindre be deem the noblest of the Dale , and long have they dwelt 
 in the House of the Face . but of his son the young , the new- 
 comer , be name Hall - face , and his brother the eld face - of - god ; 
 which name be of old use amongst the kindred , and many great man and 
 stout warrior have bear it aforetime : and this young man , in great 
 love have he be get , and in much hope have he be rear , and 
 therefore have he be name after the good of the kindre . but his 
 mother , who be hight the Jewel , and have be a very fair woman , be 
 dead now , and Iron - face lack a wife . 

 face - of - god be well - beloved of his kindred and of all the Folk of 
 the Dale , and he have get a to - name , and be call gold - mane 
 because of the abundance and fairness of his hair . 

 as for the young woman that be lead in by Iron - face , she be the 
 betroth of face - of - god , and her name be the Bride . she look 
 with such eye of love on he when she see he in the hall , as though 
 she have never see he before but once , nor love he but since 
 yesterday ; though in truth they have grow up together and have see 
 each other most day of the year for many year . she be of the 
 kindre with whom the chief and great man of the face mostly wed , 
 which be indeed far away kindre of they . she be a fair woman and 
 strong : not easily daunt amidst peril she be hardy and handy and 
 light - foot : she could swim as well as any , and could shoot well in 
 the bow , and wield sword and spear : yet be she kind and 
 compassionate , and of great courtesy , and the very dog and kine 
 trust in she and love she . her hair be dark red of hue , long and 
 fine and plenteous , her eye great and brown , her brow broad and very 
 fair , her lip fine and red : her cheek not ruddy , yet nowise sallow , 
 but clear and bright : tall she be and of excellent fashion , but 
 well - knit and well - measure rather than slender and waver as the 
 willow - bough . her voice be sweet and soft , her word few , but 
 exceed dear to the listener . in short , she be a woman bear to be 
 the ransom of her Folk . 

 now as to the name which the menfolk of the Face bore , and they an 
 ancient kindre , a kindre of chieftain , it have be say that in 
 time past their image of the God of the Earth have over his treen 
 face a mask of beat gold fashion to the shape of the image ; and 
 that when the Alderman of the Folk die , he to wit who serve the God 
 and bore on his arm the gold - ring between the people and the altar , 
 this visor or face of God be lay over the face of he who have be 
 in a manner his priest , and therewith he be bear to mound ; and the 
 new Alderman and priest have it in charge to fashion a new visor for 
 the God ; and whereas for long this great kindred have be chieftain 
 of the people , they have be , and be all so name , that the word 
 Face be ever a part of their name . 



 CHAPTER III . they talk of diver matter in the hall 



 now face - of - god , who be also call gold - mane , rise up to meet the 
 new - comer , and each of they greet he kindly , and the Bride kiss 
 he on the cheek , and he she in likewise ; and he look kindly on 
 she , and take her hand , and go on up the hall to the dai , 
 follow his father and the old man ; as for he , he be of the 
 kindre of the House , and be foster - father of Iron - face and of his 
 son both ; and his name be stone - face : a stark warrior have he be 
 when he be young , and even now he could do a man 's work in the 
 battlefield , and his understanding be as good as that of a man in 
 his prime . so go these and four other up on to the dai and sit 
 down before the thwart - table look down the hall , for the meat be 
 now on the board ; and of the other there be some fifty man and 
 woman who be deem to be of the kindred and sit at the endlong 
 table . 

 so then the Alderman stand up and make the sign of the Hammer over 
 the meat , the token of his craft and of his God . then they fall to 
 with good heart , for there be enough and to spare of meat and 
 drink . there be bread and flesh   though not Gold - mane 's venison , 
 and leek and roasted chestnut of the grove , and red - cheek apple 
 of the garth , and honey enough of that year 's gathering , and medlar 
 sharp and mellow : moreover , good wine of the western bent go up 
 and down the hall in great gild copper bowl and in mazer girt and 
 lip with gold . 

 but when they be full of meat , and have drunken somewhat , they fall 
 to speech , and Iron - face spake aloud to his son , who have but be 
 speak softly to the Bride as one playmate to the other : but the 
 Alderman say : ' Scarce be the wood - deer grown , kinsman , when I must 
 need eat sheep 's flesh on a Thursday , though my son have lain abroad 
 in the wood all night to hunt for I . ' 

 and therewith he smile in the young man 's face ; but gold - mane 
 reddened and say : ' so be it , kinsman , I can hit what I can see ; but 
 not what be hide . ' 

 iron - face laugh and say : ' Hast thou be to the Woodland - Carles ? 
 be their woman fair than our cousin ? ' 

 face - of - god take up the Bride 's hand in his and kiss it and lay it 
 to his cheek ; and then turn to his father and say : ' Nay , father , 
 I see not the Wood - carle , nor go to their abode ; and on no day do 
 I lust after their woman . moreover , I bring home a roebuck of the 
 fat ; but I be over - late for Kettel , and the flesh be ready for 
 the board by then I come . ' 

 ' well , son , ' quoth iron - face , for he be merry , ' a roebuck be but a 
 little deer for such big man as be thou and I. but I rede thee take 
 the Bride along with thee the next time ; and she shall seek whilest 
 thou sleep , and hit when thou missest . ' 

 then face - of - god smile , but he frown somewhat also , and he say : 
 ' well be that , indeed ! but if ye must need drag a true tale out 
 of I : that roebuck I shoot at the very edge of the wood nigh to the 
 mote - stead as I be come home : hart have I see in the wood and 
 its lawn , and boar , and buck , and loose not at they : for indeed 
 when I awake in the morning in that wood - lawn ye wot of , I wander 
 up and down with my bow unbent . so it be that I fare as if I be 
 seek something , I know not what , that should fill up something 
 lack to I , I know not what . thus I feel in myself even so long 
 as I be underneath the black bough , and there be none beside I 
 and before I , and none to turn aback to : but when I come out again 
 into the sunshine , and I see the fair dale , and the happy abode lie 
 before I , and folk abroad in the mead merry in the eventide ; then 
 be I full fain of it , and loathe the wood as an empty thing that 
 have nought to give I ; and lo you ! all that I have be long for in 
 the wood , be it not in this House and ready to my hand ? - and that be 
 good meseemeth . ' 

 Therewith he drink of the cup which the Bride put into his hand after 
 she have kiss the rim , but when he have set it down again he spake 
 once more : 

 ' and yet now I be sit honour and well - beloved in the House of 
 my father , with the holy hearth sparkle and gleam down there 
 before I ; and she that shall bear my child sit soft and kind 
 by my side , and the bold lad I shall one day lead in battle drink 
 out of my very cup : now it seem to I that amidst all this , the 
 dark cold wood , wherein abide but the beast and the foe of the 
 Gods , be bid I to it and draw I thither . Narrow be the Dale 
 and the World be wide ; I would it be dawn and daylight , that I 
 might be afoot again . ' 

 and he half rise up from his place . but his father bend his brow on 
 he and say : ' Kinsman , thou hast a long tongue for a half - train 
 whelp : nor see I whitherward thy mind be wander , but if it be on 
 the road of a lad 's desire to go far and fare bad . Hearken 
 then , I will offer thee somewhat ! soon shall the west - country 
 merchant be here with their winter truck . how sayest thou ? hast 
 thou a mind to fare back with they , and look on the Plain and its 
 Cities , and take and give with the stranger ? to whom indeed thou 
 shalt be nothing save a purse with a few lump of gold in it , or 
 maybe a spear in the stranger 's band on the stricken field , or a bow 
 on the wall of an alien city . this be a craft which thou mayst well 
 learn , since thou shalt be a chieftain ; a craft good to learn , 
 however grievous it be in the learning . and I myself have be 
 there ; for in my youth I desire sore to look on the world beyond the 
 mountain ; so I go , and I fill my belly with the fruit of my own 
 desire , and a bitter meat be that ; but now that it have pass 
 through I , and I yet alive , belike I be more of a grown man for 
 have endure its gripe . even so may it well be with thee , son ; so 
 go if thou wilt ; and thou shalt go with my blessing , and with gold 
 and ware and wain and spearman . ' 

 ' Nay , ' say face - of - god , ' I thank thee , for it be well offer ; but I 
 will not go , for I have no lust for the Plain and its city ; I love 
 the Dale well , and all that be round about it ; therein will I live 
 and die . ' 

 Therewith he fall a - musing ; and the Bride look at he anxiously , 
 but spake not . Sooth to say her heart be sink , as though she 
 forebode some new thing , which should thrust itself into their merry 
 life . 

 but the old man stone - face take up the word and say : 

 ' Son Gold - mane , it behoveth I to speak , since belike I know the 
 wild - wood well than most , and have do for these three - score and 
 ten year ; to my cost . now I perceive that thou long for the wood 
 and the innermost of it ; and wot ye what ? this longing will at 
 while entangle the son of our chieftain , though this Alderman that 
 now be hath be free therefrom , which be well for he . for , time 
 be this longing come over I , and I go whither it lead I : 
 overlong it be to tell of all that befell I because of it , and how 
 my heart bleed thereby . so sorry be the tiding that come of it , 
 that now meseemeth my heart should be of stone and not my face , have 
 it not be for the love wherewith I have love the son of the 
 kindre . therefore , son , it be not ill if ye go west away with 
 the merchant this winter , and learn the dealing of the city , 
 and bring we back tale thereof . ' 

 but gold - mane cry out somewhat angrily , ' I tell thee , foster- 
 father , that I have no mind for the city and their man and their 
 fool and their whore and their runagate . but as for the wood and 
 its wonder , I have do with it , save for hunt there along with 
 other of the Folk . so let thy mind be at ease ; and for the rest , I 
 will do what the Alderman commandeth , and whatso my father craveth of 
 I . ' 

 ' and that be well , son , ' say stone - face , ' if what ye say come to 
 pass , as sore I misdoubt I it will not . but well it be , well it 
 be ! for such thing be in the wood , yea and before ye come to its 
 innermost , as may well try the stoutest heart . therein be Kobbolds , 
 and wight that love not man , thing unto whom the grief of man be as 
 the sound of the fiddle - bow unto we . and there abide the ghost of 
 those that may not rest ; and there wander the dwarf and the 
 mountain - dweller , the dealer in marvel , the giver of gift that 
 destroy house ; the forger of the curse that clingeth and the murder 
 that flitteth to and fro . there moreover be the lair of Wights in 
 the shape of woman , that draw a young man 's heart out of his body , 
 and fill up the empty place with desire never to be satisfied , that 
 they may mock he therewith and waste his manhood and destroy he . 
 nor say I much of the strong - thief that dwell there , since thou art 
 a valiant sword ; or of they who have be make wolf of the Holy 
 Places ; or of the Murder - Carles , the remnant and off - scouring of 
 wicked and wretche Folks - man who think as much of the life of a man 
 as of the life of a fly . yet happiest be the man whom they shall 
 tear in piece , than he who shall live burden by the curse of the 
 foe of the Gods . ' 

 the housemaster look on his son as the old carle spake , and a cloud 
 gather on his face a while ; and when stone - face have make an end he 
 spake : 

 ' this be long and evil talk for the end of a merry day , o fosterer ! 
 Wilt thou not drink a draught , o Redesman , and then stand up and set 
 thy fiddle - bow a - dancing , and cause it draw some fair word after it ? 
 for my cousin 's face hath grow sad than a young maid 's should be , 
 and my son 's eye gleam with thought that be far away from we and 
 abroad in the wild - wood seeking marvel . ' 

 then arise a man of middle - age from the top of the endlong bench on 
 the east side of the hall : a man tall , thin and scant - haired , with a 
 nose like an eagle 's neb : he reach out his hand for the bowl , and 
 when they have give to he he handle it , and raise it aloft and 
 cry : 

 ' here I drink a double health to face - of - god and the Bride , and the 
 love that lieth between they , and the love betwixt they twain and 
 we . ' 

 he drink therewith , and the wine go up and down the hall , and all 
 man drank , both carle and queen , with shout and great joy . then 
 Redesman put down the cup   for it have come into his hand again , and 
 reach his hand to the wall behind he , and take down his fiddle 
 hang there in its case , and draw it out and fall to tune it , 
 while the hall grow silent to hearken : then he handle the bow and 
 lay it on the string till they wail and chuckle sweetly , and 
 when the song be well awake and stir briskly , then he lift up 
 his voice and sing : 


 the Minstrel saith : 

 ' o why on this morning , ye maid , be ye trip 
  Aloof from the meadow yet fresh with the dew , 
 where under the west wind the river be lip 
  the fragrance of mint , the white bloom and the blue ? 

 for rough be the Portway where pant ye wander ; 
  on your foot and your gown - hem the dust lieth dun ; 
 come trip through the grass and the meadow - sweet yonder , 
  and forget neath the willow the sword of the sun . 

 the Maidens answer : 

 though fair be the moon - daisy down by the river , 
  and soft be the grass and the white clover sweet ; 
 though twixt we and the rock - wall the hot glare doth quiver , 
  and the dust of the wheel - way be dun on our foot ; 

 yet here on the way shall we walk on this morning 
  though the sun burneth here , and sweet , cool be the mead ; 
 for here when in old day the Burg give its warning , 
  stand stark under weapon the doughty of deed . 

 here come on the alien their proud word a - crying , 
  and here on our threshold they stumble and fall ; 
 here silent at even the steel - clothe be lie , 
  and here be our mother the story to tell . 

 here then on the morn of the eve of the wedding 
  we pray to the Mighty that we too may bear 
 such war - wall for warding of orchard and steading , 
  that the new day be merry as old day be dear . ' 


 Therewith he make an end , and shout and glad cry arise all about 
 the hall ; and an old man arise and cry : ' a cup to the memory of 
 the Mighty of the Day of the warding of the Ways . ' for you must know 
 this song tell of a custom of the Folk , hold in memory of a time of 
 bygone battle , wherein they have overthrow a great host of alien on 
 the Portway betwixt the river and the cliff , two furlong from the 
 gate of Burgstead . so now two week before Midsummer those maiden 
 who be presently to be wed go early in the morning to that 
 place clothe in very fair raiment , sword girt to their side and 
 spear in their hand , and abode there on the highway from morn till 
 even as though they be a guard to it . and they make merry there , 
 sing song and tell tale of time past : and at the sunsetting 
 their groom come to fetch they away to the Feast of the Eve of the 
 wedding . 

 while the song be a - sing face - of - god take the Bride 's hand in his 
 and caress it , and be soft and blithe with she ; and she redden 
 and tremble for pleasure , and call to mind wedding feast that have 
 be , and fair bride that she have see thereat , and she forget she 
 fear and her heart be at peace again . 

 and iron - face look well - pleased on the two from time to time , and 
 smile , but forbore word to they . 

 but up and down the hall man talk with one another about thing 
 long ago betid : for their heart be high and they desire deed ; 
 but in that fair Dale so happy be the year from day to day that 
 there be but little to tell of . so deepen the night and wan , 
 and gold - mane and the Bride still talk sweetly together , and at 
 while kindly to the other ; and by seem he have clean forget 
 the wood and its wonder . 

 then at last the Alderman call for the cup of good - night , and man 
 drink thereof and go their way to bed . 



 CHAPTER IV . FACE - of - GOD FARETH to the wood again 



 when it be the early morning and dawn be but just begin , 
 face - of - god awake and rise up from his bed , and come forth into the 
 hall naked in his shirt , and stand by the hearth , wherein the piled- 
 up ember be yet red , and look about and could see nothing 
 stir in the dimness : then he fetch water and wash the night- 
 tide off he , and clothe himself in haste , and be even as he be 
 yesterday , save that he leave his bow and quiver in their place and 
 take instead a short casting - spear ; moreover he take a leathern scrip 
 and go therewith to the buttery , and set therein bread and flesh 
 and a little gilded beaker ; and all this he do with but little 
 noise ; for he would not be question , lest he should have to answer 
 himself as well as other . 

 thus he go quietly out of door , for the door be but latch , 
 since no bolt or bar or lock be use in Burgstead , and through 
 the town - gate , which stand open , save when rumour of war be about . 
 he turn his face straight towards Wildlake 's way , walk briskly , 
 but at while look back over his shoulder toward the East to note 
 what way be make by the dawning , and how the sky lighten above the 
 mountain pass . 

 by then he be come to the place where the Maiden Ward be hold in 
 the summer the dawn be so far forward that all thing have their due 
 colour , and be clear to see in the shadowless day . it be a 
 bright morning , with an easterly air stir that drave away the 
 haze and dry the meadow , which have otherwise be rimy ; for it be 
 cold . gold - mane linger on the place a little , and his eye fall on 
 the road , as dusty yet as in Redesman 's song ; for the autumn have be 
 very dry , and the strip of green that edge the outside of the way 
 be wear and dusty also . on the edge of it , half in the dusty road , 
 half on the worn grass , be a long twine of briony red - berried and 
 black - leaved ; and right in the midst of the road be two twig of 
 great - leave sturdy pollard oak , as though they have be throw aside 
 there yesterday by woman or child a - sporting ; and the deep white 
 dust yet hold the mark of foot , some bare , some shod , cross each 
 other here and there . face - of - god smile as he pass on , as a man 
 with a happy thought ; for his mind show he a picture of the Bride 
 as she would be lead the Maiden Ward next summer , and sing 
 first among the singer , and he see she as clearly as he have often 
 see she verily , and before he be the fashion of her hand and all 
 her body , and the little mark on her right wrist , and the place where 
 her arm whiten , because the sleeve guard it against the sun , 
 which have long be pleasant unto he , and the little hollow in her 
 chin , and the lock of red - brown hair wave in the wind above her 
 brow , and shin in the sun as brightly as the Alderman 's cunningest 
 work of golden wire . soft and sweet seem that picture , till he 
 almost seem to hear her sweet voice call to he , and desire of 
 she so take hold of the youth , that it stir he up to go swiftli 
 as he stride on , the day brighten behind he . 

 now be it nigh sunrise , and he begin to meet folk on the way , though 
 not many ; since for most their way lie afield , and not towards the 
 Burg . the first be a woodlander , tall and gaunt , stride beside 
 his ass , whose pannier be laden with charcoal . the carle 's 
 daughter , a little maiden of seven winter , rid on the ass 's back 
 betwixt the pannier , and prattle to herself in the cold morning ; 
 for she be pleased with the clear light in the east , and the smooth 
 wide turf of the meadow , as one who have not often be far from the 
 shadow of the heavy tree of the wood , and their dark wall round 
 about the clearing where they dwelt . face - of - god give the twain the 
 sele of the day in merry fashion as he pass they by , and the sober 
 dark - faced man nod to he but spake no word , and the child stay 
 her prattle to watch he as he go by . 

 then come the sound of the rattle of wheel , and , as he double an 
 angle of the rock - wall , he come upon a wain draw by four dun kine , 
 wherein lie a young woman all muffle up against the cold with fur 
 and cloth ; beside the yoke - beast go her man , a well - knit trim- 
 face Dalesman clothe bravely in holiday raiment , girt with a goodly 
 sword , bear a bright steel helm on his head , in his hand a long 
 spear with a gay red and white shaft do about with copper band . 
 he look merry and proud of his wain - load , and the woman be smile 
 kindly on he from out of her scarlet and fur ; but now she turn a 
 weary happy face on Gold - mane , for they know he , as do all man of 
 the Dale . 

 so he stop when they meet , for the goodman have already stay his 
 slow beast , and the goodwife have rise a little on her cushion to 
 greet he , yet slowly and but a little , for she be great with child , 
 and not far from her time . that know gold - mane well , and what be 
 toward , and why the goodman wear his fine clothe , and why the wain 
 be deck with oak - bough and the yoke - beast with their good gild 
 bell and copper - adorn harness . for it be a custom with many of 
 the kindred that the goodwife should fare to her father 's house to 
 lie in with her first babe , and the day of her come home be make a 
 great feast in the house . so then face - of - god cry out : ' hail to 
 thee , o Warcliff ! Shrewd be the wind this morning , and thou dost 
 well to heed it carefully , this thine orchard , this thy garden , this 
 thy fair apple - tree ! to a good hall thou wendest , and the Wine of 
 increase shall be sweet there this even . ' 

 then smile Warcliff all across his face , and the goodwife hang her 
 head and redden . say the goodman : ' wilt thou not be with we , son 
 of the Alderman , as surely thy father shall be ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say face - of - god , ' though I be fain of it : my own matter 
 carry I away . ' 

 ' what matter ? ' say Warcliff ; ' perchance thou art for the city 
 this autumn ? ' 

 face - of - god answer somewhat stiffly : ' Nay , I be not ; ' and then 
 more kindly , and smile , ' all road lead not down to the Plain , 
 friend . ' 

 ' what road then far thou away from we ? ' say the goodwife . 

 ' the way of my will , ' he answer . 

 ' and what way be that ? ' say she ; ' take heed , lest I get a longing to 
 know . for then must thou need tell I , or deal with the carle there 
 beside thee . ' 

 ' nay , goodwife , ' say face - of - god , ' let not that longing take thee ; 
 for on that matter I be even as wise as thou . now good speed to thee 
 and to the new - comer ! ' 

 Therewith he go close up to the wain , and reach out his hand to 
 she , and she give he hers and he kiss it , and so go his way 
 smile kindly on they . then the carle cry to his kine , and they 
 bend down their head to the yoke ; and presently , as he walk on , he 
 hear the rumble of the wain mingle with the tinkling of their 
 bell , which in a little while become measured and musical , and 
 sound above the creaking of the axle and the rattle of the gear 
 and the roll of the great wheel over the road : and so it grow 
 thin and thin till it all die away behind he . 

 he be now come to where the river turn away from the sheer rock- 
 wall , which be not so high there as in most other place , as there 
 have be in old time long scree from the cliff , which have now grow 
 together , with the waxing of herb and the washing down of the earth 
 on to they , and make a steady slope or low hill go down riverward . 
 over this the road lift itself above the level of the meadow , 
 keep a little way from the cliff , while on the other side its 
 bank be somewhat broken and steep here and there . as face - of - god 
 come up to one of these broken place , the sun rise over the eastern 
 pass , and the meadow grow golden with its long beam . he linger , 
 and look back under his hand , and as he do so hear the voice and 
 laughter of woman come up from the slope below he , and presently a 
 young woman come struggle up the broken bank with hand and knee , 
 and cast herself down on the roadside turf laugh and pant . she 
 be a long - limbed light - make woman , dark - faced and black - haired : 
 amidst her laughter she look up and see gold - mane , who have stop 
 at once when he see she ; she hold out her hand to he , and say 
 lightly , though her face flush withal : 

 ' come hither , thou , and help the other to climb the bank ; for they 
 be beat in the race , and now must they do after my will ; that be 
 the forfeit . ' 

 he go up to she , and take her hand and kiss they , as be the 
 custom of the Dale , and say : 

 ' hail to thee , long - coat ! who be they , and whither away this morning 
 early ? ' 

 she look hard at he , and fondly belike , as she answer slowly : 
 ' they be the two maiden of my father 's house , whom thou know ; and 
 our errand , all three of we , be to Burgstead , the Feast of the Wine 
 of increase which shall be drunk this even . ' 

 as she spake come another woman half up the bank , to whom go face- 
 of - god , and , take her hand , draw she up while she laugh merrily 
 in his face : he salute she as he have long - coat , and then with a 
 laugh turn about to wait for the third ; who come indeed , but after 
 a little while , for she have abide , hear their voice . her also 
 gold - mane draw up , and kiss her hand , and she lie on the grass by 
 long - coat , but the second maiden stand up beside the young man . she 
 be white - skinned and golden - haire , a very fair damsel , whereas the 
 last - comer be but comely , as be well - nigh all the woman of the 
 Dale . 

 say face - of - god , look on the three : ' how come it , maidens , that 
 ye be but in your kirtle this sharp autumn morning ? or where have 
 ye leave your gown or your cloak ? ' 

 for indeed they be clothe but in close - fit blue kirtle of fine 
 wool , embroider about the hem with gold and coloured thread . 

 the last - comer laugh and say : ' what ail thee , Gold - mane , to be 
 so careful of we , as if thou wert our mother or our nurse ? yet if 
 thou must need know , there hang our gown on the thorn - bush down 
 yonder ; for we have be run a match and a forfeit ; to wit , that 
 she who be last on the highway should go down again and bring they 
 up all three ; and now that be my day 's work : but since thou art 
 here , Alderman 's son , thou shalt go down instead of I and fetch they 
 up . ' 

 but he laugh merrily and outright , and say : ' that will I not , for 
 there be but twenty - four hour in the day , and what between eat

 CHAPTER VI . of FACE - of - GOD and those MOUNTAIN - DWELLERS

 at last wild - wearer cry out : ' now , lad , the night weareth and the

 now let he make it , for later on it shall be over - late . ' 

 then arise Wood - wont and go to his shut - bed and grope therein , and 
 take from out of it a fiddle in its case ; and he open the case and 
 draw from it a very goodly fiddle , and he stand on the floor amidst 
 of the hall and Bow - may his cousin with he ; and he lay his bow on 
 the fiddle and wake up song in it , and when it be well awake she 
 fall a - singing , and he to answer her song , and at the last all 
 they of the house sing together ; and this be the meaning of the word 
 which they sing : 


 she singeth . 

 now be the rain upon the day , 
  and every water 's wide ; 
 why busk ye then to wear the way , 
  and whither will ye ride ? 

 he singeth . 

 our kine be on the eyot still , 
  the eddy lap they round ; 
 all dyke the wind - wear water fill , 
  and waneth grass and ground . 

 she singeth . 

 o ride ye to the river 's brim 
  in war - weed fair to see ? 
 or winter water will ye swim 
  in hauberk to the knee ? 

 he singeth . 

 Wild be the day , and dim with rain , 
  our sheep be ward ill ; 
 the wood - wolf gather for the plain , 
  their ravening maw to fill . 

 she singeth . 

 Nay , what be this , and what have ye , 
  a hunter 's band , to bear 
 the Banner of our Battle - glee 
  the skulk wolf to scare ? 

 he singeth . 

 o woman , when we wend our way 
  to deal with death and dread , 
 the Banner of our father ' day 
  must flap the wind o'erhead . 

 she singeth . 

 ah , for the maiden that ye leave ! 
  who now shall save the hay ? 
 what groom shall kiss our lip at eve , 
  when June hath master May ? 

 he singeth . 

 the wheat be win , the seed be sown , 
  here toileth many a maid , 
 and ere the hay knee - deep hath grow 
  your groom the grass shall wade . 

 they sing all together . 

 then fair befall the mountain - side 
  Whereon the play shall be ! 
 and fair befall the summer - tide 
  that whoso life shall see . 


 face - of - god think the song goodly , but to the other it be well 
 know . then say Wood - father : 

 ' o foster - son , thy foster - brother hath sing well for a wood abider ; 
 but we be deeming that his singing shall be but as a starling to a 
 throstle match against thy new - come guest . therefore , Dalesman , 
 sing we a song of the Dale , and if ye will , let it be of garden and 
 pleasant house of stone , and fair damsel therein , and swain with 
 they who toil not over - much for a scant livelihood , as do they of the 
 waste , whose head may not be see in the Holy Places . ' 

 say Gold - mane : ' Father , it be ill to set the word of a lonely man 
 afar from his kin against the song that cometh from the heart of a 
 noble house ; yet may I not gainsay thee , but will sing to thee what I 
 may call to mind , and it be call the Song of the Ford . ' 

 Therewith he sing in a sweet and clear voice : and this be the 
 meaning of his word : 


 in hay - tide , through the day new - bear , 
  across the mead we come ; 
 our hauberk brush the blossom corn 
  a furlong short of home . 

 Ere yet the gable we behold 
  Forth flasheth the red sun , 
 and smite our fallow helm and cold 
  though all the fight be do . 

 in this last mend of mow - grass 
  Sweet doth the clover smell , 
 crush neath our foot red with the pass 
  where hell be blent with hell . 

 and now the willowy stream be nigh , 
  down wend we to the ford ; 
 no shaft across its fish fly , 
  nor flasheth there a sword . 

 but lo ! what gleameth on the bank 
  across the water wan , 
 as when our blood the mouse - ear drank 
  and red the river run ? 

 Nay , hasten to the ripple clear , 
  look at the grass beyond ! 
 Lo ye the dainty band and dear 
  of maiden fair and fond ! 

 lo how they need must take the stream ! 
  the water hide their foot ; 
 on fair kind arm the gold doth gleam , 
  and midst the ford we meet . 

 up through the garden two and two , 
  and on the flower we drip ; 
 their wet foot kiss the morning dew 
  as lip lie close to lip . 

 here now we sing ; here now we stay : 
  by these grey wall we tell 
 the love that live from out the fray , 
  the love that fight and fall . 


 when he be do they all say that he have sing well , and that the 
 song be sweet . yet do Wild - wearer smile somewhat ; and Bow - may say 
 outright : ' Soft be the song , and hath be make by lad and 
 minstrel rather than by warrior . ' 

 ' Nay , kinswoman , ' say Wood - father , ' thou art hard to please ; the 
 guest be kind , and hath give we that I ask for , and I give he all 
 thank therefor . ' 

 face - of - god smile , but he heed little what they say , for as he 
 sing he have note that the Friend look kindly on he ; and he 
 think he see that once or twice she put out her hand as if to touch 
 he , but draw it back again each time . she spake after a little and 
 say : 

 ' here now hath be a stream of song run betwixt the Mountain and 
 the Dale even as doth a river ; and this be good to come between our 
 dream of what hath be and what shall be . ' then she turn to 
 gold - mane , and say to he scarce loud enough for all to hear : 

 ' herewith I bid thee good - night , o Dalesman ; and this other word I 
 have to thee : heed not what befalleth in the night , but sleep thy 
 well , for nought shall be to thy scathe . and when thou wak in the 
 morning , if we be yet here , it be well ; but if we be not , then 
 abide we no long while , but break thy fast on the victual thou wilt 
 find upon the board , and so depart and go thy way home . and yet 
 thou mayst look to it to see we again before thou di . ' 

 Therewith she hold out her hand to he , and he take it and kiss it ; 
 and she go to her chamber - aloft at the low end of the hall . and 
 when she be go , once more he have a deeming of she that she be of 
 the kindre of the Gods . at her departure he - seem that the hall 
 grow dull and small and smoky , and the night seem long to he and 
 doubtful the coming of the day . 



 CHAPTER VII . FACE - of - GOD talketh with the FRIEND on the MOUNTAIN 



 so now go all man to bed ; and face - to - god 's shut - bed be over 
 against the outer door and toward the low end of the hall , and on 
 the panel about it hang the weapon and shield of man . Fair be 
 that chamber and roomy , and the man be weary despite his eagerness , 
 so that he go to sleep as soon as his head touch the pillow ; but 
 within a while   he deem about two hour after midnight   he be 
 awake by the clattering of the weapon against the panel , and the 
 sound of man 's hand take they down ; and when he be fully awake , 
 he hear withal man go up and down the house as if on errand : 
 but he call to mind what the Friend have say to he , and he do not 
 so much as turn himself toward the hall ; for he say : ' belike these 
 man be outlaw and wolf of the Holy Places , yet by seem they 
 be good fellow and nought churlish , nor have I to do with take up 
 the feud against they . I will abide the morning . yet meseemeth that 
 she draw I hither : for what cause ? ' 

 Therewith he fall asleep again , and dream no more . but when he 
 awake the sun be shin broad upon the hall - floor , and he sit up

 then he arise and clothe himself , and go forth into the hall and

 alive , and she turn her head toward he , and lo it be the Friend ; 
 and his heart rise to his mouth for wonder and fear and desire . for 
 now he doubt whether the other folk be aught save show and 
 shadow , and she the Goddess who have fashion they out of nothing 
 for his bewilderment , presently to return to nothing . 

 yet whatever he might fear or doubt , he go up the hall towards she 
 till he be quite nigh to she , and there he stand silent , wonder

 Grey - eyed she be like her brother ; but her hair the colour of red 
 wheat : her lip full and red , her chin round , her nose fine and 
 straight . her hand and all her body fashion exceed sweetly and 
 delicately ; yet not as if she be an image of which the like might 
 be find if the craftsman be but deft enough to make a perfect 
 thing , but in such a way that there be none like to she for those 
 that have eye to behold she as she be ; and none could ever be make 
 like to she , even by such a master - craftsman as could fashion a body 
 without a blemish . 

 she be clothe in a white smock , whose hem be broider with gold

 be a fillet of gold and gem , and there be wondrous gold ring on

 she smile kindly upon his solemn and troubled face , and her voice 
 sound strangely familiar to he come from all that loveliness , as

 deem last night that I should be go with the other . therefore

 for though we dwell in the wild - wood , from the solace of folk , yet 
 be we not of thrall ' blood . but come now , I bid thee break thy 
 fast and talk with I a little while ; and then shalt thou depart in 
 peace . ' 

 Spake face - of - god , and his voice tremble as he spake : ' what art 
 thou ? last night I deem at while once and again that thou wert of 
 the Gods ; and now that I behold thee thus , and it be broad daylight , 
 and of those other be no more to be see than if they have never 
 live , I can not but deem that it be even so , and that thou comest 
 from the City that shall never perish . now if thou be a goddess , I 
 have nought to pray thee , save to slay I speedily if thou hast a 
 mind for my death . but if thou art a woman - ' 

 she break in : ' gold - mane , stay thy prayer and hold thy peace for 
 this time , lest thou repent when repentance availeth not . and this I 
 say because I be none of the Gods nor akin to they , save far off 
 through the generation , as art thou also , and all man of goodly 
 kindre . now I bid thee eat thy meat , since ' tis ill talk betwixt

 Earl 's garment . ' 

 she laugh therewith , and lean toward he and put forth her hand

 eat , while she lean forward on the arm of her chair and spake to

 much of he and his : and he keep say to himself : ' she draw I 
 hither ; wherefore do she so ? ' 

 but she say : ' gold - mane , how fareth thy father the Alderman ? be he 
 as good a wright as ever ? ' 

 he tell she : Yea , that ever be his hammer on the iron , the copper , 
 and the gold , and that no wright in the Dale be as deft as he . 

 say she : ' would he not have have thee seek to the city , to see the 
 way of the outer world ? ' 

 ' yea , ' say he . 

 she say : ' thou wert wise to naysay that offer ; thou shalt have 
 enough to do in the Dale and round about it in twelve month ' time . ' 

 ' art thou foresighte ? ' say he . 

 ' folk have call I so , ' she say , ' but I wot not . but thy brother 
 Hall - face , how fareth he ? ' 

 ' well ; ' say he , ' to my deeming he be the Sword of our House , and the 
 Warrior of the Dale , if the day be ready for he . ' 

 ' and stone - face , that stark ancient , ' she say , ' doth he still love 
 the Folk of the Dale , and hate all other folk ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' he say , ' I know not that , but I know that he loveth as , and 
 above all I and my father . ' 

 again she spake : ' how fareth the Bride , the fair maid to whom thou 
 art affiance ? ' 

 as she spake , it be to he as if his heart be stricken cold ; but he 
 put a force upon himself , and neither reddened nor whiten , nor 
 change countenance in any way ; so he answer : 

 ' she be well the eve of yesterday . ' then he remember what she 
 be , and her beauty and valour , and he constrain himself to say : 
 ' each day she groweth fairer ; there be no man 's son and no daughter 
 of woman that do not love she ; yea , the very beast of field and 
 fold love she . ' 

 the Friend look at he steadily and spake no word , but a red flush 
 mount to her cheek and brow and change her face ; and he marvel 
 thereat ; for still he misdoubte that she be a Goddess . but it 
 pass away in a moment , and she smile and say : 

 ' guest , thou seemest to wonder that I know concern thee and the 
 Dale and thy kindred . but now shalt thou wot that I have be in the 
 Dale once and again , and my brother oftener still ; and that I have 
 see thee before yesterday . ' 

 ' that be marvellous , ' quoth he , ' for sure be I that I have not see 
 thee . ' 

 ' yet thou hast see I , ' she say ; ' yet not altogether as I be now ; ' 
 and therewith she smile on he friendly . 

 ' how be this ? ' say he ; ' art thou a skin - changer ? ' 

 ' yea , in a fashion , ' she say . ' Hearken ! dost thou perchance 
 remember a day of last summer when there be a market holden in 
 Burgstead ; and there stand in the way over against the House of the 
 Face a tall old carle who be truck deer - skin for diverse gear ; 
 and with he be a queen , tall and dark - skin , somewhat well- 
 liking , her hair bind up in a white coif so that none of it could be 
 see ; by the token that she have a large stone of mountain blue set in 
 silver stick in the say coif ? ' 

 as she speak she set her hand to her bosom and draw something from

 great blue stone set in silver . 

 ' wondrous as a dream be this , ' say face - of - god , ' for these twain I 
 remember well , and what follow . ' 

 she say : ' I will tell thee that . there come a man of the Shepherd- 
 Folk , drunk or foolish , or both , who begin to chaffer with the big 
 carle ; but ever on the queen be his eye set , and presently he put 
 forth his hand to she to clip she , whereon the big carle hove up his 
 fist and smite he , so that he fall to earth noseling . then run the 
 folk together to hale off the stranger and help the shepherd , and it 
 be like that the stranger should be mishandle . then there thrust

 be no matter to make a quarrel or a court case of . let the market go

 be this young man ? ' 

 Quoth Gold - mane : ' it be even I , and meseemeth it be no great deed 
 to do . ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' and the big carle be my brother , and the tall 
 queen , it be myself . ' 

 ' how then , ' say he , ' for she be as dark - skinned as a dwarf , and 
 thou so bright and fair ? ' 

 she say : ' well , if the wood be good for nothing else , yet be 
 they good for the growing of herb , and I know the craft of simple ; 
 and with one of these herb have I stain my skin and my brother 's 
 also . and it show the dark beneath the white coif . ' 

 ' yea , ' say he , ' but why must ye need fare in feigned shape ? Ye 
 would have be welcome guest in the Dale howsoever ye have come . ' 

 ' I may not tell thee hereof as now , ' say she . 

 say Gold - mane : ' yet thou mayst belike tell I wherefore be that 
 thy brother desire to slay I yesterday , if he know I , who I be . ' 

 ' gold - mane , ' she say , ' thou art not slay , so little story need be 
 make of that : for the rest , belike he know thee not at that moment . 
 so it fall with we , that we look to see foe rather than friend in

 let loose , and nothing be good to he save battle and onset ; and then 
 be he blind and know not friend from foe . ' say face - of - god : ' Thou 
 hast ask of I and mine ; wilt thou not tell I of thee and thine ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , ' not as now ; thou must betake thee to the way . 
 Whither wert thou wending when thou happenedst upon we ? ' 

 he say : ' I know not ; I be seek something , but I know not what - 
 meseemeth that now I have find it . '

 not thither to - day : for who knoweth what thou shalt meet there that 
 shall be thy foe ? ' 

 he say : ' Nay , nay ; I have nought to do but to abide here as long as 
 I may , look upon thee and hearken to thy voice . ' 

 her eye be upon his , but yet she do not seem to see he , and for

 hear tell . ' 

 she say : ' the token when it cometh to thee shall show thee thereof

 ' why should I say any word thereof ? ' say he . ' dost thou not know

 ' well be that . bide thou the token that shall lead thee to Shadowy

 she draw her hand from his , and turn and go her way swiftly to

 CHAPTER VIII . FACE - of - GOD COMETH HOME again to burgstead 



 face - of - God go back through the wood by the way he have come , pay 
 little heed to the thing about he . for whatever he think of 
 stray not one whit from the image of the Fair Woman of the 
 mountain - side . 

 he go through the wood swiftli than yesterday , and make no stay 
 for noon or aught else , nor do he linger on the road when he be 
 come into the Dale , either to speak to any or to note what they do . 
 so he come to the House of the Face about dusk , and find no man 
 within the hall either carle or queen . so he cry out on the folk , 
 and there come in a damsel of the house , whom he greet kindly and 
 she he again . he bid she bring the washing - water , and she do so 
 and wash his foot and his hand . she be a fair maid enough , as 
 be most in the Dale , but he heed her little ; and when she be 
 do he kiss not her cheek for her pain , as his will not be , but let 
 she go her way unthanke . but he go to his shut - bed and open 
 his chest , and draw fair raiment from it , and do off his wood - gear , 
 and do on he a goodly scarlet kirtle fairly broider , and a collar 
 with gem of price therein , and other bravery . and when he be so 
 attire he come out into the hall , and there be old Stone - face 
 stand by the hearth , which be blaze brightly with fresh brand , 
 so that thing be clear to see . 

 stone - face note Gold - mane 's gay raiment , for he be not will not to wear 
 such attire , save on the feast and high day when he behove to . so 
 the old man smile and say : 

 ' welcome back from the Wood ! but what be it ? Hast thou be wed 
 there , or who hath make thee Earl and King ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' Foster - father , sooth it be that I have be to 
 the wood , but there have I see nought of manfolk bad than myself . 
 now as to my raiment , need must I keep it from the moth . and I be 
 weary withal , and this kirtle be light and easy to I . moreover , I 
 look to see the Bride here again , and I would pleasure she with the
