 ' Nay , ' say stone - face , ' hast thou not see some woman in the wood 
 array like the image of a God ? and hath she not bid thee thus to 
 worship she to - night ? for I know that such wight be in the wood , 
 and that such be their wo not . ' 

 say Gold - mane : ' I worship nought save the Gods and the Fathers . 
 nor see I in the wood any such as thou sayest . ' 

 Therewith Stone - face shake his head ; but after a while he say : 

 ' art thou for the wood to - morrow ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say Gold - mane angrily , knit his brow . 

 ' the morrow of to - morrow , ' say stone - face , ' be the day when we look 
 to see the Westland merchant : after all , wilt thou not go hence 
 with they when they wend their way back before the first snow 
 fall ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say he , ' I have no mind to it , fosterer ; cease egg I on 
 hereto . ' 

 then stone - face shake his head again , and look on he long , and 
 mutter : ' to the wood wilt thou go to - morrow or next day ; or some 
 day when doom be thine undoing . ' 

 Therewith enter the service and torch , and presently after come 
 the Alderman with Hall - face ; and iron - face greet his son and say 
 to he : ' thou hast not hit the time to do on thy gay raiment , for 
 the Bride will not be here to - night ; she bideth still at the Feast at 
 the Apple - tree House : or wilt thou be there , son ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say face - of - god , ' I be over - weary . and as for my raiment , it 
 be well ; it be for thine honour and the honour of the name . ' 

 so to table they go , and Iron - face ask his son of his way again , 
 and whether he be quite fix in his mind not to go down to the 
 Plain and the Cities : ' for , ' say he , ' the morrow of to - morrow shall 
 the merchant be here , and this be great news for they if the son 
 of the Alderman should be their faring - fellow back . ' 

 but face - of - god answer without any haste or heat : ' Nay , father , it 
 may not be : fear not , thou shalt see that I have a good will to work 
 and live in the Dale . ' 

 and in good sooth , though he be a young man and love mirth and the 
 way of his own will , he be a stalwarth workman , and few could mow a 
 match with he in the hay - month and win it ; or fall tree as 
 certainly and swiftly , or drive as straight and clean a furrow 
 through the stiff land of the low Dale ; and in other matter also 
 be he deft and sturdy . 



 CHAPTER IX . those BRETHREN fare to the YEWWOOD with the BRIDE 



 next morning face - of - god dight himself for work , and take his axe ; 
 for his brother Hall - face have bid he go down with he to the Yew- 
 wood and cut timber there , since he of all man know where to go 
 straight to the stick that would quarter well for bow - stave ; 
 whereas the Alderman have the right of hewing in that wood . so they 
 go forth , those brother , from the House of the Face , but when they 
 be get to the gate , who should be there but the Bride await 
 they , and she with an ass duly saddle for bear the yew - stick . 
 because Hall - face have tell she that he and belike gold - mane be 
 go to hew in the wood , and she think it good to be of the 
 company , as oft have befall erst . when they meet she greet face- 
 of - god and kiss he as she will not be ; and he look upon she and see 
 how fair she be , and how kind and friendly be her eye that beheld 
 he , and how her whole face be eager for he as their lip part . 
 then his heart fail he , when he know that he no long desire she 
 as she do he , and he say within himself : 

 ' would that she have be of our nighest kindre ! would that I have 
 have a sister and that this be she ! ' 

 so the three go along the highway down the Dale , and Hall - face and 
 the Bride talk merrily together and laugh , for she be happy , 
 since she know that Gold - mane have be to the wood and be back safe 
 and much as he have be before . so indeed it seem of he ; for 
 though at first he be moody and of few word , yet presently he 
 curse himself for a mar - sport , and so fall into the talk , and 
 enforce himself to be merry ; and soon he be so indeed ; for he 
 think : ' she draw I thither : she hath a deed for I to do . I 
 shall do the deed and have my reward . soon will the spring - tide be 
 here , and I shall be a young man yet when it come . ' 

 so come they to the place where he have meet the three maiden 
 yesterday ; there they also turn from the highway ; and as they go 
 down the bent , Gold - mane could not but turn his eye on the beauty of 
 the Bride and the lovely way of her body : but presently he 
 remember all that have betid , and turn away again as one who be 
 note what it behove he not to note . and he say to himself : 
 ' where art thou , Gold - mane ? whose art thou ? yea , even if that have 
 be but a dream that I have dream , yet would that this fair woman 
 be my sister ! ' 

 so come they to the yew - wood , and the brother fall to work , and the 
 Bride with they , for she be deft with the axe and strong withal . 
 but at midday they rest on the green slope without the yew - wood ; 
 and they eat bread and flesh and onion and apple , and drank red 
 wine of the Dale . and while they be rest after their meat , the 
 Bride sing to they , and her song be a lay of time past ; and here ye 
 have somewhat of it : 


 ' tis over the hill and over the dale 
  Men ride from the city fast and far , 
 if they may have a soothfast tale , 
  true tiding of the host of war . 

 and first they hap on man - at - arm , 
  all clothe in steel from head to foot : 
 now tell true tale of the new - come harm , 
  and the gather host of the mountain - root . 

 fair sir , from murder - carle we flee , 
  whose fashion be as the mountain - troll ' ; 
 no man can tell how many they be , 
  and the voice of their host as the thunder roll . 

 they be weary man at the ending of day , 
  but they spur nor stay for long word . 
 now ye , O merchant , whither away ? 
  what do ye there with the helm and the sword ? 

 o we must fight for life and gear , 
  for our beast be spend and our wain be stay , 
 and the host of the mountain - man draw near , 
  that maketh all the world afraid . 

 they leave the chapman on the hill , 
  and through the eve and through the night 
 they ride to have true tiding still , 
  and be there on the way when the dawn be bright . 

 o damsel fair , what do ye then 
  to loiter thus upon the way , 
 and have no fear of the mountain - man , 
  the host of the carle that strip and slay ? 

 o rider weary with the road , 
  come eat and drink on the grass hereby ! 
 and lie you down in a fair abode 
  till the midday sun be broad and high ; 

 then unto you shall we come aback , 
  and lead you forth to the mountain - man , 
 to note their plenty and their lack , 
  and have true tiding there and then . 

 ' tis over the hill and over the dale 
  they ride from the mountain fast and far ; 
 and now have they learn a soothfast tale , 
  true tiding of the host of war . 

 it be summer - tide and the month of Hay , 
  and man and maid must fare afield ; 
 but we see the place be the bow - stave lay , 
  and the hall be hang with spear and shield . 

 when the moon be high we drink in the hall , 
  and they drink to the guest and be kind and blithe , 
 and they say : come back when the chestnut fall , 
  and the wine - cart wend across the hythe . 

 come oft and o'er again , they say ; 
  wander your way ; but we abide 
 for all the world in the little stead ; 
  for wise be we , though the world be wide . 

 Yea , come in arm if ye will , they say ; 
  and despite your host shall we abide 
 for life or death in the little stead ; 
  for wise be we , though the world be wide . 


 so she make an end and look at the fairness of the dale spreading 
 wide before she , and a robin come nigh from out of a thorn - bush and 
 sing his song also , the sweet herald of come winter ; and the 
 lapwing wheel about , black and white , above the meadow by the 
 river , send forth their wheedle pipe as they hang above the soft 
 turf . 

 she feel the brother near she , and know their friendliness from of 
 old , and she be happy ; nor have she look close at gold - mane would 
 she have note any change in he belike ; for the meat and the good

 ancient song soften his heart while it feed the desire therein . 

 so in a while they arise from their rest and do what be leave they 
 of their work , and so go back to Burgstead through the fair 
 afternoon ; by seem all three in all content . but yet gold - mane ,

 be ! ' 



 CHAPTER X. NEW tiding in the DALE 



 it be three day thereafter that gold - mane , lead an ass , go

 great gain of cheese can be get thence . '

 thereat all man laugh , for this seem to they good game , and

 about it . '

 so wear the autumn into early winter ; and the Westland merchant come

 but the first winter - snow come on with a great storm of wind from the

 CHAPTER XI . MEN make OATH AT burgstead on the HOLY BOAR 



 a week after the ransacking at Greentofts the snow and the winter 
 come on in earnest , and all the Dale lie in snow , and man go on 
 skid when they fare up and down the Dale or on the Mountain . 

 all be now tidingless till Yule over , and in Burgstead be there 
 feasting and joyance enough ; and especially at the House of the Face 
 be high - tide holden , and the Alderman and his son and stone - face

 Burgstead who have be bid . 

 face - of - god sit between his father and stone - face ; and he look up

 befall and if she be sick of sorrow . 

 but iron - face behold he how he gaze about , and he laugh ; for he

 Therewith he sit down ; and once more man shout for the love of he

 but the Bride note he that neither be his eye nor his voice like

 stone - face also note he , and he know the young man well than all

 so come the swearing to an end , and they fall to on their meat and

 many man there . 



 CHAPTER XII . stone - face telleth concern the wood - wights 



 early on the morrow Gold - mane arise and clothe himself and go out - a- 
 door and over the trodden snow on to the bridge over the Weltering

 thing seem too hard for he to deal with , and he between a mighty

 bi .

 ' Meseems , father , that the eastern sky be brighten toward dawn . ' 

 ' yea , ' quoth stone - face . 

 ' it will be light in an hour , ' say face - of - god . 

 ' even so , ' say stone - face . 

 ' and a fair day for the morrow of Yule , ' say the swain . 

 ' yea , ' say stone - face , ' and what wilt thou do with the fair day ? 
 Wilt thou to the wood ? ' 

 ' maybe , father , ' say Gold - mane ; ' hall - face and some of the swain 
 be talk of elk up the fell which may be trap in the drift , 
 and if they go a - hunt they , I may go in their company . ' 

 ' ah , son , ' quoth stone - face , ' thou wilt look to see other kind of

 say Gold - mane : ' Father , fear I not ; I shall either refrain I from

 hunter . but since thou hast come to I , tell I more about the 
 wood , for thy tale thereof be fair . ' 

 ' yea , ' say stone - face , ' fair tale of foul thing , as oft it 
 befalleth in the world . Hearken now ! if thou deem that what thou 
 seek shall come readier to thine hand because of the winter and 
 the snow , thou err . for the wight that waylay the body and 
 soul of the mighty in the wild - wood heed such matter nothing ; yea 
 and at Yule - tide be they most abroad , and most armed for the fray . 
 even such an one have I see time agone , when the snow be deep and 
 the wind be rough ; and it be in the likeness of a woman clothe in 
 such raiment as the Bride bore last night , and she trod the snow 
 light - foot in thin raiment where it would scarce bear the skid of a 
 deft snow - runner . even so she stand before I ; the icy wind blow she 
 raiment round about she , and drift the hair from her garlanded head 
 toward I , and she as fair and fresh as in the midsummer day . up 
 the fell she fare , sweetest of all thing to look on , and beckon 
 on I to follow ; on I , the Warrior , the stout - heart ; and I follow , 
 and between we grief be bear ; but I it be that foster that child 
 and not she . always when she would be , be she merry and lovely ; and 
 even so be she now , for she be of those that be long - lived . and I 
 wot that thou hast see even such an one ! ' 

 ' tell I more of thy tale , foster - father , ' say Gold - mane , ' and fear 
 not for I ! '

 that shall be young when thou art old . yet hearken ! we sit in the

 woman , or that lovely feast - hall . 

 ' all praise and honour to the House of the Face , and the bounteous

 whom they we d of the valiant and goodly House of the Steer ! ' 

 ' even so say I , ' quoth Gold - mane calmly ; ' but now wend we aback to

 so they turn from the bridge together ; and stone - face be kind and

 CHAPTER XIII . they fare to the hunting of the ELK 



 when they come into the Hall , the hearth - fire have be quicken , and

 CHAPTER XIV . concern FACE - of - GOD and the MOUNTAIN 



 but it must be tell of gold - mane that what have befall he be in

 CHAPTER XV . murder AMONGST the folk of the woodlander 



 so wear away midwinter tidingless . stone - face spake no more to face-

 and this be some of what they sing : 


 why sit ye bare in the spinning - room ? 
 why weave ye naked at the loom ? 

 Bare and white as the moon we be , 
 that the Earth and the drift night may see . 

 now what be the bad of all your work ? 
 what curse amidst the web shall lurk ? 

 the bad of the work our hand shall win 
 be wrack and ruin round the kin . 

 shall the woollen yarn and the flaxen thread 
 be gear for live man or dead ? 

 the woollen yarn and the flaxen thread 
 shall flare ' twixt live man and dead . 

 o what be the ending of your day ? 
 when shall ye rise and wend away ? 

 our day shall end to - morrow morn , 
 when we hear the voice of the battle - horn . 

 where first shall eye of man behold 
 this weaving of the moonlight cold ? 

 there where the alien host abide 
 the gathering on the mountain - side . 

 how long aloft shall the fair web fly 
 when the bow be bent and the spear draw nigh ? 

 from eve to morn and morn till eve 
 aloft shall fly the work we weave . 

 what then be this , the web ye win ? 
 what wood - beast waxeth stark therein ? 

 we weave the Wolf and the gift of war 
 from the man that be to the man that be . 


 so sing they : and much be all man move at their singing , and 
 there be none but call to mind the old day of the Fathers , and 
 the year when their banner go wide in the world . 

 but the Woodlanders feast they of Burgstead what they might , and 
 then go the dalesman back to their house ; but on the morrow 's 
 morrow they fare thither again , and wood - grey be lay in mound 
 amidst a great assemblage of the Folk . 

 many man say that there be no doubt that those two felon be of 
 the company of those who have ransack the stead of Penny - thumb and 
 Harts - bane ; and so at first deem Bristler the son of Brightling : 
 but after a while , when he have have time to think of it , he change 
 his mind ; for he say that such man as these would have slay first 
 and ransack afterwards : and some who love neither Penny - thumb nor 
 Harts - bane say that they would not have be at the pain to choose 
 for ransack the two bad man about the Dale , whose loss be no 
 loss to any but themselves . 

 as for gold - mane he know not what to think , except that his friend 
 of the Mountain have have nought to do with it . 

 so wear the day awhile . 



 CHAPTER XVI . the BRIDE SPEAKETH with FACE - of - GOD 



 February have die into March , and March be now twelve day old , on a 
 fair and sunny day an hour before noon ; and face - of - god be in a 
 meadow a scant mile down the Dale from Burgstead . he have be 
 drive a bull into a goodman 's byre nearby , and have have to spend 
 toil and patience both in get he out of the field and into the 
 byre ; for the beast be hot with the spring day and the new grass . 
 so now he be rest himself in happy mood in an exceed pleasant 
 place , a little meadow to wit , on one side whereof be a great 
 orchard or grove of sweet chestnut , which go right up to the foot 
 of the Southern Cliffs : across the meadow run a clear brook towards 
 the Weltering Water , free from big stone , in some place dam up 
 for the flooding of the deep pasture - meadow , and with the grass 
 grow on its lip down to the very water . there be a low bank 
 just outside the chestnut tree , as if someone have raise a dyke 
 about they when they be young , which have be tread low and 
 spread through the lapse of year by the faring of many man and 
 beast . the primrose bloom thick upon it now , and here and there 
 along it be a low blackthorn bush in full blossom ; from the mid- 
 meadow and right down to the lip of the brook be the grass well nigh 
 hide by the blossom of the meadow - saffron , with daffodil 
 sprinkle about amongst they , and in the tree and bush the bird , 
 and chiefly the blackbird , be sing their loud . 

 there sit face - of - god on the bank rest after his toil , and happy 
 be his mood ; since in two day ' wear he should be pace the 
 Maiden Ward await the token that be to lead he to Shadowy Vale ; 
 so he sit call to mind the Friend as he have last see she , and 
 strive as it be to set her image stand on the flowery grass 
 before he , till all the beauty of the meadow seem bare and empty 
 to he without she . then it fall into his mind that this have be a 
 beloved trysting - place betwixt he and the Bride , and that often when 
 they be little would they come to gather chestnut in the grove , 
 and thereafter sit and prattle on the old dyke ; or in spring when the 
 season be warm would they go barefoot into the brook , seek its 
 treasure of troutlet and flower and clean - wash agate pebble . 
 Yea , and time not long ago have they meet here to talk as lover , and 
 sit on that very bank in all the kindness of good day without a 
 blemish , and both he and she have love the place well for its wealth 
 of blossom and deep grass and goodly tree and clear running stream . 

 as he think of all this , and how often there he have praise to 
 himself her beauty , which he scarce dare praise to she , he frown 
 and slowly rise to his foot , and turn toward the chestnut - grove , as 
 though he would go thence that way ; but or ever he step down from 
 the dyke he turn about again , and even therewith , like the very 
 image and ghost of his thought , lo ! the Bride herself come up from 
 out the brook and wend toward he , her wet naked foot gleam in 
 the sun as they trod down the tender meadow - saffron and brush past 
 the tuft of daffodil . he stand star at she discomforte , for on 
 that day he have much to think of that seem happy to he , and he 
 deem that she would now question he , and his mind ponder diver 
 way of answer she , and none seem good to he . she draw near 
 and let her skirt fall over her foot , and come to he , her gown hem 
 drag over the flower : then she stand straight up before he and 
 greet he , but reach not forth her hand to he nor touch he . 
 her face be pale that its will not , and her voice tremble as she spake 
 to he and say : 

 ' face - of - god , I would ask thee a gift . ' 

 ' all gift , ' he say , ' that thou may ask , and I may give , lie open 
 to thee . ' 

 she say : ' if I be alive when the time come this gift thou mayst 
 well give I . ' 

 ' Sweet kinswoman , ' say he , ' tell I what it be that thou would 
 have of I . ' and he be ill - at - ease as he wait for her answer . 

 she say : ' ah , kinsman , kinsman ! woe on the day that maketh kinship 
 accurse to I because thou desirest it ! ' 

 he hold his peace and be exceed sorry ; and she say : 

 ' this be the gift that I ask of thee , that in the day to come when 
 thou art wed , thou wilt give I the second man - child whom thou 
 begett . ' 

 he say : ' this shalt thou have , and would that I might give thee 
 much more . would that we be little child together other again , 
 as when we play here in other day . ' 

 she say : ' I would have a token of thee that thou shalt show to the 
 God , and swear on it to give I the gift . for the time change . ' 

 ' what token wilt thou have ? ' say he . 

 she say : ' when next thou farest to the Wood , thou shalt bring I 
 back , it maybe a flower from the bank ye sit upon , or a splinter from 
 the dais of the hall wherein ye feast , or maybe a ring or some matter 
 that the stranger be will not to wear . that shall be the token . ' 

 she speak slowly , hang her head adown , but she lift it presently 
 and look into his face and say : 

 ' Woe 's I , woe 's me , Gold - mane ! how evil be this day , when bewail 
 I I may not bewail thee also ! for I know that thine heart be glad . 
 all through the winter have I keep this hide in my heart , and durst 
 not speak to thee . but now the spring - tide hath drive I to it . 
 let summer come , and who shall say ? ' 

 Great be his grief , and his shame keep he silent , and he have no 
 word to say ; and again she say : 

 ' tell I , gold - mane , when goest thou thither ? ' 

 he say : ' I know not surely , may happen in two day , may happen in 
 ten . why askest thou ? ' 

 ' o friend ! ' she say , ' be it a new thing that I should ask thee 
 whither thou goest and whence thou com , and the time of thy 
 come and go . farewell to - day ! forget not the token . Woe 's I , 
 that I may not kiss thy fair face ! ' 

 she spread her arm abroad and lift up her face as one who waileth , 
 but no sound come from her lip ; then she turn about and go away 
 as she have come . 

 but as for he he stand there after she be go in all confusion , as 
 if he be undo : for he feel his manhood lessen that he should 
 thus and so sorely have hurt a friend , and in a manner against his 
 will . and yet he be somewhat wroth with she , that she have come upon 
 he so suddenly , and speak to he with such mastery , and in so few 
 word , and he with none to make answer to she , and that she have so 
 mar his pleasure and his hope of that fair day . then he sit he 
 down again on the flowery bank , and little by little his heart 
 soften , and he once more call to mind many a time when they have 
 be there before , and the play and the game they have have together 
 there when they be little . and he bethink he of the day that 
 be long to he then , and now seem short to he , and as if they 
 be all grow together into one story , and that a sweet one . then 
 his breast heave with a sob , and the tear rise to his eye and 
 burn and sting he , and he fall a - weeping for that sweet tale , and 
 weep as he have weep once before on that old dyke when there have be 
 some child 's quarrel between they , and she have go away and leave 
 he . 

 then after a while he cease his weeping , and look about he lest 
 anyone might be come , and then he arise and go to and fro in the 
 chestnut - grove for a good while , and afterwards go his way from 
 that meadow , say to himself : ' yet remaineth to I the morrow of 
 to - morrow , and that be the first of the day of the watching for the 
 token . ' 

 but all that day he be slow to meet the eye of man ; and in the hall 
 that eve he be silent and moody ; for from time to time it come over 
 he that some of his manhood have depart from he . 



 CHAPTER XVII . the TOKEN COMETH from the MOUNTAIN 



 the next day wear away tidingless ; and the day after face - of - god 
 arise betime ; for it be the first day of his watch , and he be at 
 the Maiden Ward before the time appoint on a very fair and bright 
 morning , and he go to and fro on that place , and have no tiding . 
 so he come away somewhat cast down , and say within himself : ' be it 
 but a lie and a mocking when all be say ? ' 

 on the morrow he go thither again , and the morn be wild and stormy 
 with drift of rain , and low cloud hurry over the earth , though 
 for the sunrise they lift a little in the east , and the sun come up 
 over the pass , amidst the red and angry rack of cloud . this morn 
 also give he no tiding of the token , and he be wroth and perturb 
 in spirit : but towards evening he say : 

 ' it be well : ten day she give I , so that she might be able to send 
 without fail on one of they ; she will not fail I . ' 

 so again on the morrow he be there betime , and the morn be windy 
 as on the day before , but the cloud higher and of well promise for 
 the day . face - of - god walk to and fro on the Maiden Ward , and as he 
 turn toward Burgstead for the tenth time , he hear , as he deem , a 
 bow - string twang afar off , and even therewith come a shaft fly 
 heavily like a wing bird , which smite a great stand stone on the 
 other side of the way , where of old some chieftain have be bury , 
 and fall to earth at its foot . he go up to it and handle it , and 
 see that there be a piece of thin parchment wrap about it , which 
 indeed he be eager to unwrap at once , but forebore ; because he be 
 on the highway , and people be already astir , and even then pass 
 by he a goodman of the Dale with a man of his go afield together , 
 and they give he the sele of the day . so he go along the highway 
 a little till he come to a place where be a footbridge over into the 
 meadow . he cross thereby and go swiftly till he reach a rise 
 ground grow over with hazel - tree ; there he sit down among the 
 rabbit - hole , the primrose and wild - garlic blooming about he , and 
 three blackbird answer one another from the edge of the coppice . 
 Straightway when he have look and see none come he break the 
 thread that be wind about the scroll and the arrow , and unroll 
 the parchment ; and there be write thereon in black ink of small 
 letter , but very fair , and this be what he read therein : 


 come thou to the Mountain Hall by the path which thou know of , on 
 the morrow of the day whereon thou readest this . rise betime and 
 come armed , for there be other man than we in the wood ; to whom thy 
 death should be a gain . when thou art come to the Hall , thou shalt 
 find no man therein ; but a great hound only , tie to a bench nigh the 
 dais . call he by his name , sure - foot to wit , and give he to eat 
 from the meat upon the board , and give he water to drink . if the 
 day be then far spend , as it be like to be , abide thou with the hound 
 in the hall through the night , and eat of what thou shalt find there ; 
 but see that the hound fare not abroad till the morrow 's morn : then 
 lead he out and bring he to the north - east corner of the Hall , and 
 he shall lift the slot for thee that leadeth to the Shadowy Yale . 
 follow he and all good go with thee . 


 now when he have read this , earth seem fair indeed about he , and he 
 scarce know whither to turn or what to do to make the most of his 
 joy . he presently go back to Burgstead and into the House of the 
 Face , where all man be astir now , and the day be clear . he hide 
 the shaft under his kirtle , for he would not that any should see it ; 
 so he go to his shut - bed and lay it up in his chest , wherein he 
 keep his chief treasure ; but the writing on the scroll he set in 
 his bosom and so hide it . he go joyfully and proudly , as one who 
 knoweth more tiding and well than those around he . but stone- 
 face behold he , and say ' Foster - son , thou art happy . be it that 
 the spring - tide be in thy blood , and maketh thee blithe with all 
 thing , or hast thou some new tiding ? Nay , I would not have an 
 answer out of thee ; but here be good rede : when next thou goest into 
 the wood , it be nought so ill for thee to have a valiant old carle 
 by thy side ; one that loveth thee , and would die for thee if need 
 be ; one who might watch when thou wert seek . or else beware ! 
 for there be evil thing abroad in the Wood , and moreover the 
 brother of those two felon who be slay at Carlstead . ' 

 then gold - mane constrain himself to answer the old carle softly ; 
 and he thank he kindly for his offer , and say that so it should 
 be before long . so the talk between they fall , and stone - face go 
 away somewhat well - pleased . 

 and now be face - of - god become wary ; and he would not draw man 's eye 
 and speech on he ; so he go afield with Hall - face to deal with the 
 lamb and the ewe , and do like other man . no less wary be he in 
 the hall that even , and neither spake much nor little ; and when his 
 father spake to he concern the Bride , and make game of he as a 
 somewhat sluggish groom , he do not change countenance , but answer 
 lightly what come to hand . 

 on the morrow ere the early dawn he be afoot , and he clothe himself 
 and do on his hauberk , his father 's work , which be fine - wrought and 
 a stout defence , and reach down to his knee ; and over that he do 
 on a goodly green kirtle well embroider : he girt his war - sword to 
 his side , and it be the work of his father 's father , and a very good 
 sword : its name be Dale - warden . he do a good helm on his head , 
 and sling a targe at his back , and take two spear in his hand , short 
 but strong - shaft and well - steel . thus array he leave Burgstead 
 before the dawn , and come to Wildlake 's way and betook he to the 
 Woodland . he make no stop or stay on the path , but eat his meat 
 standing by an oak - tree close by the half - blind track . when he come 
 to the little wood - lawn , where be the toft of the ancient house , he 
 look all round about he , for he deem that a likely place for 
 those ugly wood - wight to set on he ; but nought befell he , though 
 he stoop and drink of the woodland rill warily enough . so he 
 pass on ; and there be other place also where he fare warily , 
 because they seem like to hold lurking felon ; though forsooth the 
 whole wood might well serve their turn . but no evil befell he , and 
 at last , when it yet lack an hour to sunset , he come to the wood- 
 lawn where Wild - wearer have make his onset that other eve . 

 he go straight up to the house , his heart beat , and he scarce 
 believe but that he should find the Friend abide he there : but 
 when he push the door it give way before he at once , and he 
 enter and find no man therein , and the wall strip bare and no 
 shield or weapon hang on the panel . but the hound he see tie to 
 a bench nigh the dai , and the bristle on the beast 's neck arise , 
 and he snarl on face - of - god , and strain on his leathern leash . 
 then face - of - god go up to he and call he by his name , Sure- 
 foot , and give he his hand to lick , and he bring he water , and 
 feed he with flesh from the meat on the board ; so the beast become 
 friendly and wag his tail and whine and slobber his hand . 

 then he go all about the house , and see and hear no living thing 
 therein save the mouse in the panel and sure - foot . so he come back 
 to the dai , and sit he down at the board and eat his fill , and 
 think concern his case . and it come into his mind that the 
 Woman of the Mountain have some deed for he to do which would try his 
 manliness and exalt his fame ; and his heart rise high and he be 
 glad , and he see himself sit beside she on the dais of a very 
 fair hall belove and honour of all the folk , and none have aught to 
 say against he or owe he any grudge . thus he please himself in 
 thinking of the good day to come , sit there till the hall grow 
 dusk and dark and the night - wind moan about it . 

 then after a while he arise and rake together the brand on the 
 hearth , and make light in the hall and look to the door . and he 
 find there be bolt and bar thereto , so he shoot the bolt and 
 draw the bar into their place and make all as sure as might be . 
 then he bring sure - foot down from the dai , and tie he up so that 
 he might lie down athwart the door , and then lie down his hauberk 
 with his naked sword ready to his hand , and sleep long while . 

 when he awake it be dark than when he have lain he for the moon 
 have set ; yet he deem that the day be at point of breaking . so he 
 fetch water and wash the night off he , and see a little glimmer 
 of the dawn . then he eat somewhat of the meat on the board , and do 
 on his helm and his other gear , and unbar the door , and lead Sure- 
 foot without , and bring he to the north - east corner of the house , 
 and in a little while he lift the slot and they depart , the man 
 and the hound , just as break dawn from over the mountain . 

 sure - foot lead right into the heart of the pine - wood , and it be dark 
 enough therein , with nought but a feeble glimmer for some while , and 
 long be the way therethrough ; but in two hour ' space be there 
 something of a break , and they come to the shore of a dark deep tarn 
 on whose windless and green water the daylight shine fully . the 
 hound skirt the water , and lead on unchecked till the tree begin to 
 grow small and the air colder for all that the sun be high ; for 
 they have be go up and up all the way . 

 so at last after a six hour ' journey they come clean out of the 
 pine - wood , and before they lie the black wilderness of the bare 
 mountain , and beyond they , look quite near now , the great ice- 
 peak , the wall of the world . it be but an hour short of noon by 
 this time , and the high sun shine down on a barren boggy moss which 
 lie betwixt they and the rocky waste . sure - foot make no stay , but 
 thread the way that go betwixt the quagmire , and in another 
 hour lead face - of - god into a wind valley blind by great rock , 
 and everywhere stony and rough , with a trickle of water run 
 amidst of it . the hound fare on up the dale to where the water be 
 bridge by a great fall stone , and so over it and up a steep bent 
 on the further side , on to a marvellously rough mountain - neck , while 
 mere black sand cumber with scatter rock and stone , while 
 beset with mire grow over with the cottony mire - grass ; here and 
 there a little scanty grass grow ; otherwhere nought but dwarf 
 willow ever die ever grow , mingle with moss or red - blossom 
 sengreen ; and all blend together into mere desolation . 

 few live thing they see there ; up on the neck a few sheep be 
 graze the scanty grass , but there be none to tend they ; yet face- 
 of - god deem the sight of they good , for there must be man anigh who 
 own they . for the rest , the whimbrel laugh across the mire ; 
 high up in heaven a great eagle be hang ; once and again a grey 
 fox leapt up before they , and the heath - fowl whir up from under 
 face - of - god 's foot . a raven who be sit croak on a rock in 
 that first dale stir uneasily on his perch as he see they , and 
 when they be pass flap his wing and fly after they croak 
 still . 

 now they fare over that neck somewhat east , make but slow way 
 because the ground be so broken and rocky ; and in another hour 's 
 space sure - foot lead down - hill due east to where the stony neck sink 
 into another desolate miry heath still fall toward the east , but 
 whose further side be wall by a rampart of crag cleft at their 
 top into marvellous - shape , coal - black , ungrassed and unmossed . 
 Thitherward the hound lead straight , and gold - mane follow wonder : 
 as he draw near they he see that they be not very high , the tall 
 peak scant fifty foot from the face of the heath . 

 they make their way through the scatter rock at the foot of these 
 crag , till , just where the rock - wall seem the close , the way 
 through the stone turn into a path go through it skew - wise ; and 
 it be now so clear a path that belike it have be better by man 's 
 hand . down thereby face - of - god follow the hound , deeming that he 
 be come to the gate of the Shadowy Vale , and the path go down 
 steeply and swiftly . but when he have go down a while , the rock on 
 his right hand sink lower for a space , so that he could look over and 
 see what lie beneath . 

 there lie below he a long narrow vale quite plain at the bottom , 
 wall on the further side as on the hither by sheer rock of black 
 stone . the plain be grow over with grass , but he could see no tree 
 therein : a deep river , dark and green , run through the vale , 
 sometimes through its midmost , sometimes lap the further rock- 
 wall : and he think indeed that on many a day in the year the sun 
 would never shine on that valley . 

 thus much he see , and then the rock rise again and shut it from his 
 sight ; and at last they draw so close together over head that he be 
 in a way go through a cave with little daylight come from above , 
 and in the end he be in a cave indeed and mere darkness : but with 
 the last feeble glimmer of light he think he see carve on a smooth 
 space of the live rock at his left hand the image of a wolf . 

 this cave last but a little way , and soon the hound and the man 
 be go once more between sheer black rock , and the path grow 
 steep yet and be cut into step . at last there be a sharp turn , 
 and they stand on the top of a long stony scree , down which sure - foot 
 bound eagerly , give tongue as he go ; but face - of - god stand 
 still and look , for now the whole Dale lie open before he . 

 that river run from north to south , and at the south end the cliff 
 draw so close to it that look thence no outgate could be see ; but 
 at the north end there be as it be a dreary street of rock , the 
 river flow amidmost and leave little foothold on either side , 
 somewhat as it be with the pass lead from the mountain into 
 Burgdale . 

 Amidmost of the Dale a little toward the north end he see a doom - ring 
 of black stone , and hard by it an ancient hall build of the same 
 black stone both wall and roof , and thitherward be sure - foot now 
 run . face - of - god look up and down the Dale and could see no 
 break in the wall of sheer rock : toward the southern end he see a 
 few booth and cot build roughly of stone and thatch with turf ; 
 thereabout he see a few folk move about , the most of whom seem to 
 be woman and child ; there be some sheep and lamb near these 
 cot , and a herd of fifty or so of somewhat goodly mountain - kine be 
 feed higher up the valley . he could look down into the river from 
 where he stand , and he see that it run between rocky bank go 
 straight down from the face of the meadow , which be rather high 
 above the water , so that it seem little likely that the water 
 should rise over its bank , either in summer or winter ; and in summer 
 be it like to be high , because the vale be so near to the high 
 mountain and their snow . 



 CHAPTER XVIII . FACE - of - GOD talketh with the FRIEND in SHADOWY VALE 



 it be now about two hour after noon , and a broad band of sunlight 
 lie upon the grass of the vale below Gold - mane 's foot ; he go 
 lightly down the scree , and stride forward over the level grass 
 toward the doom - ring , his helm and war - gear glitter bright in the 
 sun . he must need go through the doom - ring to come to the Hall , and 
 as he step out from behind the last of the big upright - stone , he 
 see a woman stand on the threshold of the Hall - door , which be but 
 some score of pace from he , and know she at once for the Friend . 

 she be clothe like himself in a green kirtle gaily embroider and 
 fitting close to her body , and have no gown or cloak over it ; she have 
 a golden fillet on her head beset with blue mountain stone , and her 
 hair hang loose behind she . 

 her beauty be so exceeding , and so far beyond all memory of she that 
 his mind have hold , that once more fear of she fall upon face - of - god , 
 and he stand still with beat heart till she should speak to he . 
 but she come forward swiftly with both her hand hold out , smile 
 and happy - faced , and look very kindly on he , and she take his 
 hand and say to he : 

 ' now welcome , gold - mane , welcome , face - of - god ! and twice welcome art 
 thou and threefold . Lo ! this be the day that thou ask for : art 
 thou happy in it ? ' 

 he lift her hand to his lip and kiss they timorously , but say 
 nought ; and therewithal sure - foot come run forth from the Hall , 
 and fall to bound round about they , bark noisily after the 
 manner of dog who have meet their master again ; and still she hold 
 his hand and behold he kindly . then she call the hound to she , 
 and pat he on the neck and quiet he , and then turn to face- 
 of - god and laugh happily and say : 

 ' I do not bid thee hold thy peace ; yet thou sayest nought . be well 
 with thee ? ' 

 ' yea , ' he say , ' and more than well . ' 

 ' thou seemest to I a goodly warrior , ' she say ; ' hast thou meet any 
 foeman yesterday or this morning ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say he , ' none hinder I ; thou hast make the way easy to 
 I . ' 

 she say soberly , ' such as I might do , I do . but we may not wield 
 everything , for our foe be many , and I fear for thee . but come 
 thou into our house , which be ours , and far more ours than the booth 
 before the pine - wood . ' 

 she take his hand again and lead he toward the door , but face - of - god 
 look up , and above the lintel he see carve on the dark stone that 
 image of the Wolf , even as he have see it carve on Wood - grey 's tie- 
 beam ; and therewith such thought come into his mind that he stop 
 to look , press the Friend 's hand hard as though bid her note 
 it . the stone wherein the image be carve be dark than the other 
 building stone , and might be call black ; the jaw of the wood- 
 beast be open and gaping , and have be paint with cinnabar , but 
 wind and weather have wear away the most of the colour . 

 spake the friend : ' so it be : thou behold the token of the God 
 and Father of out Fathers , that telleth the tale of so many day , 
 that the day which now pass by we be to they but as the drop in the 
 sea of water . Thou beholdest the sign of our sorrow , the memory of 
 our wrong ; yet be it also the token of our hope . maybe it shall lead 
 thee far . ' 

 ' Whither ? ' say he . but she answer not a great while , and he 
 look at she as she stand a - gaze on the image , and see how the 
 tear steal out of her eye and run adown her cheek . then again 
 come the thought to he of Wood - grey 's hall , and the woman of the 
 kindre stand before the Wolf and singing of he ; and though there 
 be little comeliness in they and she be so exceed beauteous , he 
 could not but deem that they be akin to she . 

 but after a while she wipe the tear from her face and turn to he 
 and say : ' my friend , the Wolf shall lead thee no - whither but where 
 I also shall be , whatsoever peril or grief may beset the road or lurk 
 at the ending thereof . thou shalt be no thrall , to labour while I 
 look on . ' 

 his heart swell within he as she speak , and he be at point to 
 beseech her love that moment ; but now her face have grow gay and 
 bright again , and she say while he be gather word to speak 
 withal : 

 ' come in , gold - mane , come into our house ; for I have many thing to 
 say to thee . and moreover thou art so hush , and so fearsome in thy 
 mail , that I think thou yet deemest I to be a Wight of the Waste , 
 such as stone - face thy Fosterer tell thee tale of , and forewarn 
 thee . so would I eat before thee , and sign the meat with the sign of 
 the Earth - god 's Hammer , to show thee that he be in error concern 
 I , and that I be a very woman flesh and fall , as my kindre be 
 before I . ' 

 he laugh and be exceed glad , and say : ' tell I now , kind 
 friend , dost thou deem that stone - face 's tale be mere mockery of 
 his dream , and that he be beguile by empty semblance or less ? or 
 be there such wight in the Waste . ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , ' the man be a true man ; and of these thing be 
 there many ancient tale which we may not doubt . yet so it be that 
 such wight have I never yet see , nor aught to scare I save evil 
 man : belike it be that I have be over - much busy in deal with 
 sorrow and ruin to look after they : or it may be that they fear I 
 and the wrath - breed grief of the kindred . ' 

 he look at she earnestly , and the wisdom of her heart seem to 
 enter into his ; but she say : ' it be of man we must talk , and of I 
 and thee . come with I , my friend . ' 

 and she step lightly over the threshold and draw he in . the Hall 
 be stern and grim and somewhat dusky , for its window be but 
 small : it be all of stone , both wall and roof . there be no 
 timber - work therein save the bench and chair , a little about the 
 door at the low end that lead to the buttery and out - bower ; and 
 this seem to have be work of late year ; yea , the chair 
 against the gable on the dai be of stone build into the wall , 
 adorn with carve somewhat sparingly , the image of the Wolf being 
 do over the midmost of they . he look up and down the Hall , and 
 deem it some seventy foot over all from end to end ; and he could 
 see in the dimness those same goodly hanging on the wall which he 
 have see in the woodland booth . 

 she lead he up to the dai , and stand there lean up against the 
 arm of one of those stone seat silent for a while ; then she turn 
 and look at he , and say : 

 ' yea , thou look a goodly warrior ; yet be I glad that thou cam 
 hither without battle . tell I , gold - mane , ' she say , take one of 
 his spear from his hand , ' art thou deft with the spear ? ' 

 ' I have be call so , ' say he . 

 she look at he sweetly and say : ' canst thou show I the feat of 
 spear - throwing in this Hall , or shall we wend outside presently that 
 I may see thee throw ? ' 

 ' the Hall sufficeth , ' he say . ' shall I set this steel in the lintel 
 of the buttery door yonder ? ' 

 ' Yea , if thou canst , ' she say . 

 he smile and take the spear from she , and poise it and shake it 
 till it quiver again , then suddenly draw back his arm and cast , and 
 the shaft speed whistle down the dim hall , and smite the aforesaid 
 door - lintel and stick there quiver : then he spring down from the 
 dais , and run down the hall , and put forth his hand and pull it 
 forth from the wood , and be on the dais again in a trice , and cast 
 again , and the second time set the spear in the same place , and then 
 take his other spear from the board and cast it , and there stand the 
 two stave in the wood side by side ; then he go soberly down the 
 hall and draw they both out of the wood and come back to she , while 
 she stand watch he , her cheek flush , her lip a little parted . 

 she say : ' good spear - casting , forsooth ! and far above what our folk 
 can do , who be no great thrower of the spear . ' 

 gold - mane laugh : ' Sooth be that , ' say he , ' or hardly be I here 
 to teach thee spear - throwing . ' 

 ' wilt thou never be pay for that simple onslaught ? ' she say . 

 ' have I be pay then ? ' say he . 

 she redden , for she remember her word to he on the mountain ; and 
 he put his hand on her shoulder and kiss she cheek , but timorously ; 
 nor do she withstand he or shrink aback , but say soberly : 

 ' good indeed be thy spear - throwing , and meseem my brother will love 
 thee when he hath see thee strike a stroke or two in wrath . but , 
 fair warrior , there be no foeman here : so get thee to the low end 
 of the Hall , and in the bower beyond shalt thou find fresh water ; 
 there wash the waste from off thee , and do off thine helm and 
 hauberk , and come back speedily and eat with I ; for I hunger , and so 
 dost thou . ' 

 he do as she bid he , and come back presently bear in his hand 
 both helm and hauberk , and he look light - limbed and trim and 
 lissome , an exceed goodly man . 



 CHAPTER XIX . the fair woman TELLETH FACE - of - GOD of her KINDRED 



 when he come back to the dais he see that there be meat upon the 
 board , and the Friend say to he : 

 ' now art thou gold - mane indeed : but come now , sit by I and eat , 
 though the wood - woman giveth thee but a sorry banquet , o guest ; but 
 from the Dale it be , and we be too far now from the dwelling of man 
 to have delicate meat on the board , though to - night when they come 
 back thy cheer shall be well . yet even then thou shalt have no 
 such dainty as stone - face hath imagine for thee at the hand of 
 the wood - wight . ' 

 she laugh therewith , and he no less ; and in sooth the meat be but 
 simple , of curd and new cheese , meat of the herdsman . but face - of- 
 god say gaily : ' sweet it shall be to I ; good be all that the 
 Friend giveth . ' 

 then she raise her hand and make the sign of the Hammer over the 
 board , and look up at he and say : 

 ' hath the Earth - god change my face , gold - mane , to what I verily be ? ' 

 he hold his face close to hers and look into it , and he - seem it 
 be as pure as the water of a mountain lake , and as fine and well- 
 work every deal of it as when his father have work in his stithy 
 many day and fashion a small piece of great mastery . he be 
 ashamed to kiss she again , but he say to himself , ' this be the 
 fair woman of the world , whom I have swear to we d this year . ' 
 then he spake aloud and say : 

 ' I see the face of the Friend , and it will not change to I . ' 

 again she redden a little , and the happy look in her face seem to 
 grow yet sweeter , and he be bewilder with longing and delight . 

 but she stand up and go to an ambrye in the wall and bring forth 
 a horn shod and lip with silver of ancient fashion , and she pour 
 wine into it and hold it forth and say : 

 ' o guest from the Dale , I pledge thee ! and when thou hast drunk to I 
 in turn we will talk of weighty matter . for indeed I bear hope in 
 my hand too heavy for the daughter of man to bear ; and thou art a 
 chieftain 's son , and mayst well help I to bear they ; so let we talk 
 simply and without guile , as folk that trust one another . ' 

 so she drink and hold out the horn to he , and he take the horn and 
 her hand both , and he kiss her hand and say : 

 ' here in this Hall I drink to the Sons of the Wolf , whosoever they 
 be . ' Therewith he drink and he say : ' simply and guilelessly indeed 
 will I talk with thee ; for I be weary of lie , and for thy sake have 
 I tell a many . ' 

 ' thou shalt tell no more , ' she say ; ' and as for the health thou hast 
 drunk , it be good , and shall profit thee . now sit we here in these 
 ancient seat and let we talk . ' 

 so they sit they down while the sun be westere in the March 
 afternoon , and she say : 

 ' tell I first what tiding have be in the Dale . ' 

 so he tell she of the ransacking and of the murder at Carlstead . 

 she say : ' these tiding have we hear before , and some deal of they 
 we know well than ye do , or can ; for we be the ransacker of 
 Penny - thumb and Harts - bane . Thereof will I say more presently . what 
 other tiding hast thou to tell of ? what oath be swear upon the 
 Boar last Yule ? ' 

 so he tell she of the oath of Bristler the son of Brightling . she 
 smile and say : ' he shall keep his oath , and yet redden no blade . ' 

 then he tell of his father 's oath , and she say : 

 ' it be good ; but even so would he do and no oath swear . all man may 
 trust iron - face . and thou , my friend , what oath didst thou swear ? ' 

 his face grow somewhat troubled as he say : ' I swear to we d the 
 fair woman in the world , though the dalesman gainsay I , and they 
 beyond the Dale . ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' and there be no need to ask thee whom thou didst 
 mean by thy " fairest woman , " for I have see that thou deemest I 
 fair enough . my friend , maybe thy kindred will be against it , and 
 the kindre of the Bride ; and it might be that my kindre would have 
 gainsay it if thing be not as they be . but though all man 
 gainsay it , yet will not i. it be meet and right that we twain we d. ' 

 she spake very soberly and quietly , but when she have speak there be 
 nothing in his heart but joy and gladness : yet shame of her 
 loveliness refrain he , and he cast down his eye before her . 
 then she say in a kind voice : 

 ' I know thee , how glad thou art of this word of mine , because thou 
 lookest on I with eye of love , and thinkest of I as well than I 
 be ; though I be no ill woman and no beguiler . but this be not all 
 that I have to say to thee , though it be much ; for there be more 
 folk in the world than thou and I only . but I tell thee this first , 
 that thou might trust I in all thing . so , my friend , if thou 
 canst , refrain thy joy and thy longing a little , and hearken to what 
 concerneth thee and I , and thy people and mine . ' 

 ' fair woman and sweet friend , ' he say , ' thou know of a gladness 
 which be hard to bear if one must lay it aside for a while ; and of a 
 longing which be hard to refrain if it mingle with another longing - 
 know thou not ? ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' I know it . ' 

 ' yet , ' say face - of - god , ' I will forbear as thou bidd I . tell 
 I , then , what be the felon who be slay at Carlstead ? know 
 thou of they ? ' 

 ' over well , ' she say , ' they be our foe this many a year ; and since 
 we meet last autumn they have become foe of you Dalesmen also . soon 
 shall ye have tiding of they ; and it be against they that I bid 
 thee arm yesterday . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' be it against they that thou wouldst have we do 
 battle along with thy folk ? ' 

 ' so it be , ' she say ; ' no other foeman have we . and now , gold - mane , 
 thou art become a friend of the Wolf , and shalt before long be of 
 affinity with our House ; that other day thou didst ask I to tell 
 thee of I and mine , and now will I do accord to thine asking . 
 Short shall my tale be ; because maybe thou shalt hear it tell again , 
 and in goodly wise , before thine whole folk . 

 ' as thou wottest we be now outlaw and wolf ' head ; and while we 
 lift the gear of man , but ever if we may of ill man and not of good ; 
 there be no worthy goodman of the Dale from whom we would take one 
 hoof , or a skin of wine , or a cake of wax . 

 ' wherefore be we outlaw ? because we have be drive from our own , 
 and we bear away our life and our weapon , and little else ; and for

 narrow and poor it be , though it hath be the nurse of warrior in 
 time past . 

 ' Hearken ! time long ago come the kindred of the Wolf to these 
 Mountains of the World ; and they be in a pass in the stony maze and 
 the utter wilderness of the Mountains , and the foe be behind they in 
 number not to be bear up against . and so it befell that the pass 
 fork , and there be two way before our Folk ; and one part of they 
 would take the way to the north and the other the way to the south ;

 they sunder into two company , and one take one way and one 
 another . now as to those who fare by the southern road , we know not 
 what befell they , nor for long and long have we any tale of they . 

 ' but we who take the northern road , we happen on this Vale amidst 
 the wilderness , and we be weary of flee from the over - mastering 
 foe ; and the dale seem enough , and a refuge , and a place to dwell 
 in , and no man be there before we , and few be like to find it , and 
 we be but a few . so we dwelt here in this Vale for as wild as it 
 be , the place where the sun shineth never in the winter , and scant be

 here many season . 

 ' we have a few sheep in the wilderness , and a few neat feed down the 
 grass of the Vale ; and we find gem and copper in the rock about we 
 wherewith at while to chaffer with the alien , and fish we draw from

 wait there till the time serve , and then drive the spoil from under

 there to bear more warrior to the Folk . 

 ' but the season pass , and the Folk multiply in Shadowy Vale , and 
 livelihood seem like to fail they , and need must they seek wide 
 land . so by way which thou wilt one day wot of , we come into a 
 valley that lieth north - west of Shadowy Vale : a land like thine of 
 Burgdale , or well ; wide it be , plenteous of grass and tree , well 
 water , full of all thing that man can desire . 

 ' be there man before we in this Dale ? sayest thou . yea , but not 
 very many , and they feeble in battle , weak of heart , though strong of 
 body . these , when they see the Sons of the Wolf with weapon in 
 their hand , feel themselves puny before we , and their heart fail 
 they ; and they come to we with gift , and offer to share the Dale 
 between they and we , for they say there be enough for both folk . 
 so we take their offer and become their friend ; and some of our 
 Houses wed wife of the stranger , and give they their woman to 
 wife . therein they do amiss ; for the blended Folk as the 
 generation pass become soft than our blood , and many be 
 untrusty and greedy and tyrannous , and the day of the whoredom fall 
 upon we , and when we deem ourselves the mighty then be we the 
 near to our fall . but the House whereof I be would never we d with 
 these Westlanders , and other Houses there be who have affinity with

 with ours into that fruitful Dale ; and these be call the Red 
 Hand , and the Silver Arm , and the Golden Bushel , and the Ragged 
 Sword . Thou hast hear those name once before , friend ? ' 

 ' yea , ' he say , and as he speak the picture of that other day come 
 back to he , and he call to mind all that he have say , and his 
 happiness of that hour seem the more and the sweeter for that 
 memory . 

 she go on : ' fair and goodly be that Dale as mine own eye have 
 see , and plentiful of all thing , and up in its mountain to the 
 east be cave and pit whence silver be digge abundantly ; therefore 
 be the Dale call silver - dale . Hast thou hear thereof , my friend ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say face - of - god , ' though I have marvel whence ye gat such 
 foison of silver . ' 

 he look on she and marvel , for now she seem as if it be 
 another woman : her eye be gleam bright , her lip be part ;

 ' Happy live the Folk in silver - dale for many and many winter and

 be strange that ye dalesman have not hear of silver - dale . '

 very far away : but no far do we know of toward that airt . Lieth 
 silver - dale anywhere nigh to rose - dale ? ' 

 she say : ' it be the next dale to it , yet be it a far journey 
 betwixt the two , for the ice - sea pusheth a horn in betwixt they ; and

 have , and therewithal our life to be cast away or use for their

 conquer long ago , and stout as they be of body their heart

 ' why make a long tale of it ? we who be leave , and could brook death

 vanquished but free man . for at the end of three day ' fight we have 
 be drive up to the easternmost end of the Dale , and up anigh to

 dale , and we have those with we who know every cranny of that way ,

 win all , they be fight to keep , and not to slay , and a few 
 stubborn carle and queen , of what use would they be , or where be 
 the gain of risk life to win they ? 

 ' so they forbear we , and night come on moonless and dark ; and it be 
 the early spring season , when the day be not yet long , and so by 
 night and cloud we flee away , and back again to Shadowy Vale . 

 ' Forsooth , we be but a few ; for when we be get into this Vale , 
 this strip of grass and water in the wilderness , and have tell up our

 ' there sit we in yonder Doom - ring and take counsel , and to some it

 beset the skirt of the foeman till the day should well ; but

 brother Folk - might   for that be his name in the kindred . and I 
 sorrow at his departure , for he have bear I thither out of the

 and froward use on he full oft . 

 ' so in this Vale abode we that be leave , and the season pass ; 
 some of the elder die , and some of the child also ; but more

 and even some of the woman , would steal through pass that we , and 
 we only , know of , and would fall upon the Aliens in silver - dale as 
 occasion serve , and lift their good both live and dead ; and this 
 become both a craft and a pastime amongst we . nor may I hide that we 
 sometimes go lift otherwhere ; for in the summer and autumn we 
 would fare west a little and abide in the wood the season through , 
 and hunt the deer thereof , and while would we drive the spoil from

 ' now that little wood - lawn with the toft of an ancient dwelling in

 ' thus then grow up our young man ; and our maid be little soft ;

 not , though love be amongst we . ' 

 she stop awhile and fall a - musing , and her face soften , and she

 ' Glad indeed be I to behold he ; and for he when he have take I in

 ' now when we ask he concern Stone - wolf and the other who be

 ' of Rose - dale we wotte already that the Folk be nought of our

 ' soon he fall in with thy Folk , and first of all with the

 ' then he go amongst the Shepherd - Folk , and he deem they good man

 ' therein wert thou wise , ' say face - of - god ; ' but now that we be

 I in the market - place . there be he gueste in your Hall , and once

 that eve   for indeed I be somewhat foresee , he say : " look thou , 
 Sun - beam , if he cometh , it be not unlike that I shall drive a spear 
 through he . " " wherefore ? " say I ; " can he serve our turn when he be

 say WHEREFORE ? I tell thee this stripling beguileth to she

 nigh our abode . Nay , Gold - mane , what would'st thou with thy sword ? 
 why art thou so red and wrathful ? Would'st thou fight with my

 another ? Hath she note nought of thee this winter and spring ? be

 changeful mountain - stone , and give it to he , and say : 

 ' thou shalt give she this and tell she whence thou hadst it ; and tell 
 she that I bid she remember that to - morrow be a new day . ' 



 CHAPTER XX . those two together hold the ring of the EARTH - GOD 



 and now they fall silent both of they , and sit hearken the sound

 array for battle against the Dusky Men ? this be easy to do , and 
 great be the reward thou offer I . ' 

 ' I look for this service of thee , ' she say , ' and none other . ' 

 ' and when I go down to the battle , ' say he , ' shalt thou be sorry for 
 our sundering ? ' 

 she say : ' there shall be no sundering ; I shall wend with thee . '

 I ? '

 again be there silence betwixt they , till at last he say :

 ' yea , ' say she . 

 again for a while no word be speak , and face - of - god look on she

 ' and , gold - mane , I have see hitherto but the outward show and image

 ' out on it ! have I beguile myself as well as thee ? these be but

 they have both rise up as she spake , and now he put forth his hand

 then sing man song of old time , and amongst they Wood - wont stand
