 sing : 


 she singeth . 

 Wild be the waste and long league over ; 
  Whither then wend ye spear and sword , 
 where nought shall see your helm but the plover , 
  far and far from the dear Dale 's sward ? 

 he singeth . 

 many a league shall we wend together 
  with helm and spear and bended bow . 
 Hark ! how the wind blow up for weather : 
  Dark shall the night be whither we go . 

 Dark shall the night be round the byre , 
  and dark as we drive the brindle kine ; 
 dark and dark round the beacon - fire , 
  Dark down in the pass round our waver line . 

 turn on thy path , o fair - foot maiden , 
  and come our way by the pathless road ; 
 look how the cloud hang low and laden 
  over the wall of the old abode ! 

 she singeth . 

 Bare be my foot for the rough waste 's wending , 
  Wild be the wind , and my kirtle 's thin ; 
 Faint shall I be ere the long way 's end 
  drop down to the Dale and the grief therein . 

 he singeth . 

 do on the brogue of the wild - wood rover , 
  do on the byrnie ' ring - close mail ; 
 take thou the staff that the barb hang over , 
  o'er the wind and the waste and the way to prevail . 

 come , for how from thee shall I sunder ? 
  come , that a tale may arise in the land ; 
 come , that the night may be hold for a wonder , 
  when the Wolf be lead by a maiden 's hand ! 

 she singeth . 

 now will I fare as ye be fare , 
  and wend no way but the way ye wend ; 
 and bear but the burden ye be bear , 
  and end the day as ye shall end . 

 and many an eve when the cloud be drift 
  down through the Dale till they dim the roof , 
 shall they tell in the Hall of the Maiden 's Lifting , 
  and how we drave the spoil aloof . 

 they sing together . 

 over the moss through the wind and the weather , 
  through the morn and the eve and the death of the day , 
 wend we man and maid together , 
  for out of the waste be bear the fray . 


 then the Sun - beam spake to Gold - mane softly , and tell he how this 
 song be make by a minstrel concern a foray in the early day of 
 their first abode in Shadowy Vale , and how in good sooth a maiden lead 
 the fray and be the captain of the warrior : 

 ' erst , ' she say , ' this be count as a wonder ; but now we be so 
 few that it be no wonder though the woman will do whatsoever they 
 may . ' 

 so they talk , and gold - mane be very happy ; but ere the good - night 
 cup be drunk , Folk - might spake to face - of - god and say : 

 ' it be well that ye rise betime in the morning : but thou shalt 
 not go back by the way thou camest . wood - wise and another shall go 
 with thee , and show thee a way across the neck and the heath , which 
 be rough enough as far as toil go , but where thy life shall be 
 safe ; and thereby shalt thou hit the ghyll of the Weltering Water , 
 and so come down safely into Burgdale . now that we be friend and 
 fellow , it be no hurt for thee to know the short way to Shadowy 
 Vale . what thou shalt tell concern we in Burgdale I leave the 
 tale thereof to thee ; yet belike thou wilt not tell everything till I 
 come to Burgstead at the spring market - tide . now must I presently to 
 bed ; for before daylight to - morrow must I be follow the hunt along 
 with two score good man of our . ' 

 ' what beast be afield then ? ' say Gold - mane . 

 say folk - might : ' the beast that beset our life , the Dusky Men . 
 in these day we have learn how to find company of they ; and 
 forsooth every week they draw nigh to this Dale ; and some day they 
 should happen upon we if we be not to look to it , and then would 
 there be a murder great and grim ; therefore we scour the heath round 
 about , and the skirt of the woodland , and we fall upon these felon 
 in diver guise , so that they may not know we for the same man ; 
 while be we clothe in homespun , as to - day , and seem like to field- 
 working carle ; while in scarlet and gold , like knight of the 
 Westland ; while in wolf - skin ; while in white glitter gear , like 
 the Wights of the Waste : and in all guise these felon , for all 
 their fierce heart , fear we , and flee from we , and we follow and 
 slay they , and so minish their number somewhat against the great day 
 of battle . ' 

 ' tell I , ' say Gold - mane ; ' when we fall upon silver - dale shall their 
 thrall , the old Dale - dweller , fight for they or for we ? ' 

 say folk - might : ' the Dusky Men will not dare to put weapon into 
 the hand of their thrall . Nay , the thrall shall help we ; for 
 though they have but small stomach for the fight , yet joyfully when 
 the fight be over shall they cut their master ' throat . ' 

 ' how be it with these thrall ? ' say Gold - mane . ' I have never see a 
 thrall . ' 

 ' but I , ' say folk - might , ' have see a many down in the Cities . and 
 there be thrall who be the tyrant of thrall , and hold the whip 
 over they ; and of the other there be some who be not very hardly 
 entreat . but with these it be otherwise , and they all bear 
 grievous pain daily ; for the Dusky Men be as hog in a garden of 
 lily . Whatsoever be fair there have they defile and deflower , 
 and they wallow in our fair hall as swine stray from the dunghill . 
 no delight in life , no sweet day do they have for themselves , and 
 they begrudge the delight of other therein . therefore their thrall 
 know no rest or solace ; their reward of toil be many stripe , and the 
 healing of their stripe grievous toil . to many have they appoint 
 to dig and mine in the silver - yield cliff , and of all the task 
 be that the sore , and there do stripe abound the most . such 
 thrall art thou happy not to behold till thou hast set they free ; as 
 we shall do . ' 

 ' tell I again , ' say face - of - god ; ' be there no mixed folk between 
 these Dusky Men and the Dalesmen , since they have no woman of their 
 own , but lie with the woman of the Dale ? moreover , do not the poor 
 folk of the Dale beget and bear child , so that there be thrall 
 bear of thrall ? ' 

 ' wisely thou ask this , ' say folk - might , ' but thereof shall I tell 
 thee , that when a Dusky Carle mingle with a woman of the Dale , the 
 child which she beareth shall oftenest favour his race and not her ; 
 or else shall it be witless , a fool natural . but as for the child 
 of these poor thrall ; yea , the master cause they to breed if so 
 their mastership will , and when the child be bear , they keep 
 they or slay they as they will , as they would with whelp or calf . 
 to be short , year by year these vile wretch grow fiercer and more 
 beastly , and their thrall more hapless and down - trodden ; and now at 
 last be come the time either to do or to die , as ye man of Burgdale 
 shall speedily find out . but now must I go sleep if I be to be where 
 I look to be at sunrise to - morrow . ' 

 Therewith he call for the sleeping - cup , and it be drunk , and all 
 man fare to bed . but the Sun - beam take Gold - mane 's hand ere they 
 part , and say : 

 ' I shall arise betime on the morrow ; so I say not farewell to - night ; 
 yea , and after to - morrow it shall not be long ere we meet again . ' 

 so gold - mane lie down in that ancient hall , and it seem to he ere 
 he sleep as if his own kindre be slip away from he and he 
 be become a child of the Wolf . ' and yet , ' say he to himself , ' I 
 be become a man ; for my friend , now she no long telleth I to do or 
 forbear , and I tremble . Nay , rather she be fain to take the word 
 from I ; and this great warrior and ripe man , he talketh with I as 
 if I be a chieftain meet for converse with chieftain . even so it 
 be and shall be . ' 

 and soon thereafter he fall asleep in the Hall in Shadowy Vale . 



 CHAPTER XXI . FACE - of - GOD LOOKETH on the dusky MEN 



 when he awake again he see a man stand over he , and know he for 
 wood - wise : he be clothe in his war - gear , and have his quiver at his 
 back and his bow in his hand , for Wood - father 's child be all 
 good bowman , though not so sure as Bow - may . he spake to face - of - god : 

 ' Dawn be in the sky , Dalesman ; there be yet time for thee to wash the 
 night off of thee in our bath of the Shivering Flood and to put thy 
 mouth to the milk - bowl ; but time for nought else : for I and Bow - may 
 be appoint thy fellow for the road , and it be well that we be 
 back home speedily . ' 

 so face - of - god leapt up and go forth from the Hall , and Wood - wise 
 lead to where be a pool in the river with step cut down to it in the 
 rocky bank . 

 ' this , ' say Wood - wise , ' be the Carle 's bath ; but the Queen 's be 
 low down , where the water be wide and shallow below the little 
 mid - dale force . ' 

 so gold - mane strip off his raiment and leapt into the ice - cold 
 pool ; and they have bring his weapon and war - gear with they ; so 
 when he come out he clothe and arm himself for the road , and then 
 turn with wood - wise toward the outgate of the Dale ; and soon they 
 see two man come from low down the water in such wise that they 
 would presently cross their path , and as yet it be little more than 
 twilight , so that they see not at first who they be , but as they 
 draw near they know they for the Sun - beam and Bow - may . the Sun- 
 beam be clothe but in her white linen smock and blue gown as he have 
 first see she , her hair be wet and dripping with the river , she 
 face fresh and rosy : she carry in her two hand a great bowl of 
 milk , and step delicately , lest she should spill it . but Bow - may 
 be clothe in her war - gear with helm and byrny , and a quiver at she 
 back , and a bended bow in her hand . so they greet each other 
 kindly , and the Sun - beam give the bowl to face - of - god and say : 

 ' drink , guest , for thou hast a long and thirsty road before thee . ' 

 so face - of - god drank , and give she the bowl back again , and she 
 smile on he and drank , and the other after she till the bowl be 
 empty : then Bow - may put her hand on Wood - wise 's shoulder , and they 
 lead on toward the outgate , while those twain follow they hand in 
 hand . but the Sun - beam say : 

 ' this then be the new day I speak of , and lo ! it bringeth our 
 sunder with it ; yet shall it be no long than a day when all be 
 say , and new day shall follow after . and now , my friend , I shall 
 see thee no later than the April market ; for doubt not that I shall 
 go thither with folk - might , whether he will or not . also as I lead 
 thee out of the house when we last meet , so shall I lead thee out of 
 the Dale to - day , and I will go with thee a little way on the waste ; 
 and therefore be I shod this morning , as thou seest , for the way on 
 the waste be rough . and now I bid thee have courage while my hand 
 holdeth thine . for afterwards I need not bid thee anything ; for thou 
 wilt have enough to do when thou com to thy Folk , and must need 
 think more of warrior then than of maidens . ' 

 he look at she and long for she , but say soberly : ' thou art 
 kind , o friend , and thinkest kindly of I ever . but methink it be 
 not well do for thee to wend with I over a deal of the waste , and 
 come back by thyself alone , when ye have so many foeman nearby . ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , ' they be nought so near as that yet , and I wot that 
 folk - might hath go forth toward the north - west , where he looketh to 
 fall in with a company of the foeman . his battle shall be a guard 
 unto we . ' 

 ' I pray thee turn back at the top of the outgate , ' say he , ' and be 
 not venturesome . Thou wottest that the pitcher be not break the 
 first time it goeth to the well , nor maybe the twentieth , but at last 
 it cometh not back . ' 

 she say : ' nevertheless I shall have my will herein . and it be but 
 a little way I will wend with thee . ' 

 Therewith be they come to the scree , and talk fall down between 
 they as they clomb it ; but when they be in the darksome passage of 
 the rock , and could scarce see one another , face - of - god say : 

 ' where then be another outgate from the Dale ? be it not up the 
 water ? ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' and there be none other : at the low end the 
 rock rise sheer from out the water , and a little far down be a 
 great force thunder betwixt they ; so that by no boat or raft may 
 ye come out of the Dale . but the outgate up the water be call the 
 Road of War , as this be name the Path of Peace . but now be all 
 way way of war . ' 

 ' there be peace in my heart , ' say Gold - mane . 

 she answer not for a while , but press his hand , and he feel she 
 breath on his cheek ; and even therewithal they come out of the dark , 
 and gold - mane see that her cheek be flush ; and now she spake : 

 ' one thing would I say to thee , my friend . Thou hast see I amongst 
 man of war , amongst outlaw who seek violence ; thou hast hear I bid 
 my brother to count the slain , and I shrink not ; thou know   for 
 I have tell thee   how I have scheme and scheme for victorious 
 battle . yet I would not have thee think of I as a Chooser of the 
 Slain , a warrior maiden , or as of one who hath no joy save in the 
 battle whereto she biddeth other . o friend , the many peaceful hour 
 that I have have on the grass down yonder , sit with my rock and 
 spindle in hand , the child round about my knee hearken to some 
 old story so well remember by I ! or the milking of the kine in the 
 dewy summer even , when all be still but for the voice of the water 
 and the cry of the happy child , and there round about I be 
 the dear and beauteous maiden with whom I have grow up , happy amidst 
 all our trouble , since their life be free and they know no guile . 
 in such time my heart be at peace indeed , and it seem to I as if 
 we have win all we need ; as if war and turmoil be over , after they 
 have bring about peace and good day for our little folk . 

 ' and as for the day that be , be they not as that rugge pass , full 
 of bitter wind and the voice of hurrying water , that leadeth yonder 
 to silver - dale , as thou hast divine ? and there be nought good in it 
 save that the breath of life be therein , and that it leadeth to 
 pleasant place and the peace and plenty of the fair dale . ' 

 ' sweet friend , ' he say , ' what thou sayest be well than well : for 
 time shall be , if we come alive out of this pass of battle and bitter 
 strife , when I shall lead thee into Burgdale to dwell there . and 
 thou wottest of our people that there be little strife and grudge 
 amongst they , and that they be merry , and fair to look on , both man 
 and woman ; and no man there lacketh what the earth may give we , and 
 it be a saying amongst we that there may a man have that which he 
 desireth save the sun and moon in his hand to play with : and of 
 this gladness , which be make up of many little matter , what story 
 may be tell ? yet amongst it shall I live and thou with I ; and ill 
 indeed it be if it weary thee and thou wert ever long for some 
 day of victorious strife , and to behold I come back from battle 
 high - raise on the shield of man and crown with bay ; if thine ear 
 must ever be tickle with the talk of man and their song concern 
 my warrior deed . for thus it shall not be . when I drive the herd 
 it shall be at the neighbour ' bidding whereso they will ; not neck 
 of man shall I smite , but the stalk of the tall wheat , and the bole 
 of the timber - tree which the woodreeve hath mark for felling ; the 
 stilt of the plough rather than the hilt of the sword shall harden 
 my hand ; my shaft shall be for the deer , and my spear for the 
 wood - boar , till war and sorrow fall upon we , and I fight for the 
 ceasing of war and trouble . and though I be call a chief and of 
 the blood of chief , yet shall I not be masterful to the goodman of 
 the Dale , but rather to my hound ; for my chieftainship shall be that 
 I shall be well beloved and trust , and that no man shall grudge 
 against I . Canst thou learn to love such a life , which to I 
 seemeth lovely ? and thou ? of whom I say that thou art as if thou 
 wert come down from the golden chair of the Burg of the Gods . ' 

 they be well - nigh out of the steep path by now , and the daylight 
 be bright about they ; there she stay her foot a moment and turn 
 to he and say : 

 ' all this should I love even now , if the grief of our Folk be but 
 heal , and hereafter shall I learn yet more of thy well - beloved 
 face . ' 

 Therewith she lay her face to his and kiss he fondly , and put his 
 hand to her side and hold it there , say : ' soon shall we be one in 
 body and in soul . ' 

 and he laugh with joy and pride of life , and take her hand and lead 
 she on again , and say : 

 ' yet feel the cold ring of my hauberk , my friend ; look at the spear 
 that cumber my hand , and at Dale - warden hanging by my side . thou 
 shalt yet see I as the Slain 's Chooser would see her speech - friend ; 
 for there be much to do ere we win wheat - harvest in Burgdale . ' 

 Therewith they step together on to the level ground of the waste , 
 and see Bow - may sit on a stone hard by , and wood - wise standing 
 beside she bend his bow . Bow - may smile on gold - mane and rise up , 
 and they all go on together , turn so that they go nearly 
 alongside the wall of the Vale , but westere a little ; then the Sun- 
 beam say : 

 ' many a time have I trodden this heath alongside our rock - wall ; for 
 if ye wend a little far as our face be turn , ye come to the 
 crag over the place where the Shivering Flood goeth out of Shadowy 
 Vale . there when ye have clomb a little may'st thou stand on the 
 edge of the rock - wall , and look down and behold the Flood swirling 
 and eddying in the black gorge of the rock , and see presently the 
 reek of the force go up , and hear the thunder of the water as they 
 pour over it : and all this about we now be as the garden of our 
 house - be it not so , Bow - may ? ' 

 ' yea , ' say she , ' and there be goodly cluster - berry to be get 
 hereabout in the autumn ; many a time have the Sun - beam and I redden 
 our lip with they . yet be it good to be wary when war be abroad and 
 hot withal . ' 

 ' yea , ' say the Sun - beam , ' and all this place come into the story of 
 our House : lo ! gold - mane , two score pace before we a little on our 
 right hand those five grey stone . they be call the Rocks of the 
 Elders : for there in the first day of our abiding in Shadowy Vale 
 the Elders be will not to come together to talk privily upon our 
 matter . ' 

 face - of - god look thither as she speak , but therewith see Bow - may , 
 who go on the left hand of the Sun - beam , as face - of - god on her 
 right hand , notch a shaft on her bent bow , and wood - wise , who be on 
 his right hand , see it also and do the like , and therewithal face- 
 of - god get his target on to his arm , and even as he do so Bow - may 
 cry out suddenly : 

 ' yea , yea ! cast thyself on to the ground , Sun - beam ! gold - mane , 
 targe and spear , targe and spear ! for I see steel gleam yonder 
 out from behind the Elders ' Rocks . ' 

 Scarce be the word out of her mouth ere three shaft come fly , 
 and the bow - string twang . gold - mane feel that one smite his helm 
 and glance from it . therewithal he see the Sun - beam fall to earth , 
 though he know not if she have but cast herself down as Bow - may bade . 
 Bow - may 's string twang at once , and a yell come from the foeman : 
 but wood - wise loose not , but set his hand to his mouth and give a 
 loud wild cry - ha ! ha ! ha ! ha ! how - ow - ow ! - end in a long and 
 exceed great whoop like nought but the wolf 's howl . now gold - mane 
 thinking swiftly , in a moment of time , as war - meet man do , judge 
 that if the Sun - beam be hurt   and she have make no cry , it be yet 
 wise to fall on the foe before turn to tend she , or else all 
 might be lose ; so he rush forward spear in hand and target on arm , 
 and see , as he open up the flank of the Elders ' Rocks , six man , 
 whereof one lean aback on the rock with Bow - may 's shaft in his 
 shoulder , and two other be just in act of loosing at he . in a 
 moment , as he rush at they , one shaft go whistle by he , and 
 the other glance from off his target ; he cast a spear as he bound 
 on , and see it smite one of the shooter full in the naked face , and 
 see the blood spout out and change his face and the man roll over , 
 and then in another moment four man be hew at he with their 
 short steel axis . he thrust out his target against they , and then 
 let the weight of his body come on his other spear , and drave it 
 through the second shooter 's throat , and even therewith be smite 
 on the helm so hard that , though the Alderman 's work hold out , he 
 fall to his knee , hold his target over his head and strive to 
 draw forth Dale - warden ; in that nick of time a shaft whistle close 
 by his ear , and as he rise to his foot again he see his foeman 
 roll over and over , clutch at the ling with both hand . then 
 ring out again the terrible wolf - whoop from Wood - wise 's mouth , and 
 both he and Bow - may loose a shaft , for the two other foe have turn 
 their back and be flee fast . again Bow - may hit the clout , and 
 the Dusky Man fall dead at once , but wood - wise 's arrow fly over the 
 felon 's shoulder as he run . then in a trice be gold - mane bounding 
 after he like the hare just rouse from her form ; for it come into 
 his head that these felon have behold they come up out of the Vale , 
 and that if even this one man escape , he would bring his company 
 down upon the Vale - dweller . 

 strong and light - foot as any be face - of - god , and though he be 
 cumber with his hauberk , yet be iron - face 's handiwork far lighter 
 than the war - coat of the Dusky Man , and the race be soon over . the 
 felon turn breathless to meet gold - mane , who drave his target 
 against he and cast he to earth , and as he strove to rise smote off 
 his head at one stroke ; for Dale - warden be a good sword and the 
 Dalesman as fierce of mood as might be . there he let the felon lie , 
 and , turning , walk back swiftly toward the Elders ' Rocks , and find 
 there wood - wise and the dead foeman , for the carle have slay the 
 wound , and he be now draw the silver arm - ring off the slain 
 man ; for all these Dusky Felons bear silver arm - ring . but Bow - may 
 be walk towards the Sun - beam , and thitherward follow Gold - mane 
 speedily . 

 he find she sit on a tussock of grass close by where she have 
 fall , her face pale , her eye eager and gleaming ; she look up at 
 he as he draw nigh and say : 

 ' friend , art thou hurt ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' he say , ' and thou ? thou art pale . ' 

 ' I be not hurt , ' she say . then she smile and say again : 

 ' do I not tell thee that I be no warrior like Bow - may here ? such 
 deed make maiden pale . ' 

 say Bow - may : ' if ye will have the truth , gold - mane , she be not will not 
 to grow pale when battle be nigh she . look you , she hath have the 
 gift of a new delight , and findeth it sweet and soft than she have 
 any thought of ; and now hath she fear lest it should be take from 
 she . ' 

 ' bow - may saith but the sooth , ' say the Sun - beam simply , ' and kind it 
 be of she to say it . I see thee , Bow - may , and good be thy shooting , 
 and I love thee for it . ' 

 say Bow - may : ' I never shoot otherwise than well . but those idle 
 shooter of the Dusky Ones , whereabout nigh to thee go their 
 shaft ? ' 

 say the Sun - beam : ' one just lift the hair by my left ear , and 
 that be not so ill - aim ; as for the other , it pierce my raiment by 
 my right knee , and pin I to the earth , so that I totter and 
 fall , and my gown and smock be grievously wound , both of they . ' 

 and she take the fold of the garment in her hand to show the rent 
 therein ; and her colour be come again , and she be glad . 

 ' what be good to do now ? ' she say . 

 say face - of - god : ' let we tarry a little ; for some of thy carle 
 shall surely come up from the Vale : because they will have hear 
 Wood - wise 's whoop , since the wind set that way . ' 

 ' yea , they will come , ' say the Sun - beam . 

 ' Good be that , ' say face - of - god ; ' for they shall take the dead 
 felon and cast they where they be not see if perchance any more 
 stray hereby . for if they wind they , they may well happen on the 
 path down to the Vale . also , my friend , it be well if thou wert to 
 bid a good few of the carle that be in the Vale to keep watch and 
 ward about here , lest there be more foeman wander about the 
 waste . ' 

 she say : ' thou art wise in war , gold - mane ; I will do as thou 
 bidd I . but soothly this be a perilous thing that the Dusky Men 
 be get so close to the Vale . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' this will folk - might look to when he cometh home ; 
 and it be most like that he will deem it good to fall on they 
 somewhere a good way aloof , so as to draw they off from wander 
 over the waste . also I will do my good to busy they when I be home 
 in Burgdale . ' 

 Therewith come up wood - wise , and fall to talk with they ; and his mind 
 it be that these foeman be but a band of strayer , and have have no 
 inkling of Shadowy Vale till they have hear they talk together as 
 they come up the path from the Vale , and that then they have make that 
 ambush behind the Elders ' Rocks , so that they might slay the man , and 
 then bear off the woman . he say withal that it would be good to 
 carry their corps far on , so that they might be cast over the 
 cliff into the fierce stream of the Shivering Flood . 

 amidst this talk come up man from the Vale , a score of they , well 
 arm ; and they run to meet the wayfarer ; and when they hear what 
 have befall , they rejoice exceedingly , and be above all glad that 
 face - of - god have show himself doughty and deft ; and they deem his 
 rede wise , to set a watch thereabout till Folk - might come home , and 
 say that they would do even so . 

 then spake the Sun - beam and say : 

 ' now must ye wayfarer depart ; for the road be but rough , and the day 
 not over - long . ' 

 then she turn to face - of - god and put her hand on his shoulder , and 
 bring her face close to his and spake to he softly : 

 ' doth this second parting seem at all strange to thee , and that I be 
 now so familiar to thee , I whom thou didst once deem to be a very 
 goddess ? and now thou hast see I redden before thine eye because 
 of thee ; and thou hast see I grow pale with fear because of thee ;

 day ' wonder , and then all be over save an ache heart - wilt thou do

 ask thee to scorn I ? for thus desire dealeth both with maid and 
 man . ' 

 he say : ' in all this there be but one thing for I to say , and that 
 be that I love thee ; and surely none the less , but rather the more , 
 because thou lovest I , and art of my kind , and mayest share in my 
 deed and think well of they . now be my heart full of joy , and one 
 thing only weigheth on it ; and that be that my kinswoman the Bride 
 begrudgeth our love together . for this be the thing that of all 
 thing most misliketh I , that any should bear a grudge against I . ' 

 she say : ' forget not the token , and my message to she . ' 

 ' I will not forget it , ' say he . ' and now I bid thee to kiss I even 
 before all these that be look on ; for there be nought to belittle 
 we therein , since we be troth - plight . ' 

 and indeed those folk stand all round about they gaze on they , but 
 a little aloof , that they might not hear their word if they be 
 minded to talk privily . for they have long love the Sun - beam , and 
 now the love of face - of - god have begin to spring up in their heart . 

 so the twain embrace and kiss one another , and make no haste 
 thereover ; and those man deem that but meet and right , and clash 
 their weapon on their shield in token of their joy . 

 then face - of - god turn about and stride out of the ring of man , with 
 Bow - may and wood - wise beside he , and they go on their journey over 
 the neck towards Burgstead . but the Sun - beam turn slowly from 
 that place toward the Vale , and two of the stout carle go along 
 with she to guard she from harm , and she go down into the Vale 
 ponder all these thing in her heart . 

 then the other carle drag off the corps of the Dusky Men till 
 they have bring they to the sheer rock above the Shivering Flood , 
 and there they toss they over into the boil caldron of the 
 force , and so depart take with they the silver arm - ring of the 
 slain to add to the tale . 

 but when they come back into the Vale the Sun - beam duly order that 
 watch and ward to keep the ingate thereto , and note all that should 
 befall till Folk - might come home . 



 CHAPTER XXII . FACE - of - GOD COMETH home to BURGSTEAD 



 but face - of - god with Bow - may and Wood - wise fare over the waste , 
 go at first alongside the cliff of the Shivering Flood , and then 
 afterwards turn somewhat to the west . they soon have to climb a 
 very high and steep bent go up to a mountain - neck ; and the way 
 over the neck be rough indeed when they be on it , and they toil 
 out of it into a barren valley , and out of the valley again on to a 
 rough neck ; and such - like their journey the day long , for they be 
 go athwart all those great dyke that go from the ice - mountain 
 toward the low dale like the outspread finger of a hand or the 
 root of a great tree . and the ice - mountain they have on their left 
 hand and while at their back . 

 they go very warily , with their bow bend and spear in hand , but 
 see no man , good or bad , and but few live thing . at noon they 
 rest in a valley where be a stream , but no grass , nought but 
 stone and sand ; but where they be at least sheltered from the 
 wind , which be mostly very great in these high waste ; and there 
 Bow - may drew meat and wine from a wallet she bear , and they eat and 
 drank , and be merry enough ; and Bow - may say : 

 ' I would I be go all the way with thee , Gold - mane ; for I long 
 sore to let my eye rest a while on the land where I shall one day 
 live . ' 

 ' yea , ' say face - of - god , ' art thou minded to dwell there ? we shall 
 be glad of that . ' 

 ' Whither be thy wit stray ? ' say she ; ' whether I be minded to it 
 or not , I shall dwell there . ' 

 and wood - wise nod a yea to she . but face - of - god say : 

 ' good will be thy dwelling ; but wherefore must it be so ? ' 

 then wood - wise laugh and say : ' I shall tell thee in few word 
 than she will , and time press now : wood - father and Wood - mother ,

 brotherhood or what not , and never shall we sunder from she in life 
 or in death . so when thou goest to Burgdale with she , there shall we 
 be . ' 

 then be face - of - god glad when he find that they deem his wedding 
 so settled and sure ; but wood - wise fall to make ready for the road . 
 and face - of - god say to he :

 we be at handy - stroke e'en now - be it but a cry of thine own or be 
 it of thy Folk , and shall I hear it again ? ' 

 ' thou may'st look to hear it many a time , ' say Wood - wise , ' for it be 
 the cry of the Wolf . seldom indeed hath battle be join where man 
 of our blood be , but that cry be give forth . come now , to the 
 road ! ' 

 so they go their way and the road worsen upon they , and toilsome 
 be the climbing up steep bent and the scaling of doubtful path in 
 the cliff - side , so that the journey , though the distance of it be 
 not so long to the fowl fly , be much eke out for they , and it 
 be not till near nightfall that they come on the ghyll of the 
 weltering Water some six mile above Burgstead . Forsooth Wood - wise 
 say that the way might be make less toilsome though far long by 
 turn back eastward a little past the vale where they have rest at 
 midday ; and that seem good to gold - mane , in case they should be 
 wend hereafter in a great company between Burgdale and Shadowy 
 Vale . 

 but now those two go with face - of - god down a path in the side of 
 the cliff whereby he - seem he have go before ; and they come down

 and face - of - god spake to they kindly , for he deem they good and 
 trusty faring - fellow . 

 ' Bow - may , ' say he , ' thou saidst a while ago that thou wouldst be 
 fain to look on Burgdale ; and indeed it be fair and lovely , and ye 
 may soon be in it if ye will . ye shall both be more than welcome to 
 the house of my father , and heartily I bid you thither . for night be 
 on we , and the way back be long and toilsome and beset with peril . 
 Sister Bow - may , thou wottest that it would be a sore grief to I if 
 thou camest to any harm , and thou also , fellow wood - wise . daylight 
 be a good faring - fellow over the waste . ' 

 say Bow - may : ' thou art kind , gold - mane , and that be thy will not , I 
 know ; and fain be I to - night of the candle in thine hall . but we 
 may not tarry ; for thou wottest how busy we be at home ; and Sun - beam 
 needeth I , if it be only to make she sure that no Dusky Man be 
 bear off thine head by its lovely lock . neither shall we journey 
 in the mirk night ; for look you , the moon yonder . ' 

 ' well , ' say face - of - god , ' parting be ill at the good , and I would I 
 could give you twain a gift , and especially to thee , my sister Bow- 
 may . ' 

 say wood - wise : ' thou may'st well do that ; or at least promise the 
 gift ; and that be all one as if we hold it in our hand . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Bow - may , ' wood - wise and I have be think in one way 
 belike ; and I be at point to ask a gift of thee . ' 

 ' what be it ? ' say Gold - mane . ' surely it be thine , if it be but a 
 guerdon for thy good shooting . ' 

 she laugh and handle the skirt of his hauberk as she say :

 morning . ' 

 ' there be no such great dint , ' say he ; ' my father forge that helm , 
 and his work be well than good . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Bow - may , ' and might I have hauberk and helm of his 
 handiwork , and wood - wise a good sword of the same , then be I a glad 
 woman , and this man a happy carle . ' 

 say Gold - mane : ' I be well pleased at thine asking , and so shall

 be now his only son but for thy close shooting . but now must I to

 Burgstead this while I have be aloof . ' 

 so they rise all three , and Bow - may say : 

 ' thou art a kind brother , and soon shall we meet again ; and that will 
 be well . ' 

 then he put his hand on her shoulder and kiss both her cheek ;

 stony tangle about the Weltering Water , which be now at middle

 torch move swiftly hither and thither ; so that he look to hear 
 of new tiding , and stay his foot and look keenly about he on

 Water . then he spin round on his heel , heed not that another 
 stroke have fall on his right shoulder , yet ill - aim , and not with 
 the full edge , so that it run down his byrny and rent it not . so he 
 send the thrust of his spear crash through the face and skull of 
 the smiter , and look not to he as he fall , but stand still ,

 for the Burg and the face ! ' 

 no other foe come against he , but like to the echo of his cry rise a

 and the face ! ' he mutter , ' so end the day as it begin , ' and 
 shout loud again , ' for the Burg and the face ! ' and in a minute

 we like stone - fist of the Mountain . art thou hurt ? with whom hast 
 thou deal ? where be they ? whence com thou ? ' 

 ' Nay , I be not hurt , ' say face - of - god . ' stint thy question then , 
 till thou hast tell I whom thou seekest with spear and sword and

 ' two felon be they , ' say Hall - face , ' even such as ye see lie 
 dead at Wood - grey 's the other day . ' 

 ' then may ye sheathe your sword and go home , ' say Gold - mane , ' for

 then arise a rumour of praise and victory , and they bring the

 turn about toward the Thorp ; and many look on face - of - god and

 indeed , they would have ask he thereof , but could not get at he

 so as they go along Hall - face do verily ask he whence he come :

 seek some adventure early in the morning the day before 
 yesterday ? ' 

 ' Sooth be that , ' say face - of - god , ' and I come to Shadowy Vale , and 
 thence be I come this morning . ' 

 say Hall - face : ' I know not Shadowy Vale , nor doth any of we . this 
 be a new word . how say ye , friend , doth any man here know of 
 Shadowy Vale ? ' 

 they all say , ' Nay . ' 

 then say Hall - face : ' hast thou be amongst mere ghost and 
 marvel , brother , or cometh this tale of thy minstrelsy ? ' 

 ' for all your word , ' say Gold - mane , ' to that Vale have I be ; and , 
 to speak shortly   for I desire to have your tale , and be wait for 
 it , I will tell thee that I find there no marvel or strange 
 wight , but a folk of valiant man ; a folk small in number , but great

 Dusky Men of Silver - dale would slay they every man if they might ; and 
 if we look not to it they will soon be do the same by we ; for they 
 be many , and as venomous as adder , as fierce as bear , and as foul

 thereto ; and they shall show we the way to silver - dale by blind 
 path , so that we may fall upon these felon while they dwell there 
 torment the poor people of the land , and thus may we destroy they 
 as lad a hornet 's nest . or else the day shall be hard for we . ' 

 the man who hang about they drink in his word greedily . but hall-

 ' yea , in good time , ' say face - of - god . 

 ' well , ' quoth Hall - face , ' we go into the wood that day and in the

 ' thus armed we go up Wildlake 's way and come to Carlstead , where

 ' I look from behind my tree - bole , a great one ; and far off down the

 hundred yard from my cover , I see an arm clothe in scarlet come out

 ' Straightway be a yelling and howl about we like the cry of 
 scald cur , and the oak - wood swarm thick with these felon 
 rush on we ; for it seem that the man whom I have slay be a chief

 ' methought then our last day be come . what could we do but run 
 together again after we have loose at a venture , and so withstand

 many , for they come on very swiftly . 

 ' so they fall on we ; but for all their fierceness and their number

 be hard . 

 ' yet hearken ! in that nick of time rise up a strange cry not far

 and then another and another and another , till the whole wood ring

 ' at first we deem that here be come fresh foeman , and that we 
 be undo indeed ; but when they hear it , the foe - man before we 
 falter and give way , and at last turn their back and flee , and

 down , till both fleer and pursuer pass out of our sight like man 
 in a dream , or as when ye roll up a picture cloth to lay it in the 
 coffer . 

 ' but to stone - face 's mind those brown - clothe man be the Wights of the

 diver hurt man that need look to . 

 ' so what with one thing , what with another , we turn back : but War-

 Hewer , a woodlander . 

 ' so come we sadly at eventide to Burgstead with the two dead

 go afoot ; and ye may judge that they of Burgdale and our father

 for one and all we deem , and still deem , that war be on we and that 
 new folk have gather on our skirt . 

 ' so the captive be shut up in the red out - bower of our house ; and 
 our father be minded that thou mightest tell we somewhat of they 
 when thou wert come home . but about dusk to - day the word go that

 so it be . 

 ' but to - morrow morning will a Gate - thing be holden , and there it will

 ' and that be well , ' say Hall - face , ' and no less do I look for of 
 thee . but lo ! here be we come to the Gate of the Burg that abideth

 CHAPTER XXIII . talk in the hall of the HOUSE of the face 



 in sooth they be come to the very Gate of Burgstead , and the great

 ' good people , all be well ! the runaway be slain , and face - of - god be 
 come back with we ; give place a little , that we may come into our

 then the throng set up a shout , and make way a little , so that hall-

  for he have enter with they from the street   go up on to the

 but when face - of - god come up on to the dai , there sit iron - face

 ' Kinswoman , thou art pale ; thou hast fear for thy mate amidst all 
 these tiding of war , and still fear for he . but pluck up a 
 heart ; for the man be a deft warrior for all his fair face , which 
 thou lovest as a woman should , and his hand may yet save his head . 
 and if he be slain , yet be there other man of the kindred , and the 
 earth will not be a desert to thee even then . ' 

 she look at Iron - face , and the colour be come back to her face 
 somewhat , and she say : 

 ' it be true ; I have fear for he ; for he goeth into perilous 
 place . but for thee , thou art kind , and I thank thee for it . ' 

 and therewith she kiss Iron - face and sit down in her place , and 
 strove to overmaster her grief , that her face might not be change by 
 it ; for now be thought of battle , and valiant hope arise in 
 man 's heart ; and it seem to she too grievous if she should mar 
 that feast on the eve of battle . 

 but iron - face kiss and embrace his son and say : ' art thou late 
 come from the waste ? Hast thou see new thing ? we look to have a 
 notable tale from thee ; though here also have be tiding , and it be 
 not unlike that we shall presently have new work on our hand . ' 

 ' Father , ' quoth face - of - god , ' I deem that when thou hast hear my 
 tale thou wilt think no less of it than that there be valiant folk 
 to be holpen , poor folk to be deliver , and evil folk to be sweep 
 from off the face of the earth . ' 

 ' it be well , son , ' say Iron - face . ' I see that thy tale be long ; let 
 it alone for to - night . to - morrow shall we hold a gate - thing , and 
 then shall we hear all that thou hast to tell . now eat thy meat and 
 drink a bowl of wine , and comfort thy troth - plight maiden . ' 

 so gold - mane sit down by the Bride , and eat and drink as he need 
 must ; but he be ill at ease and he durst not speak to she . for , on 
 the one hand , he think concern his love for the Sun - beam , and 
 how sweet and good a thing it be that she should take he by the

 softly and sweetly the while ; and , on the other hand , there sit the 
 Bride beside he , sorrowful and angry , begrudge all that sweetness 
 of love , as though it be something foul and unseemly ; and heavy on 
 he lie the weight of that grudge , for he be a man of a friendly 
 heart . 

 stone - face sit outward from he on the other side of the Bride ; and

 ' fair shall be thy tale to - morrow , if thou tell we all thine 
 adventure . or wilt thou tell we less than all ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' in good time shalt thou know it all , foster- 
 father ; but it be not unlike that by the time that thou hast hear

 shall seem of little account to thee - even as the saw saith that one 
 nail driveth out the other . ' 

 ' yea , ' say stone - face , ' but one tale belike shall be knit up with 
 the other , as it fareth with the figure that come one after other 
 on the weaver 's cloth ; though one maketh not the other , yet one 
 cometh of the other . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' wise art thou now , foster - father , but thou shalt 
 be wise yet in this matter by then a month hath wear : and to - morrow

 so the talk fall between they ; and the night wear , and the man of 
 Burgdale feast in their ancient hall with merry heart , little 
 weigh down by thought of the battle that might be and the trouble 
 to come ; for they be valorous and kindly folk . 



 CHAPTER XXIV . FACE - of - GOD giveth that TOKEN to the BRIDE 



 now on the morrow , when face - of - god arise and other man with he , and 
 the Hall be astir and there be no little throng therein , the Bride 
 come up to he ; for she have sleep in the House of the Face by the 
 bidding of the Alderman ; and she spake to he before all man , and 
 bid he come forth with she into the garden , because she would speak 
 to he apart . he yeasaid she , though with a heavy heart ; and to the 
 folk about that seem meet and due , since those twain be deem to 
 be troth - plight , and they smile kindly on they as they go out of 
 the Hall together . 

 so they come into the garden , where the pear - tree be blossom 
 over the spring lily , and the cherry be shower their flower 
 on the deep green grass , and everything smell sweetly on the warm 
 windless spring morning . 

 she lead the way , go before he till they come by a smooth grass 
 path between the berry bush , to a square space of grass about which 
 be barberry tree , their first tender leaf bright green in the 
 sun against the dry yellowish twig . there be a sundial amidmost of 
 the grass , and betwixt the garden - bough one could see the long grey 
 roof of the ancient hall ; and sweet familiar sound of the nesting 
 bird and man and woman go on their errand be all about in the 
 scented air . she turn about at the sundial and face face - of - god , 
 her hand lightly lay on the score brass , and spake with no anger in 
 her voice : 

 ' I ask thee if thou hast bring I the token whereon thou shalt 
 swear to give I that gift . ' 

 ' yea , ' say he ; and therewith draw the ring from his bosom , and hold 
 it out to she . she reach out her hand to he slowly and take it , 
 and their finger meet as she do so , and he note that her hand be 
 warm and firm and wholesome as he well remember it . 

 she say : ' whence hadst thou this fair finger - ring ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' my friend there in the mountain - valley draw it 
 from off her finger for thee , and bid I bear thee a message . ' 

 her face flush red : ' yea , ' she say , ' and doth she send I a 
 message ? then doth she know of I , and ye have talk of I 
 together . well , give the message ! ' 

 say face - of - god : ' she saith , that thou shalt bear in mind , that to- 
 morrow be a new day . ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' for she it be so , and for thee ; but not for I . 
 but now I have bring thee here that thou mightest swear thine oath 
 to I ; lie thine hand on this ring and on this brazen plate whereby 
 the sun measure the hour of the day for happy folk , and swear by 
 the spring - tide of the year and all glad thing that find a mate , and 
 by the God of the Earth that rejoiceth in the life of man . ' 

 then he lay his hand on the finger - ring as it lie on the dial - plate 
 and say : 

 ' by the spring - tide and the live thing that long to multiply their 
 kind ; by the God of the Earth that rejoiceth in the life of man , I 
 swear to give to my kinswoman the Bride the second man - child that I 
 beget ; to be her , to leave or cherish , to love or hate , as she will 
 may bid she . ' then he look on her soberly and say : ' it be duly 
 swear ; be it enough ? ' 

 ' yea , ' she say ; but he see how the tear run out of her eye and 
 wet the bosom of her kirtle , and she hang her head for shame of 
 her grief . and Gold - mane be all abash , and have no word to say ; 
 for he know that no word of his might comfort she ; and he deem it 
 ill do to stay there and behold her sorrow ; and he know not how to 
 get he go , and be glad elsewhere , and leave she alone . 

 then , as if she have read his thought , she look up at he and say 
 smile a little amidst of her tear : 

 ' I bid thee stay by I till the flood be over ; for I have yet a word 
 to say to thee . ' 

 so he stand there gaze down on the grass in his turn , and not 
 dare to raise his eye to her face , and the minute seem long to 
 he : till at last she say in a voice scarcely yet clear of weeping : 

 ' wilt thou say anything to I , and tell I what thou hast do , and 
 why , and what thou deemest will come of it ? ' 

 he say : ' I will tell the truth as I know it , because thou askest it 
 of I , and not because I would excuse myself before thee . what have 
 I do ? yesterday I plight my troth to we d the woman that I meet 
 last autumn in the wood . and why ? I wot not why , but that I long 
 for she . yet I must tell thee that it seem to I , and yet seemeth , 
 that I might do no otherwise - that there be nothing else in the 
 world for I to do . what do I deem will come of it , sayest thou ? 
 this , that we shall be happy together , she and I , till the day of our 
 death . ' 

 she say : ' and even so long shall I be sorry : so far be we 
 sunder now . alas ! who look for it ? and whither shall I turn to 
 now ? ' 

 say Gold - mane : ' she bid I tell thee that to - morrow be a new day : 
 meseemeth I know her meaning . ' 

 ' no word of hers hath any meaning to I , ' say the Bride . 

 ' Nay , ' say he , ' but hast thou not hear these rumour of war that 
 be in the Dale ? shall not these thing avail thee ? much may grow 
 out of they ; and thou with the mighty heart , so faithful and 
 compassionate ! ' 

 she say : ' what sayest thou ? what may grow out of they ? yea , I 
 have hear those rumour as a man sick to death heareth man talk of 
 their business down in the street while he lieth on his bed ; and 
 already he hath do with it all , and hath no world to mend or mar . 
 for I nought shall grow out of it . what mean thou ? ' 

 say Gold - mane : ' be there nought in the fellowship of Folks , and the 
 aiding of the valiant , and the deliverance of the hapless ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , ' there be nought to I . I can not think of it to - day 
 nor yet to - morrow belike . yet true it be that I may mingle in it , 
 though think nought of it . but this shall not avail I . ' 

 she be silent a little , but presently spake and say : ' thou sayest 
 right ; it be not thou that hast do this , but the woman who send I 
 the ring and the message of an old saw . o that she should be bear to 
 sunder we ! how hath it befall that I be now so little to thee and 
 she so much ? ' 

 and again she be silent ; and after a while face - of - god spake kindly 
 and softly and say : ' Kinswoman , wilt thou for ever begrudge our 
 love ? this grudge lieth heavy on my soul , and it be I alone that have 
 to bear it . ' 

 she say : ' this be but a light burden for thee to bear , when thou 
 hast nought else to bear ! but do I begrudge thee thy love , gold- 
 mane ? I know not that . rather meseemeth I do not believe in it - nor 
 shall do ever . ' 

 then she hold her peace a long while , nor do he speak one word : and 
 they be so still , that a robin come hop about they , close to 
 the hem of her kirtle , and a starling pitch in the apple - tree hard 
 by and whistle and chuckle , turn about and about , heed they 
 nought . then at last she lift up her face from look on the 
 grass and say : ' these be idle word and avail nothing : one thing 
 only I know , that we be sunder . and now it repenteth I that I 
 have show thee my tear and my grief and my sickness of the earth 
 and those that dwell thereon . I be ashamed of it , as if thou hadst 
 smite I , and I have come and show thee the stripe , and say , see 
 what thou hast do ! hast thou no pity ? yea , thou pitiest I , and 
 wilt try to forget thy pity . belike thou art right when thou sayest , 
 to - morrow be a new day ; belike matter will arise that will call I 
 back to life , and I shall once more take heed of the joy and sorrow 
 of my people . Nay , it be most like that this I shall feign to do 
 even now . but if to - morrow be a new day , it be to - day now and not 
 to - morrow , and so shall it be for long . hereof belike we shall talk 
 no more , thou and I. For as the day wear , the dealing between we 
 shall be that thou shalt but get thee away from my life , and I shall 
 be nought to thee but the name of a kinswoman . thus should it be 
 even wert thou to strive to make it otherwise ; and thou shalt not 
 strive . so let all this be ; for this be not the word I have to say to 
 thee . but hearken ! now be we sunder , and it irketh I beyond 
 measure that folk know it not , and be kind , and rejoice in our love , 
 and deem it a happy thing for the folk ; and this burden I may bear no 
 long . so I shall declare unto man that I will not we d thee ; and 
 belike they may wonder why it be , till they see thee wed to the 
 Woman of the Mountain . art thou content that so it shall be ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' Nay , thou shalt not take this all upon thyself ; I 
 also shall declare unto the folk that I will we d none but she , the 
 Mountain - Woman . ' 

 she say : ' this shalt thou not do ; I forbid it thee . and I will 
 take it all upon myself . shall I have it say of I that I be unmeet 
 to we d thee , and that thou hast find I out at last and at late ? 
 I lay this upon thee , that wheresoever I declare this and whatsoever 
 I may say , thou shalt hold thy peace . this at least thou may'st do 
 for I . Wilt thou ? ' 

 ' yea , ' he say , ' though it shall put I to shame . ' 

 again she be silent for a little ; then she say : 

 ' o gold - mane , this would I take upon myself not soothly for any shame 
 of seem to be thy cast - off ; but because it be I who need must 
 bear all the sorrow of our sundering ; and I have the will to bear it 
 great and heavy , that I may be as the woman of old time , and they 
 that have come from the Gods , lest I belittle my life with malice and 
 spite and confusion , and it become poisonous to I . be at peace ! be 
 at peace ! and leave all to the wearing of the year ; and forget not 
 that which thou hast swear ! ' 

 Therewith she turn and go from that green place toward the House 
 of the Face , walk slowly through the garden amongst the sweet 
 odour , beneath the fair blossom , a body most dainty and beauteous 
 of fashion , but the casket of grievous sorrow , which all that 
 goodliness avail not . 

 but face - of - god linger in that place a little , and for that little 
 while the joy of his life be dull and overworn ; and the day 
 before his wandering on the mountain seem to he free and careless 
 and happy day that he could not but regret . he be ashamed , 
 moreover , that this so unquenchable grief should come but of he , and 
 the pleasure of his life , which he himself have find out for himself , 
 and which be but such a little portion of the Earth and the deed 
 thereof . but presently his thought wander from all this , and as he 
 turn away from the sundial and go his way through the garden , he 
 call to mind his longing for the day of the spring market , when he 
 should see the Sun - beam again and be cherish by the sweetness of 
 her love . 



 CHAPTER XXV . of the GATE - thing AT burgstead 



 but now must he hasten , for the Gate - thing be to be holden two hour 
 before noon ; so he betake he speedily to the Hall , and take his 
 shield and do on a goodly helm and girt his sword to his side , for 
 man must need go to all folk - mote with their weapon and clothe in 
 war - gear . thus he go forth to the Gate with many other , and there 
 already be many folk assemble in the space aforesaid betwixt the 
 Gate of the Burg and the sheer rock on the face of which be the 
 step that lead up to the ancient tower on the height . the Alderman 
 be sit on the great stone by the Gate - side which be his 
 appoint place , and beside he on the stone bench be the six 
 Wardens of the Burg ; but of the six Wardens of the Dale there be 
 but three , for the other have not yet hear tell of the battle or have 
 get the summon to the thing , since they have be about their 
 business down the Dale . 

 face - of - god take his place silently amongst the neighbour , but man 
 make way for he , so that he must need stand in front , face his 
 father and the Wardens ; and there go up a murmur of expectation 
 round about he , both because the word have go about that he have a 
 tale of new tiding to tell , and also because man deem he their 
 good and handy man , though he be yet so young . 

 now the Alderman look around and beheld a great throng gather 
 together , and he look on the shadow of the Gate which the 
 southere sun be cast on the hard white ground of the Thing- 
 stead , and he see that it have just take in the standing - stone which 
 be in the midst of the place . on the face of the say stone be 
 carven the image of a fight man with shield on arm and axe in 
 hand ; for it have be set there in old time in memory of the man who 
 have bid the Folk build the Gate and its wall , and have show they 
 how to fashion it : for he be a deft house - smith as well as a great 
 warrior ; and his name be iron - hand . so when the Alderman see that 
 this stone be wholly within the shadow of the Gate he know that it 
 be the due time for the hallowing - in of the thing . so he bid one 
 of the warden who sit beside he and have a great slug - horn slung 
 about he , to rise and set the horn to his mouth . 

 so that man arise and blow three great blast that go bellow 
 about the tower and down the street , and beat back again from the 
 face of the sheer rock and up they and over into the wild - wood ; and 
 the sound of it go on the light west - wind along the lip of the 
 Dale toward the mountain waste . and many a goodman , when he hear 
 the voice of the horn in the bright spring morning , leave spade or axe 
 or plough - stilt , or the foddering of the ewe and their youngling , 
 and turn back home to fetch his sword and helm and hasten to the 
 Thing , though he know not why it be summon : and woman wend 
 over the meadow , who have not yet hear of the battle in the wood , 
 hearken and stand still on the green grass or amidst the ripple of 
 the ford , and the threat of come trouble smite heavy on their 
 heart , for they know that great tiding must be towards if a thing 
 must need be summon so close to the Great Folk - mote . 

 but now the Alderman stand up and spake amidst the silence that 
 follow the last echo of the horn : 

 ' now be hallowed in this Gate - thing of the Burgstead Men and the man 
 of the Dale , wherein they shall take counsel concern matter late 
 befall , that press hard upon they . let no man break the peace of 
 the Holy Thing , lest he become a man accurse in holy place from the 
 plain up to the mountain , and from the mountain down to the plain ; a 
 man not to be cherish of any man of good will , not be holpen with 
 victual or edge - tool or draught - beast ; a man to be shelter under 
 no roof - tree , and warm at no hearth of man : so help we the Warrior 
 and the God of the Earth , and he of the face , and all the father ! ' 

 when he have speak man clash their weapon in token of assent ; and 
 he sit down again , and there be silence for a space . but presently 
 come thrust forward a goodman of the Dale , who seem as if he have 
 come hurriedly to the thing ; for his face be run down with 
 sweat , his wide - rim iron cap sit awry over his brow , and he be 
 girt with a rusty sword without a scabbard , and the girdle be ill- 
 brace up about his loin . so he say : 

 ' I be red - coat of waterless of the Lower Dale . early this morning as 
 I be go afield I meet on the way a man akin to I , Fox of Upton to 
 wit , and he tell I that man be be summon to a Gate - thing . so 
 I turn back home , and catch up any weapon that come handy , and 
 here I be , Alderman , ask thee of the tiding which hath drive 
 thee to call this thing so hard on the Great Folk - mote , for I know 
 they nothing so . ' 

 then stand up Iron - face the Alderman and say : ' this be well ask , 
 and soon shall ye be as wise as I be on this matter . know ye , o man 
 of Burgstead and the Dale , that we have not call this Gate - thing so 
 hard on the Great Folk - mote have not great need be to look into 
 troublous matter . long have ye dwelt in peace , and it be year on 
 year now since any foeman hath fall on the Dale : but , as ye will 
 bear in mind , last autumn be there ransacking in the Dale and 
 amidst of the Shepherds after the manner of deed of war ; and it 
 troubleth we that none can say who work these ill deed . next , 
 but a little while agone , be wood - grey , a valiant goodman of the 
 Woodlanders , slay close to his own door by evil man . these man we 
 take at first for mere gangrel felon and outcast from their own 
 folk : though there be some who speak against that from the 
 beginning . 

 ' but thirdly be new tiding again : for three day ago , while some 
 of the folk be hunt peaceably in the wild - wood and think no 
 evil , they be fall upon of set purpose by a host of man - at - arm , 
 and nought would serve but mere battle for dear life , so that many of 
 our neighbour be hurt , and three slain outright ; and now mark 
 this , that those who there fall upon our folk be clothe and arm 
 even as the two felon that slay wood - grey , and moreover be like 
 they in aspect of body . now stand forth Hall - face my son , and answer 
 to my question in a loud voice , so that all may hear thee . ' 

 so hall - face stand forth , clothe in gleam war - gear , with an axe over 
 his shoulder , and seem a doughty warrior . and Iron - face say to 
 he : 

 ' tell I , son , those whom ye meet in the wood , and of whom ye bring 
 home two captive , how much like be they to the murder - carle at 
 Wood - grey 's ? ' 

 say Hall - face : ' as like as pea out of the same cod , and to our 
 eye all those whom we see in the wood might have be son of one 
 father and one mother , so much alike be they . ' 

 ' yea , ' say the Alderman ; ' now tell I how many by thy deeming fall 
 upon you in the wood ? ' 

 say Hall - face : ' we deem that if they be any less than 
 threescore , they be little less . ' 

 ' Great be the odd , ' say the Alderman . ' or how many be ye ? ' 

 ' one score and seven , ' say Hall - face . 

 say the Alderman : ' and yet ye escape with life all save those 
 three ? ' 

 hall - face say : ' I deem that scarce one should have come back alive , 
 have it not be that as we fight come a noise like the howling of 
 wolf , and thereat the foeman turn and flee , and there follow on 
 the fleer tall man clothe in sheep - brown raiment , who smite they down 
 as they flee . ' 

 ' here then be the story , neighbour , ' say the Alderman , ' and ye may 
 see thereby that if those slayer of Wood - grey be outcast , their 
 band be a great one ; but it seemeth rather that they be man of a 
 folk whose craft it be to rob with the armed hand , and to slay the 
 robbed ; and that they be now gather on our border for war . yet , 
 moreover , they have foeman in the wood who should be fellow - in - arm 
 of we . how sayest thou , stone - face ? thou art old , and hast see 
 many war in the Dale , and know the wild - wood to its innermost . 

 ' Alderman , ' say stone - face , ' and ye neighbour of the Dale , maybe 
 these foe whom ye have meet be not of the race of man , but be 
 troll and wood - wight . now if they be troll it be ill , for then be 
 the world grow worser , and the wood shall be right perilous for 
 those who need must fare therein . yet if they be man it be a worse 
 matter ; for the troll would not come out of the waste into the 
 sunlight of the Dale . but these foe , if they be man , be lust 
 after our fair Dale to eat it up , and it be most like that they be 
 gather a huge host to fall upon we at home . such thing I have 
 hear of when I be young , and the aspect of the evil man who overrun 
 the kindred of old time , accord to all tale and lay that I have 
 hear , be even such as the aspect of those whom we have see of late . 
 as to those wolf who save the neighbour and chase their foeman , 
 there be one here who belike knoweth more of all this than we do , and 
 that , o Alderman , be thy son whom I have foster , face - of - god to 
 wit . bid he answer to thy questioning , and tell we what he hath 
 see and hear of late ; then shall we verily know the whole story as 
 far as it can be know . ' 

 then man press round , and be eager to hear what face - of - god would 
 be say . but or ever the Alderman could begin to question he , the 
 throng be cleave by new - comer , and these be the man who have be 
 send to bring home the corps of the Dusky Men : so they have cast 
 load hook into the Weltering Water , and have drag up he whom 
 face - of - god have shove into the eddy , and who have sink like a stone 
 just where he fall , and now they be bring he on a bi along 
 with he who have be slay a - land . they be set down in the place 
 before the Alderman , and man who have not see they before look 
 eagerly on they that they might behold the aspect of their foeman ; 
 and nought lovely be they to look on ; for the drowned man be 
 already bleach and swell with the water , and the other , his face 
 be all wrye and twist with that spear - thrust in the mouth . 

 then the Alderman say : ' I would question my son Face - of - god . let 
 he stand forth ! ' 

 and therewith he smile merrily in his son 's face , for he be 
 stand right in front of he ; and he say : 

 ' ask of I , Alderman , and I will answer . ' 

 ' Kinsman , ' say Iron - face , ' look at these two dead man , and tell I , 
 if thou hast see any such besides those two murder - carle who be 
 slay at Carlstead ; or if thou know aught of their folk ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' yesterday I see six other like to these both in 
 array and of body , and three of they I slay , for we be in battle 
 with they early in the morning . ' 

 there be a murmur of joy at this word , since all man take these 
 felon for deadly foeman ; but iron - face say : ' what mean thou by 
 " we " ? ' 

 ' I and the man who have gueste I overnight , ' say face - of - god , ' and 
 they slay the other three ; or rather a woman of they slay the 
 felon . ' 

 ' Valiant she be ; all good go with her hand ! ' say the Alderman . 
 ' but what be these people , and where do they dwell ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' as to what they be , they be of the kindre of 
 the Gods and the Fathers , valiant man , and guest - cherishing : rich 
 have they be , and now be poor : and their poverty cometh of these 
 same felon , who master they by number not to be withstand . as to 
 where they dwell : when I say the name of their dwelling - place man 
 mock at I , as if I name some valley in the moon : yet come I to 
 burgdale thence in one day across the mountain - neck lead by sure 
 guide , and I tell thee that the name of their abode be Shadowy 
 Vale . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Iron - face , ' knoweth any man here of Shadowy Vale , or 
 where it be ? ' 

 none answer for a while ; but there be an old man who be sit 
 on the shaft of a wain on the outskirt of the throng , and when he 
 hear this word he ask his neighbour what the Alderman be say , 
 and he tell he . then say that elder : 

 ' give I place ; for I have a word to say hereon . ' Therewith he 
 arise , and make his way to the front of the ring of man , and say : 
 ' Alderman , thou know I ? ' 

 ' yea , ' say Iron - face , ' thou art call the Fiddle , because of thy 
 sweet speech and thy minstrelsy ; whereof I mind I well in the time 
 when I be young and thou no long young . ' 

 ' so it be , ' say the Fiddle . ' now hearken ! when I be very young I 
 hear of a vale lie far away across the mountain - neck ; a vale 
 where the sun shine never in winter and scantily in summer ; for my 
 swear foster - brother , Fight - fain , a bold man and a great hunter , have 
 happen upon it ; and on a day in full midsummer he bring I 
 thither ; and even now I see the Vale before I as in a picture ; a 
 marvellous place , well grassed , treeless , narrow , betwixt great 
 cliff - wall of black stone , with a green river run through it 
 towards a yawn gap and a huge force . amidst that Vale be a doom- 
 ring of black stone , and nigh thereto a feast - hall well build of 
 the like stone , over whose door be carven the image of a wolf with 
 red gap jaw , and within it   for we enter into it   be stone 
 bench on the dais . thence we come away , and thither again we go 
 in late autumn , and so dusk and cold it be at that season , that we 
 know not what to call it save the valley of deep shade . but its real 
 name we never know ; for there be no man there to give we a name or 
 tell we any tale thereof ; but all be waste there ; the wimbrel 
 laugh across its water , the raven croak from its crag , the eagle 
 scream over it , and the voice of its water never cease ; and thus 
 we leave it . so the season pass , and we go thither no more : for 
 fight - fain die , and without he wander over the waste be irksome 
 to I ; so never have I see that valley again , or hear man tell 
 thereof . 

 ' now , neighbour , have I tell you of a valley which seemeth to be 
 Shadowy Vale ; and this be true and no make - up story . ' 

 the Alderman nod kindly to he , and then say to face - of - god : 
 ' Kinsman , be this word accord with what thou know of Shadowy 
 Vale ? ' 

 ' yea , on all point , ' say face - of - god ; ' he hath put before I a 
 picture of the valley . and whereas he saith , that in his youth it 
 be waste , this also goeth with my knowledge thereof . for once be 
 it people , and then be waste , and now again be it people . ' 

 ' tell we then more of the folk thereof , ' say the Alderman ; ' be they 
 many ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say face - of - god , ' they be not . how might they be many , 
 dwelling in that narrow Vale amid the waste ? but they be valiant , 
 both man and woman , and strong and well - liking . once they dwelt in a 
 fair dale call silver - dale , the name whereof will be to you as a 
 name in a lay ; and there be they wealthy and happy . then fall upon 
 they this murderous Folk , whom they call the Dusky Men ; and they 
 fight and be overcome , and many of they be slain , and many 
 enthral , and the remnant of they escape through the pass of the 
 mountain and come back to dwell in Shadowy Vale , where their 
 forefather have dwelt long and long ago ; and this overthrow befell 
 they ten year agone . but now their old foeman have break out from 
 silver - dale and have take to scour the wood seek prey ; so they 
 fall upon these Dusky Men as occasion serve , and slay they without 
 pity , as if they be adder or evil dragon ; and indeed they be 
 bad . and these valiant man know for certain that their foeman be 
 now of mind to fall upon this Dale and destroy it , as they have do 
 with other nigh to they . and they will slay our man , and lie with 
 our woman against their will , and enthrall our child , and torment 
 all those that lie under their hand till life shall be bad than 
 death to they . therefore , o Alderman and Wardens , and ye neighbour 
 all , it behoveth you to take counsel what we shall do , and that 
 speedily . ' 

 there be again a murmur , as of man nothing daunt , but intent on 
 take some way through the come trouble . but no man say aught 
 till the Alderman spake : 

 ' when didst thou first happen upon this Earl - folk , son ? ' 

 ' late last autumn , ' say face - of - god . 

 say Iron - face : ' then might thou have tell we of this tale 
 before . ' 

 ' yea , ' say his son , ' but I know it not , or but little of it , till 
 two day agone . in the autumn I wander in the woodland , and on the 
 fall I happen on a few of this folk dwelling in a booth by the 
 pine - wood ; and they be kind and guest - fain with I , and give I 
 meat and drink and lodge , and bid I come to Shadowy Vale in the 
 spring , when I should know more of they . and that be I fain of ; for 
 they be wise and goodly man . but I deem no more of those that I 
 see there save as man who have be outlaw by their own folk for 
 deed that be unlawful belike , but not shameful , and be bid 
 their time of return , and be live as they might meanwhile . but
