 two day agone , when I meet they again in Shadowy Vale . also I think 
 before long ye shall see their chieftain in Burgstead , for he hath a 
 word for we . lastly , my mind it be that those brown - clothe man who 
 help hall - face and his company in the wood be nought but man of 
 this Earl - kin seek their foeman ; for indeed they tell I that they 
 have come upon a battle in the woodland wherein they have slay their 
 foeman . now have I tell you all that ye need to know concern 
 these matter . ' 

 again be there silence as Iron - face sit ponder a question for his 
 son ; then a goodman of the Upper Dale , Gritgarth to wit , spake and 
 say : 

 ' gold - mane mine , tell we how many be this folk ; I mean their 
 fighting - man ? ' 

 ' well ask , neighbour , ' say Iron - face . 

 say face - of - god : ' their fighting - man of full age may be five score ; 
 but besides that there shall be some two or three score of woman that 
 will fight , whoever say they nay ; and many of these be little bad 
 in the field than man ; or no bad , for they shoot well in the bow . 
 moreover , there will be a full score of swain not yet twenty winter 
 old whom ye may not hinder to fight if anything be a - doing . ' 

 ' this be no great host , ' say the Alderman ; ' yet if they deem there 
 be little to lose by fighting , and nought to gain by sit still , 
 they may go far in win their desire ; and that more especially if 
 they may draw into their quarrel some other valiant folk more in 
 number than they be . I marvel not , though , they be kind to thee , 
 son Gold - mane , if they know who thou wert . ' 

 ' they know it , ' say face - of - god . 

 ' neighbour , ' say the Alderman , ' have ye any rede hereon , and aught 
 to say to back your rede ? ' 

 then spake the fiddle : ' as ye know and may see , I be now very old , 
 and , as the word go , unmeet for battle : yet might I get I to the 
 field , either on mine own leg or on the leg of some four - foot 
 beast , I would strike , if it be but one stroke , on these pest of 
 the earth . and , Alderman , meseemeth we shall do amiss if we bid not 
 the Earl - folk of Shadowy Vale to be our fellow in arm in this 
 adventure . for look you , how few soever they be , they will be sure 
 to know the way of our foeman , and the mountain pass , and the 
 sure and nighest road across the neck and the mire of the waste ; 
 and though they be not a host , yet shall they be worth a host to we ? ' 

 when man hear his word they shout for joy of they ; for hatred of 
 the Dusky Men who should so mar their happy life in the Dale be 
 grow up in they , and the more that hatred wax , the more waxed 
 their love of those valiant one . 

 now red - coat of waterless spake again : he be a big man , both tall 
 and broad , ruddy - face and red - haired , some forty winter old . he 
 say : 

 ' life hath be well with we of the Lower Dale , and we deem that we 
 have much to lose in lose it . yet ill would the bargain be to buy 
 life with thralldom : we have be over - merry hitherto for that . 
 therefore I say , to battle ! and as to these man , these well - wisher 
 of face - of - god , if they also be minded for battle with our foe , we 
 be fool indeed if we do not join they to our company , be they 
 but one score instead of six . ' 

 man shout again , and they say that Red - coat have speak well . then 
 one after other the goodman of the Dale come and give their word for 
 fellowship in arm with the Men of Shadowy Vale , if there be such 
 as face - of - god have say , which they doubt not ; and amongst they 
 that spake be Fox of Nethertown , and Warwell , and Gritgarth , and 
 Bearswain , and Warcliff , and Hart of Highcliff , and Worm of 
 Willowholm , and Bullsbane , and highneb of the Marsh : all these be 
 stout man - at - arm and man of good counsel . 

 last of all the Alderman spake and say : 

 ' as to the war , that must we need meet if all be sooth that we have 
 hear , and I doubt it not . 

 ' now therefore let we look to it like wise man while time yet serve . 
 Ye shall know that the muster of the Dalesmen will bring under shield 
 eight long hundred of man well - arm , and of the Shepherd - Folk four 
 hundred , and of the Woodlanders two hundred ; and this be a goodly 
 host if it be well order and wisely lead . now be I your Alderman 
 and your Doomster , and I can heave up a sword as well as another 
 maybe , nor do I think that I shall blench in the battle ; yet I 
 misdoubt I that I be no leader or orderer of man - of - war : therefore 
 ye will do wisely to choose a wise man - at - arm than I be for your 
 war - leader ; and if at the Great Folk - mote , when all the Houses and 
 Kindreds be gather , man yeasay your choosing , then let he abide ; 
 but if they naysay it , let he give place to another . for time 
 press . will ye so choose ? ' 

 ' yea , yea ! ' cry all man . 

 ' Good be that , neighbour , ' say the Alderman . ' whom will ye have 
 for war - leader ? consider well . ' 

 Short be their rede , for every man open his mouth and cry out 
 ' face - of - god ! ' then say the Alderman : 

 ' the man be young and untried ; yet though he be so near akin to I , I 
 will say that ye will do wisely to take he ; for he be both deft of 
 his hand and brisk ; and moreover , of this matter he knoweth more 
 than all we together . now therefore I declare he your war - leader 
 till the time of the Great Folk - mote . ' 

 then all man shout with great glee and clash their weapon ; but 
 some few put their head together and spake apart a little while , and 
 then one of they , red - coat of waterless to wit , come forward and 
 say : ' Alderman , some of we deem it good that stone - face , the old 
 man wise in war and in the way of the Wood , should be name as a 
 counsellor to the war - leader ; and hall - face , a very brisk and strong 
 young man , to be his right hand and sword - bearer . ' 

 ' good be that , ' say Iron - face . ' neighbour , will ye have it so ? ' 
 this also they yeasaid without delay , and the Alderman declare 
 Stone - face and Hall - face the helper of face - of - god in this business . 
 then he say : 

 ' if any hath aught to say concern what be good to be do at once , 
 it be good that he say it now before all and not to murmur and 
 grudge hereafter . ' 

 none spake save the Fiddle , who say : ' Alderman and War - leader , one 
 thing would I say : that if these foeman be anywise akin to those 
 overrunner of the folk of whom the tale go in my youth   for I 
 also as well as stone - face mind I well of those tale concern 
 they , it shall not avail we to sit still and await their onset . for 
 then may they not be withstood , when they have gather head and 
 burst out and over the folk that have be happy , even as the water 
 that overtop a dyke and cover with their muddy ruin the deep green 
 grass and the flower - bud of spring . therefore my rede be , as soon 
 as may be to go seek these folk in the woodland and wheresoever else 
 they may be wander . what sayest thou , face - of - god ? ' 

 ' my rede be as thine , ' say he ; ' and to begin with , I do now call 
 upon ten ten of good man to meet I in arm at the beginning of 
 Wildlake 's way to - morrow morning at daybreak ; and I bid my brother 
 Hall - face to summon such as be most meet thereto . for this I deem 
 good , that we scour the wood daily at present till we hear fresh 
 tiding from they of Shadowy Vale , who be nigh than we to the 
 foeman . now , neighbour , be ye ready to meet I ? ' 

 then all shout , ' yea , we will go , we will go ! ' 

 say the Alderman : ' now have we make provision for the war in that 
 which be near to our hand . yet have we to deal with the matter 
 of the fellowship with the Folk whom face - of - god hath see . this be 
 a matter for thee , son , at least till the Great Folk - mote be holden . 
 tell I then , shall we send a messenger to Shadowy Vale to speak with 
 this folk , or shall we abide the chieftain be come ? ' 

 ' by my rede , ' say face - of - god , ' we shall abide his coming : for 
 first , though I might well make my way thither , I doubt if I could 
 give any the bearing , so that he could come there without I ; and 
 belike I be need at home , since I be become war - leader . moreover , 
 when your messenger cometh to Shadowy Vale , he may well chance to 
 find neither the chieftain there , nor the good of his man ; for while 
 be they here , and while there , as they wend follow after the 
 Dusky Men . ' 

 ' it be well , son , ' say the Alderman , ' let it be as thou sayest : 
 soothly this matter must need be bring before the Great Folk - mote . 
 now will I ask if any other hath any word to say , or any rede to give 
 before this Gate - thing sundereth ? ' 

 but no man come forward , and all man seem well content and of good 
 heart ; and it be now well past noontide . 



 CHAPTER XXVI . the ending of the GATE - thing 



 but just as the Alderman be on the point of rise to declare the 
 breaking - up of the thing , there come a stir in the throng and it 
 open , and a warrior come forth into the innermost of the ring of 
 man , array in goodly glitter war - gear ; clothe in such wise that a 
 tunicle of precious gold - wrought web cover the hauberk all but the 
 sleeve thereof , and the hem of it beset with blue mountain - stone 
 smite against the ankle and well - nigh touch the foot , shod with 
 sandal gold - embroider and gem . this warrior bear a goodly 
 gild helm on the head , and hold in hand a spear with gold - garland 
 shaft , and be gird with a sword whose hilt and scabbard both be 
 adorn with gold and gem : beardless , smooth - cheeked , exceed 
 fair of face be the warrior , but pale and somewhat haggard - eye : 
 and those who be nearby beheld and wonder ; for they see that 
 there be come the Bride array for war and battle , as if she be a 
 messenger from the House of the Gods , and the Burg that endureth for 
 ever . 

 then she fall to speech in a voice which at first be somewhat hoarse 
 and break , but clear as she go on , and she say : 

 ' there sitt thou , o Alderman of Burgdale ! be face - of - god thy son 
 anywhere nigh , so that he can hear I ? ' 

 but iron - face wonder at her word , and say : ' he be beside thee , as 
 he should be . ' for indeed face - of - god be touch she , shoulder to 
 shoulder . but she look not to the right hand nor the left , but 
 say : 

 ' Hearken , Iron - face ! chief of the House of the Face , Alderman of the 
 Dale , and ye also , neighbour and goodman of the Dale : I be a woman 
 call the Bride , of the House of the Steer , and ye have hear that I 
 have plight my troth to face - of - god to we d with he , to love he , 
 and lie in his bed . but it be not so : we be not troth - plight ; nor 
 will I we d with he , nor any other , but will wend with you to the 
 war , and play my part therein accord to what might be in I ; nor 
 will I be worse than the wife of Shadowy Vale . ' 

 face - of - god hear her word with no change of countenance ; but Iron- 
 face redden over all his face , and star at she , and knit his 
 brow and say : 

 ' Maiden , what be these word ? what have we do to thee ? have I 
 not be to thee as a father , and love thee dearly ? be not my son 
 goodly and manly and deft in arm ? hath it not ever be the will not of 
 the House of the Face to we d in the House of the Steer ? and in these 
 two Houses there hath never yet be a goodly man and a lovelier 
 maiden than be ye two . what have we do then ? ' 

 ' ye have do nought against I , ' she say , ' and all that thou sayest 
 be sooth ; yet will I not we d with face - of - god . ' 

 yet fiercer wax the face of the Alderman , and he say in a loud 
 voice : 

 ' but how if I tell thee that I will speak with thy kindre of the 
 Steer , and thou shalt do after my bidding whether thou wilt or 
 whether thou wilt not ? ' 

 ' and how will ye compel I thereto ? ' she say . ' be there thrall in 
 the Dale ? or will ye make I an outlaw ? who shall heed it ? or I 
 shall betake I to Shadowy Vale and become one of their warrior- 
 maiden . ' 

 now be the Alderman 's face change from red to white , and belike he 
 forgat the thing , and what he be do there , and he cry out : 

 ' this be an evil day , and who shall help I ? Thou , face - of - god , what 
 hast thou to say ? wilt thou let this woman go without a word ? what 
 hath bewitch thee ? ' 

 but never a word spake his son , but stand look straight forward , 
 cold and calm by seem . then turn Iron - face again to the Bride , 
 and say in a soft voice : 

 ' tell I , maiden , whom I erst call daughter , what hath befall , 
 that thou wilt leave my son ; thou who wert once so kind and love to 
 he ; whose hand be always seek his , whose eye be ever 
 follow his ; who wouldst go where he bid , and come when he call . 
 what hath betid that ye have cast he out , and flee from our House ? ' 

 she flush red beneath her helm and say : 

 ' there be war in the land , and I have see it come , and that thing 
 shall change around we . I have look about I and see man happy 
 and woman content , and child weary for mere mirth and joy . and I 
 have think , in a day , or two day or three , all this shall be 
 change , and the woman shall be , some anxious and weary with 
 waiting , some cast all hope away ; and the man , some shall come 
 back to the garth no more , and some shall come back maimed and 
 useless , and there shall be loss of friend and fellow , and mirth 
 depart , and dull day and empty hour , and the child wander 
 about marvel at the sorrow of the house . all this I see before 
 I , and grief and pain and wounding and death ; and I say : shall I 
 be any well than the bad of the folk that loveth I ? Nay , this 
 shall never be ; and since I have learn to be deft with mine hand 
 in all the play of war , and that I be as strong as many a man , and as 
 hardy - hearted as any , I will give myself to the Warrior and the God 
 of the face ; and the battle - field shall be my home , and the after- 
 grief of the fight my banquet and holiday , that I may bear the burden 
 of my people , in the battle and out of it ; and know every sorrow that 
 the Dale hath ; and cast aside as a grievous and ugly thing the bed of 
 the warrior that the maiden desire , and the toying of lip and hand 
 and soft word of desire , and all the joy that dwelleth in the Castle 
 of Love and the Garden thereof ; while the world outside be sick and 
 sorry , and the field lie waste and the harvest burneth . even so 
 have I swear , even so will I do . ' 

 her eye glitter and her cheek be flushed , and her voice be clear 
 and ring now ; and when she end there arise a murmur of praise 
 from the man round about she . but Iron - face say coldly : 

 ' these be great word ; but I know not what they mean . if thou wilt 
 to the field and fight among the carle   and that I would not naysay , 
 for it hath oft be do and praise aforetime , why shouldest thou 
 not go side by side with face - of - god and as his plighted maiden ? ' 

 the light which the sweetness of speech have bring into her face have 
 die out of it now , and she look weary and hapless as she answer 
 he slowly : 

 ' I will not we d with face - of - god , but will fare afield as a virgin of 
 war , as I have swear to the Warrior . ' 

 then wax iron - face exceeding wroth , and he rise up before all man 
 and cry loudly and fiercely : 

 ' there be some lie abroad , that windeth about we as the gossamer in 
 the lane of an autumn morning . ' 

 and therewith he stride up to face - of - god as though he have nought to 
 do with the thing ; and he stand before he and cry out at he while 
 all man wonder : 

 ' Thou ! what hast thou do to turn this maiden 's heart to stone ? who 
 be it that be devise guile with thee to throw aside this worthy 
 wedding in a worthy House , with whom our son be ever will not to we d ? 
 speak , tell the tale ! ' 

 but face - of - god hold his peace and stand calm and proud before all 
 man . 

 then the blood mount to iron - face 's head , and he forgat folk and 
 kindre and the war to come , and he cry so that all the place ring 
 with the word of his anger : 

 ' Thou dastard ! I see thee now ; it be thou that hast do this , and 
 not the maiden ; and now thou hast make she bear a double burden , and 
 set she on to speak for thee , whilst thou stand by say nought , 
 and wilt take no scruple 's weight of her shame upon thee ! ' 

 but his son spake never a word , and Iron - face cry : ' out on thee ! 
 I know thee now , and why thou would not to the west - land last 
 winter . I be no fool ; I know thee . where hast thou hide the 
 stranger woman ? ' 

 Therewith he draw forth his sword and hove it aloft as if to hew down 
 face - of - god , who spake not nor flinch nor raise a hand from his 
 side . but the Bride throw herself in front of gold - mane , while there 
 arise an angry cry of ' the Peace of the Holy Thing ! peace - breaking , 
 peace - breaking ! ' and some cry , ' for the war - leader , the War- 
 leader ! ' and as man could for the press they draw forth their sword , 
 and there be tumult and noise all over the thing - stead . 

 but stone - face catch hold of the Alderman 's right arm and drag 
 down the sword , and the big carle , red - coat of Waterless , come up 
 behind he and cast his arm about his middle and draw he back ; and 
 presently he look around he , and slowly sheathe his sword , and 
 go back to his place and sit he down ; and in a little while the 
 noise abate and sword be sheathe , and man wax quiet again , and 
 the Alderman arise and say in a loud voice , but in the wonte way of 
 the head man of the thing : 

 ' here hath be trouble in the Holy Thing ; a violent man hath 
 trouble it , and draw sword on a neighbour ; will the neighbour give 
 the doom hereof into the hand of the Alderman ? ' 

 now all know Iron - face , and they cry out , ' that will we . ' so he 
 spake again : 

 ' I doom the troubler of the Peace of the Holy Thing to pay a fine , to 
 wit double the blood - wite that would be duly pay for a full - grow 
 freeman of the kindred . ' 

 then the cry go up and man yeasaid his doom , and all say that it 
 be well and fairly doom ; and iron - face sit still . 

 but stone - face stand forth and say : 

 ' here have be wild word in the air ; and dream have take shape 
 and come amongst we , and have bewitch we , so that friend and kin 
 have wrangle . and meseemeth that this be through the wizardry of 
 these felon , who , even dead as they be , have cast spell over we . 
 good it be to cast they into the Death Tarn , and then to get to our 
 work ; for there be much to do . ' 

 all man yeasaid that ; and Forkbeard of Lea go with those who have 
 bear the corps thither to cast they into the black pool . 

 but the Fiddle spake and say : 

 ' stone - face sayeth sooth . o Alderman , thou art no young man , yet be 
 I old enough to be thy father ; so will I give thee a rede , and say 
 this : face - of - god thy son be no liar or dastard or beguiler , but he 
 be a young man and exceed goodly of fashion , well - speak and kind ; 
 so that few woman may look on he and hear he without desire his 
 kindness and love , and to such man as this many thing happen . 
 moreover , he hath now become our captain , and be a deft warrior with 
 his hand , and as I deem , a sober and careful leader of man ; 
 therefore we need he and his courage and his skill of lead . so 
 rage not against he as if he have do an ill deed not to be 
 forgive - whatever he hath do , whereof we know not - for life be 
 long before he , and most like we shall still have to thank he for 
 many good deed towards we . as for the maiden , she be both lovely 
 and wise . she hath a sorrow at her heart , and we deem that we know 
 what it be . yet hath she not lie when she say that she would bear 
 the burden of the grief of the people . even so shall she do ; and 
 whether she will , or whether she will not , that shall heal her own 
 grief . for to - morrow be a new day . therefore , if thou do after my 
 rede , thou wilt not meddle betwixt these twain , but wilt remember all 
 that we have to do , and that war be come upon we . and when that be 
 over , we shall turn round and behold each other , and see that we be 
 not wholly what we be before ; and then shall that which be hard 
 to forgive , be forget , and that which be remember be easy to 
 forgive . ' 

 so he spake ; and iron - face sit still and put his left hand to his 
 beard as one who pondereth ; but the Bride look in the face of the 
 old man the Fiddle , and then she turn and look at Gold - mane , and 
 her face soften , and she stand before the Alderman , and bent down 
 before he and hold out both her hand to he the palm upward . then 
 she say : ' Thou hast be wroth with I , and I marvel not ; for thy 
 hope , and the hope which we all have , hath deceive thee . but kind 
 indeed hast thou be to I ere now : therefore I pray thee take it 
 not amiss if I call to thy mind the oath which thou swearedst on the 
 Holy Boar last Yule , that thou wouldst not gainsay the prayer of any 
 man if thou could perform it ; therefore I bid thee naysay not 
 mine : and that be , that thou wilt ask I no more about this matter , 
 but wilt suffer I to fare afield like any swain of the Dale , and to 
 deal so with my folk that they shall not hinder I . also I pray thee 
 that thou wilt put no shame upon face - of - god my playmate and my 
 kinsman , nor show thine anger to he openly , even if for a little 
 while thy love for he be abate . no more than this will I ask of 
 thee . ' 

 all man who hear she be move to the heart by her kindness and the 
 sweetness of her voice , which be like to the robin sing suddenly 
 on a frosty morning of early winter . but as for gold - mane , his heart 
 be smite sorely by it , and her sorrow and her friendliness grieve 
 he out of measure . 

 but iron - face answer after a little while , speak slowly and 
 hoarsely , and with the shame yet cling to he of a man who have 
 be wroth and have speedily let his wrath run off he . so he say : 

 ' it be well , my daughter . I have no will to forswear myself ; nor 
 hast thou ask I a thing which be over - hard . yet indeed I would 
 that to - day be yesterday , or that many day be wear away . ' 

 then he stand up and cry in a loud voice over the throng : 

 ' let none forget the muster ; but hold he ready against the time that 
 the Warden shall come to he . let all man obey the war - leader , face- 
 of - god , without question or delay . as to the fine of the peace- 
 breaker , it shall be lay on the altar of the God at the Great folk- 
 mote . Herewith be the thing break up . ' 

 then all man shout and clash their weapon , and so sunder , and 
 go about their business . 

 and the talk of man it be that the breaking of the troth - plight 
 between those twain be ill ; for they love face - of - god , and as for 
 the Bride they deem she the Dearest of the kindred and the Jewel 
 of the Folk , and as if she be the fair and the kind of all 
 the Gods . neither do the wrath of Iron - face mislike any ; but they 
 say he have do well and manly both to be wroth and to let his wrath 
 run off he . as to the war which be to come , they keep a good heart 
 about it , and deem it as a game to be play , wherein they might 
 show themselves deft and valiant , and so get back to their merry life 
 again . 

 so wear the day through afternoon to even and night . 



 CHAPTER XXVII . FACE - of - GOD leadeth a band through the wood 



 Next morning tryst be hold faithfully , and an hundred and a half 
 be gather together on Wildlake 's way ; and face - of - god order 
 they into three company . he make hall - face leader over the first 
 one , and bid he hold on his way northward , and then to make for 
 Boars - bait and see if he should meet with anything thereabout where 
 the battle have be . red - coat of waterless he make captain of the 
 second band ; and he have it in charge to wend eastward along the edge 
 of the Dale , and not to go deep into the wood , but to go as far as he 
 might within the time appoint , toward the Mountains . furthermore , 
 he bid both Hall - face and red - coat to bring their band back to 
 Wildlake 's way by the morrow at sunset , where other goodman should be 
 come to take the place of their man ; and then if he and his company 
 be back again , he would bid they far what to do ; but if not , as 
 seem likely , then hall - face 's band to go west toward the Shepherd 
 country half a day 's journey , and so back , and red - coat 's east along 
 the Dale 's lip again for the like time , and then back , so that there 
 might be a constant watch and ward of the Dale keep against the 
 Felons . 

 all be order Gold - mane lead his own company north - east through 
 the thick wood , think that he might so fare as to come nigh to 
 silver - dale , or at least to hear tiding thereof . this intent he 
 tell to stone - face , but the old man shake his head and say : 

 ' good be this if it may be do ; but it be not for everyone to go 
 down to Hell in his lifetime and come back safe with a tale thereof . 
 however , whither thou wilt lead , thither will I follow , though 
 assure death waylayeth we . ' 

 and the old carle be joyous and proud to be on this adventure , and 
 say , that it be good indeed that his foster - son have with he a man 
 well stricken in year , who have both see many thing , and learn 
 many , and have good rede to give to valiant man . 

 so they go on their way , and fare very warily when they be 
 get beyond those part of the wood which they know well . by this 
 time they be string out in a long line ; and they note their road 
 carefully , blaze the tree on either side when there be tree , 
 and pile up little stone - heap where the tree fail they . for 
 Stone - face say that oft it befell man amidst the thicket and the 
 waste to be mislead by wight that begrudge man their life , so that 
 they go round and round in a ring which they might not depart from 
 till they die ; and no man doubt his word herein . 

 all day they go , and meet no foe , nay , no man at all ; nought but the 
 wild thing of the wood ; and that day the wood change little about 
 they from mile to mile . there be many thicket across their road 
 which they have to go round about ; so that to the crow fly over the 
 tree - top the journey have not be long to the place where night come 
 upon they , and where they have to make the wood their bedchamber . 

 that night they light no fire , but eat such cold victual as they 
 might carry with they ; nor have they shoot any venison , since they have 
 with they more than enough ; they make little noise or stir therefore 
 and fall asleep when they have set the watch . 

 on the morrow they arise betime , and break their fast and go their 
 way till noon : by then the wood have thin somewhat , and there be 
 little underwood betwixt the scrubby oak and ash which be pretty 
 nigh all the tree about : the ground also be break , and here and 
 there rocky , and they go into and out of rough little dale , most 
 of which have in they a brook of water run west and southwest ; and 
 now face - of - god lead his man somewhat more easterly ; and still for 
 some while they meet no man . 

 at last , about four hour after noon , when they be go less 
 warily , because they have hitherto come across nothing to hinder they , 
 rise over the brow of a somewhat steep ridge , they see down in the 
 valley below they a half score of man sit by the brook - side 
 eating and drink , their weapon lie beside they , and along with 
 they stand a woman with her hand tie behind her back . 

 they see at once that these man be of the Felons , so they that have 
 their bow bent , loose at they without more ado , while the other 
 run in upon they with sword and spear . the felon leapt up and run 
 scatter down the dale , such of they as be not smite by the 
 shaft ; but he who be nighest to the woman , ere he run , turn and 
 catch up a sword from the ground and thrust it through she , and the 
 next moment fall across the brook with an arrow in his back . 

 no one of the felon be nimble enough to escape from the fleet - foot 
 hunter of Burgdale , and they be all slay there to the number of 
 eleven . 

 but when they come back to the woman to tend she , she breathe she 
 last in their hand : she be a young and fair woman , black - haire 
 and dark - eyed . she have on her body a gown of rich web , but nought 
 else : she have be bruise and sore mishandle , and the Burgdale 
 carle weep for pity of she , and for wrath , as they straighten she 
 limb on the turf of the little valley . they let she lie there a 
 little , whilst they search round about , lest there should be any 
 other poor soul need their help , or any felon lurk thereby ; but 
 they find nought else save a bundle wherein be another rich gown 
 and diver woman 's gear , and sundry ring and jewel , and therewithal 
 the weapon and war - gear of a knight , delicately work after the 
 Westland fashion : these seem to they to betoken other foul deed 
 of these murder - carle . so when they have abide a while , they lay 
 the dead woman in mould by the brook - side , and bury with her the 
 other woman 's attire and the knight 's gear , all but his sword and 
 shield , which they have away with they : then they cast the carcass 
 of the felon into the brake , but bring away their weapon and the 
 silver ring from their arm , which they wear like all the other of 
 they whom they have fall in with ; and so go on their way to the 
 north - east , full of wrath against those dastard of the Earth . 

 it be hard on sunset when they leave the valley of murder , and they 
 go no long way thence before they must need make stay for the 
 night ; and when they have array their sleeping - stead the moon be 
 up , and they see that before they lie the close wood again , for they 
 have make their lair on the top of a little ridge . 

 there then they lay , and nought stir they in the night , and 
 betime on the morrow they be afoot , and enter the abovesaid 
 thicket , wherein two of they , keen hunter , have be aforetime , but 
 have not go deep into it . through this wood they go all day 
 toward the north - east , and meet nought but the wild thing therein . 
 at last , when it be near sunset , they come out of the thicket into a 
 small plain , or shallow dale rather , with no great tree in it , but 
 thorn - brake here and there where the ground sink into hollow ; a 
 little river run through the midst of it , and wind round about a 
 height whose face toward the river go down sheer into the water , 
 but away from it sink down in a long slope to where the thick wood 
 begin again : and this height or burg look well - nigh west . 

 Thitherward they go ; but as they be draw nigh to the river , 
 and be on the top of a bent above a bushy hollow between they and 
 the water , they espy a man stand in the river near the bank , who 
 see they not , because he be stoop down intent on something in the 
 bank or under it : so they gat they speedily down into the hollow 
 without noise , that they might get some tiding of the man . 

 then face - of - god bid his man abide hide under the bush and stole 
 forward quietly up the further bank of the hollow , his target on his 
 arm and his spear poise . when he be behind the last bush on the 
 top of the bent he be within half a spear - cast of the water and the 
 man ; so he look on he and see that he be quite naked except for a 
 clout about his middle . 

 face - of - god see at once that he be not one of the Dusky Men ; he be 
 a black - haired man , but white - skin , and of fair stature , though 
 not so tall as the Burgdale folk . he be busy in tickle trout , 
 and just as face - of - god come out from the bush into the westering 
 sunlight , he throw up a fish on to the bank , and look up 
 therewithal , and beheld the weapone man glitter , and utter a 
 cry , but flee not when he see the spear poise for cast . 

 then face - of - god spake to he and say : ' come hither , Woodsman ! we 
 will not harm thee , but we desire speech of thee : and it will not 
 avail thee to flee , since I have bowman of the good in the hollow 
 yonder . ' 

 the man put forth his hand towards he as if pray he to forbear 
 casting , and look at he hard , and then come dripping from out the 
 water , and seem not greatly afeard ; for he stoop down and pick 
 up the trout he have take , and come towards face - of - god string 
 the last - catch one through the gill on to the withy whereon be 
 the other : and face - of - god see that he be a goodly man of some 
 thirty winter . 

 then face - of - god look on he with friendly eye and say : 

 ' art thou a foeman ? or wilt thou be helpful to we ? ' 

 he answer in the speech of the kindred with the hoarse voice of a 
 much weather - beat man : 

 ' Thou seest , lord , that I be naked and unarmed . ' 

 ' yet may'st thou bewray we , ' say face - of - god . ' what man art thou ? ' 

 say the man : ' I be the runaway thrall of evil man ; I have flee from 
 rose - dale and the Dusky Men . Hast thou the heart to hurt I ? ' 

 ' we be the foeman of the Dusky Men , ' say face - of - God ; ' wilt thou 
 help we against they ? ' 

 the man knit his brow and say : ' yea , if ye will give I your word 
 not to suffer I to fall into their hand alive . but whence art 
 thou , to be so bold ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' we be of Burgdale ; and I will swear to thee on 
 the edge of the sword that thou shalt not fall alive into the hand 
 of the Dusky Men . ' 

 ' of Burgdale have I hear , ' say the man ; ' and in sooth thou seemest 
 not such a man as would bewray a hapless man . but now have I well 
 bring you to some lurking - place where ye shall not be easily find of 
 these devil , who now oft - time scour the wood hereabout . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' come first and see my fellow ; and then if thou 
 think we have nee to hide , it be well . ' 

 so the man go side by side with he towards their lair , and as they 
 go gold - mane note mark of stripe on his back and side , and 
 say : ' sorely hast thou be mishandle , poor man ! ' 

 then the man turn on he and say somewhat fiercely : ' say I not 
 that I have be a thrall of the Dusky Men ? how then should I have 
 escape tormenting and scourging , if I have be with they for but 
 three day ? ' 

 as he spake they come about a thorn - bush , and there be the burgdale 
 man down in the hollow ; and the man say : ' be these thy fellow ? 
 call to mind that thou hast swear by the edge of the sword not to 
 hurt I . ' 

 ' poor man ! ' say face - of - god ; ' these be thy friend , unless thou 
 bewray we . ' 

 then he cry aloud to his folk : ' here be now a good hap ! this be a 
 runaway thrall of the Dusky Men ; of he shall we hear tiding ; so 
 cherish he all ye may . ' 

 so the carle throng about he and bestir themselves to help 
 he , and one give he his surcoat for a kirtle , and another cast a 
 cloak about he ; and they bring he meat and drink , such as they 
 have ready to hand : and the man look as if he scarce believe in 
 all this , but deem himself to be in a dream . but presently he 
 turn to face - of - god and say : 

 ' now I see so many man and weapon I deem that ye have no need to 
 skulk in cave to - night , though I know of good one : yet shall ye do 
 well not to light a fire till moon - setting ; for the flame ye may 
 lightly hide , but the smoke may be see from far aloof . ' 

 but they bid he to meat , and he need no second bidding but ate 
 lustily , and they give he wine , and he drink a great draught and 
 sigh as for joy . then he say in a trembling voice , as though he 
 fear a naysay : 

 ' if ye be from Burgdale ye shall be fare back again presently ; and 
 I pray you to take I with you . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' yea surely , friend , that will we do , and rejoice 
 in thee . ' 

 then he drink another cup which Warcliff hold out to he , and spake 
 again : ' yet if ye would abide here till about noon to - morrow , or 
 mayhappen a little later , I would bring other runaway to see you ; 
 and they also might ye take with you : ye may think when ye see they 
 that ye shall have small gain of their company ; for poor wretched 
 folk they be , like to myself . yet since ye seek for tiding , herein 
 might they do you more service than I ; for amongst they be some who 
 come out of the hapless Dale within this moon ; and it be six month 
 since I escape . moreover , though they may look spend and outwear 
 now , yet if ye give they a little rest , and feed they well , they 
 shall yet do many a day 's work for you : and I tell you that if ye 
 take they for thrall , and put collar on their neck , and use they 
 no bad than a goodman useth his oxen and his ass , beat they 
 not save when they be idle or at fault , it shall be to they as if 
 they be come to heaven out of hell , and to such goodhap as they 
 have not think of , save in dream , for many and many a day . and 
 thus I entreat you to do because ye seem to I to be happy and 
 merciful man , who will not begrudge we this happiness . ' 

 the carle of Burgdale listen eagerly to what he say , and they 
 look at he with great eye and marvel ; and their heart be 
 move with pity towards he ; and stone - face say : 

 ' Herein , O war - leader , need I give thee no rede , for thou mayst see 
 clearly that all we deem that we should lose our manhood and become 
 the dastard of the Warrior if we do not abide the coming of these 
 poor man , and take they back to the Dale , and cherish they . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Wolf of Whitegarth , ' and great thank we owe to this man 
 that he biddeth we this : for great will be the gain to we if we 
 become so like the Gods that we may deliver the poor from misery . 
 now must I need think how they shall wonder when they come to 
 burgdale and find out how happy it be to dwell there . ' 

 ' surely , ' say face - of - god , ' thus shall we do , whatever cometh of it . 
 but , friend of the wood , as to thrall , there be none such in the 
 Dale , but therein be all man friend and neighbour , and even so 
 shall ye be . ' 

 and he fall a - musing , when he bethink he of how little he have 
 know of sorrow . 

 but that man , when he behold the happy face of the Burgdalers , and 
 hearken to their friendly voice , and understand what they say , 
 and he also be become strong with the meat and drink , he bow his 
 head adown and weep a long while ; and they meddle not with he , till 
 he turn again to they and say : 

 ' since ye be in arm , and seem to be seek your foeman , I suppose 
 ye wot that these tyrant and man - queller will fall upon you in 
 Burgdale ere the summer be well worn . ' 

 ' so much we deem indeed , ' say face - of - god , ' but we be fain to hear 
 the certainty of it , and how thou know thereof . ' 

 say the man : ' it be six moon ago that I flee , as I have tell you ; 
 and even then it be the common talk amongst our master that there 
 be fair dale to the south which they would overrun . Man would say 
 to man : we be over many in silver - dale , and we need more 
 thrall , because those we have be lessen , and especially the 
 woman ; now be we more at ease in Rose - dale , though we have send 
 thrall to silver - dale ; but yet we can bear no more man from thence 
 to eat up our stock from we : let they fare south to the happy dale , 
 and conquer they , and we will go with they and help therein , whether 
 we come back to Rose - dale or no . such talk do I hear then with mine 
 own ear : but some of those whom I shall bring to you to - morrow 
 shall know well what be do , since they have flee from Rose - dale 
 but a few day . moreover , there be a man and a woman who have flee 
 from Silver - dale itself , and be but a month from it , journey all 
 the time save when they must need hide ; and these say that their 
 master have get to know the way to Burgdale , and be minded for it 
 before the winter , as I say ; and nought else but the way thither do 
 they desire to know , since they have no fear . ' 

 by then be night come , and though the moon be high in heaven , and 
 light all that waste , the Burgdalers must need light a fire for 
 cook their meat , whatsoever that woodsman might say ; moreover , the 
 night be cold and somewhat frosty . a little before they have come to 
 that place they have shoot a fat buck and some small deer , but of 
 other meat they have no great store , though there be wine enough . so 
 they light their fire in the thickest of the thorn - bush to hide it all 
 they might , and thereat they cook their venison and the trout 
 which the runaway have take , and they fall to , and eat and drank and 
 be merry , make much of that poor man till he - seem he be 
 get into the company of the kind of the Gods . 

 but when they be full , face - of - god spake to he , and ask he his 
 name ; and he name himself Dallach ; but say he : ' Lord , this be 
 accord to the naming of man in Rose - dale before we be 
 enthral : but now what name have thrall ? also I be not 
 altogether of the blood of they of Rose - dale , but of well and more 
 warrior - like kin . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' Thou hast name Silver - dale ; know thou it ? ' 

 Dallach answer : ' I have never see it . it be far hence ; in a 
 week 's journey , make all diligence , and not be force to hide 
 and skulk like those runaway , ye shall come to the mouth thereof 
 lie west , where its rock - wall fall off toward the plain . ' 

 ' but , ' say face - of - god , ' be there no other way into that Dale ? ' 

 ' Nay , none that folk wot of , ' say Dallach , ' except to bold cragsman 
 with their life in their hand . ' 

 ' know thou aught of the affair of silver - dale ? ' say face - of - god . 

 say Dallach : ' somewhat I know : we wot that but a few year ago 
 there be a valiant folk dwell therein , who be lord of the 
 whole dale , and that they be vanquish by the Dusky Men : but 
 whether they be all slay and enthral we wot not ; but we deem it 
 otherwise . as for I it be of their blood that I be partly come ; for 
 my father 's father come thence to settle in Rose - dale , and wed a 
 woman of the Dale , who be my father 's mother . ' 

 ' when be it that ye fall under the Dusky Men ? ' say face - of - god . 

 say Dallach : ' it be five year ago . they come into the Dale a 
 great company , all in arm . ' 

 ' be there battle betwixt you ? ' say face - of - god . 

 ' alas ! not so , ' say Dallach . ' we be a happy folk there ; but soft 
 and delicate : for the Dale be exceed fertile , and beareth wealth 
 in abundance , both corn and oil and wine and fruit , and of beast for 
 man 's service the good that may be . would that there have be 
 battle , and that I have die therein with those that have a heart to 
 fight ; and even so saith now every man , yea , every woman in the Dale . 
 but it be not so when the elder meet in our Council - House on the day 
 when the Dusky Men bid we pay they tribute and give they house to 
 dwell in and land to live by . then have we weapon in our hand , but 
 no heart to use they . ' 

 ' what befell then ? ' say the goodman of Whitegarth . 

 say Dallach : ' look ye to it , lord , that it befall not in Burgdale ! 
 we give they all they ask for , and deem we have much left . what 
 befell , sayst thou ? we sit quiet ; we go about our work in fear and 
 trembling , for grim and hideous be they to look on . at first they 
 meddle not much with we , save to take from our house what they 
 would of meat and drink , or raiment , or plenishe . and all this we 
 deem we might bear , and that we need no more than to toil a 
 little more each day so as to win somewhat more of wealth . but soon 
 we find that it would not be so ; for they have no mind to till the 
 teem earth or work in the acre we have give they , or to sit at 
 the loom , or hammer in the stithy , or do any manlike work ; it be we 
 that must do all that for their behoof , and it be altogether for 
 they that we labour , and nought for ourselves ; and our body be 
 only so much our own as they be needful to be keep alive for 
 labour . Herein be our task hard than the toil of any mule or 
 ass , save for the young and goodly of the woman , whom they 
 would keep fair and delicate to be their bed - thrall . 

 ' yet not even so be our body safe from their malice : for these 
 man be not only tyrant , but fool and madman . let alone that 
 there be few day without stripe and torment to satiate their 
 fury or their pleasure , so that in all street and nigh any house 
 might you hear wail and scream and groaning ; but moreover , 
 though a wise man would not willingly slay his own thrall any more 
 than his own horse or ox , yet do these man so wax in folly and 
 malice , that they would often hew at man or woman as they meet they in 
 the way from mere grimness of soul ; and if they slay they it be 
 well . Thereof indeed come quarrel enough betwixt master and master , 
 for they be much give to man - slay amongst themselves : but what 
 profit to we thereof ? Nay , if the dead man be a chieftain , then 
 woe betide the thrall ! for thereof must many an one be slain on his 
 grave - mound to serve he on the hell - road . to be short : we have 
 hear of man who be fierce , and man who be grim ; but these we may 
 scarce believe we to be man at all , but troll rather ; and ill will 
 it be if their race waxeth in the world . ' 

 the burgdale man hearken with all their ear , and wonder that 
 such thing could befall ; and they rejoice at the work that lay 
 before they , and their heart rise high at the thought of battle in 
 that behalf , and the fame that should come of it . as for the 
 runaway , they make so much of he that the man marvel ; for they 
 deal with he like a woman cherish a son , and know not how to be 
 kind enough to he . 



 CHAPTER XXVIII . the MEN of BURGDALE meet the RUNAWAYS 



 now ere the night be far spend , Dallach arise and say : 

 ' kind folk , ye will presently be sleep ; but I bid you keep a good 
 watch , and if ye will be rule by I , ye will kindle no fire on the 
 morrow , for the smoke riseth thick in the morning air , and be as a 
 beacon . as for I , I shall leave you here to rest , and I myself will 
 fare on mine errand . ' 

 they bid he sleep and rest he after so many toil and hardship , 
 say that they be not tie to an hour to be back in Burgdale ; but 
 he say : ' Nay , the moon be high , and it be as good as daylight to 
 I , who could find my way even by starlight ; and your tarrying here 
 be nowise safe . moreover , if I could find those folk and bring they 
 part of the way by night and cloud it be well ; for if we be take 
 again , burn quick would be the good death by which we should die . 
 as for I , now be I strong with meat and drink and hope ; and when I 
 come to Burgdale there will be time enough for rest and slumber . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' shall I not wend with thee to see these people 
 and the lair wherein they hide ? ' 

 the man smile : ' Nay , earl , ' say he , ' that shall not be . for wot 
 ye what ? if they be to see I in company of a man - at - arm they 
 would deem that I be bring the foe upon they , and would flee , or 
 mayhappen would fall upon we . for as for I , when I see thee , thou 
 wert close anigh I , so I know thee to be no Dusky Man ; but they 
 would see the glitter of thine arm from afar , and to they all 
 weapone man be foeman . Thou , lord , know not the heart of a 
 thrall , nor the fear and doubt that be in it . Nay , I myself must 
 cast off these clothe that ye have give I , and fare naked , lest 
 they mistrust I . only I will take a spear in my hand , and sle a 
 knife round my neck , if ye will give they to I ; for if the bad 
 happen , I will not be take alive . ' 

 Therewith he cast off his raiment , and they give he the weapon and 
 wish he good speed , and he go his way twixt moonlight and 
 shadow ; but the Burgdalers go to sleep when they have set a watch . 

 early in the morning they awake , and the sun be shin and the 
 thrush singing in the thorn - brake , and all seem fair and 
 peaceful , and a little haze still hang about the face of the burg 
 over the river . so they go down to the water and wash the night 
 from off they ; and thence the most part of they go back to their 
 lair among the thorn - bush : but four of they go up the dale into 
 the oak - wood to shoot a buck , and five more they send out to watch 
 their skirt around they ; and face - of - god with old Stone - face go 
 over a ford of the stream , and come on to the low slope of the 
 burg , and so go up it to the top . thence they look about to see 
 if aught be stir , but they see little save the waste and the 
 wood , which on the north - east be thick of big tree stretch out a 
 long way . their own lair be clear to see over its bank and the 
 bush thereof , and that mislike face - of - god , lest any foe should 
 climb the burg that day . the morning be clear , and face - of - god 
 look north - and - by - west deem he see smoke rise into the air 
 over the tree - cover ridge that hide the further distance toward 
 that airt , though far east upheave the black shoulder of the 
 Great that Waste and the snowy peak behind they . the say smoke be 
 not such as cometh from one great fire , but be like a thin veil 
 stain the pale blue sky , as when man be burn ling on the 
 heath - side and it be see aloof . 

 he show that smoke to stone - face , who smile and say : 

 ' now will they be light the cook fire in Rose - dale : would I 
 be there with a few hundred of axis and stave at my back ! ' 

 ' yea , ' say face - of - god , smile in his face , ' but where I pray thee 
 be these elf and wood - wight , that we meet they not ? grim thing 
 there be in the wood , and thing fair enough also : but meseemeth 
 that the troll and the elf of thy young year have be fright 
 away . ' 

 say stone - face : ' maybe , foster - son ; that hath be see ere now , 
 that when one race of man overrunneth the land inhabit by another , 
 the wight and elve that love the vanquished be see no more , or 
 get they away far off into the outermost wild , where few man ever 
 come . ' 

 ' yea , ' say face - of - god , ' that may well be . but deem thou by that 
 token that we shall be vanquish ? ' 

 ' as for we , I know not , ' say stone - face ; ' but thy friend of Shadowy 
 Vale have be vanquish . moreover , concern these felon whom 
 now we be hunt , be we all so sure that they be man ? certain it 
 be , that when I go into battle with they , I shall smite with no more 
 pity than my sword , as if I be smite thing that may not feel the 
 woe of man . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' yea , even so shall it be with I . but what 
 think thou of these runaway ? shall we have tiding of they , or 
 shall Dallach bring the foe upon we ? it be for the sake of that 
 question that I have clomb the burg : and that we might watch the 
 land about we . ' 

 ' Nay , ' say stone - face , ' I have see many man , and I deem of Dallach 
 that he be a true man . I deem we shall soon have tiding of his 
 fellow ; and they may have see the elf and wood - wight : I would 
 fain ask they thereof , and be eager to see they . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' and I somewhat dread to see they , and their rag 
 and their misery and the weal of their stripe . it irk I to see 
 dallach when he first fall to his meat last night , how he eat like a 
 dog for fear and famine . how shall it be , moreover , when we have 
 they in the Dale , and they fall to the deed of kind there , as they 
 need must . will they not bear we evil and thrall - like man ? ' 

 ' maybe , ' say stone - face , ' and maybe not ; for they have be thrall 
 but for a little while : and I deem that in no long time shall ye see 
 they much better by plenteous meat and rest . and after all be 
 say , this Dallach bear he like a valiant man ; also it be valiant 
 of he to flee ; and of the other may ye say the like . but look you ! 
 there be man go down yonder towards our lair : belike those shall 
 be our guest , and there be no Dusky Men amongst they . come , let we 
 home ! ' 

 so face - of - god look and beheld from the height of the burg shape 
 of man grey and colourless creep toward the lair from sunshine to 
 shadow , like wild creature shy and fearful of the hunter , or so he 
 deem of they . 

 so he turn away , angry and sad of heart , and the twain go down 
 the burg and across the water to their camp , have see little to 
 tell of from the height . 

 when they come to their campment there be their folk stand in a 
 ring round about Dallach and the other runaway . they make way for 
 the war - leader and stone - face , who come amongst they and beheld the 
 Runaways , that they be many more than they look to see ; for they 
 be of carle one score and three , and of woman eighteen , all tell 
 save Dallach . when they see those twain come through the ring of man 
 and perceive that they be chieftain , some of they fall down on 
 their knee before they and hold out their joined hand to they , and 
 kiss the Burgdalers ' foot and the hem of their garment , while the 
 tear stream out of their eye : some stand move little and 
 star before they stupidly : and some keep glance from face to 
 face of the well - like happy burgdale carle , though for a while 
 even their face be sad and downcast at the sight of the poor man : 
 some also keep murmur one or two word in their country tongue , 
 and Dallach tell face - of - god that these be cry out for victual . 

 it must be say of these poor folk that they be of diver 
 condition , and chiefly of three : and first there be seven of 
 rose - dale and five of silver - dale late come to the wood   of these 
 silver - daler Dallach have tell but of two , for the other three be 
 but just come . of these twelve be seven woman , and all , save two 
 of the woman , be clothe in one scanty kirtle or shirt only ; for such 
 be the will not of the Dusky Men with their thrall . they have bring 
 away weapon , and have amongst they six axis and a spear , and a sword , 
 and five knife , and one man have a shield . 

 yet though these be clothe and armed , yet in some wise be they the 
 bad of all ; they be so timorous and cringing , and most of they 
 heavy - eyed and sullen and down - look . many of they have be 
 grievously mishandle : one man have have his left hand smite off ; 
 another be dock of three of his toe , and the gristle of his nose 
 slit up ; one be halt , and four have be ear - cropped , nor do any 
 lack weal of whipping . of the silver - dale new - comer the three man 
 be the bad of all the Runaways , with wild wandering eye , but 
 sullen also , and cringe if any draw nigh , and would not look anyone 
 in the face , save presently face - of - god , on whom they be soon fond 
 to fawn , as a dog on his master . but the woman who be with they , 
 and who be well - nigh as timorous as the man , be those two gaily- 
 dad one , and they be soft - handed and white - skin , save for the 
 last day of weather in the wood ; for they have be bed - thrall of 
 the Dusky Men . 

 such be the new - comer to the wood . but other have be , like 
 Dallach , month therein ; it may be say that there be eighteen of 
 these , carle and queen together . little raiment they have amongst 
 they , and some be all but stark naked , so that on these might well 
 be see as on Dallach the mark of old stripe , and of these also 
 be there man who have be shorn of some member or other , and they 
 be all burn and blacken by the weather of the woodland ; yet for 
 all their nakedness , they bear themselves bolder and more manlike 
 than the later comer , nor do they altogether lack weapon take 
 from their foeman , and most of they have some edge - tool or another . 
 of these folk be four from silver - dale , though Dallach know it not . 

 besides these be a half score and one who have be year in the 
 wood instead of month ; weather - beat indeed be these , shaggy and 
 rough - skinned like wild man of kind . some of they have make 
 themselves skin breech or clout , some go stark naked ; of weapon 
 of the Dale have they few , but they bear bow of hazel or wych - elm 
 string with deer - gut , and shaft head with flint stone ; stave 
 also of the same fashion , and great club of oak or holly : some of 
 they also have make they target of skin and willow - twig , for these 
 be the warrior of the Runaways : they have a few steel knife 
 amongst they , but have mostly learn the craft of use sharp flint 
 for knife : but four of these be woman . 

 three of these man be of the kindred of the Wolf from silver - dale , 
 and have be in the wood for hard upon ten year , and wild as they 
 be , and without hope of meet their fellow again , they go 
 proudly and boldly amongst the other , overtop they by the head 
 and more . for the great part of these man be somewhat short of 
 stature , though by nature strong and stout of body . 

 it must be tell that though Dallach have thus get all these many 
 Runaways together , yet have they not be dwell together as one 
 folk ; for they durst not , lest the Dusky Men should hear thereof and 
 fall upon they , but they have keep themselves as well they could in 
 cave and in brake three together or two , or even fare alone as 
 Dallach do : only as he be a strong and stout - hearted man , he go 
 to and fro and wander about more than the other , so that he 
 foregather with most of they and know they . he say also that he 
 doubt not but that there be more Runaways in the wood , but these 
 be all he could come at . diver who have flee have die from time to 
 time , and some have be catch and cruelly slay by their master . 
 they be none of they old ; the old , say Dallach , scant of forty 
 winter , though many from their aspect might have be old enough . 

 so face - of - god look and beheld all these poor people ; and say to 
 himself , that he might well have dread that sight . for here be he 
 bring face to face with the Sorrow of the Earth , whereof he have 
 know nought heretofore , save it might be as a tale in a minstrel 's 
 song . and when he think of the minute that have make the hour , 
 and the hour that have make the day that these man have pass 
 through , his heart fail he , and he be dumb and might not speak , 
 though he perceive that the man of Burgdale look for speech from 
 he ; but he wave his hand to his folk , and they understand he , for 
 they have hear Dallach say that some of they be cry for victual . 
 so they set to work and dighte for they such meat as they have , and 
 they set they down on the grass and make themselves their carver and 
 serve - man , and bid they eat what they would of such as there be . 
 yet , indeed , it grieve the Burgdalers again to note how these folk 
 be drive to eat ; for they themselves , though they be merry folk , 
 be exceed courteous at table , and of great observance of 
 manner : whereas these poor Runaways eat , some of they like hungry 
 dog , and some hide their meat as if they fear it should be take 
 from they , and some cower over it like falcon , and scarce any 
 with a manlike pleasure in their meal . and , their eat over , the 
 more part of they sit dull and mopish , and as if all thing be 
 forget for the time present . 

 albeit presently Dallach bestir he and say to face - of - god : 
 ' Lord of the Earl - folk , if I might give thee rede , it be good to 
 turn your face to Burgdale without more tarrying . for we be over- 
 nigh to rose - dale , be but thus many in company . but when we come 
 to our next resting - place , then shall bring thee to speech with the 
 last - comer from silver - dale ; for there they talk with the tongue of 
 the kindred ; but we of Rose - dale for the more part talk otherwise ; 
 though in my house it come down from father to son . ' 

 ' yea , ' say face - of - god , gaze still on that unhappy folk , as they 
 sit or lie upon the grass at rest for a little while : but he - seem 
 as he gaze that some memory of past time stir in some of they ; 
 for some , they hang their head and the tear steal out of their eye 
 and roll down their cheek . but those old Runaways of Silver- 
 dale be not crouch down like most of the other , but stride up 
 and down like beast in a den ; yet be the tear on the face of one 
 of these . then face - of - god constrain himself , and spake to the 
 folk , and say : ' we be now over - nigh to our foe of rose - dale to 
 lie here any long , be too few to fall upon they . we will come 
 hither again with a host when we have duly question these man who 
 have seek refuge with we : and let we call yonder height the Burg 
 of the Runaways , and it shall be a landmark for we when we be on the 
 road to rose - dale . ' 

 then the Burgdalers bid the Runaways courteously and kindly to arise 
 and take the road with they ; and by that time be their man all come 
 in ; and four of they have venison with they , which be needful , if 
 they be to eat that night or the morrow , as the guest have eat 
 they to the bone . 

 so they tarry no more , but set out on the homeward way ; and face- 
 of - god bade Dallach walk beside he , and ask he such concern 
 rose - dale and its Dusky Men . Dallach tell he that these be not so 
 many as they be masterful , not be above eight hundred of man , 
 all fight - man . as to woman , they have none of their own race , but 
 lie with the Daleswomen at their will , and begat child of they ; 
 and all or most of the say child favour the race of their 
 begetter . of the man - child they rear most , but the women- 
 child they slay at once ; for they value not woman of their own 
 blood : but besides the woman of the Dale , they would go at while in 
 band to the edge of the plain and beguile wayfarer , and bring back 
 with they thence woman to be their bed - thrall ; albeit some of these 
 be buy with a price from the Westland man . 

 as to the number of the folk of Rose - dale , its own folk , he say they 
 would number some five thousand soul , one with another ; of whom some 
 thousand might be fit to bear arm if they have the heart thereto , as 
 they have none . yet be closely question , he deem that they 
 might fall on their master from behind , if battle be join . 

 he say that the folk of Rose - dale have be a goodly folk before they 
 be enthral , and peaceable with one another , but that now it be 
 a sport of the Dusky Men to set a match between their thrall to 
 fight it out with sword and buckler or otherwise ; and the vanquish 
 man , if he be not sore hurt , they would scourge , or shear some 
 member from he , or even slay he outright , if the match between the 
 owner be so make . and many other sad and grievous tale he tell 
 to face - of - god , more than need be tell again ; so that the war - leader 
 go along sorry and angry , with his tooth set , and his hand on the 
 sword - hilt . 

 thus they go till night fall on they , and they could scarce see the 
 sign they have make on their outward journey . then they make stay in 
 a little valley , have set a watch duly ; and since they be by this 
 time far from Rose - dale , and be a great company as regard 
 scatter band of the foe , they light their fire and cook their 
 venison , and make good cheer to the Runaways , and so go to sleep in 
 the wild - wood . 

 when morning be come they gat they at once to the road ; and if the 
 Burgdalers be eager to be out of the wood , their eagerness be as

 easy now , and deem every minute lose unless they be wend on to

 ground , whereas they have not set out on their road till afternoon 
 yesterday . 

 Howsoever , they rest at noontide , and face - of - god bade Dallach

 silver - dale in early day . so Dallach bring they to he ; but he 
 find that though they spake the tongue , they be so few - speak from 
 wildness and loneliness , at least at first , that nought could come 
 from they that be not drag from they . 

 these man say that they have be in the wood more than nine year , 
 so that they know but little of the condition of the Dale in that 
 present day . however , as to what Dallach have say concern the 
 Dusky Men , they strengthen his word ; and they say that the Dusky 
 Men take no delight save in behold torment and misery , and that 
 they doubt if they be man or troll . they say that since they 
 have dwell in the wood they have slay not a few of the foeman , 
 waylay they as occasion serve , but that in this warfare they have 
 lose two of their fellow . when face - of - god ask they of their 
 deeming of the number of the Dusky Men , they say that before those 
 band have break into Rose - dale , they count they , as far as they 
 could call to mind , at about three thousand man , all warrior ; and 
 that somewhat less than one thousand have go up into Rose - dale , and 
 some have die , and many have be cast away in the wild - wood , their 
 fellow know not how . yet have not their number in silver - dale 
 diminish ; because two year after they   the speaker   have flee , 
 come three more Dusky Companies or Tribes into silver - dale , and each 
 of these tribe be of three long hundred ; and with their come have 
 the cruelty and misery much increase in the Dale , so that the 
 thrall begin to die fast ; and that drave the Dusky Men beyond the 
 border of silver - dale , so that they fall upon Rose - dale . when ask 
 how many of the kindred might yet be abide in silver - dale , their 
 face cloud , and they seem exceed wroth , and answer , that 
 they would willingly hope that most of those that have not be slay 
 at the time of the overthrow be now dead , yet indeed they fear 
 there be yet some alive , and mayhappen not a few woman . 

 by then must they get on foot again , and so the talk fall between 
 they ; but when they make stay for the night , after they have do

 late - come folk from Silver - dale , and he bring to he the man and

 if those of the Earl - folk have be few - speak from fierceness and 
 wildness , he be no less so from mere dulness and weariness of 
 misery ; but the woman 's tongue go glibly enough , and it seem to 
 pleasure she to talk about her past misery . as aforesaid , she be 
 well clothe than most of those of rose - dale , and indeed might be 
 call gaily clothe , and where her raiment be befoul or rent , it be 
 from the roughness of the wood and its weather , and not from the 
 thralldom . she be a young and fair woman , black - haired and grey-

 as she might , and have pluck some fumitory , wherewith she have make a 
 garland for her head . she sit down on the grass in front of face - of- 
 god , while the man her mate stand lean against a tree and look

 story , and look kindly on the twain . she smile on they , but 
 especially on face - of - god , and say : 

 ' Thou hast send for I , lord , and I wot well thou wouldst hear my 
 tale shortly , for it would be long to tell if I be to tell it 
 fully , and bring into it all that I have endure , which have be 
 bitter enough , for all that ye see I smooth of skin and well - liking 
 of body . I have be the bed - thrall of one of the chieftain of the 
 Dusky Men , at whose house many of their great man would assemble , so 
 that ye may ask I whatso ye will ; as I have hear much talk and may 
 call it to mind . now if ye ask I whether I have flee because of the 
 shame that I , a free woman come of free folk , should be a mere thrall 
 in the bed of the foe of my kin , and with no price pay for I , I 
 must need say it be not so ; since over long have we of the Dale be 
 thrall to be ashamed of such a matter . and again , if ye deem that I 
 have flee because I have be burden with grievous toil and be 
 drive thereto by the whip , ye may look on my hand and my body and 
 ye will see that I have toil little therewith : nor again do I 
 flee because I could not endure a few stripe now and again ; for such 
 usage do thrall look for , even when they be delicately keep for the 
 sake of the fairness of their body , and this they may well endure ; 
 yea also , and the mere fear of death by torment now and again . but 
 before I lie death both assured and horrible ; so I take mine own 
 counsel , and tell none for fear of bewrayal , save he who guard I ; 
 and that be this man ; who flee not from fear , but from love of I , 
 and to he I have give all that I might give . so we get out of the 
 house and down the Dale by night and cloud , and hide for one whole day 
 in the Dale itself , where I tremble and fear , so that I deem I 
 should die of fear ; but this man be well pleased with my company , 
 and with the lack of toil and beat even for the day . and in the 
 night again we flee and reach the wild - wood before dawn , and well- 
 nigh fall into the hand of those who be hunt we , and have 
 outgone we the day before , as we lie hid . well , what be to say ? 
 they see we not , else have we not be here , but scatter piece - meal 
 over the land . this carle know the pass of the wood , because he 
 have follow his master therein , who be a great hunter in the 
 waste , contrary to the will not of these man , and he have lie a night on 
 the burg yonder ; therefore he bring I thither , because he know 
 that thereabout be plenty of prey easy to take , and he have a bow 
 with he ; and there we fall in with other of our folk who have flee 
 before , and with Dallach ; who e'en now tell we what be hard to 
 believe , that there be a fair young man like one of the Gods lead 
 a band of goodly warrior , and seek for we to bring we into a 
 peaceful and happy land ; and this man would not have go with he 
 because he fear that he might fall into thralldom of other folk , 
 who would take I away from he ; but for I , I say I would go in any 
 case , for I be weary of the wood and its roughness and toil , and 
 that if I have a new master he would scarcely be bad than my old one 
 be at his good , and he I could endure . so I go , and glad and 
 glad I be , whatever ye will do with I . and now will I answer whatso 
 ye may ask of I . ' 

 she lay her limb together daintily and look fondly on face - of- 
 god , and the carle scowl at she somewhat at first , but presently , 
 as he watch she , his face smooth itself out of its wrinkle . 

 but face - of - god ponder a little while , and then ask the woman if

 ' I pray thee , sister , be truthful in thine answer , for somewhat lieth 
 on it . ' 

 she say : ' how could I speak aught but the sooth to thee , o lovely

 you , but ye be fool , and know nothing ; we be go to conquer a 
 new land where the woman be plenty , and far fair than ye be ; and 
 we shall leave you to fare afield like the other thrall , or work in 
 the digging of silver ; and belike ye wot what that meaneth . also he 
 say that they would leave we to the new tribe of their folk , far 
 wilder than they , whom they look for in the Dale in about a moon 's

 talk would we hear whenever it please any of they to mock we their 
 bed - thrall . now , my sweet lord , this be nought but the very sooth . ' 

 again spake face - of - god after a while : 

 ' tell I , sister , hast thou hear of any of the Dusky Men be slay 
 in the wood ? ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , and turn pale therewith and catch her breath as 
 one choke ; but say in a little while : 

 ' this alone be it hard for I to tell thee amongst all the I grief 
 I have bear , whereof I might have tell thee many tale , and will do 
 one day if thou wilt suffer it ; but fear make this hard for I . for 
 in very sooth this be the cause of my fleeing , that my master be 
 bring in slain by an arrow in the wood ; and he be to be bear to 
 bale and burn in three day ' wear ; and we three bed - thrall of 
 his , and three of the good of the man - thrall , be to be burn 
 quick on his bale - fire after sore torment ; therefore I flee , and hide 
 a knife in my bosom , that I might not be take alive ; but sweet be 
 life to I , and belike I should not have smite myself . ' 

 and she weep sore for pity of herself before they all . but face - of- 
 god say : 

 ' know thou , sister , by whom the man be slay ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , still sob ; ' but I hear nought thereof , nor have 
 I note it in my terror . the death of other , who be slay before 
 he , and the loss of many , we know not how , make they more bitterly 
 cruel with we . ' 

 and again be she weep ; but face - of - god say kindly to she : ' weep 
 no more , sister , for now shall all thy trouble be over ; I feel in my 
 heart that we shall overcome these felon , and make an end of they , 
 and there then be Burgdale for thee in its length and breadth , or 
 thine own Dale to dwell in freely . ' 

 ' Nay , ' she say , ' never will I go back thither ! ' and she turn round 
 to he and kiss his foot , and then arise and turn a little toward 
 her mate ; and the carle catch she by the hand and lead she away , and 
 seem glad so to do . 

 so once again they fall asleep in the wood , and again the next 
 morning fare on their way early that they might come into Burgdale 
 before nightfall . when they stay a while at noontide and eat , 
 face - of - god again have talk with the Runaways , and this time with 
 those of rose - dale , and he hear much the same story from they that 
 he have hear before , tell in diver way , till his heart be sick

 on this last day face - of - god lead his man well athwart the wood , so

 down into the Dale some four hour after noon on a bright day of 
 latter March . at the ingate to the Dale he find watch set , the

 face 's company have fall in with a band of the Felons three score in

 run for the tangled thicket : of the Burgdalers have two be slay 
 and five hurt in this battle . 

 as for red - coat 's company , they have fall in with no foeman . 



 CHAPTER XXIX . they bring the runaway to burgstead 



 so now be out of the wood , they go peaceably and safely along 
 the Portway , the Runaways mingle with the Dalesmen . Strange show 
 amidst the health and wealth of the Dale the rag and misery and 
 nakedness of the thrall , like a dream amidst the trim gaiety of 
 spring ; and whomsoever they meet , or come up with on the road , whatso 
 his business might be , could not refrain himself from follow they , 
 but mingle with the man - at - arm , and ask they of the tiding ; and 
 when they hear who these poor people be , even deliver thrall of 
 the Foemen , they be glad at heart and cry out for joy ; and many 
 of the woman , nay , of the man also , when they first come across that 
 misery from out the heart of their own pleasant life , weep for pity 
 and love of the poor folk , now at last set free , and bless the 
 sword that should do the like by the whole people . 

 they go slowly as man begin to gather about they ; yea , some of the 
 good folk that live hard by must need fare home to their house to 
 fetch cake and wine for the guest ; and they make they sit down and 
 rest on the green grass by the side of the Portway , and eat and drink 
 to cheer their heart ; other , woman and young swain , while they 
 rest go down into the meadow and pluck of the spring flower , 
 and twin they hastily with deft and well - wont finger into chaplet 
 and garland for their head and body . thus indeed they cover 
 their nakedness , till the lower face and weather - beat skin of 
 those hardly - entreat thrall look grimly out from amidst the 
 knot of cowslip and oxlip , and the branch of the milk - white 
 blackthorn bloom , and the long trumpet of the daffodil , of the hue 
 that wrappeth round the quill which the webster take in hand when 
 she would pleasure her soul with the sight of the yellow grow upon 
 the dark green web . 

 so they go on again as the evening be wan , and when they be 
 get within a furlong of the Gate , lo ! there be come the 
 minstrelsy , the pipe and the tabor , the fiddle and the harp , and the 
 folk that have learn to sing the sweetest , both man and woman , and 
 Redesman at the head of they all . 

 then fall the throng into an order company ; first go the music , 
 and then a score of face - of - god 's warrior with draw sword and 
 uplifted spear ; and then the flower - bedeck misery of the Runaways , 
 man and woman go together , gaunt , befoul , and hollow - eye , with 
 here and there a flushed cheek or gleam eye , or tear - bedew face , 
 as the joy and triumph of the eve pierce through their wonte 
 weariness of grief ; then the rest of the warrior , and lastly the 
 mingle crowd of Dalesfolk , tall man and fair woman gaily array , 
 clean - faced , clear - skin , and sleek - haired , with glance eye and 
 ruddy lip . 

 and now Redesman turn about to the music and draw his bow across 
 his fiddle , and the other bow run out in concert , and the harp 
 follow the story of they , and he lift up his voice and sing the 
 word of an old song , and all the singer join he and blend 
 their voice with his . and these be some of the word which they 
 sing : 


 Lo ! here be Spring , and all we be live , 
  we that be wan with Winter 's fear ; 
 reach out your hand to her hand that be give , 
  Lest ye lose her love and the light of the year . 

 many a morn do we wake to sorrow , 
  when low on the land the cloud - wrath lay ; 
 many an eve we fear to - morrow , 
  the unbegun unfinishe day . 

 ah we - we hop not , and thou wert tardy ; 
  Nought wert thou helping ; nought we pray . 
 where be the eager heart , the hardy ? 
  where be the sweet - voice unafraid ? 

 but now thou lovest , now thou leadest , 
  where be go the grief of our mind ? 
 what be the word of the tale , that thou heed 
  e'en as the breath of the bygone wind ? 

 Green and green be thy garment grow 
  over thy blossoming limb beneath ; 
 up o'er our foot rise the blade of thy sowing , 
  Pierced be our heart with thine odorous breath . 

 but where art thou wending , thou new - comer ? 
  hurry on to the Courts of the Sun ? 
 where art thou now in the House of the Summer ? 
  tell be thy day and thy deed be do . 

 Spring have be here for we that be live 
  after the day of Winter 's fear ; 
 here in our hand be the wealth of her giving , 
  the Love of the Earth , and the Light of the Year . 


 thus come they to the Gate , and lo ! the Bride thereby , lean 
 against a buttress , gaze with no dull eye at the come throng . 
 she be now clothe in her woman 's attire again , to wit a light flame- 
 coloured gown over a green kirtle ; but she yet bear a gild helm on 
 her head and a sword girt to her side in token of her oath to the 
 God . she have be in Hall - face 's company in that last battle , and 
 have do a man 's service there , fight very valiantly , but have not 
 be hurt , and have come back to Burgstead when the shift of man be .

 look forth on the throng , and when her eye beheld the Runaways 
 amidst of the weapone carle of Burgdale , her face flush , and her

 and on the dainty cloth and trim broidery of her sleeve ; and she

 nostril ; and therewith she call to mind the aspect of her lovely 
 body , as while she have see it image , all its full measure , in the
