 Westland mirror . she think also with what joy she draw the breath 
 of life , yea , even amidst of grief , and of how sweet and pure and 
 well - nurture she be , and how well beloved of many friend and the 
 whole folk , and she set all this beside those woeful body and

 so come the Burgdalers and their guest into the street of Burgstead

 CHAPTER XXX . HALL - FACE goeth toward ROSE - DALE 



 on the morrow face - of - god take counsel with Hall - face and stone - face 
 as to what be good to be do , and they sit on the dais in the Hall 
 to talk it over . 

 Short be the time that have wear since that day in Shadowy Vale , for 
 it be but eight day since then ; yet so many thing have befall in 
 that time , and , to speak shortly , the outlook for the Burgdalers have 
 change so much , that the time seem long to all the three , and 
 especially to face - of - god . 

 it be yet twenty day till the Great Folk - mote should beholden , and 
 to Hall - face the time seem long enough to do somewhat , and he 
 deem it be good to gather force and fall on the Dusky Men in 
 rose - dale , since now they have get man who could lead they the 
 nighest way and by the safe pass , and who know all the way of 
 the foeman . but to stone - face this rede seem not so good ; for they 
 would have to go and come back , and fight and conquer , in less time 
 than twenty day , or be belate of the Folk - mote , and meanwhile much 
 might happen . 

 ' for , ' say stone - face , ' we may deem the fighting - man of Rose - dale to 
 be little less than one thousand , and however we fall on they , even 
 if it be unawares at first , they shall fight stubbornly ; so that we 
 may not send against they many less than they be , and that shall 
 strip Burgdale of its fighting - man , so that whatever befall , we that 
 be leave shall have to bide at home . ' 

 now be face - of - god of the same mind as stone - face ; and he say 
 moreover : ' when we go to Rose - dale we must abide there a while 
 unless we be overthrow . for if ye conquer it and come away at once , 
 presently shall the tiding come to the ear of the Dusky Men in 
 silver - dale , and they shall join themselves to those of rose - dale who 
 have flee before you , and between they they shall destroy the unhappy 
 people therein ; for ye can not take they all away with you : and that 
 shall they do all the more now , when they look to have new thrall in 
 Burgdale , both man and woman . and this we may not suffer , but must 
 abide till we have meet all our foeman and have overcome they , so that 
 the poor folk there shall be safe from they till they have learn 
 how to defend their dale . now my rede be , that we send out the War- 
 arrow at once up and down the Dale , and to the Shepherds and 
 Woodlanders , and appoint a day for the Muster and Weapon - show of all 
 our Folk , and that day to be the day before the Spring Market , that 
 be to say , four day before the Great Folk - mote , and meantime that we 
 keep sure watch about the border of the wood , and now and again scour 
 the wood , so as to clear the Dale of their wandering band . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Hall - face ; ' and I pray thee , brother , let I have an 
 hundred of man and thy Dallach , and let we go somewhat deep into the 
 wood towards rose - dale , and see what we may come across ; peradventure 
 it might be something well than hart or wild - swine . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' I see no harm therein , if Dallach goeth with thee 
 freely ; for I will have no force put on he or any other of the 
 Runaways . yet meseemeth it be not ill for thee to find the road to 
 rose - dale ; for I have it in my mind to send a company thither to give 
 those Rose - dale man - queller somewhat to do at home when we fall upon 
 silver - dale . therefore go find Dallach , and get thy man together at 
 once ; for the soon thou art go on thy way the well . but this I 
 bid thee , go no furth than three day out , that ye may be back home 
 betime . ' 

 at this word hall - face 's eye gleam with joy , and he go out from 
 the Hall straightway and seek Dallach , and find he at the Gate . 
 iron - face have give he a new sword , a good one , and have bid he 
 call it thicket - clear , and he would not leave it any moment of the 
 day or night , but would lay it under his pillow at night as a child 
 do with a new toy ; and now he be lean against a buttress and 
 draw the say sword half out of the scabbard and pore over its 
 blade , which be indeed fair enough , be work with dark grey 
 wave line like the eddy of the Weltering Water . 

 so hall - face greet he , and smile and say : 

 ' Guest , if thou wilt , thou may'st take that new blade of my father 's 
 work which thou lov so , a journey which shall rejoice it . ' 

 ' yea , ' say Dallach , ' I suppose that thou would fare on thy 
 brother 's footstep , and deemest that I be the man to lead thee on 
 the road , and even far than he go ; and though it might be 
 think by some that I have see enough of rose - dale and the part 
 thereabout for one while , yet will I go with thee ; for now be I a man 
 again , body and soul . ' 

 and therewith he draw thicket - clear right out of his sheath and 
 wave he in the air . and Hall - face be glad of he and say he be 
 well apaid of his help . so they go away together to gather man , 
 and on the morrow Hall - face depart and go into the wild - wood with 
 Dallach and an hundred and two score man . 

 but as for face - of - god , he fare up and down the Dale follow the 
 War - arrow , and go into all house , and talk with the folk , both 
 young and old , man and woman , and tell they closely all that have 
 betid and all that be like to betide ; and he be well pleased with 
 that which he see and hear ; for all take his word well , and be 
 nought afeard or dismay by the tiding ; and he see that they would 
 not hang aback . meantime the day wear , and Hall - face come not back 
 till the seventh day , and he bring with he twelve more Runaways , 
 of whom five be woman . but he have lose four man , and have with he 
 Dallach and five other of the dalesman bear upon litter sore hurt ; 
 and this be his story : 

 they get to the Burg of the Runaways on the forenoon of the third 
 day , and thereby come on five carle of the Runaways - man who have 
 miss meeting Dallach that other day , but know what have be do ; 
 for one of they have be sick and could not come with he , and he have 
 tell the other : so now they be hang about the Burg of the 
 Runaways hop somewhat that he might come again ; and they meet the 
 Burgdalers full of joy , and bring they trout that they have catch 
 in the river . 

 as for the other runaway , namely , five woman and two more carle - 
 they have get they close to the entrance into silver - dale , where by 
 night and cloud they come on a campment of the Dusky Men , who be 
 lead home these seven poor wretch , runaway whom they have 
 catch , that they might slay they most evilly in Rose - stead . so 
 Hall - face fall on the Dusky Men , and deliver their captive , but 
 slew not all the foe , and they that flee bring pursuer on they who 
 come up with they the next day , so near be rose - dale , though they 
 make all diligence homeward . the Burgdalers must need turn and 
 fight with those pursuer , and at last they drave they aback so that 
 they might go on their way home . they let not the grass grow 
 beneath their foot thereafter , till they be assure by meet a 
 band of the Woodlanders , who have go forth to help they , and with 
 whom they rest a little . but neither so be they quite do with 
 the foeman , who come upon they next day a very many : these however 
 they and the Woodlanders , who be all fresh and unwounded and very 
 valiant , speedily put to the bad ; and so they come on to Burgstead , 
 leave those of they who be sorest hurt to be tend by the 
 Woodlanders at Carlstead , who , as might be look for , deal with they 
 very lovingly . 

 it be in the first fight that they suffer that loss of slain and 
 wound ; and therein the newly deliver thrall fight valiantly 
 against their master : as for Dallach , it be no marvel , say Hall- 
 face , that he be hurt ; but rather a marvel that he be not slain , so 
 little he recke of point and edge , if he might but slay the foeman . 

 such be Hall - face's - tale ; and face - of - god deem that he have do 
 unwisely to let he go that journey ; for the slaying of a few Dusky 
 Men be but a light gain to set against the loss of so many 
 Burgdalers ; yet be he glad of the deliverance of those Runaways , and 
 deem it a gain indeed . but henceforth would he hold all still till 
 he should have tiding of Folk - might ; so nought be do thereafter 
 save the warding of the Dale , from the country of the Shepherds to 
 the Waste above the Eastern pass . 

 but face - of - god himself go up amongst the Shepherds , and abode with 
 a goodman hight Hound - under - Greenbury , who gather to he the folk 
 from the country - side , and they go up on to Greenbury , and sit on 
 the green grass while he speak with they and tell they , as he have 
 tell the other , what have be do and what should be do . and 
 they hear he gladly , and he deem that there would be no blenching 
 in they , for they be all in one tale to live and die with their 
 friend of Burgdale , and they say that they would have no other word 
 save that to bear to the Great Folk - mote . 

 so he go away well - pleased , and he fare on thence to the 
 Woodlanders , and gueste at the house of a valiant man hight 
 Wargrove , who on the morrow morn call the folk together to a green 
 lawn of the wild - wood , so that there be scarce a soul of they that 
 be not there . then he lay the whole matter before they ; and if the 
 Dalesmen have be merry and ready , and the Shepherds stout - hearted 
 and friendly , yet be the Wood - lander more eager still , so that 
 every hour seem long to they till they stand in their war - gear ; and 
 they tell he that now at last be the hour draw nigh which they 
 have dream of , but have scarce dare to hope for , when the lose way 
 should be find , and the crooked make straight , and that which have 
 be break should be mend ; that their meat and drink , and sleep 
 and wake , and all that they do be now become to they but the 
 mean of living till the day be come whereon the two remnant of the 
 child of the Wolf should meet and become one folk to live or die 
 together . 

 then go face - of - god back to Burgstead again , and as he stand anigh 
 the thing - stead once more , and look down on the Dale as he have 
 behold it last autumn , he bethink he that with all that have be 
 do and all that have be promise , the earth be clear of her 
 trouble , and that now there be nought betwixt he and the happy day 
 of life which the Dale should give to the dweller therein , save the 
 gathering host of the battle - field and the day when the last word 
 should be speak and the first stroke smitten . so he go down on to 
 the Portway well content . 

 thereafter till the day of the Weapon - show there be nought to tell 
 of , save that Dallach and the other wounded man begin to grow whole 
 again ; and all man sit at home , or go on the woodland ward , 
 expect great tiding after the holding of the Folk - mote . 



 CHAPTER XXXI . of the WEAPON - SHOW of the MEN of BURGDALE and their 
 neighbour 



 now on the day appoint for the Weapon - show come the Folk flock - meal 
 to the great and wide meadow that be cleave by wildlake as it run to 
 join the Weltering Water . early in the morning , even before sunrise , 
 have the wain full of woman and child begin to come thither . also 
 there come little horse and ass from the Shepherd country with one 
 or two or three damsel or child sit on each , and by wain - side 
 or by beast strode the man of the house , merry and fair in their war- 
 gear . the Woodlanders , moreover , man and woman , eld and swain and 
 young damsel , stream out of the wood from Carlstead , eager to make 
 the day begin before the sunrise , and end before his setting . 

 then all man fall to pitching of tent and tilt over of wain ; for 
 the April sun be hot in the Dale , and when he arise the mead be 
 gay with more than the spring flower ; for the tent and the tilt 
 be stain and broider with many colour , and there be none who 
 have not furbish up his war - gear so that all shone and glitter . 
 and many wear gay surcoat over their armour , and the woman be clothe 
 in all their bravery , and the Houses mostly of a suit ; for one bore 
 blue and another corn - colour , and another green , and another brazil , 
 and so forth , and all gleam and glow with broidery of gold and 
 bright hue . but the woman of the Shepherds be all clothe in white , 
 embroider with green bough and red blossom , and the Woodland 
 woman wear dark red kirtle . moreover , the woman have set garland of 
 flower on their head and the helm of the man , and for the most 
 part they be slim of body and tall and light - limbed , and as dainty 
 to look upon as the willow - bough that wave on the brook - side . 

 Thither have the goodman who be gueste the Runaways bring their 
 guest , even now much better by their new soft day ; and much the 
 poor folk marvel at all this joyance , and they scarce know where 
 they be ; but to some it bring back to their mind day of joyance 
 before the thralldom and all that they have lose , so that their heart 
 be heavy a while , till they see the warrior of the kindred 
 streaming into the mead and bethink they why they carry steel . 

 now by then the sun be fully up there be a great throng on the 
 Portway , and this be the folk of the Burg on their way to the 
 Weapon - mead . the man - at - arm be in the midst of the throng , and at 
 the head of they be the war - leader , with the banner of the face 
 before he , wherein be do the image of the God with the ray - ring 
 head . but at the rearward of the warrior go the Alderman and the 
 Burg - warden , before whom be bear the banner of the Burg picture 
 with the Gate and its tower ; but in the midst betwixt those two be 
 the banner of the Steer , a white beast on a green field . 

 so when the Dale - warden who be down in the meadow hear the music 
 and beheld who be come , they bid the company of the Dale and 
 the Shepherds and the Woodlanders who be down there to pitch their 
 banner in a half circle about the ingle of the meadow which be make 
 by the stream of Wildlake and the Weltering Water , and gather to 
 they to be order there under their leader of score and half- 
 hundred and hundred ; and even so they do . but the banner of the 
 Dale without the Burg be the Bridge , and the Bull , and the Vine , 
 and the Sickle . and the Shepherds have three banner , to wit 
 Greenbury , and the Fleece , and the Thorn . 

 as for the Woodlanders , they say that they be abide their great 
 banner , but it should come in good time ; ' and meantime , ' say they , 
 ' here be the war - token that we shall fight under ; for they be good 
 enough banner for we poor man , the remnant of the valiant of time 
 past . ' therewith they show two great spear , and athwart the one 
 be tie an arrow , its point dip in blood , its feather sing 
 with fire ; and they say , ' this be the banner of the War - shaft . ' 

 on the other spear there be nought ; but the head thereof be great 
 and long , and they have so burnish the steel that the sun smite out 
 a ray of light from it , so that it might be see from afar . and they 
 say : ' this be the Banner of the Spear ! down yonder where the 
 raven be gather ye shall see a banner fly over we . there 
 shall fall many a mother 's son . ' 

 smile the dale - warden , and say that these be good banner to 
 fight under ; and those that stand nearby shout for the valiancy of 
 the Woodland Carles . 

 now the Dale - warden go to the entrance from the Portway to the 
 meadow , and there meet the Men of the Burg , and two of they go one 
 on either side of the war - leader to show he to his seat , and the 
 other abode till the Alderman and Burg - warden come up , and then 
 join themselves to they , and the horn blow up both in the meadow 
 and on the road , and the new - comer go their way to their 
 appoint place amidst the shout of the Dalesmen ; and the woman and 
 child and old man from the Burg follow after , till all the mead 
 be cover with bright raiment and glitter gear , save within the 
 ring of man at the further end . 

 so come the war - leader to his seat of green turf raise in the ingle 
 aforesaid ; and he stand beside it till the Alderman and Wardens have 
 take their place on a seat behind he raise higher than his ; below 
 he on the step of his seat sit the Scrivener with his pen and ink- 
 horn and scroll of parchment , and man have bring he a smooth shield 
 whereon to write . 

 on the left side of face - of - god stand the man of the face all 
 glitter in their arm , and amongst they be Wolf - stone and his 
 two fellow , but Dallach be not yet whole of his hurt . on his 
 right be the folk of the House of the Steer : the leader of that 
 House be an old white - bearded man , grandfather of the Bride , for her 
 father be dead ; and who but the Bride herself stand beside he in 
 her glorious war - gear , look as if she be new come from the City 
 of the Gods , think most man ; but those who behold she closely 
 deem that she look heavy - eyed and haggard , as if she be aweary . 
 nevertheless , wheresoever she pass , and whosoever look on she 
  and all man look on she , there arise a murmur of praise and love ; 
 and the woman , and especially the young one , say how fair her deed 
 be , and how meet she be for it ; and some of they be for do on 
 war - gear and fare to battle with the carle ; and of these some be 
 sober and solemn , as be well see afterwards , and some spake 
 lightly : some also fall to boasting of how they could run and climb 
 and swim and shoot in the bow , and fall to baring of their arm to 
 show how strong they be : and indeed they be no weakling , though 
 their arm be fair . 

 there then stand the ring of man , each company under its banner ; and 
 beyond they stand the woman and child and man unmeet for battle ; 
 and beyond they again the tilt wain and the tent . 

 now face - of - god sit he down on the turf - seat with his bright helm on 
 his head and his naked sword across his knee , while the horn blow 
 up loudly , and when they have do , the elder of the Dale - warden 
 cry out for silence . then again arise face - of - god and say : 

 ' man of the Dale , and ye friend of the Shepherds , and ye , O valiant 
 Woodlanders ; we be not assemble here to take counsel , for in three 
 day ' time shall the Great Folk - mote be holden , whereat shall be 
 counsel enough . but since I have be appoint your Chief and War- 
 leader , till such time as the Folk - mote shall either yeasay or naysay 
 my leadership , I have send for you that we may look each other in the 
 face and number our host and behold our weapon , and see if we be 
 meet for battle and for the dealing with a great host of foeman . for 
 now no long can it be say that we be go to war , but rather 
 that war be on our border , and we be blend with it ; as many have 
 learn to their cost ; for some have be slay and some sorely hurt . 
 therefore I bid you now , all ye that be weapone , wend past we that 
 the tale of you may be take . but first let every hundred - leader and 
 half - hundred - leader and score - leader make sure that he hath his tale 
 aright , and give his word to the captain of his banner that he in 
 turn may give it out to the Scrivener with his name and the House and 
 Company that he leadeth . ' 

 so he spake and sit he adown ; and the horn blow again in token that 
 the company should go past ; and the first that come be hall - ward 
 of the House of the Steer , and the first of those that go after he 
 be the Bride , go as if she be his son . 

 so he cry out his name , and the name of his House , and say , ' an 
 hundred and a half , ' and pass forth , his man follow he in most 
 goodly array . each man be gird with a good sword and bear a long 
 heavy spear over his shoulder , save a score who bare bow ; and no man 
 lack a helm , a shield , and a coat of fence . 

 then come a goodly man of thirty winter , and stay before the 
 Scrivener and cry out : 

 ' write down the House of the Bridge of the Upper Dale at one hundred , 
 and War - well their leader . ' 

 and he stride on , and his man follow clothe and weapone like those 
 of the Steer , save that some have axis hang to their girdle 
 instead of sword ; and most bore casting - spear instead of the long 
 spear , and half a score be bowman . 

 then come Fox of Upton lead the man of the Bull of Middale , an 
 hundred and a half lack two ; very great and tall be his man , and 
 they also bear long spear , and one score and two be bowman . 

 then Fork - beard of Lea , a man well on in year , lead on the man of the 
 Vine , an hundred and a half and five man thereto ; two score of they 
 bare bow in hand and be gird with sword ; the rest bear their sword 
 naked in their right hand , and their shield   which be but small 
 buckler   hang at their back , and in the left hand each bore two 
 casting - spear . with these go two doughty woman - at - arm among the 
 bowman , tall and well - knit , already grow brown with the spring 
 sun , for their work lie among the stock of the vine on the 
 southward - look bent . 

 Next come a tall young man , yellow - haired , with a thin red beard , and 
 give himself out for Red - beard of the Knolls ; he bear his father 's 
 name , as the custom of their house be , but the old man , who have long 
 be head man of the House of the Sickle , be late dead in his bed , 
 and the young man have not see twenty winter . he bid the Scrivener 
 write the tale of the Men of the Sickle at an hundred and a half , and 
 his folk fare past the war - leader joyously , be one half of they 
 bowman ; and fall shooter they be ; the other half be gird with 
 sword , and bore withal long ashen stave armed with great blade 
 curved inward , which weapon they call heft - sax . 

 all these band , as the name and the tale of they be declare be 
 greet with loud shout from their fellow and the bystander ; but 
 now arise a great shout still , as stone - face , clothe in goodly 
 glitter array , come forth and say : 

 ' I be stone - face of the House of the Face , and I bring with I two 
 hundred of man with their good war - gear and weapon : write it down , 
 Scrivener ! ' 

 and he stride on like a young man after those who have go past , and 
 after he come the tall Hall - face and his man , a gallant sight to 
 see : two score bowman girt with sword , and the other with naked 
 sword wave aloft , and each bear two casting - spear in his left 
 hand . 

 then come a man of middle age , broad - shoulder , yellow - haired , blue- 
 eyed , of wide and ruddy countenance , and after he a goodly company ; 
 and again great be the shout that go up to the heaven ; for he 
 say : 

 ' Scrivener , write down that hound - under - Greenbury , from amongst the 
 dweller in the hill where the sheep feed , leadeth the man who go 
 under the banner of Greenbury , to the tale of an hundred and four 
 score . ' 

 Therewith he pass on , and his man follow , stout , stark , and 
 merry - faced , girt with sword , and bear over their shoulder long- 
 stave axis , and spear not so long as those which the Dalesmen bore ; 
 and they have but a half score of arrow - shot with they . 

 Next come a young man , blue - eyed also , with hair the colour of flax 
 on the distaff , broad - faced and short - nosed , low of stature , but very 
 strong - build , who cry out in a loud , cheerful voice : 

 ' I be Strongitharm of the Shepherds , and these valiant man be of the 
 Fleece and the thorn blend together , for so they would have it ; and 
 their tale be one hundred and two score and ten . ' 

 then the man of those kindred go past merry and shout , and they 
 be clothe and weapone like to they of Greenbury , but have with they a 
 score of bowman . and all these Shepherd - folk wear over their 
 hauberk white woollen surcoat broider with green and red . 

 now again uprise the cry , and there stand before the war - leader a 
 very tall man of fifty winter , dark - faced and grey - eye , and he 
 spake slowly and somewhat softly , and say : 

 ' war - leader , this be Red - wolf of the Woodlanders lead the man who 
 go under the sign of the War - shaft , to the number of an hundred and 
 two . ' 

 then he pass on , and his man after he , tall , lean , and silent 
 amidst the shouting . all these man bare bow , for they be keen 
 hunter ; each have at his girdle a little axe and a wood - knife , and 
 some have long sword withal . they wear , everyone of the carle , 
 short green surcoat over their coat of fence ; but amongst they be 
 three woman who bear like weapon to the man , but be clothe in red 
 kirtle under their hauberk , which be of good ring - mail gleaming 
 over they from throat to knee . 

 last come another tall man , but young , of twenty - five winter , and 
 spake : 

 ' Scrivener , I be Bears - bane of the Woodlanders , and these that come 
 after I wend under the sign of the Spear , and they be of the tale 
 of one hundred and seven . ' 

 and he pass by at once , and his man follow he , clothe and weapone 
 no otherwise than they of the war - shaft , and with they be two 
 woman . 

 now go all those company back to their banner , and stand there ; 
 and there arise among the bystander much talk concern the Weapon- 
 show , and who be the good array of the Houses . and of the old 
 man , some spake of past weapon - show which they have see in their 
 youth , and they set they beside this one , and praise and blame . so 
 it go on a little while till the horn blow again , and once more 
 there be silence . then arise face - of - god and say : 

 ' Men of Burgdale , and ye Shepherd - folk , and ye of the Woodland , now 
 shall ye wot how many weaponed man we may bring together for this 
 war . Scrivener , arise and give forth the tale of the company , as 
 they have be tell unto you . ' 

 then the Scrivener stand up on the turf - bench beside face - of - god , and 
 spake in a loud voice , read from his scroll : 

 ' of the Men of Burgdale there have pass by I nine hundred and 
 six ; of the Shepherds three hundred and eight and ten ; and of the 
 Woodlanders two hundred and nine ; so that all tell our man be 
 fourteen hundred and thirty and three . ' 

 now in those day man reckon by long hundred , so that the whole 
 tale of the host be one thousand , five hundred , and four score and 
 one , tell the tale in short hundred . 

 when the tale have be give forth and heard , man shout again , and 
 they rejoice that they be so many . for it exceed the reckoning 
 which the Alderman have give out at the Gate - thing . but face - of - god 
 say : 

 ' neighbour , we have hold our Weapon - show ; but now hold you ready , 
 each man , for the Hosting toward very battle ; for belike within seven 
 day shall the leader of hundred and twenty summon you to be 
 ready in arm to take whatso fortune may befall . now be sunder the 
 Weapon - show . be ye as merry to - day as your heart bid you to be . ' 

 Therewith he come down from his seat with the Alderman and the 
 Wardens , and they mingle with the good folk of the Dale and the 
 Shepherds and the Woodlanders , and merry be their converse there . 
 it yet lack an hour of noon ; so presently they fall to and feast 
 in the green meadow , drink from wain to wain and from tent to 
 tent ; and thereafter they play and sport in the mead , shoot 
 at the butt and wrestling , and try other mastery . then they 
 fall to dance one and all , and so at last to supper on the green 
 grass in great merriment . nor might you have know from the 
 demeanour of any that any threat of evil overhang the Dale . Nay , so 
 glad be they , and so friendly , that you might rather have deem 
 that this be the land whereof tale tell , wherein people die not , 
 but live for ever , without grow any old than when they first 
 come thither , unless they be bear into the land itself , and then they 
 grow into fair manhood , and so abide . in sooth , both the land and 
 the folk be fair enough to be that land and the folk thereof . 

 but a little after sunset they sunder , and some fare home ; but 
 many of they abode in the tent and tilt wain , because the morrow 
 be the first day of the Spring market : and already be some of the 
 Westland chapman come ; yea , two of they be with the bystander in 
 the meadow ; and more be look for ere the night be far spend . 



 CHAPTER XXXII . the MEN of SHADOWY VALE COME to the spring market AT 
 burgstead 



 on the morrow betime in the morning the Westland chapman , who be 
 now all come , go out from the House of the Face , where they be 
 ever will not to be lodge , and set up their booth adown the street 
 betwixt gate and bridge . Gay be the show ; for the booth be 
 tilt over with paint cloth , and the merchant themselves be 
 clothe in long gown of fine cloth ; scarlet , and blue , and white , and 
 green , and black , with broidered welt of gold and silver ; and their 
 knave be gaily attire in short coat of diver hue , with silver 
 ring about their arm , and short sword girt to their side . People 
 begin to gather about these chapman at once when they fall to open 
 their bale and their pack , and unload their wain . there have 
 they iron , both in pig and forge scrap and nail ; steel they have , 
 and silver , both in ingot and vessel ; pearl from over sea ; cinnabar 
 and other colour for staining , such as be not in the mountain : 
 madder from the marsh , and purple of the sea , and scarlet grain 
 from the holm - oak by its edge , and woad from the deep clayey field 
 of the plain ; silken thread also from the outer ocean , and rare webs 
 of silk , and jar of olive oil , and fine pottery , and scented wood , 
 and sugar of the cane . but gold they have none with they , for that 
 they take there ; and for weapon , save a few silver - gilt toy , they 
 have no market . 

 so presently they fall to chaffer ; for the carle bring they little 
 bag of the river - bear gold , so that the weight and scale be at 
 work ; other have with they scroll and tally to tell the number of 
 the beast which they have to sell , and the chapman give they ware 
 therefor without behold the beast ; for they wotte that the 
 Dalesmen lie not in chaffer . while the day be yet young withal 
 come the Dalesmen from the mid and nether Dale with their ware and 
 set up their booth ; and they have with they flask and keg of the 
 wine which they have to sell ; and bale of the good winter - weave 
 cloth , some grey , some dye , and piece of fine linen ; and blade of 
 sword , and knife , and axis of such fashion as the Westland man 
 use ; and golden cup and chain , and fair ring set with mountain-

 well of her grief . 



 CHAPTER XXXIV . the chieftain take COUNSEL in the hall of the face 



 then turn face - of - god back into the Hall , and see where iron - face 
 sit at the dai , and with he Folk - might and stone - face and the elder 
 of the Dale - warden , and Sun - beam withal ; so he go soberly up to 
 the board , and sit himself down thereat beside stone - face , over 
 against Folk - might and his father , beside whom sit the Sun - beam ; and 
 Folk - might look on he gravely , as a man powerful and trustworthy , 
 yet be his look somewhat sour . 

 then the Alderman say : ' my son , I say not to thee come back 
 presently , because I wotte that thou wouldst surely do so , know 
 that we have much to speak of . for , whatever these thy friend may 
 have do , or whatsoever thou hast do with they to grieve we , all 
 that must be set aside at this present time , since the matter in hand 
 be to save the Dale and its folk . what sayest thou hereon ? since , 
 young as thou mayst be , thou art our war - leader , and doubtless shalt 
 so be after the Folk - mote hath be holden . ' 

 face - of - god answer not hastily : indeed , as he sit think for a 
 minute or two , the fair spring day seem to darken about they or to 
 glare into the light of flame amidst the night - tide ; and the joyous 
 clamour without door seem to grow hoarse and fearful as the sound 
 of wail and shriek . but he spake firmly and simply in a clear 
 voice , and say : 

 ' there can be no two word concern what we have to aim at ; these 
 Dusky Men we must slay everyone , though we be few than they be . ' 

 folk - might smile and nod his head ; but the other sit star 
 down the hall or into the hanging . 

 then spake folk - might : ' thou wert a boy methought when I cast my 
 spear at thee last autumn , face - of - god , but now hast thou grow into 
 a man . now tell I , what deem thou we must do to slay they all ? ' 

 say face - of - god : ' once again it be clear that we must fall upon 
 they at home in Rose - dale and silver - dale . ' 

 again folk - might nod : but iron - face say : 

 ' Needeth this ? may we not ward the Dale and send many band into the 
 wood to fall upon they when we meet they ? yea , and so do these 
 our guest have already slay many , as this valiant man hath tell I 
 e'en now . will ye not slay so many at last , that they shall learn to 
 fear we , and abide at home and leave we at peace ? ' 

 but face - of - god say : ' Meseemeth , father , that this be not thy rede , 
 and that thou sayest this but to try I : and perchance ye have be 
 talk about I when I be without in the street e'en now . even if 
 it might be that we should thus cow these felon into abide at home 
 and torment their own thrall at their ease , yet how then be our 
 friend of the Wolf holpen to their own again ? and I shall tell thee 
 that I have promise to this man and this woman that I will give they 
 no less than a man 's help in this matter . moreover , I have speak in 
 every house of the Dale , and to the Shepherds and the Woodlanders , 
 and there be no man amongst they but will follow I in the quarrel . 
 furthermore , they have hear of the thralldom that be do on man no 
 great way from their own house ; yea , they have see it ; and they 
 remember the old saw , " Grief in thy neighbour 's hall be grief in thy 
 garth , " and sure it be , father , that whether thou or I gainsay they , 
 go they will to deliver the thrall of the Dusky Men , and will leave 
 we alone in the Dale . ' 

 ' this be no less than sooth , ' say the Dale - warden , ' never have man 
 go forth more joyously to a merry - making than all man of we shall 
 wend to this war . ' 

 ' but , ' say face - of - god , ' of one thing ye may be sure , that these man 
 will not abide our pleasure till we cut they all off in scattered 
 band , nor will they sit deedless at home . nor indeed may they ; for 
 we have hear from their thrall that they look to have fresh tribe 
 of they come to hand to eat their meat and waste their servant , and 
 these and they must find new abode and new thrall ; and they be now 
 warn by the overthrow and slaying that they have have at our hand 
 that we be astir , and they will not delay long , but will fall upon 
 we with all their host ; it might even be to - day or to - morrow . ' 

 say folk - might : ' in all this thou say sooth , brother of the 
 Dale ; and to cut this matter short , I will tell you all , that 
 yesterday we have with we a runaway from Silver - dale   it be overlong 
 to tell how we fall in with she ; for it be a woman . but she tell 
 we that this very moon be a new tribe come into the Dale , six long 
 hundred in number , and twice as many more be look for in two 
 eight of day , and that ere this moon hath wan , that is , in 
 twenty - four day , they will wend their way straight for Burgdale , 
 for they know the way thereto . so I say that face - of - god be right 
 in all wise . but tell I , brother , hast thou think of how we shall 
 come upon these man ? ' 

 ' how many man wilt thou lead into battle ? ' say face - of - god . 

 Folk - might reddened , and say : ' a few , a few ; maybe two - hundred all 
 tell . ' 

 ' yea , ' say face - of - god , ' but some special gain wilt thou be to we . ' 

 ' so I deem at least , ' say Folk - might . 

 say face - of - god : ' Good be that . now have we hold our Weapon - show 
 in the Dale , and we find that we together with you be sixteen long 
 hundred of man ; and the tale of the foeman that be now in Silver- 
 dale , new - comer and all , shall be three thousand or thereabout , and 
 in Rose - dale hard on a thousand . ' 

 ' scarce so many , ' say folk - might ; ' some of the felon have die ; we 
 tell over our silver arm - ring yesterday , and the tale be three 
 hundred and eighty and six . besides , they be never so many as thou 
 deemest . ' 

 ' well , ' say face - of - god , ' yet at least they shall outnumber we 
 sorely . we may scarce leave the Dale unguarde when our host be 
 go ; therefore I deem that we shall have but one thousand of man for 
 our onslaught on silver - dale . ' 

 ' how come ye to that ? ' say stone - face . 

 say face - of - god : ' abide a while , fosterer ! though the odd between 
 we be great , it be not to be hide that I wot how ye of the Wolf 
 know of privy pass into silver - dale ; yea , into the heart thereof ; 
 and this be the special gain ye have to give we . therefore we , the 
 thousand man , fall on the foe unawares , shall make a great 
 slaughter of they ; and if the murder be but grim enough , those 
 thrall of theirs shall fear we and not they , as already they hate 
 they and not we , so that we may look to they for root out these 
 sorry weed after the overthrow . and what with one thing , what with 
 another , we may cherish a good hope of clear silver - dale at one 
 stroke with the said thousand man . 

 ' there remaineth rose - dale , which will be easy to deal with , 
 because the Dusky Men therein be few and the thrall as many : 
 that also would I fall on at the same time as we fall on silver - dale 
 with the man that be leave over from the silver - dale onslaught . 
 wherefore my rede be , that we gather all those unmeet for battle in 
 the field into this Burg , with ten ten of man to strengthen they ; 
 which shall be enough for they , along with the old man , and lad , and 
 sturdy woman , to defend themselves till help come , if aught of evil 
 befall , or to flee into the mountain , or at the bad to die 
 valiantly . then let the other five hundred fare up to Rose - dale , 
 and fall on the Dusky Men therein about the same time , but not before 
 our onslaught on silver - dale : thus shall hand help foot , so that 
 stumbling be not fall ; and we may well hope that our rede shall 
 thrive . ' 

 then be he silent , and the Sun - beam look upon he with gleam 
 eye and part lip , wait eagerly to hear what Folk - might would 
 say . he hold his peace a while , drum on the board with his 
 finger , and none else spake a word . at last he say : 

 ' war - leader of Burgdale , all that thou hast speak like I well , and 
 even so must it be do , save that parting of our host and send 
 one part to fall upon Rose - dale . I say , nay ; let we put all our 
 might into that one stroke on silver - dale , and then we be undo 
 indeed if we fail ; but so shall we be if we fail anywise ; but if we 
 win silver - dale , then shall rose - dale lie open before we . ' 

 ' my brother , ' say face - of - god , ' thou art a try warrior , and I but 
 a lad : but dost thou not see this , that whatever we do , we shall not 
 at one onslaught slay all the Dusky Men of silver - dale , and those 
 that flee before we shall betake they to Rose - dale , and tell all the 
 tale , and what shall hinder they then from fall on Burgdale   since 
 they be no great way from it   after they have murder what they 
 will of the unhappy people under their hand ? ' 

 say folk - might : ' I say not but that there be a risk thereof , but in 
 war we must need run such risk , and all should be risk rather 
 than that our blow on silver - dale be light . for we be the few ; and 
 if the foeman have time to call that to mind , then be we all lose . ' 

 say stone - face : ' Meseemeth , war - leader , that there be nought much 
 to dread in leave Rose - dale to itself for a while ; for not only may 
 we follow hard on the fleer if they flee to rose - dale , and be there 
 no long time after they , before they have time to stir their host but 
 also after the overthrow we shall be free to send man back to 
 Burgdale by way of Shadowy Vale . I deem that herein Folk - might hath 
 the right of it . ' 

 ' even so say I , ' say the Alderman ; ' besides , we might theft leave 
 more folk behind we for the warding of the Dale . so , son , the risk 
 whereof thou speak groweth the less the long it be look on . ' 

 then spake the Dale - warden : ' yet save your wisdom , Alderman , the 
 risk be there yet . for if these felon come into the Dale at all , 
 even if the folk leave behind hold the Burg and keep themselves 
 unmurdered , yet may they not hinder the foe from spoil our 
 homestead ; so that our folk come back in triumph shall find ruin 
 at home , and spend weary day in hunt their foeman , who shall , 
 many of they , escape into the wild - wood . ' 

 ' yea , ' say the Sun - beam , ' sooth be that ; and face - of - god be wise to 
 think of it and of other matter . yet one thing we must bear in 
 mind , that all may not go smoothly in our day 's work in silver - dale ; 
 so we must have force there to fall back on , in case we miss our 
 stroke at first . therefore , I say , send we no man to rose - dale , and 
 leave we no able man - at - arm behind in the Burg , so that we have with 
 we every blade that may be gather . ' 

 iron - face smile and say : ' thou art wise , damsel ; and I marvel that 
 so fair - fashioned a thing as thou can think so hardly of the meeting 
 of the fallow blade . but hearken ! will not the Dusky Men hear that 
 we have strip the Dale of fighting - man , and may they not then give 
 our host the go - by and send folk to ruin we ? ' 

 there be silence while face - of - god look down on the board ; but 
 presently he lift up his face and say : 

 ' folk - might be right when he say that all must be risk . let we 
 leave rose - dale till we have overcome they of silver - dale . moreover , 
 my father , thou must not deem of these felon as if they be of like 
 wit to we , to forecast the deed to come , and weigh the chance 
 nicely , and unravel tangle clew . rather they move like to the 
 stare in autumn , or the winter wild - geese , and will all be thrust 
 forward by some sting that entereth into their imagination . 
 therefore , if they have appoint one moon to wear before they fall 
 upon we , they will not stir till then , and we have time enough to do 
 what must be do . wherefore be I now of one mind with the rest of 
 you . now meseemeth it be well that these thing which we have 
 speak here , and shall speak , should not be noise abroad openly ; 
 nay , at the Folk - mote it would be well that nought be say about the 
 day or the way of our onslaught on silver - dale , lest the foe take 
 warning and be on their guard . though , sooth to say , do I deem that 
 if they have word of our intent they of Rose - dale would join 
 themselves to they of silver - dale , and that we should thus have all 
 our foe in one net , then be I fain if the word would reach they . 
 for my soul loathe the hunting that shall befall up and down the 
 wood for the slaying of a man here , and two or three there , and the 
 wearing of the day in wander up and down with weapon in the 
 hand , and the spinning out of hatred and delaying of peace . ' 

 then iron - face reach his hand across the board and take his son 's 
 hand , and say : 

 ' hail to thee , son , for thy word ! Herein thou speak as if from my 
 very soul , and fain be I of such a war - leader . ' 

 and desire draw the eye of the Sun - beam to face - of - god , and she 
 behold he proudly . but he say : 

 ' all hath be speak that the other of we may speak ; and now it 
 falleth to the part of Folk - might to order our going for the tryst 
 for the onslaught , and the trysting - place shall be in Shadowy Vale . 
 how sayest thou , Chief of the Wolf ? ' 

 say folk - might : ' I have little to say ; and it be for the war - leader 
 to see to this closely and piecemeal . I deem , as we all deem , that 
 there should be no delay ; yet be it good to wend not all together 
 to Shadowy Vale , but in diver band , as soon as ye may after the 
 Folk - mote , by the sure and nigh way that we shall show you . and 
 when we be gather there , short be the rede , for all be ready there 
 to wend by the pass which we know throughly , and whereby it be but 
 two day ' journey to the head of silver - dale , nigh to the cave of 
 the silver , where the felon dwell the thickest . ' 

 he set his tooth , and his colour come and go : for as constantly as 
 the onslaught have be in his mind , yet whenever he spake of the 
 great day of battle , hope and joy and anger work a tumult in his 
 soul ; and now that it be so nigh withal , he could not refrain his 
 joy . 

 but he spake again : ' now therefore , war - leader , it be for thee to 
 order the going of thy folk . but I will tell thee that they shall 
 not need to take aught with they save their weapon and victual for 
 the way , that is , for thirty hour ; because all be ready for they in 
 Shadowy Vale , though it be but a poor place as to victual . canst 
 thou tell we , therefore , what thou wilt do ? ' 

 face - of - god have knit his brow and become gloomy of countenance ; but 
 now his face clear , and he set his hand to his pouch , and draw 
 forth a write parchment , and say : 

 ' this be the order whereof I have bethink I . before the Folk - mote 
 I and the Wardens shall speak to the leader of hundred , who be 
 mostly here at the Fair , and give they the day and the hour whereon 
 they shall , each hundred , take their weapon and wend to Shadowy 
 Vale , and also the place where they shall meet the man of yours who 
 shall lead they across the Waste . these hundred - leader shall then 
 go straightway and give the word to the captain of score , and the 
 captain of score to the captain of ten ; and if , as be scarce 
 doubtful , the Folk - mote yea - say the onslaught and the fellowship 
 with you of the Wolf , then shall those leader of ten bring their 
 man to the trysting - place , and so go their way to Shadowy Vale . now 
 here I have the roll of our Weapon - show , and I will look to it that 
 none shall be pass over ; and if ye ask I in what order they have 
 good get on the way , my rede be that a two hundred should depart on 
 the very evening of the day of the Folk - mote , and these to be of our 
 folk of the Upper Dale ; and on the morning of the morrow of the folk- 
 mote another two hundred from the Dale ; and in the evening of the 
 same day the folk of the Shepherds , three hundred or more , and that 
 will be easy to they ; again on the next day two more band of the 
 Lower Dale , one in the morning , one in the evening . lastly , in the 
 early dawn of the third day from the Folk - mote shall the 
 Woodlanders wend their way . but one hundred of man let we leave 
 behind for the warding of the Burg , even as we agree before . as for 
 the place of tryst for the faring over the Waste , let it be the end 
 of the knoll just by the jaw of the pass yonder , where the 
 Weltering Water come into the Dale from the East . how say ye ? ' 

 they all say , and Folk - might especially , that it be right well 
 devise , and that thus it should be do . 

 then turn face - of - god to the Dale - warden , and say : 

 ' it be good , brother , that we see the other warden as soon as may 
 be , to do they to wit of this order , and what they have to do . ' 

 Therewith he arise and take the elder of the Dale - warden away with 
 he , and the twain set about their business straight - way . neither 
 do the other abide long in the Hall , but go out into the Burg to 
 see the chapman and their ware . there the Alderman buy what he 
 need of iron and steel and other matter ; and folk - might cheapen 
 he a dagger curiously work , and a web of gold and silk for the 
 Sun - beam , for which ware he pay in silver arm - ring , new - wrought 
 and of strange fashion . 

 but amidst of the chaffer be now a great ring of man ; and in the 
 midst of the ring stand Redesman , fiddle and bow in hand , and with 
 he be four damsel wondrously array ; for the first be clothe in a 
 smock so craftily work with thread of green and many colour , 
 that it seem like a piece of the green field beset with primrose 
 and cowslip and harebell and windflower , rather than a garment 
 weave and sew ; and in her hand she bear a naked sword , with golden 
 hilt and gleaming blade . but the second bore on her rose do in 
 like manner , both blossom and green leaf , wherewith her body be 
 cover decently , which else have be naked . the third be clothe as 
 though she be wad the wheat - field to the waist , and above be 
 wrap in the leaf and bunch of the wine - tree . and the fourth 
 be clothe in a scarlet gown fleck with white wool to set forth the 
 winter 's snow , and broider over with the burn brand of the Holy 
 Hearth ; and she bear on her head a garland of mistletoe . and these 
 four damsel be clearly see to image the four season of the year- 
 -spring , Summer , Autumn , and winter . but amidst they stand a 
 fountain or conduit of gild work cunningly wrought , and full of the 
 good wine of the Dale , and gild cup and beaker hang about it . 

 so now Redesman fall to caress his fiddle with the bow till it 
 begin to make sweet music , and therewith the heart of all dance 
 with it ; and presently word come into his mouth , and he fall to 
 sing ; and the damsel answer he : 


 earth - wielder , that fashion the Dale - dweller ' treasure , 
  Soft be ye by seem , yet hardy of heart ! 
 no warrior amongst we withstandeth your pleasure ; 
  no man from his meadow may thrust you apart . 

 fresh and fair be your body , but far beyond tell 
  be the year of your life , and the craft ye have store . 
 come give we a word , then , concern our dwelling , 
  and the day to befall we , the fruit of the sword . 

 Winter saith : 

 when last in the feast - hall the Yule - fire flicker , 
  the foot of no foeman fare over the snow , 
 and nought but the wind with the ash - branch bicker : 
  Next Yule ye may deem it a long time ago . 

 Autumn saith : 

 Loud laugh ye last year in the wheat - field a - smiting ; 
  and ye laugh as your back drave the beam of the press . 
 when the edge of the war - sword the acre be light 
  look up to the Banner and laugh ye no less . 

 Summer saith : 

 Ye call and I come , and how good be the greeting , 
  when ye wrap I in rose both bosom and side ! 
 here yet shall I long , and be fain of our meeting , 
  as hide from battle your come I bide . 

 Spring saith : 

 I be here for your comfort , and lo ! what I carry ; 
  the blade with the bright edge bar to the sun . 
 to the field , to the work then , that e'en I may tarry 
  for the end of the tale in my first day begin ! 


 Therewith the throng open , and a young man step lightly into the 
 ring , clothe in very fair armour , with a gild helm on his head ; and 
 he take the sword from the hand of the Maiden of Spring , and wave it 
 in the air till the westere sun flash back from it . then each of 
 the four damsel go up to the swain and kiss his mouth ; and 
 Redesman draw the bow across the string , and the four damsel sing 
 together , stand round about the young warrior : 


 it be but a while since for earth 's sake we tremble , 
  lest the increase our life - day have win for the Dale , 
 all the wealth that the moon and the year have assemble , 
  should be but a mock for the day of your bale . 

 but now we behold the sun smite on the token 
  in the hand of the Champion , the heart of a man ; 
 we look down the long year and see they unbroken ; 
  Forth fareth the Folk by the way it begin . 

 so bid ye these chapman in autumn return , 
  to bring iron for ploughshare and steel for the scythe , 
 and the over - sea oil that hath feel the sun 's burning , 
  and fair webs for your woman soft - spoken and blithe ; 

 and pledge ye your word in the market to meet they , 
  as many a man and as many a maid , 
 as eager as ever , as guest - fain to greet they , 
  and bide till the booth from the waggon be make . 

 come , guest of our lover ! for we , the year - wielder , 
  bid each man and all to come hither and take 
 a cup from our hand midst the peace of our shielder , 
  and drink to the day of the Dale that we make . 


 then go the damsel to that wine - fountain , and draw thence cup of 
 the good and bright wine of the Dale , and go round about the 
 ring , and give drink to whomsoever would , both of the chapman and the 
 other ; while the weapone youth stand in the midst bear aloft his 
 sword and shield like an image in a holy place , and Redesman 's bow 
 still go up and down the string , and draw forth a sweet and merry 
 tune . 

 great game it be now to see the stark Burgdale carle drag the 
 Men of the plain , little loth , up to the front of the ring , that they 
 might stretch out their hand for a cup , and how many a one , as he 
 take it , take as much as he might of the damsel 's hand withal . as 
 for the damsel , they play the Holy Play very daintily , neither 
 redden nor laugh , but fare so solemnly , and withal so sweetly 
 and bright - faced , that it might well have be deem that they be 
 in very sooth Maidens of the God of Earth send from the ever - endure 
 Hall to cheer the heart of man . 

 so simply and blithely do the Men of Burgdale disport they after the 
 manner of their father , trust in their valour and behold the 
 good day to be . 

 so wear the evening , and when night be come , man feast throughout 
 the Burg from house to house , and every hall be full . but the 
 Guests from Shadowy Vale feast in the Hall of the Face in all glee 
 and goodwill ; and with they be the chief of the chapman and two 
 other ; but the rest of they have be lay hold of by goodman of the 
 Burg , and drag into their feast - hall , for they be fain of those 
 guest and their tale . one of the chapman in the House of the Face 
 know Folk - might , and hail he by the name he have bear in the 
 Cities , regulus to wit ; indeed , the chief chapman know he , and even 
 somewhat over - well , for he have be hold to ransom by Folk - might in 
 those past day , and even yet fear he , because he , the chapman , 
 have play somewhat of a dastard 's part to he . but the other be an 
 open - hearted and merry fellow , and no weakling ; and folk - might be 
 fain of his talk concern times bygone , and the field they have 
 foughten in , and other adventure that have befall they , both good 
 and evil . 

 as for face - of - god , he go about the Hall soberly , and spake no more 
 than behove he , so as not to seem a mar - feast ; for the image of the 
 slaughter to be yet abode with he , and his heart forebode the 
 after - grief of the battle . he have no speech with the Sun - beam till 
 man be sunder after the feast , and then he come close to she 
 amidst of the turmoil , and say : 

 ' time press on I these day ; but if thou would speak with I 
 to - morrow as I would with thee , then might thou go on the Bridge 
 of the Burg about sunrise , and I will be there , and we two only . ' 

 her face , which have be somewhat sad that evening   for she have be 
 watch his , brighten at that word , and she take his hand as folk 
 come throng round about they , and say : 

 ' yea , friend , I shall be there , and fain of thee . ' and therewithal 
 they sunder for that night . 

 and all man go to sleep throughout the Burg : howbeit they set a 
 watch at the Burg - Gate ; and hall - face , when he be come back from 
 the woodland ward about sunset , fall in with Redcoat of Waterless and 
 four score man on the Portway come to meet he and take his place . 
 all which be clean contrary to the will not of the Burgdalers , who at 
 most while hold no watch and ward , not even in fair - time . 



 CHAPTER XXXV . FACE - of - GOD talketh with the SUN - BEAM 



 face - of - God be at the Bridge on the morrow before sun - rise , and as 
 he turn about at the Bridge - foot he see the Sun - beam come down 
 the street ; and his heart rise to his mouth at the sight of she , and 
 he go to meet she and take she by the hand ; and there be no word 
 between they till they have kiss and caress each other , for there 
 be no one stir about they . so they go over the Bridge into 
 the meadow , and eastward of the beat path thereover . 

 the grass be grow thick and strong , and it be full of flower , 
 as the cowslip and the oxlip , and the chequered daffodil , and the 
 wild tulip : the black - thorn be well - nigh do blooming , but the 
 hawthorn be in bud , and in some place grow white . it be a fair 
 morning , warm and cloudless , but the night have be misty , and the 
 haze still hang about the meadow of the Dale where they be 
 wet , and the grass and its flower be heavy with dew , so that 
 the Sun - beam go barefoot in the meadow . she have a dark cloak cast 
 over her kirtle , and have leave she glitter gown behind she in the 
 House . 

 they go along hand in hand exceed fain of each other , and the 
 sun rise as they go , and the long beam of gold shine through the 
 top of the tall tree across the grass they trod , and a light wind 
 rise up in the north , as face - of - god stay a moment and turn 
 toward the Face of the Sun and pray to he , while the Sun - beam 's 
 hand leave the war - leader 's hand and steal up to his golden lock and 
 lay amongst they . 

 presently they go on , and the foot of face - of - god lead he unwitte 
 toward the chestnut grove by the old dyke where he have meet the Bride 
 such a little while ago , till he bethink whither he be go and 
 stop short and redden ; and the Sun - beam note it , but spake not ; 
 but he say : ' Hereby be a fair place for we to sit and talk till the 
 day 's work beginneth . ' 

 so then he turn aside , and soon they come to a hawthorn brake out 
 of which arise a great tall - stemmed oak , show no green as yet save 
 a little on its low twig ; and anigh it , yet with room for its 
 bough to grow freely , be a great bird - cherry tree , all cover now 
 with sweet - smell white blossom . there they sit down on the trunk 
 of a tree fell last year , and she cast off her cloak , and take his 
 face between her two hand and kiss he long and fondly , and for a 
 while their joy have no word . but when speech come to they , it be 
 she that spake first and say : 

 ' gold - mane , my dear , sorely I wonder at thee and at I , how we be 
 change since that day last autumn when I first see thee . while I 
 think , didst thou not laugh when thou wert by thyself that day , and 
 mock at I privily , that I must need take such wisdom on myself , and 
 lesson thee stand like a stripling before I . dost thou not call 
 it all to mind and make merry over it , now that thou art become a 
 great chieftain and a wise warrior , and I be yet what I always be , a 
 young maiden of the kindre ; save that now I abide no long for my 
 love ? ' 

 her face be exceed bright and ripple with joyous smile , and he 
 look at she and deem that her heart be overflow with 
 happiness , and he wonder at she indeed that she be so glad of he , 
 and he say : 

 ' yea , indeed , oft do I see that morning in the woodland hall and thee 
 and I therein , as one looketh on a picture ; yea verily , and I laugh , 
 yet be it for very bliss ; neither do I mock at all . do I not deem 
 thee a God then ? and be I not most happy now when I can call it thus 
 to mind ? and as to thee , thou wert wise then , and yet art thou wise 
 now . yea , I think thee a God ; and if we be change , be it not 
 rather that thou hast lift I up to thee , and not come down to I ? ' 

 yet therewithal he knit his brow somewhat and say : 

 ' yet thou hast not to tell I that all thy love for thy Folk , and thy 
 yearn hope for its recoverance , be but a paint show . else why 
 shouldst thou love I the well now that I be become a chieftain , 
 and therefore be more meet to understand thy hope and thy sorrow ? 
 do I not behold thee as we stand before the Wolf of the Hall of 
 Shadowy Vale , how the tear stand in thine eye as thou beheldest 
 he , and thine hand in mine quiver and clung to I , and thou wert 
 all change in a moment of time ? be all this then but a seeming and 
 a beguilement ? ' 

 ' o young man , ' she say , ' hast thou not say it , that we stand there 
 close together , and my hand in thine and desire grow up in I ? 
 dost thou not know how this also quickeneth the story of our Folk , 
 and our goodwill towards the living , and remembrance of the dead ? 
 shall they have live and desire , and we deny desire and life ? or 
 tell I : what be it make thee so chieftain - like in the Hall 
 yesterday , so that thou wert the master of all our will , for as 
 self - willed as some of we be ? be it not that I , whom thou deemest 
 lovely , be thereby watch thee and rejoice in thee ? do not the 
 sweetness of thy love quicken thee ? yet because of that be thy 
 warrior 's wisdom and thy foresight an empty show ? heed thou 
 nought the Folk of the Dale ? would thou sunder from the child 
 of the Fathers , and dwell amongst stranger ? ' 

 he kiss she and smile on she and say : ' do I not say of thee 
 that thou wert wise than the daughter of man ? see how wise thou 
 hast make I ! ' 

 she spake again : ' nay , nay , there be no feigning in my love for my 
 people . how could thou think it , when the Fathers and the kindre 
 have make this body that thou lov , and the voice of their song be 
 in the speech thou deemest sweet ? ' 

 he say : ' sweet friend , I deem not that there be feign in 
 thee : I be but wonder what I be and how I be fashion , that I 
 should make thee so glad that thou couldst for a while forget thy 
 hope of the day before we meet . ' 

 she say : ' o how glad , how glad ! yet be I nought hapless . in 
 despite of all trouble I have no down - weigh grief , and I have the 
 hope of my people before I . good be my day ; but I know not till 
 now how glad a child of man may be . ' 

 their word be hush for a while amidst their caress . then she 
 say : 

 ' gold - mane , my friend , I mock not my past self because I deem that 
 I be a fool then , but because I see now that all that my wisdom 
 could do , would have come about without my wisdom ; and that thou , 
 deem thyself something less than wise , didst accomplish the thing 
 I crave , and that which thou didst crave also ; and withal wisdom 
 embrace thee , along with love . ' 

 Therewith she cast her arm about he and say : 

 ' o friend , I mock myself of this : that erst thou deemedst I a God 
 and fearedst I , but now thou seemest to I to be a God , and I fear 
 thee . yea , though I have long so sore to be with thee since the 
 day of Shadowy Vale , and though I have weary of the slow wearing of 
 the day , and it hath torment I ; yet now that I be with thee , I 
 bless the torment of my longing ; for it be but my longing that 
 compelleth I to cast away my fear of thee and caress thee , because I 
 have learn how sweet it be to love thee thus . ' 

 he wind his arm about she , and sweet be their longing than mere 
 joy ; and though their love be beyond measure , yet be therein no 
 shame to aught , not even to the lovely Dale and that fair season of 
 spring , so goodly they be among the child of man . 

 in a while they arise and turn homeward , and go over the open 
 meadow , and it be yet early , and the dew be as heavy on the grass 
 as before , though the wide sunlight be now upon it , glitter on 
 the wet blade , and shin through the bell of the chequer 
 daffodil till they look like gout of blood . 

 ' look , ' say Sun - beam , as they go along by the same way whereas 
 they come , ' deem thou not that other speech - friend besides we 
 have be abroad to talk together apart on this morning of the eve of 
 battle . it be nought unwonted , that we do , even though we forget the 
 trouble of the people to think of our own joy for a while . ' 

 the smile die out of her face as she speak , and she say : 

 ' o friend , this much may I say for myself in all sooth , that indeed I 
 would die for the kindred and its good day , nor falter therein ; but 
 if I be to die , might I but die in thine arm ! ' 

 he look very lovingly on she , and put his arm about she and kiss 
 she and say : ' what ail we to stand in the doom - ring and bear 
 witness against ourselves before the kindred ? now I will say , that 
 whatsoever the kindre may or can call upon I to do , that will I do , 
 nor grudge the deed : I be sackless before they . but that be true 
 which I spake to thee when we come together up out of Shadowy Vale , 
 to wit , that I be no strifeful man , but a peaceful ; and I look to it 
 to win through this war , and find on the other side either death , or 
 life amongst a happy folk ; and I deem that this be mostly the mind of 
 our people . ' 

 she say : ' thou shalt not die , thou shalt not die ! ' 

 ' mayhappen not , ' he say ; ' yet yesterday I could not but look into 
 the slaughter to come , and it seem to I a grim thing , and darken 
 the day for I ; and I grow acold as a man walk with the dead . but 
 tell I : thou sayest I shall not die ; dost thou say this only 
 because I be become dear to thee , or dost thou speak it out of thy 
 foresight of thing to come ? ' 

 she stop and look silently a while over the meadow towards the 
 house of the Thorp : they be stand now on the border of a 
 shallow brook that run down toward the Weltering Water ; it have a 
 little strand of fine sand like the sea - shore , drive close together , 
 and all moist , because that brook be use to flood the meadow for 
 the feeding of the grass ; and the last evening the hatch which hold 
 up the water have be draw , so that much have ebb away and leave the 
 strand aforesaid . 

 after a while the Sun - beam turn to face - of - god , and she be become 
 somewhat pale ; she say : 

 ' Nay , I have strive to see , and can see nought save the picture of 
 hope and fear that I make for myself . so it oft befalleth foresee 
 woman , that the love of a man cloudeth their vision . be content , 
 dear friend ; it be for life or death ; but whichso it be , the same for 
 I and thee together ? ' 

 ' yea , ' he say , ' and well content I be ; so now let each of we trust 
 in the other to be both good and dear , even as I trust in thee the 
 first hour that I look on thee . ' 

 ' it be well , ' she say ; ' it be well . how fair thou art ; and how fair 
 be the morn , and this our Dale in the goodly season ; and all this 
 abideth we when the battle be over . ' 

 once more her voice become sweet and wheedling , and the smile light up 
 her face again , and she point down to the sand with her finger , and 
 say : 

 ' see thou ! here indeed have other lover pass by across the brook . 
 shall we wish they good luck ? ' 

 he laugh and look down on the sand , and say : 

 ' thou art in haste to make a story up . indeed I see that these first 
 footprint be of a woman , for no carle of the Dale have a foot as 
 small ; for we be tall fellow ; and these other withal be a man 's 
 footprint ; and if they show that they have be walk side by 
 side , simple have be thy tale ; but so it be not . I can not say that 
 these two pair of foot go over the brook within five minute of 
 each other ; but sure it be that they could not have be fare side 
 by side . well , belike they be lover bicker , and we may wish 
 they luck out of that . truly it be well see that Bow - may hath do 
 thine hunting for thee , dear friend ; or else would thou have 
 lack venison ; for thou hast no hunter 's eye . ' 

 ' well , ' she say , ' but wish they luck , and give I thine hand upon 
 it . ' 

 he take her hand , and fondle it , and say : ' by this hand of my 
 speech - friend , I wish these twain all luck , in love and in leisure , 
 in fare and fighting , in sowing and samming , in get and give . 
 be it well enough wish ? if so it be , then come thy way , dear 
 friend ; for the day 's work be at hand . ' 

 ' it be well wish , ' she say . ' now hearken : by the valiant hand of 
 the war - leader , by the hand that shall unloose my girdle , I wish 
 these twain to be as happy as we be . ' 

 he make as if to draw she away , but she hang aback to set the print 
 of her foot beside the woman 's foot , and then they go on together , 
 and soon cross the Bridge , and come home to the House of the Face . 

 when they have break their fast , face - of - god would straight get to 
 his business of order matter for the warfare , and be wishful to 
 speak with Folk - might ; but find he not , either in the House or the 
 street . but a man say : 

 ' I see the tall Guest come abroad from the House and go toward the 
 Bridge very early in the morning . ' 

 the Sun - beam , who be anigh when that be speak , hear it and 
 smile , and say : ' gold - mane , deemest thou that it be my brother 
 whom we bless ? ' 

 ' I wot not , ' he say ; ' but I would he be here , for this gear must 
 speedily be look to . ' 

 nevertheless it be nigh an hour before Folk - might come home to the 
 House . he stride in lightly and gaily , and shake the crest of his 
 war - helm as he go . he look friendly on face - of - god , and say to 
 he : 

 ' Thou hast be seek I , war - leader ; but grudge it not that I have 
 cause thee to tarry . for as thing have go , I be twice the man 
 for thine help that I be yester - eve ; and thou art so ready and 
 deft , that all will be do in due time . ' 

 he look as if he would have have face - of - god ask of he what make 
 he so fain , but face - of - god say only : 

 ' I be glad of thy gladness ; but now let we dally no long , for I 
 have many folk to see to - day and much to set a - going . ' 

 so therewith they spake together a while , and then go their way 
 together toward Carlstead and the Woodlanders . 



 CHAPTER XXXVI . folk - might SPEAKETH with the BRIDE 



 it must be tell that those footprint which face - of - god and the Sun- 
 beam have bless betwixt jest and earnest have more to do with they 
 than they wotte of . for folk - might , who have have many thought and 
 longing since he have see the Bride again , rise up early about 
 sunrise , and go out - a - door , and wander about the Burg , let 
 his eye stray over the goodly stone house and their trim garden , 
 yet note they little , since the Bride be not there . 

 at last he come to where there be an open place , straight - sided , 
 long than it be wide , with a wall on each side of it , over which 
 show the blossom bough of pear and cherry and plum - tree : on 
 either hand before the wall be a row of great linden , now show 
 their first tender green , especially on their low twig , where they 
 be shelter by the wall . at the nether end of this place folk- 
 might see a grey stone house , and he go towards it betwixt the 
 linden , for it seem right great , and presently be but a score of 
 pace from its door , and as yet there be no man , carle or queen , 
 stir about it . 

 it be a long low house with a very steep roof ; but belike the hall 
 be build over some undercroft , for many step go up to the door on 
 either hand ; and the doorway be low , with a straight lintel under 
 its arch . this house , like the House of the Face , seem ancient and 
 somewhat strange , and Folk - might could not choose but take note of 
 it . the front be all of good ashlar work , but it be carven all 
 over , without heed be pay to the joint of the stone , into one 
 picture of a flowery meadow , with tall tree and bush in it , and 
 fowl perch in the tree and run through the grass , and sheep 
 and kine and oxen and horse feed down the meadow ; and over the 
 door at the top of the stair be work a great steer big than 
 all the other neat , whose head be turn toward the sun - rise and 
 uplifted with open mouth , as though he be low aloud . exceeding 
 fair seem that house to folk - might , and as though it be the 
 dwelling of some great kindre . 

 but he have scarce go over it with his eye , and be just about to 
 draw nigh yet to it , when the door at the top of those step 
 open , and a woman come out of the house clothe in a green kirtle and 
 a gown of brazil , with a golden - hilte sword girt to her side . folk- 
 might see at once that it be the Bride , and draw aback behind one of 
 the tree so that she might not see he , if she have not already see 
 he , as it seem not that she have , for she stay but for a moment 
 on the top of the stair , look out down the tree - row , and then 
 come down the stair and go soberly along the road , pass so close 
 to folk - might that he could see the fashion of her beauty closely , as 
 one look into the work of some deft artificer . then it come 
 suddenly into his head that he would follow she and see whither she 
 be wend . ' at least , ' say he to himself , ' if I come not to 
 speech with she , I shall be nigh unto she , and shall see somewhat of 
 her beauty . ' 

 so he come out quietly from behind the tree , and follow she softly ; 
 and he be clothe in no garment save his kirtle , and bare no weapon to 
 clash and jingle , though he have his helm on his head for lack of a

 and look not back . she go by the way whereas he have come , till 
 they be in the main street , wherein as yet be no one afoot ; she 
 make her way to the Bridge , and pass over it into the meadow ; but
