 the ground , and as she do so turn a little toward folk - might , who 
 have draw back into the last of the refuge over the up - stream 
 buttress . he see that there be a half - smile on her face , but he 
 could not tell whether she be glad or sorry . a light wind be 
 begin to blow , that stir her raiment and raise a lock of hair 
 that have stray from the golden fillet round about her head , and she 
 look most marvellous fair . 

 now she look along the grass that glitter under the beam of the 
 newly - rise sun , and note belike how heavy the dew lie on it ; and 
 the grass be high already , for the spring have be hot , and haysel 
 would be early in the Dale . so she put off her shoe , that be of 
 deerskin and broider with golden thread , and turn somewhat from 
 the way , and hang they up amidst the new green leaf of a hawthorn 
 bush that stand nearby , and so go thwart the meadow somewhat 
 eastward straight from that bush , and her foot shine out like pearl 
 amidst the deep green grass . 

 Folk - might follow presently , and she stay not again , nor turn , 
 nor beheld he ; he recke not if she have , for then would he have come 
 up with she and hail she , and he know that she be no foolish 
 maiden to start at the sight of a man who be the friend of her Folk . 

 so they go their way till she come to the strand of the water- 
 meadow brook aforesaid , and she go through the little ripple of 
 the shallow without stay , and on through the tall deep grass of 
 the meadow beyond , to where they meet the brook again ; for it sweep 
 round the meadow in a wide curve , and turn back toward itself ; so 
 it be some half furlong over from water to water . 

 she stand a while on the brink of the brook here , which be brim - full 
 and nigh run into the grass , because there be a dam just below 
 the place ; and folk - might drew nigh to she under cover of the 
 thorn - bush , and look at the place about she and beyond she . the 
 meadow beyond stream be very fair and flowery , but not right great ; 
 for it be bound by a grove of ancient chestnut tree , that go on 
 and on toward the southern cliff of the Dale : in front of the 
 chestnut wood stand a broken row of black - thorn bush , now grow 
 green and lose their blossom , and he could see betwixt they that 
 there be a grassy bank run along , as if there have once be a 
 turf - wall and ditch round about the chestnut tree . for indeed this 
 be the old place of tryst between gold - mane and the Bride , whereof 
 the tale hath tell before . 

 the Bride stay scarce long than give he time to note all this ; 
 but he deem that she be weep , though he could not rightly see 
 her face ; for her shoulder heave , and she hang her face adown and 
 put up her hand to it . but now she go a little high up the 
 stream , where the water be shallow , and wad the stream and go 
 up over the meadow , still weep , as he deem , and go between the 
 black - thorn bush , and sit she down on the grassy bank with her back 
 to the chestnut tree . 

 Folk - might be ashamed to have see her weeping , and be half - minded 
 to turn he back again at once ; but love constrain he , and he say 
 to himself , ' where shall I see she again privily if I pass by this 
 time and place ? ' so he wait a little till he deem she might have 
 master the passion of tear , and then come forth from his bush , and 
 go down to the water and cross it , and go quietly over the 
 meadow straight towards she . but he be not half - way across , when 
 she lift up her face from between her hand and beheld the man 
 come . she neither start nor rise up ; but straighten herself as 
 she sit , and look right into Folk - might 's eye as he draw near , 
 though the tear be not dry on her cheek . 

 now he stand before she , and say : ' hail to the daughter of a mighty 
 House ! Mayst thou live happy ! ' 

 she answer : ' hail to thee also , guest of our Folk ! hast thou be 
 wander about our meadow , and happen on I perchance ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' he say ; ' I see thee come forth from the House of the Steer , 
 and I follow thee hither . ' 

 she redden a little , and knit her brow , and say : 

 ' thou wilt have something to say to I ? ' 

 ' I have much to say to thee , ' he say ; ' yet it be sweet to I to 
 behold thee , even if I might not speak with thee . ' 

 she look on he with her deep simple eye , and neither redden 
 again , nor seem wroth ; then she say : 

 ' speak what thou hast in thine heart , and I will hearken without 
 anger whatsoever it may be ; even if thou hast but to tell I of the 
 pass folly of a mighty man , which in a month or two he will not 
 remember for sorrow or for joy . sit here beside I , and tell I thy 
 thought . ' 

 so he sit he adown and say : ' yea , I have much to say to thee , but 
 it be hard to I to say it . but this I will say : to - day and 
 yesterday make the third time I have see thee . the first time thou 
 wert happy and calm , and no shadow of trouble be on thee ; the second 
 time thine happy day be wan , though thou scarce knewest it ; but 
 to - day and yesterday thou art constrain by the bond of grief , and 
 would loosen they if thou mightest . ' 

 she say : ' what mean thou ? how know thou this ? how may a 
 stranger partake in my joy and my sorrow ? ' 

 he say : ' as for yesterday , all the people might see thy grief and 
 know it . but when I behold thee the first time , I see thee that thou 
 wert more fair and lovely than all other woman ; and when I be away 
 from thee , the thought of thee and thine image be with I , and I 
 might not put they away ; and oft at such and such a time I wonder 
 and say to myself , what be she do now ? though god wot I be 
 deal with tangle and trouble and rough deed enough . but the 
 second time I behold thee , when I have look to have great joy in the 
 sight of thee , my heart be smite with a pang of grief ; for I see 
 thee hang on the word and the look of another man , who be 
 light - minded toward thee , and that thou wert trouble with the 
 anguish of doubt and fear . and he know it not , nor see it , though I 
 see it . ' 

 her face grow troubled , and the tearful passion stir within she . 
 but she hold it aback , and say , as anyone might have say it : 

 ' how wert thou in the Dale , mighty man ? we see thee not . ' 

 he say : ' I come hither hide in other semblance than mine own . 
 but meddle not therewith ; it availeth nought . let I say this , and 
 do thou hearken to it . I see thee yesterday in the street , and thou 
 wert as the ghost of thine old gladness ; although belike thou hast 
 striven with sorrow ; for I see thee with a sword by thy side , and we 
 have be tell that thou , o fairest of woman , hast give thyself to 
 the Warrior to be his damsel . ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' that be sooth . ' 

 he go on : ' but the face which thou bearedst yesterday against thy 
 will , amidst all the people , that be because thou hadst see my 
 sister the Sun - beam for the first time , and face - of - god with she , 
 hand cling to hand , lip longing for lip , desire unsatisfie , but 
 glad with all hope . ' 

 she lay hand upon hand in the lap of her gown , and look down , and 
 her voice tremble as she say : 

 ' Doth it avail to talk of this ? ' 

 he say : ' I know not : it may avail ; for I be grieve , and shall be 
 whilst thou art grieve ; and it be my will not to strive with my grief 
 till I amend they . ' 

 she turn to he with kind eye and say : 

 ' o mighty man , canst thou clear away the tangle which besetteth the 
 soul of she whose hope hath bewray she ? Canst thou make hope grow 
 up in her heart ? friend , I will tell thee that when I we d , I shall 
 we d for the sake of the kindred , hop for no joy therein . yea , or 
 if by some chance the desire of man come again into my heart , I 
 should strive with it to rid myself of it , for I should know of it 
 that it be but a wasting folly , that should but beguile I , and 
 wound I , and depart , leave I empty of joy and heedless of life . ' 

 he shake his head and say : ' even so thou deem now ; but one day 
 it shall be otherwise . or dost thou love thy sorrow ? I tell thee , 
 as it wear thee and wear thee , thou shalt hate it , and strive to 
 shake it off . ' 

 ' Nay , nay , ' she say ; ' I love it not ; for not only it grieveth I , 
 but also it beateth I down and belittleth I . ' 

 ' good be that , ' say he . ' I know how strong thine heart be . now , 
 wilt thou take mine hand , which be verily the hand of thy friend , and 
 remember what I have tell thee of my grief which can not be sunder 
 from thine ? shall we not talk more concern this ? for surely I 
 shall soon see thee again , and often ; since the Warrior , who loveth 
 I belike , leadeth thee into fellowship with I . yea , I tell thee , O 
 friend , that in that fellowship shalt thou find both the seed of 
 hope , and the sun of desire that shall quicken it . ' 

 Therewith he arise and stand before she , and hold out to she his hand 
 all harden with the sword - hilt , and she take it , and stand up 
 face he , and say : 

 ' this much will I tell thee , o friend ; that what I have say to thee 
 this hour , I think not to have say to any man ; or to talk with a 
 man of the grief that weareth I , or to suffer he to see my tear ; 
 and marvellous I deem it of thee , for all thy might , that thou hast 
 draw this speech from out of I , and leave I neither angry nor 
 ashame , in spite of these tear ; and thou whom I have know not , 
 though thou knewest I ! 

 ' but now it be good that thou depart , and get thee home to the 
 House of the Face , where I be once so frequent ; for I wot that thou 
 hast much to do ; and as thou sayest , it will be in warfare that I 
 shall see thee . now I thank thee for thy word and the thought thou 
 hast have of I , and the pain which thou hast take to heal my hurt : 
 I thank thee , I thank thee , for as grievous as it be to show one 's 
 hurt even to a friend . ' 

 he say : ' o Bride , I thank thee for hearken to my tale ; and one 
 day shall I thank thee much more . Mayest thou fare well in the Field 
 and amidst the Folk ! ' 

 Therewith he kiss her hand , and turn away , and go across the 
 meadow and the stream , glad at heart and blithe with everyone ; for 
 kindness grow in he as gladness grow . 



 CHAPTER XXXVII . of the FOLK - mote of the DALESMEN , the SHEPHERD - FOLK , 
 and the WOODLAND CARLES : the banner of the WOLF DISPLAYED 



 now come the day of the Great Folk - mote , and there be much throng 
 from everywhere to the mote - stead , but most from Burgstead itself , 
 whereas few of the Dale - dweller who have be at the fair have go 
 back home . albeit some of the Shepherds and of the Dalesmen of the 
 westernmost Dale have bring light tent , and tilt themselves in in 
 the night before the Mote down in the meadow below the mote - stead . 
 from early morning there have be a stream of folk on the Portway 
 set westward ; and many come thus early that they might hold 
 converse with friend and well - wisher ; and some that they might 
 disport they in the wood . man go in no ordered band , as the 
 Burgstead man at least have do on the day of the Weapon - show , save 
 that a few of they who be array the brave gather about the 
 banner , and go with they to the mote - stead ; for all the banner 
 must need be there . 

 the Folk - mote be to be hallow - in three hour before noon , as all 
 man know ; therefore an hour before that time be all man of the Dale 
 and the Shepherds assemble that might be look for , save the 
 Alderman and the chieftain with the banner of the Burg , and these 
 be not like to come many minute before the Hallowing . folk be 
 gather on the field in such wise , that the man - at - arm make a great 
 ring round about the doom - ring ,   albeit there be many old man 
 there , girt with sword that they should never heave up again in 
 battle , so that without that ring there be nought save woman and 
 child . but when all the other Houses be assemble , man look 
 around , and behold the place of the Woodlanders that it be empty ; 
 and they marvel that they be thus belate . for now all be 
 ready , and a watcher have go up to the Tower on the height , and have 
 with he the great Horn of Warning , which could be hear past the 
 mote - stead and a great way down the Dale : and if he see foe come 
 from the East he should blow one blast ; if from the South , two ; if 
 from the West , three ; if from the North , four . 

 so half an hour from the appoint time of Hallowing rise the rumour 
 that the Alderman be on the road , and presently they of the woman 
 who be on the outside of the throng , by draw nigh to the edge of 
 the sheer rock , could behold the Banner of the Burg on the Portway , 
 and soon after could see the wain , do about with green bough , 
 wherein sit the chieftain in their glitter war - gear . speedily 
 they spread the tiding , and a confused shout go up into the air ; 
 and in a little while the wain stay on Wildlake 's way at the bottom 
 of the steep slope that go up to the mote - stead , and the banner of 
 the Burg come on proudly up the hill . soon all man behold it , and 
 see that the tall Hall - face bear it in front of his brother face - of- 
 god , who come on gleam in war - gear well than most man have see ; 
 which be indeed of his father 's fashioning , and his father 's gift to 
 he that morning . 

 after face - of - god come the Alderman , and with he Folk - might lead 
 the Sun - beam by the hand , and then stone - face and the elder of the 
 Dale - warden ; and then the six burg - warden : as to the other dale- 
 warden , they be in their place on the Field . 

 so now those who have be stand up turn their face toward the 
 Altar of the Gods , and those who have be sit down sprang to 
 their foot , and the confused rumour of the throng rise into a clear 
 shout as the chieftain go to their place , and sit they down on 
 the turf - seat amidst the doom - ring face the Speech - hill and the 
 Altar of the Gods . amidmost sit the Alderman , on his right hand 
 face - of - god , and out from he Hall - face , and then stone - face and 
 three of the Wardens ; but on his left hand sit first the two Guests , 
 then the elder of the Dale - warden , and then the other three Burg- 
 warden ; as for the Banner of the Burg , its staff be stick into the 
 earth behind they , and the Banner raise itself in the morning wind 
 and flap and ripple over their head . 

 there then they sit , and folk abide , and it still lack some 
 minute of the due time , as the Alderman wotte by the shadow of the 
 great stand - stone betwixt he and the Altar . Therewithal come the 
 sound of a great horn from out of the wood on the north side , and man 
 know it for the horn of the Woodland Carles , and be glad ; for they 
 could not think why they should be belate ; and now man stand up a- 
 tiptoe and on other 's shoulder to look over the head of the woman 
 and child to behold their coming ; but their empty place be at the 
 southwest corner of the ring of man . 

 so presently man behold they march toward their place , cleave 
 the throng of the woman and child , a great company ; for besides 
 that they have with they two score more of man under weapon than on 
 the day of the Weapon - show , all their little one and woman and 
 outworn elder be with they , some on foot , some rid on oxen and 
 ass . in their forefront go the two sign of the battle - shaft and 
 the War - spear . but moreover , in front of all be bear a great staff 
 with the cloth of a banner wrap round about it , and tie up with a 
 hempen yarn that it might not be see . 

 Stark and mighty man they look ; tall and lean , broad - shoulder , 
 dark - faced . as they come amongst the throng the voice of their horn 
 die out , and for a few moment they fare on with no sound save the 
 tramp of their foot ; then all at once the man who bare the hide 
 banner lift up one hand , and straightway they fall to singing , and 
 with that song they come to their place . and this be some of what 
 they sing : 


 o white , white Sun , what thing of wonder 
  Hast thou beheld from thy wall of the sky ! 
 all the Roofs of the Rich and the grief thereunder , 
  as the fear of the Earl - folk flitteth by ! 

 Thou hast see the Flame steal forth from the Forest 
  to slay the slumber of the land , 
 as the Dusky Lord whom thou abhorrest 
  Clomb up to thy Burg unbuilt with hand . 

 thou lookest down from thy door the golden , 
  nor batest thy wide - shin mirth , 
 as the rampart fall , and the roof - tree olden 
  lie smoulder low on the burn earth . 

 when flitteth the half - dark night of summer 
  from the face of the murder great and grim , 
 ' tis thou thyself and no new - comer 
  Shines golden - bright on the deed undim . 

 art thou our friend , o day - dawn 's Lover ? 
  full oft thine hand hath send aslant 
 Bright beam athwart the Wood - bear 's cover , 
  where the feeble folk and the nameless haunt . 

 Thou hast see we quail , thou hast see us cower , 
  Thou hast see we crouch in the Green Abode , 
 while for we wert thou slay slow hour by hour , 
  and smooth down the war - rough road . 

 Yea , the rock of the Waste be thy dawn upheaving , 
  to let the day of the year go through ; 
 and thy Noons the tangled brake be cleave 
  the slow - foot season ' deed to do . 

 then gaze adown on this gift of our giving , 
  for the wolf come wend frith and ford , 
 and the Folk fare forth from the dead to the living , 
  for the love of the Lief by the light of the Sword . 


 then cease the song , and the whole band of the Woodlanders come 
 pour tumultuously into the space allot they , like the water 
 pour over a river - dam , their white sword wave aloft in the 
 morning sunlight ; and wild and strange cry rise up from amidst 
 they , with sob and weeping of joy . but soon their troubled front 
 sink back into order rank , their bright blade stand upright in 
 their hand before they , and folk look on their company , and deem 
 it the very Terror of battle and Render of the rank of war . right 
 well be they arm ; for though many of their weapon be ancient 
 and somewhat worn , yet be they the work of good smith of old day ; 
 and moreover , if any of they lack good war - gear of his own , that 
 have the Alderman and his son make good to they . 

 but before the hedge of steel stand the two tall man who hold in 
 their hand the war - token of the battle - shaft and the War - spear , and 
 betwixt they stand one who be indeed the tall man of the whole 
 assembly , who hold the great staff of the hidden banner . and now he 
 reach up his hand , and pluck at the yarn that bind it , which of 
 set purpose be but feeble , and tear it off , and then shake the staff 
 aloft with both hand , and shout , and lo ! the Banner of the Wolf 
 with the Sun - burst behind he , glitter - bright , new - weave by the 
 woman of the kindred , run out in the fresh wind , and flap and 
 ripple before his warrior there assemble . 

 then from all over the mote - stead arise an exceeding great shout , and 
 all man wave aloft their weapon ; but the man of Shadowy Vale who 
 be stand amidst the man of the face know not how to demean 
 themselves , and some of they run forth into the field and leapt for 
 joy , toss their sword into the air , and catch they by the 
 hilt as they fall : and amidst it all the Woodlanders now stand 
 silent , unmoving , as man abide the word of onset . 

 as for that brother and sister : the Sun - beam flush red all over 
 her face , and press her hand to her bosom , and then the passion of 
 tear over - master she , and her breast heave , and the tear gush 
 out of her eye , and her body be shake with weeping . but folk- 
 might sit still , look straight before he , his eye glitter , 
 his tooth set , his right hand clutch hard at the hilt of his 
 sword , which lie naked across his knee . and the Bride , who stand 
 clothe in her begemmed and glitter war - array in the forefront of the 
 Men of the Steer , nigh unto the seat of the chieftain , beheld folk- 
 might , and her face flush and brighten , and still she look upon 
 he . the Alderman 's face be as of one pleased and proud ; yet be 
 its joy shadow as it be by a cloud of compassion . face - of - god 
 sit like the very image of the War - god , and stir not , nor look 
 toward the Sun - beam ; for still the thought of the after - grief of 
 battle , and the death of friend and folk that love he , lie heavy 
 on his heart , for all that it beat wildly at the shouting of the man . 



 CHAPTER XXXVIII . of the GREAT FOLK - MOTE : atonement give , and MEN 
 make sackles 



 amidst the clamour uprose the Alderman ; for it be clear to all man 
 that the Folk - mote should be holden at once , and the matter of the 
 War , and the Fellowship , and the choosing of the war - leader , speedily 
 deal with . so the Alderman fall to hallow in the Folk - mote : he 
 go up to the Altar of the Gods , and take the gold - ring off it , and 
 do it on his arm ; then he draw his sword and wave it toward the 
 four airt , and spake ; and the noise and shouting fall , and there be 
 silence but for he : 

 ' herewith I hallow in this Folk - mote of the Men of the Dale and the 
 Sheepcotes and the Woodland , in the name of the Warrior and the 
 Earth - god and the Fathers of the kindred . now let not the peace of 
 the Mote be break . let not man rise against man , or bear blade or 
 hand , or stick or stone against any . if any man break the Peace of 
 the Holy Mote , let he be a man accurse , a wild - beast in the Holy 
 Places ; an outcast from home and hearth , from bed and board , from 
 mead and acre ; not to be holpen with bread , nor flesh , nor wine ; nor 
 flax , nor wool , nor any cloth ; nor with sword , nor shield , nor axe , 
 nor plough - share ; nor with horse , nor ox , nor ass ; with no saddle- 
 beast nor draught - beast ; nor with wain , nor boat , nor way - lead ; 
 nor with fire nor water ; nor with any world 's wealth . thus let he 
 who hath cast out man be cast out by man . now be hallow - in the 
 Folk - mote of the Men of the Dale and the Sheepcotes and the 
 Woodlands . ' 

 Therewith he wave his sword again toward the four airt , and go 
 and sit down in his place . but presently he arise again , and say : 

 ' now if man hath aught to say against man , and claimeth boot of any , 
 or would lay guilt on any man 's head , let he come forth and declare 
 it ; and the judge shall be name , and the case shall be try this 
 afternoon or to - morrow . yet first I shall tell you that I , the 
 Alderman of the Dalesmen , doom one Iron - face of the House of the 
 Face to pay a double fine , for that he draw a sword at the Gate - thing 
 of Burgstead with the intent to break the peace thereof . Thou , 
 green - sleeve , bring forth the peace - breaker 's fine , that iron - face 
 may lay the same on the Altar . ' 

 then come forth a man from the man of the face bear a bag , and he 
 bring it to iron - face , who go up to the Altar and pour forth 
 weigh gold from the bag thereon , and say : 

 ' Warden of the Dale , come thou and weigh it ! ' 

 ' nay , ' quoth the Warden , ' it needeth not , no man here doubteth thee , 
 Alderman Iron - face . ' 

 a murmur of yeasay go up , and none have a word to say against the 
 Alderman , but they praise he rather : also man be eager to hear 
 of the war , and the fellowship , and to be do with these petty 
 matter . then the Alderman rise again and say : 

 ' hath any man a grief against any other of the Kindreds of the Dale , 
 or the Sheepcotes , or the Woodlands ? ' 

 none answer or stir ; so after he have wait a while , he say : 

 ' be there any who hath any guilt to lay against a Stranger , an 
 Outlander , be such a man as he deem we can come at ? ' 

 Thereat be a stir amongst the Men of the Fleece of the Shepherds , 
 and their rank open , and there come forth an ill - favour lean old 
 man , long - nebbe , blear - eye , and bent , girt with a rusty old sword , 
 but not otherwise armed . and all man know Penny - thumb , who have be 
 ransack last autumn . as he come forth , it seem as if his 
 neighbour have be try to hold he back ; but a stout , broad- 
 shoulder man , black - haired and red - bearded , make way for the old 
 man , and lead he out of the throng , and stand by he ; and this man 
 be well armed at all point , and look a doughty carle . he stand 
 side by side with Penny - thumb , right in front of the man of his 
 house , and look about he at first somewhat uneasily , as though he 
 be ashamed of his fellow ; but though many smile , none laugh 
 aloud ; and they forbore , partly because they know the man to be a 
 good man , partly because of the solemn tide of the Folk - mote , and 
 partly in sooth because they wish all this to be over , and be as 
 man who have no time for empty mirth . 

 then say the Alderman : ' what would thou , Penny - thumb , and thou , 
 Bristler , son of Brightling ? ' 

 then penny - thumb begin to speak in a high squeaky voice : 

 ' Alderman , and Lord of the Folk ! ' but therewithal Bristle , pull 
 he back , and say : 

 ' I be the man who hath take this quarrel upon I , and have swear 
 upon the Holy Boar to carry this feud through ; and we deem , Alderman , 
 that if they who slay Rusty and ransack Penny - thumb be not know 
 now , yet they soon may be . ' 

 as he spake , come forth those three man of the Shepherds and the two 
 Dalesmen who have swear with he on the Holy Boar . then up stand 
 folk - might , and come forth into the field , and say : 

 ' Bristler , son of Brightling , and ye other good man and true , it be 
 but sooth that the ransacker and the slayer may soon be know ; and 
 here I declare they unto you : I it be and none other who slay 
 Rusty ; and I be the leader of those who ransack Penny - thumb , and 
 cow Harts - bane of Greentofts . as for the slaying of Rusty , I slay 
 he because he chase I , and would not forbear , so that I must 
 either slay or be slay , as hath befall I erewhile , and will 
 befall again , methink . as for the ransacking of Penny - thumb , I 
 need the good that I take , and he need they not , since he 
 neither use they , nor give they away , and , they be go , he hath 
 live no worse than aforetime . now I say , that if ye will take the 
 outlawry off I , which , as I hear , ye lay upon I , not know I , 
 then will I handsel self - doom to thee , Bristler , if thou wilt bear 
 thy grief to purse , and I will pay thee what thou wilt out of hand ; 
 or if perchance thou wilt call I to Holm , thither will I go , if thou 
 and I come unslain out of this war . as to the ransack and cowing 
 of Harts - bane , I say that I be sackless therein , because the man be 
 but a ruffler and a man of violence , and hath cow many man of the 
 Dale ; and if he gainsay I , then do I call he to the Holm after this 
 war be over ; either he or any man who will take his place before my 
 sword . ' 

 then he hold his peace , and man spake to man , and a murmur arose , as 
 they say for the more part that it be a fair and manly offer . but 
 Bristler call his fellow and penny - thumb to he , and they spake 
 together ; and sometimes Penny - thumb 's shrill squeak be hear above 
 the deep - voice talk of the other ; for he be a man that harbour 
 malice . but at last Bristler spake out and say : 

 ' tall man , we know that thou art a chieftain and of good will to the 
 man of the Dale and their friend , and that want drave thee to the 
 ransacking , and need to the manslaying , and neither the living nor 
 the dead to whom thou art guilty be to be call good man ; therefore 
 will I bring the matter to purse , if thou wilt handsel I self - doom . ' 

 ' yea , even so let it be , ' quoth Folk - might ; and step forward and 
 take Bristler by the hand , and handsel he self - doom . then say 
 Bristler : 

 ' though Rusty be no good man , and though he follow thee to slay 
 thee , yet be he in his right therein , since he be follow up his 
 goodman 's gear ; therefore shalt thou pay a full blood - wite for he , 
 that be to say , the worth of three hundred in weed - stuff in whatso 
 good thou wilt . as for the ransacking of Penny - thumb , he shall deem 
 himself well pay if thou give he our hundred in weed - stuff for 
 that which thou didst borrow of he . ' 

 then penny - thumb set up his squeak again , but no man hearken to 
 he , and each man say to his neighbour that it be well doomed of 
 Bristler , and neither too much nor too little . but folk - might bade 
 Wood - wont to bring thither to he that which he have bear to the 
 Mote ; and he bring forth a big sack , and folk - might empty it on 
 the earth , and lo ! the silver ring of the slain felon , and they lay 
 in a heap on the green field , and they be the good of silver . then 
 the elder of the Dale - warden weigh out from the heap the blood- 
 wite for Rusty , accord to the due measure of the hundred in weed- 
 stuff , and deliver it unto Bristler . and Folk - might say : 

 ' draw nigh now , Penny - thumb , and take what thou wilt of this gear , 
 which I need not , and grudge not at I henceforward . ' 

 but Penny - thumb be afraid , and abode where he be ; and Bristler 
 laugh , and say : ' take it , goodman , take it ; spare not other man 's 
 good as thou dost thine own . ' 

 and folk - might stand by , smile faintly : so penny - thumb pluck up 
 a heart , and draw nigh trembling , and take what he durst from that 
 heap ; and all that stand by say that he have get a full double of 
 what have be award to he . but as for he , he go his way 
 straight from the mote - stead , and make no stay till he have get he 
 home , and lay the silver up in a strong coffer ; and thereafter he 
 bewail he sorely that he have not take the double of that which he 
 take , since none would have say he nay . 

 when he be go , the Alderman arise and say : 

 ' now , since the fine have be pay duly and freely , accord to 
 the dooming of Bristler , take we off the outlawry from Folk - might and 
 his fellow , and account they to be sackless before we . ' 

 then he call for other case ; but no man have aught more to bring 
 forward against any man , either of the kindred or the Strangers . 



 CHAPTER XXXIX . of the GREAT FOLK - MOTE : MEN take REDE of the war- 
 FARING , the fellowship , and the WAR - LEADER . folk - might TELLETH 
 whence his PEOPLE come . the FOLK - MOTE sunder 



 now a great silence fall upon the throng , and they stand as man 
 abide some new matter . unto they arise the Alderman , and say : 

 ' man of the Dale , and ye Shepherds and Woodlanders ; it be well know 
 to you that we have foeman in the wood and beyond it ; and now have we 
 get sure tiding , that they will not abide at home or in the wood , 
 but be minded to fall upon we at home . now therefore I will not ask 
 you whether ye will have peace or war ; for with these foeman ye may 
 have peace no otherwise save by war . but if ye think with I , three 
 thing have ye to determine : first , whether ye will abide your foe 
 in your own house , or will go meet they at theirs ; next , whether ye 
 will take to you as fellow in arm a valiant folk of the child of 
 the Gods , who be foeman to our foeman ; and lastly , what man ye will 
 have to be your war - leader . now , I bid all those here assemble , to 
 speak hereof , any man of they that will , either what they may have 
 conceive in their own mind , or what their kindre may have put into 
 their mouth to speak . ' 

 Therewith he sit down , and in a little while come forth old Hall - ward 
 of the House of the Steer , and stand before the Alderman , and say : 
 ' o Alderman , all we say : since war be awake we will not tarry , but 
 will go meet our foe while it be yet time . the valiant man of whom 
 thou tell shall be our fellow , be there but three of they . we 
 know no well war - leader than face - of - god of the House of the Face . 
 let he lead we . ' 

 Therewith he go his way ; and next come forth War - well , and say : 
 ' the House of the Bridge would have face - of - god for war - leader , these 
 tall man for fellow , and the short way to meet the foe . ' and he 
 go back to his place . 

 next come Fox of Upton , and say : ' time press , or much might be 
 speak . thus saith the House of the Bull : let we go meet the foe , 
 and take these valiant stranger for way - leader , and face - of - god for 
 war - leader . ' and he also go back again . 

 then come forth two man together , an old man and a young , and the old 
 man spake as soon as he stand still : ' the Men of the Vine bid I say 
 their will : they will not stay at home to have their house burn 
 over their head , themselves slay on their own hearth , and their 
 wife hale off to thralldom . they will take any man for their 
 fellow in arm who will smite stark stroke on their side . they know 
 face - of - god , and be liefer of he for war - leader than any other , 
 and they will follow he wheresoever he leadeth . thus my kindre 
 biddeth I say , and I hight Fork - beard of Lea . if I live through 
 this war , I shall have live through five . ' 

 Therewith he go back to his place ; but the young man lift up his 
 voice and say : ' to all this I say yea , and so be I bid by the 
 kindre of the Sickle . I be Red - beard of the Knolls , the son of my 
 father . ' and he go to his place again . 

 then come forth stone - face , and say : ' the House of the Face saith : 
 lead we through the wood , o face - of - god , thou War - leader , and ye 
 warrior of the Wolf . I be stone - face , as man know , and this word 
 hath be give to I by the kindred . ' and he take his place again . 

 then come forth together the three chief of the Shepherds , to wit 
 Hound - under - Greenbury , Strongitharm , and the Hyllier ; and 
 Strongitharm spake for all three , and say : 

 ' the Men of Greenbury , and they of the Fleece and the Thorn , be of 
 one accord , and bid we say that they be well pleased to have Face- 
 of - god for war - leader ; and that they will follow he and the warrior 
 of the Wolf to live or die with they ; and that they be ready to go 
 meet the foe at once , and will not skulk behind the wall of 
 Greenbury . ' 

 Therewith the three go back again to their place . 

 then come forth that tall man that bare the Banner of the Wolf , when 
 he have give the staff into the hand of he who stand next . he come 
 and stand over against the seat of the chieftain ; and for a while he 
 could say no word , but stand struggle with the strong passion of 
 his joy ; but at last he lift his hand aloft , and cry out in a 
 loud voice : 

 ' o war , war ! o death ! o wounding and grief ! o loss of friend and 
 kindre ! let all this be rather than the drawing back of meeting 
 hand and the sundering of yearn heart ! ' and he go back hastily 
 to his place . but from the rank of the Woodlanders run forth a 
 young man , and cry out : 

 ' as be the word of Red - wolf , so be my word , Bears - bane of Carlstead ; 
 and this be the word which our little folk hath put into our mouth ; 
 and O ! that our hand may show the meaning of our mouth ; for nought 
 else can . ' 

 then indeed go up a great shout , though many forebore to cry out ; 
 for now be they too much move for word or sound . and in special 
 be face - of - god move ; and he scarce know which way to look , lest he 
 should break out into sob and weeping ; for of late he have be much 
 among the Woodlanders , and love they much . 

 then all the noise and clamour fall , and it be to man as if they who 
 have come thither a folk , have now become an host of war . 

 but once again the Alderman rise up and spake : 

 ' now have ye yeasaid three thing : that we take face - of - god of the 
 House of the Face for our war - leader ; that we fare under weapon at 
 once against they who would murder we ; and that we take the valiant 
 Folk of the Wolf for our fellow in arm . ' 

 Therewith he stay his speech , and this time the shout arise clear 
 and most mighty , with the tossing up of sword and the clashing of 
 weapon on shield . 

 then he say : ' now , if any man will speak , here be the war - leader , 
 and here be the chief of our new friend , to answer to whatso any of 
 the kindred would have answer . ' 

 Thereon come forth the Fiddle from amongst the Men of the Sickle , and 
 draw somewhat nigh to the Alderman , and say : 

 ' Alderman , we would ask of the war - leader if he hath devise the 
 manner of our assembling , and the way of our war - fare , and the day 
 of our hosting . More than this I will not ask of he , because we wot 
 that in so great an assembly it may be that the foe may have some spy 
 of whom we wot not ; and though this be not likely , yet some folk may 
 babble ; therefore it be good for the wise to be wise everywhere and 
 always . therefore my rede it be , that no man ask any more concern 
 this , but let it lie with the war - leader to bring we face to face 
 with the foe as speedily as he may . ' 

 all man say that this be well counselled . but face - of - god arise 
 and say : ' Ye Men of the Dale , ye Shepherds and Woodlanders , 
 meseemeth the Fiddle hath speak wisely . now therefore I answer he 
 and say , that I have so order everything since the Gate - thing be 
 holden at Burgstead , that we may come face to face with the foeman by 
 the short of road . every man shall be duly summon to the 
 Hosting , and if any man fail , let it be account a shame to he for 
 ever . ' 

 a great shout follow on his word , and he sit down again . but Fox 
 of Upton come forth and say : 

 ' o Alderman , we have yeasaid the fellowship of the valiant man who 
 have come to we from out of the waste ; but this we have do , not 
 because we have know they , otherwise than by what our kinsman face- 
 of - god hath tell we concern they , but because we have see clearly 
 that they will be of much avail to we in our warfare . now , 
 therefore , if the tall chieftain who sitteth beside thee be to do 
 we to wit what he be , and whence he and his be come , it be well , 
 and fain be we thereof ; but if he listeth not to tell we , that also 
 shall be well . ' 

 then arise Folk - might in his place ; but or ever he could open his 
 mouth to speak , the tall Red - wolf strode forward bear with he the 
 Banner of the Wolf and the Sun - burst , and come and stand beside he ; 
 and the wind run through the fold of the banner , and ripple it out 
 above the head of those twain . then folk - might spake and say : 


 ' o men of the Dale and the Sheepcotes , I will do as ye bid I do ; 
 and fain be ye of the story if every deal ye know . 
 but long , long be its telling , be I to tell it all : 
 let it bide till the Cup of Deliverance ye drink from hall to hall . 

 ' like you we be of the kindred , of the Sons of the Gods we come , 
 Midst the Mid - earth 's mighty Woodland of old we have our home ; 
 but of old time we abide ' neath the mountain of the Earth , 
 o'er which the Sun ariseth to waken woe and mirth . 

 Great be we then and many ; but the long day wear we thin , 
 and war , wherein the winner hath weary work to win . 
 and the woodland wall behind we e'en like ourselves be wear , 
 and the tramp of the host of the foeman adown its glade be bear 
 on the wind that bent our wheat - field . so in the morn we rise , 
 and leave behind the stubble and the autumn - fruit close , 
 and go our way to the westward , nor turn aback to see 
 the glare of our burn house rise over brake and tree . 
 but the foe be fierce and speedy , nor long they tarry there , 
 and through the wood of battle our laden wain must fare ; 
 and the Sons of the Wolf be minishe , and the maid of the Wolf 
 wax few , 
 as amidst the victory - singing we fare the wild - wood through . 

 ' so saith the ancient story , that west and west we go , 
 and many a day of battle we have in brake , on bent ; 
 Whilst here a while we tarry , and there we hasten on , 
 and still the battle - harvest from many a folk we win . 

 ' of the tale of the day who wotteth ? of the year what man can 
 tell , 
 while the Sons of the Wolf be wander , and know not where to 
 dwell ? 
 but at last we clomb the mountain , and mickle be our toil , 
 as high the spear - wood clamber of the driver of the spoil ; 
 and tangle be the pass and the beacon flare behind , 
 and the horn of gathering onset come up upon the wind . 
 so saith the ancient story , that we stand in a mountain - cleft , 
 where the way and the valley sunder to the right hand and the 
 left . 
 there in the place of sunder all woeful be the rede ; 
 we know no land before we , and behind be heavy need . 
 as the sword cleave through the byrny , so there the mountain flank 
 Cleft through the God - kin 's people ; and ne'er again we drank 
 the wine of war together , or feast side by side 
 in the Feast - hall of the Warrior on the fruit of the battle - tide . 
 for there we turn and sunder ; unto the North we go 
 and up along the water , and the clattering stony bent ; 
 and unto the South and the Sheepcotes down go our sister 's son ; 
 and o for the year pass over since we see those valiant one ! ' 


 he cease , and lay his right hand on the banner - staff a little below 
 the left hand of Red - wolf ; and man be so keen to hear each word 
 that he spake , that there be no cry nor sound of voice when he have 
 do , only the sound of the ripple banner of the Wolf over the 
 head of those twain . the Sun - beam bow her head now , and weep 
 silently . but the Bride , she have draw her sword , and hold it 
 upright in her hand before she , and the sun smote fire from out of 
 it . 

 then it be but a little while before Red - wolf lift up his voice , 
 and sing : 


 ' hearken a wonder , o folk of the Field , 
 how they that do sunder stand shield beside shield ! 

 Lo ! the old will not and manner by fearless folk make , 
 on the Bole of the Banner the brother ' hand lay . 

 Lo ! here the token of what hath betid ! 
 grow whole be the break , find that which be hide . 

 now one way we follow whate'er shall befall ; 
 as seeketh the swallow his yesteryear 's hall . 

 seldom folk few to fight - stead hath fare ; 
 Ne'er have man truer the battle - reed bar . 

 Grey lock now I carry , and old be I grow , 
 nor look I to tarry to meet with mine own . 

 for we who remember the deed of old day 
 be nought but the ember of battle ablaze . 

 for what man might aid we ? what deed and what day 
 should come where Weird lay we aloof from the way ? 

 what man save that other of Twain rent apart , 
 our war - friend , our Brother , the piece of our heart . 

 then hearken the wonder how shield beside shield 
 the twain that do sunder wend down to the Field ! ' 


 now when he have make an end , man could no long forebear the shout ; 
 and it go up into the heaven , and be bear by the west - wind down 
 the Dale to the ear of the stay - at - home woman and man unmeet to go 
 abroad , and it quicken their blood and the spirit within they as 
 they hear it , and they smile and be fain ; for they know that 
 their kinsfolk be glad . 

 but when there be quiet on the mote - field again , Folk - might spake 
 again and say ; 


 ' it be sooth that my Brother sayeth , and that now again we wend , 
 all the Sons of the Wolf together , till the trouble hath an end . 
 but as for that tale of the ancient , it saith that we who go 
 to the northward , climb and stumble o'er many a stony bent , 
 till we happe on that isle of the waste - land , and the grass of 
 Shadowy Vale , 
 where we dwelt till we throve a little , and feel our might avail . 
 then we fare abroad from the shadow and the little - light hold , 
 and the increase fall to the valiant , and the spoil to the battle- 
 bold , 
 and never a man gainsay we with the weapon in our hand ; 
 and in Silver - dale the happy we gat us life and land . 

 ' so wear the year o'er - wealthy ; and meseemeth that ye know 
 how we sow and reap destruction , and the Day of the overthrow : 
 how we lean on the staff we have break , and put our life in the 
 hand 
 of those whom we have vanquish and the feeble of the land ; 
 and these be the stone of stumbling , and the burden not to be 
 bear , 
 when the battle - blast fall on we and our day be over - worn . 
 thus then do our wealth bewray we , and leave we wise and sad ; 
 and to you , bold man , it falleth once more to make we glad , 
 if so your heart be bid , and ye deem the deed of worth . 
 such be we ; what we shall be , ' tis yours to say henceforth . ' 


 he say furthermore : ' how great we have be I have tell you 
 already ; and ye shall see for yourself how little we be now . be it 
 enough , and will ye have we for friend and brother ? how say ye ? ' 

 they answer with shout upon shout , so that all the place and the 
 wild - wood round about be full of the voice of their crying ; but when 
 the clamour fall , then spake the Alderman and say : 

 ' Friend , and chieftain of the Wolf , thou mayst hear by this shouting 
 of the people that we have no mind to naysay our yea - say . and know 
 that it be not our use and manner to seek the strong for friend , and 
 to thrust aside the weak ; but rather to choose for our friend they 
 who be of like mind to we , man in whom we put our trust . from 
 henceforth then there be brotherhood between we ; we be your , and ye 
 be ours ; and let this endure for ever ! ' 

 then be all man full of joy ; and now at last the battle seem at 
 hand , and the peace beyond the battle . 

 then man bring the hallowed beast all garland with flower into 
 the doom - ring , and there be they slay and offer up unto the 
 Gods , to wit the Warrior , the Earth - god , and the Fathers ; and 
 thereafter be solemn feast holden on the field of the Folk - mote , and 
 all man be fain and merry . nevertheless , not all man abode there 
 the feast through ; for or ever the afternoon be well worn , be many 
 man wend along the Portway eastward toward the Upper Dale , each 
 man in his war - gear and with a scrip hang about he ; and these be 
 they who be bind for the trysting - place and the journey over the 
 waste . 

 so the Folk - mote be sunder ; and man go to their house , and 
 there abode in peace the time of their summoning ; since they wotte 
 well that the Hosting be afoot . 

 but as for the Woodlanders , who be at the Mote - stead with all their 
 folk , woman , child , and old man , they go not back again to 
 Carlstead ; but pray the neighbour of the Middle Dale to suffer 
 they to abide there awhile , which they yeasaid with a good will . so 
 the Woodlanders tilt themselves in , the more part of they , down in 
 the meadow below the mote - stead , along either side of Wildlake 's 
 Way ; but their ancient folk , and some of the woman and child , the 
 neighbour would have into their house , and the rest they furnish 
 with victual and all that they need without price , look upon 
 they as their very guest . for indeed they deem that they could 
 see that these man would never return to Carlstead , but would abide 
 with the Men of the Wolf in silver - dale , once it be win . and this 
 they deem but meet and right , yet be they sorry thereof ; for the 
 Woodlanders be well beloved of all the Dalesmen ; and now that they 
 have get to know that they be come of so noble a kindre , they 
 be well beloved yet , and more look upon . 



 CHAPTER XL . of the HOSTING in SHADOWY VALE 



 it be on the evening of the fourth day after the Folk - mote that 
 there come through the Waste to the rocky edge of Shadowy Vale a band 
 of some fifteen score of man - at - arm , and with they a multitude of 
 woman and child and old man , some afoot , some rid on ass and 
 bullock ; and with they be sumpter ass and neat laden with 
 household good , and a few goat and kine . and this be the whole 
 folk of the Woodlanders come to the Hosting in Shadowy Vale and the 
 Home of the child of the Wolf . their leader of the way be 
 Wood - father and Wood - wont and two other carle of Shadowy Vale ; and 
 Red - wolf the tall , and Bears - bane and war - grove be the captain and 
 chieftain of their company . 

 thus then they enter into the narrow pass aforesaid , which be the 
 ingate to the Vale from the Waste , and little by little its dimness 
 swallow up their long line . as they go by the place where the 
 lowering of the rock - wall give a glimpse of the valley , they look 
 down into it as face - of - god have do , but much change be there in 
 little time . there be the black wall of crag on the other side 
 stretch down to the ghyll of the great Force ; there run the deep 
 green water of the Shivering Flood ; but the grass which face - of - god 
 have see naked of everything but a few kine , thereon now the tent of 
 man stand thick . their heart swell within they as they behold it , 
 but they forebore the shout and the cry till they should be well 
 within the Vale , and so go down silently into the darkness . but as 
 their eye catch that dim image of the Wolf on the wall of the pass , 
 man point it out to man , and not a few turn and kiss it 
 hurriedly ; and to they it seem that many a kiss have be lay on 
 that dear token since the day of old , and that the hard stone have 
 be wear away by the fervent lip of man , and that the air of the 
 mirk place yet quiver with the vow swear over the sword - blade . 

 but down through the dark they go , and so come on to the stony 
 scree at the end of the pass and into the Vale ; and the whole Folk 
 save the three chieftain flow over it and stand about it down on 
 the level grass of the Vale . but those three stand yet on the top of 
 the scree , bear the war - sign of the Shaft and the Spear , and 
 betwixt they the banner of the Wolf and the Sunburst newly display 
 to the wind of Shadowy Vale . 

 up and down the Vale they look , and see before the tent of man the 
 old familiar banner of Burgdale rise and fall in the evening 
 wind . but amidst of the doom - ring be pitch a great banner , 
 whereon be do the image of the Wolf with red gap jaw on a 
 field of green ; and about he stand other banner , to wit , the silver 
 Arm on a red field , the Red Hand on a white field , and on green 
 field both , the Golden Bushel and the Ragged Sword . 

 all about the plain shone glitter war - gear of man as they move 
 hither and thither , and a stream of folk begin at once to draw toward 
 the scree to look on those new - comer ; and amidst the helmed 
 Burgdalers and the white - coat Shepherds go the tall man of the 
 Wolf , bare - head and unarmed save for their sword , mingle with the 
 fair strong woman of the kindre , treading barefoot the soft grass of 
 their own Vale . 

 presently there be a great throng gather round about the 
 Woodlanders , and each man as he join it wave hand or weapon toward 
 they , and the joy of their welcome send a confused clamour through 
 the air . then forth from the throng step Folk - might , unarmed save 
 his sword , and behind he be face - of - god , in his war - gear save his 
 helm , hand in hand with the Sun - beam , who be clothe in her goodly 
 flower green kirtle , her foot naked like her sister of the 
 kindre . 

 then folk - might cry aloud : ' a full and free greeting to our 
 brother ! well be ye , o son of our Ancient Fathers ! and to - day be 
 ye the dearer to we because we see that ye have bring we a gift , to 
 wit , your wife and child , and your grandsire unmeet for war . by 
 this token we see how great be your trust in we , and that it be your 
 meaning never to sunder from we again . o well be ye ; well be ye ! ' 

 then spake Red - wolf , and say : ' Ye Sons of the Wolf , who part from 
 we of old time in that cleft of the mountain , it be our very self 
 that we give unto you ; and these be a part of ourselves ; how then 
 should we leave they behind we ? Bear witness , o man of Burgdale and 
 the Sheepcotes , that we have become one folk with the man of Shadowy 
 Vale , never to be sunder again ! ' 

 then all that multitude shout with a loud voice ; and when the shout 
 have die away , Folk - might spake again : 

 ' o warrior of the sundering , here shall your wife and child 
 abide , while we go a little journey to rejoice our heart with the 
 hard handplay , and take to we that which we have miss : and to- 
 morrow morn be appoint for this same journey , unless ye be over 
 foot - weary with the way of the Waste . ' 

 Red - wolf smile as he answer : ' this ye say in jest , brother ; for 
 ye may see that our day 's journey hath not be over - much for our old 
 man ; how then should it weary those who may yet bear sword ? we be 
 ready for the road and eager for the handplay . ' 

 ' this be well , ' say folk - might , ' and what be to be look for . 
 therefore , brother , do ye and your counsel - mate come straightway to 
 the Hall of the Wolf ; wherein , after ye have eat and drunken , shall 
 we take counsel with our brother of Burgdale and the Sheepcotes , so 
 that all may be order for battle ! ' 

 say Red - wolf : ' Good be that , if we must need abide till to - morrow ; 
 for verily we come not hither to eat and drink and rest our body ; 
 but it must be as ye will have it . ' 

 then the Sun - beam leave the hand of face - of - god and come forward , and 
 hold out both her palm to the Woodland - folk , and spake in a voice 
 that be hear afar , though it be a woman 's , so clear and sweet it 
 be ; and she say : 

 ' o warrior of the Sundering , ye who be not need in the Hall , and 
 ye our sister with your little one and your father , come now to we 
 and down to the tent which we have array for you , and there think 
 for a little that we be all at our very home that we long for and 
 have yet to win , and be ye merry with we and make we merry . ' 

 Therewith she step forward daintily and enter into their throng , 
 and take an old man of the Woodlanders by the hand , and kiss his 
 cheek and lead he away , and the come rest seem sweet to he . and 
 then come other woman of the Vale , kind and fair and smile , and lead 
 away , some an old mother of the Wood - lander , some a young wife , some 
 a pair of lad ; and not a few forsooth kiss and embrace the stark 
 warrior , and go away with they toward the tent , which stand along 
 the side of the Shivering Flood where it be at its quiet ; for 
 there be the grass the soft and most abundant . there on the 
 green grass be table array , and lamp be hang above they on 
 spear , to be litten when the daylight should fail . and the good of 
 the victual which the Vale could give be spread on the board , along 
 with wine and dainty , buy in silver - dale , or on the edge of the 
 Westland with sword - stroke and arrow - flight . 

 there then they feast and be merry ; and the Sun - beam and Bow - may 
 and the other woman of the Vale serve they at table , and be very 
 blithe with they , caress they with soft word , and with clipping 
 and kissing , as folk who be grow exceed dear to they ; so that 
 that eve of battle be soft and sweet to they than any hour of 
 their life . with these feaster be God - swain and Spear - fist of the 
 deliver thrall of silver - dale as glad as glad might be ; but wolf- 
 stone their eld be go with Dallach to the Council in the Hall . 

 the man of Burgdale and the Shepherds feast otherwhere in all 
 content , nor lack folk of the Vale to serve they . amongst the man 
 of the Face be the ten deliver thrall who have heart to meet 
 their master in arm : seven of they be of Rose - dale and three of 
 silver - dale . 

 the Bride be with she kindre of the Steer , with whom be many man 
 of Shadowy Vale , and she serve her friend and fellow clothe in her 
 war - gear , save helm and hauberk , bear herself as one who be 
 serve dear guest . and man equal she for her beauty to the Gods 
 of the High Place and the chooser of the Slain ; and they who have not 
 beheld she before marvel at she , and her loveliness hold all man 's 
 heart in a net of desire , so that they forebore their meat to gaze 
 upon she ; and if perchance her hand touch some young man , or her 
 cheek or sweet - breathe mouth come nigh to his face , he become 
 bewildered and wist not where he be , nor what to do . yet be she as 
 lowly and simple of speech and demeanour as if she be a gooseherd 
 of fourteen winter . 

 in the Hall be a goodly company , and all the leader of the Folk 
 be therein , and folk - might and the war - leader sit in the midst 
 of those stone seat on the day . there then they agree on the 
 whole ordering of the battle and the wending of the host , as shall be 
 tell later on ; and this matter be long a - doing , and when it be 
 do , man go to their place to sleep , for the night be well worn . 

 but when man have depart and all be still , folk - might , light - clad 
 and without a weapon , leave the Hall and walk briskly toward the 
 nether end of the Vale . he pass by all the tent , the last whereof 
 be of the House of the Steer , and come to a place where be a great 
 rock rise straight up from the plain like sheaf of black stave 
 stand close together ; and it be call Staff - stone , and tale of 
 the elf have be tell concern it , so that stone - face have behold 
 it gladly the day before . 

 the moon be just shin into Shadowy Vale , and the grass be bright 
 wheresoever the shadow of the high cliff be not , and the face of 
 Staff - stone shine bright grey as Folk - might come within sight of it , 
 and he behold someone sit at the base of the rock , and as he draw 
 nigh he see that it be a woman , and know she for the Bride ; for he 
 have pray she to abide he there that night , because it be nigh to 
 the tent of the House of the Steer ; and his heart be glad as he 
 draw nigh to she . 

 she sit quietly on a fragment of the black rock , clothe as she have be 
 all day , in her glitter kirtle , but without hauberk or helm , a 
 wreath of wind - flower about her head , her foot cross over each 
 other , her hand lay palm uppermost in her lap . she move not as he 
 draw nigh , but say in a gentle voice when he be close to she : 

 ' Chief of the Wolf , great warrior , thou would speak with I ; and 
 good it be that friend should talk together on the eve of battle , 
 when they may never meet alive again . ' 

 he say : ' my talk shall not be long ; for thou and I both must sleep 
 to - night , since there be work to hand to - morrow . now since , as thou 
 sayest , o fairest of woman , we may never meet again alive , I ask thee 
 now at this hour , when we both live and be near to one another , to 
 suffer I to speak to thee of my love of thee and desire for thee . 
 surely thou , who art the sweetest of all thing the Gods and the 
 kindred have make , wilt not gainsay I this ? ' 

 she say very sweetly , yet smile : ' brother of my father 's son , 
 how can I gainsay thee thy speech ? Nay , hast thou not say it ? what 
 more canst thou add to it that will have fresh meaning to mine ear ? ' 

 he say : ' thou say sooth : might I then but kiss thine hand ? ' 

 she say , no long smile : ' yea surely , even so may all man do who 
 can be call my friend - and thou art much my friend . ' 

 he take her hand and kiss it , and hold it thereafter ; nor do she 
 draw it away . the moon shine brightly on they ; but by its light he 
 could not see if she redden , but he deem that her face be 
 troubled . then he say : ' it be well for I if I might kiss thy 
 face , and take thee in mine arm . ' 

 then say she : ' this only shall a man do with I when I long to do 
 the like with he . and since thou art so much my friend , I will tell 
 thee that as for this longing , I have it not . bethink thee what a 
 little while it be since the lack of another man 's love grieve I 
 sorely . ' 

 ' the time be short , ' say folk - might , ' if we tell up the hour 
 thereof ; but in that short space have a many thing betid . ' 

 she say : ' dost thou know , canst thou guess , how sorely ashamed I 
 go amongst my people ? I durst look no man in the face for the 
 aching of mine heart , which methought all might see through my face . ' 

 ' I know it well , ' he say ; ' yet of I wert thou not ashamed but a 
 little while ago , when thou didst tell I of thy grief . ' 

 she say : ' true it be ; and thou wert kind to I . Thou didst become 
 a dear friend to I , methought . ' 

 ' and wilt thou hurt a dear friend ? ' say he . 

 ' o no , ' she say , ' if I might do otherwise . yet how if I might not 
 choose ? shall there be no forgiveness for I then ? ' 

 he answer nothing ; and still he hold her hand that strove not to be 
 go from his , and she cast down her eye . then he spake in a while : 

 ' my friend , I have be think of thee and of I ; and now hearken : 
 if thou wilt declare that thou feel no sweetness embrace thine 
 heart when I say that I desire thee sorely , as now I say it ; or when 
 I kiss thine hand , as now I kiss it ; or when I pray thee to suffer I 
 to cast mine arm about thee and kiss thy face , as now I pray it : if 
 thou wilt say this , then will I take thee by the hand straightway , 
 and lead thee to the tent of the House of the Steer , and say 
 farewell to thee till the battle be over . Canst thou say this out of 
 the truth of thine heart ? ' 

 she say : ' what then if I can not say this word ? what then ? ' 

 but he answer nothing ; and she sit still a little while , and then 
 arise and stand before he , look he in the eye , and say : 

 ' I can not say it . ' 

 then he catch she in his arm and strain she to he , and then 
 kiss her lip and her face again and again , and she strove not with 
 he . but at last she say : 

 ' yet after all this shalt thou lead I back to my folk straight - way ; 
 and when the battle be do , if both we be live , then shall we 
 speak more thereof . ' 

 so he take her hand and lead she on toward the tent of the Steer , and 
 for a while he spake nought ; for he doubt himself , what he should 
 say ; but at last he spake : 

 ' now be this well for I than if it have not be , whether I live or 
 whether I die . yet thou hast not say that thou lov I and 
 desir I . ' 

 ' Wilt thou compel I ? ' she say . ' to - night I may not say it . who 
 shall say what word my lip shall fashion when we stand together 
 victorious in silver - dale ; then indeed may the time seem long from 
 now . ' 

 he say : ' yea , true be that ; yet once again I say that so measured 
 long and long be the time since first I see thee in Burgdale before 
 thou knewest I . yet now I will not bicker with thee , for be sure 
 that I be glad at heart . and lo you ! our foot have bring we to the 
 tent of thy people . all good go with thee ! ' 

 ' and with thee , sweet friend , ' she say . then she linger a little , 
 turn her head toward the tent , and then turn her face toward 
 he and lay her hand on his neck , and draw his head adown to she and 
 kiss his cheek , and therewith swiftly and lightly depart from 
 he . 

 now the night wear and the morning come ; and face - of - god be abroad 
 very early in the morning , as his custom be ; and he wash the night 
 from off he in the Carles ' Bath of the Shivering Flood , and then 
 go round through the encampment of the host , and see none stir 
 save here and there the last watchman of the night . he spake with 
 one or two of these , and then go up to the head of the Vale , where 
 be the pass that lead to silver - dale ; and there he see the watch , and 
 spake with they , and they tell he that none have as yet come forth 
 from the pass , and he bid they to blow the horn of warn to rouse 
 up the host as soon as the messenger come thence . for forerunner 
 have be send up the pass , and have be set to hold watch at diver 
 place therein to pass on the word from place to place . 

 Thence go face - of - god back toward the Hall ; but when he be yet 
 some way from it , he see a slender glitter warrior come forth from 
 the door thereof , who stand for a moment look round about , and 
 then come lightly and swiftly toward he ; and lo ! it be the sun- 
 beam , with a long hauberk over her kirtle fall below her knee , a 
 helm on her head and plat shoe on her foot . she come up to he , 
 and lay her hand to his cheek and the golden lock of his head   for 
 he be bare - headed , and say to he , smile : 

 ' gold - mane ! thou badest I bear arm , and Folk - might also constrain 
 I thereto . Lo thou ! ' 

 say face - of - god : ' folk - might be wise then , even as I be ; and 
 forsooth as thou art . for bethink thee if the bow draw at a venture 
 should speed the eyeless shaft against thy breast , and send I forth 
 a wanderer from my Folk ! for how could I bear the sight of the fair 
 Dale , and no hope to see thee again therein ? ' 

 she say : ' the heart be light within I to - day . Deemest thou that 
 this be strange ? or dost thou call to mind that which thou spak 
 the other day , that it be of no avail to stand in the doom - ring of 
 the Folk and bear witness against ourselves ? this will I not . this 
 be no light - mindedness that thou beholdest in I , but the valiancy 
 that the Fathers have set in mine heart . deem not , O gold - mane , fear 
 not , that we shall die before they dight the bride - bed for we . ' 

 he would have kiss her mouth , but she put he away with her hand , 
 and doff her helm and lay it on the grass , and say : 

 ' this be not the last time that thou shalt kiss I , gold - mane , my 
 dear ; and yet I long for it as if it be , so high as the father 
 have raise I up this morn above fear and sadness . ' 

 he say nought , but draw she to he , and wonder so move he , that he 
 look long and closely at her face before he kiss she ; and 
 forsooth he could find no blemish in it : it be as if it be but 
 new come from the smithy of the Gods , and exceed longing take hold 
 of he . but even as their lip meet , from the head of the Vale come 
 the voice of the great horn ; and it be answer straightway by the 
 watcher all down the tent ; and presently arise the shout of man 
 and the clash of weapon as folk arm themselves , and laughter 
 therewith , for most man be battle - merry , and the cry of woman 
 shrilly - clear as they hasten about , busy over the morning meal 
 before the departure of the Host . but face - of - god say softly , still 
 caress the Sun - beam , and she he : 

 ' thus then we depart from this Valley of the Shadows , but as thou 
 saidst when first we meet therein , there shall be no sundering of thee 
 and I , but thou shalt go down with I to the battle . ' 

 and he lead she by the hand into the Hall of the Wolf , and there they 
 eat a morsel , and thereafter face - of - god tarry not , but busy 
 himself along with Folk - might and the other chieftain in array 
 the Host for departure . 



 CHAPTER XLI . the host DEPARTETH from SHADOWY VALE : the first DAY 'S 
 JOURNEY 



 it be about three hour before noon that the Host begin to enter 
 into the pass out of Shadowy Vale by the river - side ; and the woman 
 and child , and man unfightworthy , stand on the high ground at 
 the foot of the cliff to see the Host wend on the way . of these a 
 many be of the Woodlanders , who be now one folk with they of 
 Shadowy Vale . and all these have choose to abide tiding in the Vale , 
 deeming that there be little danger therein , since that last 
 slaughter which folk - might have make of the Dusky Men ; albeit face - of- 
 god have offer to send they all to Burgstead with two score and ten 
 man - at - arm to guard they by the way and to eke out the warder of 
 the Burg . 

 now the fighting - man of Shadowy Vale be two long hundred lack 
 five ; of whom two score and ten be woman , and three score and ten 
 lad under twenty winter ; but the woman , though you might scarce see 
 fairer of face and body , be doughty in arm , all good shooter in 
 the bow ; and the swain be eager and light - foot , cragsman of the 
 good , will not to scale the cliff of the Vale in search of the nest 
 of gerfalcon and such - like fowl , and swim the strong stream of 
 the Shivering Flood ; tough body and wiry , strong than most grow 
 man , and as fearless as the good . 

 the order of the departure of the Host be this : 

 the Woodlanders go first into the pass , and with they be two 
 score of the ripe warrior of the Wolf . then come of the kindred of 
 Burgdale , the Men of the Steer , the Bridge , and the Bull ; then the 
 Men of the Vine and the Sickle ; then the Shepherd - folk ; and lastly , 
 the Men of the face lead by stone - face and hall - face . with these go 
 another two score of the dweller in Shadowy Vale , and the rest be 
 scatter up and down the band of the Host to guide they into the 
 good path and to make the way easy to they . face - of - god be 
 sunder from his kindred , and go along with Folk - might in the 
 forefront of the Host , while his father the Alderman go as a simple 
 man - at - arm with his House in the rearward . the Sun - beam follow 
 her brother and face - of - god amidst the warrior of the Wolf , and with 
 she be Bow - may clothe in the Alderman 's gift , and Wood - father and his 
 child . Bow - may have cause she to doff her hauberk for that day , 
 whereon they look to fall in with no foeman . as for the Bride , she 
 go with she kindre in all her war - gear ; and the morning sun shine 
 in the gem of her apparel , and her jewel foot fall like flower 
 upon the deep grass of the upper Vale , and shine strange and bright 
 amongst the black stone of the pass . she bear a quiver at she back 
 and a shin yew bow in her hand , and go amongst the bowman , for 
 she be a very deft archer . 

 so fare they into the pass , leave peace behind they , with all 
 their banner display , and the banner of the Red - mouthed Wolf go 
 with the Wolf and the Sun - burst in the forefront of their battle next 
 after the two captain . 

 as for their road , the grassy space between the rock - wall and the 
 water be wide and smooth at first , and the cliff rise up like 
 bundle of spear - shafts high and clear from the green grass with no 
 confused litter of fall stone ; so that the man stride on briskly , 
 their heart high - raise and full of hope . and as they go , the 
 sweetness of song stir in their soul , and at last Bow - may fall to 
 sing in a loud clear voice , and her cousin wood - wise answer she , 
 and all the warrior of the Wolf who be in their band fall into the 
 song at the ending , and the sound of their melody go down the water 
 and reach the ear of those that be enter the pass , and of 
 those who be abide till the way should be clear of they : and 
 this be some of what they sing : 


 Bow - may singeth : 

 hear ye never a voice come cry 
  out from the waste where the wind fare wide ? 
 ' son of the Wolf , the day be die , 
  and where in the cleft of the rock do ye hide ? 

 ' into your hand hath the Sword be give , 
  Hard be the palm with the kiss of the hilt ; 
 through the trackless waste hath the road be rive 
  for the blade to seek to the heart of the guilt . 

 ' and yet ye bide and yet ye tarry ; 
  dear deem ye the sleep ' twixt hearth and board , 
 and sweet the maiden mouth ye marry , 
  and bright the blade of the bloodless sword . ' 

 wood - wise singeth : 

 Yea , here we dwell in the arm of our mother 
  the Shadowy Queen , and the hope of the Waste ; 
 here first we come , when never another 
  adown the rocky stair make haste . 

 Far be the foe , and no sword beholdeth 
  what deed we work and whither we wend ; 
 Dear be the day , and the Year enfoldeth 
  the love of our life from end to end . 

 Voice of our father , why will ye move we , 
  and call up the sun our sword to behold ? 
 why will ye cry on the foeman to prove we ? 
  why will ye stir up the heart of the bold ? 

 Bow - may singeth : 

 purblind be I , the voice of the chiding ; 
  then tell I what be the thing ye bear ? 
 what be the gift that your hand be hide , 
  the gold - adorn , the dread and dear ? 

 wood - wise singeth : 

 Dark in the sheath lie the Anvil 's Brother , 
  Hid be the hammered Death of Men . 
 would ye look on the gift of the green - clothe Mother ? 
  how then shall ye ask for a gift again ? 

 the warrior sing : 

 show we the Sunlight the Gift of the Mother , 
  as foot follow foot to the foeman 's den ! 
 Gleam Sun , breathe Wind , on the Anvil 's Brother , 
  for bare be the hammered Death of Men . 


 Therewith they shake their naked sword in the air , and fare on 
 eagerly , and as swiftly as the pass would have they fare . but so it 
 be , that when the rearward of the Host be enter the first of the 
 pass , and be go on the wide smooth sward , the vanward be get 
 to where there be but a narrow space clear betwixt water and cliff ; 
 for otherwhere be a litter of great rock and small , hard to be 
 thread even by those who know the pass well ; so that man have to 
 tread along the very verge of the Shivering Flood , and wary must they 
 be , for the water run swift and deep betwixt bank of sheer rock half 
 a fathom below their very foot - sol , which have but bare space to go 
 on the narrow a way . so it hold on for a while , and then get safe , 
 and there be more space for go betwixt cliff and flood ; albeit it 
 be toilsome enough , since for some way yet there be a drift of 
 stone to cumber their foot , some big and some little , and some very 
 big . after a while the way grow well , though here and there , where 
 the cliff lower , be wide scree of loose stone that they must 
 need climb up and down . thereafter for a space be there an end of 
 the stony cumber , but the way betwixt the river and the cliff 
 narrow again , and the black crag grow high , and at last so 
 exceed high , and the way so narrow , that the sky overhead be to 
 they as though they be at the bottom of a well , and man deem that 
 thence they could see the star at noontide . for some time withal 
 have the way be mount up and up , though the cliff grow high 
 over it ; till at last they be but go on a narrow shelf , the 
 Shivering Flood swirl and rattle far below they betwixt sheer 
 rock - wall grow exceed high ; and above they the cliff go up 
 towards the heaven as black as a moonless starless night of winter . 
 and as the flood thunder below , so above they roar the ceaseless 
 thunder of the wind of the pass , that blow exceed fierce down that 
 strait place ; so that the skirt of their garment be wrap about 
 their knee by it , and their foot be well - nigh stay at while as 
 they breast the push thereof . 

 but as they mount higher and high yet , the noise of the water 
 swell into a huge roar that drown the bellowing of the prisone 
 wind , and down the pass come drift a fine rain that fall not from 
 the sky , for between the cloud of that drift could folk see the 
 heaven bright and blue above they . this rain be but the spray of 
 the great force up to whose step they be climb . 

 now the way get rough as they mount ; but this toil be cause by 
 their gain ; for the rock - wall , which thrust out a buttress there as 
 if it would have go to the very edge of the gap where - through the 
 flood run , and so have cut the way off utterly , be here somewhat 
 break down , and its stone scatter down the steep bent , so that 
 there be a passage , though a toilsome one . 

 thus then through the wind - bear drift of the great force , through 
 which man could see the white water toss down below , amidst the 
 clattering thunder of the Shivering Flood and the rumble of the wind

 of wind and water . 

 a little far on and all that turmoil be behind they ; the sun ,

 now when the man of the Host look from the high place whereon they
