 CHAPTER LIV . tiding of dallach : a FOLK - MOTE in SILVER - DALE 



 three day thereafter come two swift runner from Rose - dale with 
 tiding of Dallach . in all wise have he thriven , and have slay many 
 of the runaway , and have come happily to rose - dale : therein by the 
 mere shaking of their sword have they all their will ; for there be 
 but a few of the Dusky Warriors in the Dale , since the more part have 
 fare to the slaughter in silver - stead . now therefore have Dallach 
 be make Alderman of Rose - dale ; and the Burgdalers who have go with 
 he should abide the come thither of the rest of the Burgdale Host , 
 and meantime of their come should uphold the new Alderman in Rose- 
 dale . Howbeit Dallach send word that it be not to be doubt but 
 that many of the Dusky Men have escape to the wood , and should yet 
 be the death of many a mother 's son , unless it be well look to . 

 and now the more part of the burgdale man and the Shepherds begin to 
 look toward home , albeit some amongst they have not be ill - pleased 
 to abide there yet a while ; for life be exceed soft to they 
 there , though they help the poor folk gladly in their husbandry . 
 for especially the woman of the Dale , of whom many be very goodly , 
 hanker after the fair - faced tall Burgdalers , and be as kind to 
 they as might be . Forsooth not a few , both carle and queen , of the 
 old thrall - folk pray they of Burgdale to take they home thither , 
 that they might see new thing and forget their old torment once for 
 all , yea , even in dream . the Burgdalers would not gainsay they , and 
 there be no one else to hinder ; so that there go with the burgdale 
 man at their departure hard on five score of the silver - dale folk who 
 be not of the kindred . 

 and now be a great Folk - mote holden in silver - dale , whereto the 
 burgdale man and the Shepherds be bid ; and thereat the War- 
 leader give out the morrow of the morrow for the day of the departure 
 of the Host . there also be the matter of silver - dale duly 
 order : the Men of the Wolf would have have the Woodlanders dwell 
 with they in the fair - build stead , and take to they of the goodly 
 stone house there what they would ; but this they naysaid , choose 
 rather to dwell in scattered house , which they build for themselves 
 at the utmost limit of the tillage . 

 indeed , the most abode not even there a long while ; for they love 
 the wood and its deed . so they go forth into the wood , and 
 clear they space to dwell in , and build they hall such as they 
 love , and fall to their old woodland craft of charcoal - burning and 
 hunting , wherein they throve well . and good for silver - dale be 
 their abide there , since they become a sure defence and stout 
 outpost against all foeman . for the rest , wheresoever they dwelt , 
 they be guest - cherishing and blithe , and be well belove by all 
 people ; and they wed with the other Houses of the child of the 
 Wolf . 

 as to the other matter whereof they take rede at this Folk - mote , 
 they have mostly to do with the warding of the Dale , and the learning 
 of the deliver thrall to handle weapon duly . for man deem it 
 most like that they would have to meet other man of the kindred of 
 the Felons ; which indeed fall out as the year wear . 

 moreover , Folk - might   by the rede of Stone - face   send messenger to 
 the Plain and the Cities , unto man whom he know there , do they to 
 wit of the tiding of silver - dale , and how that a peaceful and guest- 
 love people , have good store of ware , now dwelt therein , so that 
 chapman might have recourse thither . 

 lastly spake Folk - might and say : 

 ' guest and brother - in - arm , we have be look about our new 
 house , which be our old one , and therein we find great store of 
 ware which we need not , and which we can but use if ye use they . of 
 your kindness therefore we pray you to take of those thing what ye 
 can easily carry . and if ye say the way be long , as indeed it be , 
 since ye be bent on go through the wood to rose - dale , and so on 
 to Burgdale , yet shall we furnish you with beast to bear your good , 
 and with such wain as may pass through the woodland way . ' 

 then rise up Fox of Upton and say : ' o folk - might , and ye man of the 
 Wolf , be it know unto you , that if we have do anything for your 
 help in the winning of silver - dale , we have thus do that we might 
 help ourselves also , so that we might live in peace henceforward , and 
 that we might have your friendship and fellowship therewithal , so 
 that here in Silver - dale might wax a mighty folk who join unto we 
 should be strong enough to face the whole world . such be the rede 
 of wise man when they go a - warring . but we have no will to go back 
 home again make rich with your wealth ; this hath be far from our 
 thought in this matter . ' 

 and there go up a murmur from all the Burgdalers yeasaye his 
 word . 

 but folk - might take up the word again and spake : 

 ' Men of Burgdale and the Sheepcotes , what ye say be both manly and 
 friendly ; yet , since we look to see a road make plain through the 
 woodland betwixt Burgdale and silver - dale , and that often ye shall 
 face we in the feast - hall , and while stand beside we in the fray , we 
 must need pray you not to shame we by depart empty - handed ; for 
 how then may we look upon your face again ? stone - face , my friend , 
 thou art old and wise ; therefore I bid thee to help we herein , and 
 speak for we to thy kindred , that they naysay we not in this matter . ' 

 then stand up stone - face and say : ' Forsooth , friend , Folk - might be 
 in the right herein ; for he may look for anger from the wight that 
 come and go betwixt his kindre and the Gods , if they see we fare 
 back giftless through the wood . moreover , now that ye have see 
 silver - dale , ye may wot how rich a land it be of all good thing , and 
 able to bring forth enough and to spare . and now meseemeth the god 
 love this folk that shall dwell here ; and they shall become a mighty 
 Folk , and a part of our very self . therefore let we take the gift 
 of our friend , and thank they blithely . for surely , as saith folk- 
 might , henceforth the wood shall become a road betwixt we , and the 
 thicket a halting - place for friend bear goodwill in their hand . ' 

 when he have speak , man yeasaid his word and forbore the gift no 
 long ; and the Folk - mote sunder in all love - kindness . 



 CHAPTER LV . departure from SILVER - DALE 



 on morrow of the morrow be the burgdale man and they of the 
 Shepherds gather together in the market - stead early in the morning , 
 and they be all ready for departure ; and the man of the Wolf and 
 the Woodlanders , and of the deliver thrall a great many , stand 
 round about they grieve that they must go . there be much talk 
 between the folk of the Dale and the Guests , and many promise be 
 give and take to come and go betwixt the two Dales . there also 
 be the man of the thrall - folk who be to wend home with the 
 Burgdalers ; and they have be stuff with good thing by the man of 
 the kindred , and be as fain as might be . 

 as for the Sun - beam , she be somewhat out of herself at first , be 
 eager and restless beyond she will not , and yet at while weeping - ripe 
 when she call to mind that she be now leave all those thing , 
 the gain whereof have be a dream to she both wake and sleep for 
 these year past . but at last , as she stand in the door of the Mote- 
 house , and behold all the throng of folk happy and friendly , it come 
 over she that she herself have do her full share to bring all this 
 about , and that all those pleasant place of silver - dale now full of 
 the goodly life of man would be there even as she have strive for 
 they , and that they would be a part of she leave behind , though she 
 be dwell otherwhere . 

 Therewithal she say to herself that it be now her part to wield the 
 life of man in Burgdale , and begin once more her day of a chieftain 
 and a swayer of the Folk , and the life of a stir woman , which the 
 edge of the sword and the need of the hard hand - play have take out of 
 her hand for a while , make she as a child in the hand of the 
 strong wielder of the blade . 

 so now she become calm once more , and her face be clothe again with 
 the full measure of that majesty of beauty which have once overawe 
 face - of - god amidst his love of she ; and folk behold she and marvel 
 at her fairness , and say : ' she hath an inward sorrow at leave the 
 fair Dale wherein her father dwelt , and where her mother 's ash lie 
 in earth . ' albeit now be her sorrow but little , and much be her 
 hope , and her foresight of day to be ; though all the Dale , yea , 
 every leaf and twig of it whereby her foot have ever pass , and each 
 stone of the fair house , be to she as a picture that she could look 
 on from henceforth for ever . 

 of the Bride it be to be say that she be now much mend , and she 
 cause man bear she on a litter out into the Marketplace , that she 
 might look on the departure of her folk . she have see face - of - god 
 once and again since the Day of Battle , and each time have be kind 
 and blithe with he ; and for Iron - face , she love he so well that 
 she be ever loth to let he depart from she , save when folk - might 
 be with she . 

 and now be the Alderman stand beside she , and she say to he : 
 ' Friend and kinsman , this be the day of departure , and though I must 
 need abide behind , and be content to abide , yet doth mine heart ache 
 with the sundering ; for to - morrow when I wake in the morning there 
 will be no more sending of a messenger to fetch thee to I . indeed , 
 great hath be the love between I and my people , and nought hath 
 come between we to mar it . now , kinsman , I would see gold - mane , my 
 cousin , that I may bid he farewell ; for who knoweth if I shall see 
 he again hereafter ? ' 

 then go iron - face and find face - of - god where he be speak with 
 Folk - might and the chieftain , and say to he : 

 ' come quickly , for thy cousin the Bride would speak with thee . ' 

 face - of - god redden , and pal afterwards , but he go along with 
 his father silently ; and his heart beat as he come and stand before 
 the litter whereas the Bride lay , clothe all in white and prop up on 
 fair cushion of red silk . she be frail to look on , and wear and 
 pale yet ; but he deem that she be very happy . 

 she smile on he , and reach out her hand and say : 

 ' welcome once more , cousin ! ' and he hold her hand and kiss it , and 
 be nigh weeping , so sore be he beset by a throng of memory 
 concern she and he in the day when they be little ; and he 
 bethink he of her love - kindness of past day , beyond that of 
 most child , beyond that of most maiden ; and how there be nothing 
 in his life but she have a share in it , till the day when he find the 
 Hall on the Mountain . 

 so he say to she : ' Kinswoman , be it well with thee ? ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' I be now nigh whole of my hurt . ' 

 he be silent a while ; then he say : 

 ' and otherwise art thou merry at heart ? ' 

 ' yea , indeed , ' say she ; ' yet thou wilt not find it hard to deem that 
 I be sorry of the sundering betwixt I and Burgdale . ' 

 again be he silent , and say in a while : ' dost thou deem that I 
 work that sunder ? ' 

 she smile kindly on he and say : ' gold - mane , my playmate , thou art 
 become a mighty warrior and a great chief ; but thou art not so mighty 
 as that . many thing lie behind the sundering which be neither 
 thou nor I. ' 

 ' yet , ' say he , ' it be but such a little time agone that all thing 
 seem so sure ; and we - to both of we be the outlook happy . ' 

 ' let it be happy still , ' she say , ' now begrudge be go . Belike 
 the sundering come because we be so sure , and have no defence 
 against the wearing of the day ; even as it fareth with a folk that 
 hath no foe . ' 

 he smile and say : ' even as it hath befall THY folk , o Bride , a 
 while ago . ' 

 she redden , and reach her hand to he , and he take it and hold 
 it , and say : ' shall I see thee again as the day wear ? ' 

 say she : ' o chieftain of the Folk , thou shalt have much to do in 
 Burgdale , and the way be long . yet would I have thee see my 
 child . forget not the token on my hand which thou holdest . but 
 now get thee to thy folk with no more word ; for after all , playmate , 
 the sundering be grievous to I , and I would not spin out the time 
 thereof . farewell ! ' 

 he say no more , but stoop down and kiss her lip , and then 
 turn from she , and take his way to the head of the Host , and fall 
 to ask and answering , and bidding and arraying ; and in a little 
 time be his heart dance with joy to think of the day that lay 
 before he , wherein now all seem happy . 

 so be all array for departure when it lack three hour of noon . 
 as Folk - might have promise , there be certain light wain draw by 
 bullock abide the departure of the Host , and of sumpter bullock 
 and horse no few ; and all these be laden with fair gift of the 
 Dale , as silver , and raiment , and weapon . there be many thing 
 fair - wrought in the time of the Sorrow , that henceforth should see 
 but little sorrow . moreover , there be plenty of provision for the 
 way , both meal and wine , and sheep and neat ; and all thing as fair 
 as might be , and well - arrayed . 

 it be the Shepherds who be to lead the way ; and after they be 
 array the man of the Vine and the Sickle ; then they of the Steer , 
 the Bridge , and the Bull ; and lastly the House of the Face , with old 
 stone - face lead they . the Sun - beam be to journey along with the 
 House of the Steer , which have take she in as a maiden of their 
 blood ; and though she have so much liefer have fare with the House of 
 the Face , yet she go meekly as she be bid , as one who have 
 get a great thing , and will make no stir about a small one . 

 along with she be Wood - father and Wood - mother , and Wood - wise , now 
 whole of his hurt , and wood - wont , and Bow - may . Save Bow - may , they 
 be not very joyous ; for they be fain of silver - dale , and it irk 
 they to leave it ; moreover , they also have liefer have go along with 
 the House of the war - leader . 

 last of all go those people of the once thrall of the Dusky Men 
 who have cast in their lot with the Burgdalers , and they be 
 exceed merry ; and especially the woman of they , they be 
 chatter like the stare in the autumn evening , when they gather 
 from the field in the tall elm - tree before they go to roost . 

 now all the man of the Dale , both of the kindred and of the thrall- 
 folk , make way for the Host and its having , that they might go their 
 way down the Dale ; albeit the Woodlanders clung close to the line of 
 their ancient friend , and with they , as man who be sorry for the 
 sundering , be Wolf - stone and God - swain and Spear - fist . but the 
 chief , they draw around Folk - might a little beside the way . 

 now red - coat of Waterless , who have be hurt , and be now whole 
 again , cast his arm about Folk - might and kiss he , and say : 

 ' all the way hence to Burgdale will I sow with good wish for thee 
 and thine , and especially for my dear friend God - swain of the Silver 
 Arm ; and I would wish and long that they might turn into spell to 
 draw thy foot to usward ; for we love thee well . ' 

 in like wise spake other of the Burgdalers ; and folk - might be kind 
 and blithe with they , and he say : 

 ' friend , forget ye not that the way be no long from you to we than 
 it be from we to you . one half of this matter it be for you to deal 
 with . ' 

 ' true be that , ' say Red - beard of the Knolls , ' but look you , folk- 
 might , we be but simple husbandman , and may not often stir from our 
 meadow and acre ; even now I bethink I that may be amidst we , and I 
 be begin to be draw by the thought of the haysel . whereas thou- 
 - '   and therewith he redden   ' I doubt that thou hast little to do 
 save the work of chieftain , and we know that such work be but little 
 miss if it be undo . ' 

 Thereat Folk - might laugh ; and when the other see that he laugh , 
 they laugh also , else have they foreborne for courtesy 's sake . 

 but folk - might answer : ' nay , chief of the Sickle , I be not 
 altogether a chieftain , now we have get we peace ; and somewhat of 
 a husbandman shall I be . moreover , doubt ye not that I shall do my 
 utmost to behold the fair Dale again ; for it be but mountain that 
 meet not . ' 

 now spake face - of - god to folk - might , smile and somewhat softly , and 
 say : ' be all forgive now , since the day when we first feel each 
 other 's arm ? ' 

 ' yea , all , ' say folk - might ; ' now hath befall what I foretold thee 
 in Shadowy Vale , that thou might pay for all that have come and 
 go , if thou would but look to it . indeed thou wert angry with 
 I for that say on that eve of Shadowy Vale ; but see thou , in 
 those day I be an old man than thou , and might admonish thee 
 somewhat ; but now , though but few day have go over thine head , yet 
 many deed have abide in thine hand , and thou art much aged . anger 
 hath leave thee , and wisdom hath wax in thee . as for I , I may now 
 say this word : may the Folk of Burgdale love the Folk of silver - dale 
 as well as I love thee ; then shall all be well . ' 

 then face - of - god cast his arm about he and kiss he , and turn 
 away toward stone - face and Hall - face his brother , where they stand at 
 the head of the array of the face ; and even therewith come up the 
 Alderman somewhat sad and sober of countenance , and he push by the 
 War - leader roughly and would not speak with he . 

 and now blow up the horn of the Shepherds , and they begin to move on 
 amidst the shouting of the man of silver - dale ; yet be there amongst 
 the Woodlanders those who weep when they see their friend verily 
 depart from they . 

 but when they of the foremost of the Host be get so far forward 
 that the man of the face could begin to move , lo ! there be redesman 
 with his fiddle amongst the leader ; and he have do a man 's work in 
 the day of battle , and all look kindly on he . about he on this 
 morn be some who have learn the craft of sing well together , 
 and know his minstrelsy , and he turn to these and nod as their 
 array move on , and he draw his bow across the string , and 
 straightway they fall a - singing , even as it might be thus : 


 back again to the dear Dale where bear be the kindre , 
  here wend we all live , and liveth our mirth . 
 here afoot fare our joyance , whatever man hindre , 
  through all wrath of the heaven , all storm of the earth . 

 o true , we have leave here a part of our treasure , 
  the ash of stout one , the stem of the shield ; 
 but the bold life they spend have sow we new pleasure , 
  fair tale for the telling in fold and on field . 

 for as oft as we sing of their edge ' upheaving , 
  when the yellow window shine forth o'er the night , 
 their name unforgotten with song interweave 
  shall draw forth dear drop from the depth of delight . 

 or when down by our foot the grey sickle be lie , 
  and behind we be curl the supper - tide smoke , 
 no whit shall they grudge we the joyance undye , 
  remembrance of man that put from we the yoke . 

 when the huddle of ewe from the fell we have drive , 
  and we see down the Dale the grey reach of the roof , 
 we shall tell of the gift in the battle - joy give , 
  all the fierceness of friend that drave sorrow aloof . 

 once then we lament , and mourn they depart ; 
  once only , no oftener . henceforth shall we fle 
 their name up aloft , when the merry hearte 
  to the Fathers unseen of our life - day we sing . 


 then be there silence in the rank of man ; and many murmur the 
 name of the fall as they fare on their way from out the market- 
 place of silver - stead . then once more Redesman and his mate take up 
 the song : 


 come tell I , o friend , for whom bideth the maiden 
  wet - foot from the river - ford down in the Dale ? 
 for whom hath the goodwife the ox - waggon laden 
  with the babble of child , brown - handed and hale ? 

 come tell I for what be the woman abide , 
  till each on the other aweary they lean ? 
 be it loiter of evil that thus they be chide , 
  the slow - footed bearer of sorrow unseen ? 

 Nay , yet be they toil if sorrow have wear they , 
  or hush have they bid with lip parch and wan . 
 the bird of the air other tiding have bear they - 
  how glad through the wood goeth man beside man . 

 then fare forth , o valiant , and loiter no long 
  than the cry of the cuckoo when May be at hand ; 
 late waxeth the spring - tide , and daylight grow long , 
  and nightly the star - street hang high o'er the land . 

 many life , many day for the Dale do ye carry ; 
  when the Host breaketh out from the thicket unshorn , 
 it shall be as the sun that refuseth to tarry 
  on the crown of all morning , the Midsummer morn . 


 again the song fall down till they be well on the western way down 
 silver - dale ; and then Redesman handle his fiddle once more , and 
 again the song rise up , and such - like be the word which be bear 
 back into the market - place of silver - stead : 


 and yet what be this , and why fare ye so slowly , 
  while our echo hall of our voice be dumb , 
 and abideth unlitten the hearth - brand the holy , 
  and the foot of the kind fare afield till we come ? 

 for not yet through the wood and its tangle ye wander ; 
  now skirt we no thicket , no path by the mere ; 
 far aloof for our foot lead the Dale - road out yonder ; 
  full fair be the morning , its doing all clear . 

 there be nought now our foot on the highway delaying 
  save the friend 's love - kindness , the sundering of speech ; 
 the well - willer 's word that end word with the saying , 
  the loth to depart while each looketh on each . 

 fare on then , for nought be ye laden with sorrow ; 
  the love of this land do ye bear with you still . 
 in two Dales of the earth for to - day and to - morrow 
  be wax the oak - tree of peace and good - will . 


 thus then they depart from Silver - dale , even as man who be a 
 portion thereof , and have not utterly leave it behind . and that night 
 they lie in the wild - wood not very far from the Dale 's end ; for they 
 go softly , fare amongst so many friend . 



 CHAPTER LVI . talk UPON the WILD - WOOD WAY 



 on the morrow morning when they be on their way again face - of - god 
 leave his own folk to go with the House of the Steer a while ; and 
 amongst they he fall in with the Sun - beam go along with Bow - may . 
 so they greet he kindly , and face - of - god fall into talk with the 
 Sun - beam as they go side by side through a great oak - wood , where 
 for a space be plain green - sward bare of all underwood . 

 so in their talk he say to she : ' what deem thou , my speech- 
 friend , concern our come back to guest in silver - dale one day ? ' 

 ' the way be long , ' she say . 

 ' that may hinder we but not stay we , ' say face - of - god . 

 ' that be sooth , ' say the Sun - beam . 

 say face - of - god : ' what thing shall stay we ? or deemest thou that 
 we shall never see silver - dale again ? ' 

 she smile : ' even so I think thou deemest , gold - mane . but many 
 thing shall hinder we besides the long road . ' 

 say he : ' yea , and what thing ? ' 

 ' thinkest thou , ' say the Sun - beam , ' that the winning of silver - stead 
 be the last battle which thou shalt see ? ' 

 ' Nay , ' say he , ' nay . ' 

 ' shall thy Dale - our Dale - be free from all trouble within itself 
 henceforward ? be there a wall build round it to keep out for ever 
 storm , pestilence , and famine , and the waywardness of its own folk ? ' 

 ' so it be as thou sayest , ' quoth face - of - god , ' and to meet such 
 trouble and overcome they , or to die in strife with they , this be a 
 great part of a man 's life . ' 

 ' yea , ' she say , ' and hast thou forget that thou art now a great 
 chieftain , and that the folk shall look to thee to use thee many day 
 in the year ? ' 

 he laugh and say : ' so it be . how many day have go by since I 
 wander in the wood last autumn , that the world should have change 
 so much ! ' 

 ' many deed shall now be in thy day , ' she say , ' and each deed as 
 the corn of wheat from which cometh many corn ; and a man 's day on 
 the earth be not over many . ' 

 ' then farewell , silver - dale ! ' say he , wave his hand toward the 
 north . ' war and trouble may bring I back to thee , but it maybe 
 nought else shall . farewell ! ' 

 she look on he fondly but unsmile , as he go beside her strong 
 and warrior - like . three pace from he go Bow - may , barefoot , in 
 her white kirtle , but bear her bow in her hand ; a leash of arrow 
 be in her girdle , her quiver hang at she back , and she be gird with 
 a sword . on the other side go wood - wont and Wood - wise , lightly 
 clothe but weapone . wood - mother be rid in an ox - wain just behind 
 they , and Wood - father go beside her bear an axe . scatter all 
 about they be the man of the Steer , gaily clothe , bear weapon , so 
 that the oak - wood be bright with they , and the glade merry with 
 their talk and singing and laughter , and before they down the glade 
 go the banner of the Steer , and the White Beast lead they the 
 near way to Burgdale . 



 CHAPTER LVII . how the host come HOME again 



 it be fourteen day before they come to Rose - dale ; for they have much 
 baggage with they , and they have no mind to weary themselves , and the 
 wood be nothing loathsome to they , whereas the weather be fair and 
 bright for the more part . they fall in with no mishap by the way . 
 but a score and three of runaway join themselves to the Host , 
 have watch their going and wotte that they be not foeman . 
 of these , some have hear of the overthrow of the Dusky Men in Silver- 
 dale , and other not . the Burgdalers receive they all , for it 
 seem to they no great matter for a score or so of new - comer to the 
 Dale . 

 but when the Host be come to Rose - dale , they find it fair arid 
 lovely ; and there they meet with those of their folk who have go with 
 Dallach . but Dallach welcome the kindred with great joy , and bade 
 they abide ; for he say that they have the less need to hasten , since 
 he have send messenger into Burgdale to tell man there of the 
 tiding . albeit they be mostly loth to tarry ; yet when he lie hard 
 on they not to depart as man on the morrow of a gild - feast , they 
 abode there three day , and be as well gueste as might be , and on 
 their departure they be laden with gift from the wealth of Rose- 
 dale by Dallach and his folk . 

 before they go their way Dallach spake with face - of - god and the 
 chief of the Dalesmen , and say : 

 ' ye have give I much from the time when ye find I in the wood a 
 naked wastrel ; yet now I would ask you a gift to lay on the top of 
 all that ye have give I . ' 

 say face - of - god : ' name the gift , and thou shalt have it ; for we 
 deem thee our friend . ' 

 ' I be no less , ' say Dallach , ' as in time to come I may perchance be 
 able to show you . but now I be ask you to suffer a score or two 
 of your man to abide here with I this summer , till I see how this 
 folk new - bear again be like to deal with I . for pleasure and a fair 
 life have become so strange to they , that they scarce know what to do 
 with they , or how to live ; and unless all be to go awry , I must need 
 command and forbid ; and though belike they love I , yet they fear I 
 not ; so that when my commandment pleaseth they , they do as I bid , and 
 when it pleaseth they not , they do contrary to my bidding ; for it 
 hath get into their mind that I shall in no case lift a hand against 
 they , which indeed be the very sooth . but your folk they fear as 
 warrior of the world , who have slay the Dusky Men in the market- 
 place of silver - stead ; and they be of alien blood to they , man who 
 will do as their friend biddeth   think our folk   against they who be 
 neither friend or foe . with such help I shall be well holpen . ' 

 in such wise spake Dallach ; and face - of - god and the chief say that 
 so it should be , if man could be find willing to abide in Rose - dale 
 for a while . and when the matter be put abroad , there be no lack 
 of such man amongst the young warrior , who have note that the dale 
 be fair amongst dale and its woman fair yet amongst woman . 

 so two score and ten of the burgdale man abode in Rose - dale , no one 
 of whom be of more than twenty and five winter . Forsooth diver of 
 they set up house in Rose - dale , and never come back to Burgdale , save 
 as guest . for a half score be wed in Rose - dale before the 
 year 's ending ; and seven more , who have also take to they wife of 
 the goodly of the rose - dale woman , betook they the next spring to 
 the Burg of the Runaways , and there build they a stead , and draw a 
 garth about it , and dig and sow the bank of the river , which they 
 call Inglebourne . and as year pass , this same stead throve 
 exceedingly , and man resort thither both from Rose - dale and 
 Burgdale ; for it be a pleasant place ; and the land , when it be 
 cure , be sweet and good , and the wood thereabout be full of deer 
 of all kind . so their stead be call Inglebourne after the 
 stream ; and in latter day it become a very goodly habitation of man . 

 moreover , some of the once - enthral folk of Rose - dale , when they 
 know that man of their kindre from Silver - dale be go home with 
 the man of Burgdale to dwell in the Dale , pray hard to go along 
 with they ; for they look on the Burgdalers as if they be new Gods 
 of the Earth . the Burgdale chief would not gainsay these man 
 either , but take with they three score and ten from Rose - dale , man 
 and woman , and promise they dwelling and livelihood in Burgdale . 

 so now with good heart the Host of Burgdale turn their face 
 toward their well - beloved Dale ; and they make good diligence , so that 
 in three day ' time they be come anigh the edge of the woodland 
 wilderness . Thither in the even - tide , as they be make ready for 
 their last supper and bed in the wood , come three man and two woman 
 of their folk , who have be abide their come ever since they have 
 have the tiding of silver - dale and the battle from Dallach . great 
 be the joy of these messenger as they go from company to company 
 of the warrior , and see the familiar face of their friend , and 
 hear their wonte voice tell all the story of battle and 
 slaughter . and for their part the man of the Host feast these 
 stay - at - home , and make much of they . but one of they , a man of the 
 House of the Face , leave the host a little after nightfall , and bore 
 back to Burgstead at once the tiding of the come home of the Host . 
 albeit since Dallach 's tiding of victory have come to the Dale , the 
 dweller in the stead of the country - side have leave Burgstead and 
 go home to their own house ; so that there be no great multitude 
 abide in the Thorp . 

 so early on the morrow be the Host astir ; but ere they come to 
 Wildlake 's Way , the Shepherd - folk turn aside westward to go home , 
 after they have bid farewell to their friend and fellow of the 
 Dale ; for their soul long for the sheepcote in the wind 
 valley under the long grey down ; and the garth where the last 
 year 's rick shoulder up against the old stone gable , and where 
 the daw be busy in the tall unfrequent ash - tree ; and the green 
 flowery meadow adown along the bright stream , where the crowfoot 
 and the paigle be bloom now , and the harebell be in flower 
 about the thorn - bush at the down 's foot , whence go the savour of 
 their blossom over sheep - walk and water - meadow . 

 so these go their way with many kind word ; and two hour 
 afterwards all the rest of the Host stand on the level ground of the 
 Portway ; but presently be the rank of war disorder and break up 
 by the joy of the woman and child , as they fall to draw goodman 
 or brother or lover out of the throng to the way that lead speediest 
 to their homestead and hall . for the war - leader would not hold the 
 host together any long , but suffer each man to go to his home , 
 deeming that the man of Burgstead , and chiefly they of the Face and 
 the Steer , would suffice for a company if any need be , and they

 CHAPTER LVIII . how the MAIDEN WARD be hold in BURGDALE 



 now May be well wear when the Host come home to Burgdale ; and on the

 the sun will not tarry ; now changeth the light , 
 fail the colour that marry the day to the Night . 

 amid the sun 's burn bright weapon we bore , 
 for this eve of our earning come once and no more . 

 for to - day hath no brother in yesterday 's tide ,

 this day be the token of oath and behest

 be the Dale for our living and dear day therein . 

 o Sun , now thou wanest ! yet come back and see

 o witness of sorrow wide over the earth , 
 rise up on the morrow to look on our mirth !

 and thy bird be a - singing each summer again . 

 but to man little - hearted what winter be bad

 lo the seeker , the finder of Death in the Blade ! 
 what lip shall be kind on lip of mine lay ?

 Sweet - breathed be thine even , but sweeter his mouth .

 in the garnering of glory that no man may hide . 

 o sun sink under ! o fragrance of earth ! 
 o heart ! o the wonder whence longing have birth !

 above all , when he take the Bride once more in his arm , and caress 
 she whom he have deem should be a very daughter of his House . 

 now telleth the tale of all these kindred , to wit , the Men of 
 Burgdale and the Sheepcotes ; and the child of the Wolf , and the 
 Woodlanders , and the Men of Rose - dale , that they be friend 
 henceforth , and become as one Folk , for well or bad , in peace and 
 in war , in wan and waxing ; and that whatsoever befell they , they 
 ever hold Shadowy Vale a holy place , and for long and long after they 
 meet there in mid - autumn , and hold converse and counsel together . 

 no MORE as now TELLETH the tale of these KINDREDS and FOLKS , but 
 MAKETH an ending .
